

code with mosh sql:
===================

Let's start with a quick overview of Databases, what they are and how we use them. A database is a collection of data stored in a format that can easily be accessed. In order to manage our Databases, we use a software application called Database Management System or DBMS. We connect to a DBMS and give it instructions for querying or modifying data. The DBMS will execute our instructions and send results back. 

Now, we have several database management systems out there, and these are classified into two categories. 
Relational and non-relational, also called NoSQL. In Relational Databases, we store data in tables that are linked to each other using relationships. That's why we call these databases Relational Databases. Each table stores data about a specific type of object, like customer, product, order, and so on. 
SQL is the language that we use to work with these Relational Database Management Systems. It looks like this. 

SELECT *
FROM products
WHERE category = 'food'
ORDER BY price


We use SQL to query or modify our data. In this course, you're going to learn everything about this powerful language. Now, there are many different Relational database management systems out there. Some of the most popular ones are MySQL, SQL Server by Microsoft, and Oracle. But of course, there are plenty more. Each Database management system has a different flavor of SQL, But all these implementations are very similar and are based on the standard SQL specification. So most of the SQL code that you will learn in this course will work with any Database Management system. In this course, we'll be using MySQL, which is the most popular open source Database in the world. 

Alright, Now, What about Non-relational databases? In Non-relational databases, we don't have tables or relationships. These databases are very different from Relational databases, but that's a topic for an entirely different course. What you need to know is that Non-relational Database Management Systems don't understand SQL. They have their own query language. So we use SQL to work with Relational Database Management Systems. 

Now, before we jump in and install MySQL, let me clarify something quickly. As you talk to different people, you will hear two different pronunciations of SQL, SQL or Sequel. What is the correct way? Well, it depends on who you ask. And of course, everybody thinks their way of pronouncing this word is the right way. But here's a little history about this language. 
SQL was originally developed at IBM in the 70s, and back then it was initially called SeQuEL, short for Structured English Query Language. But they changed the acronym to SQL because Sequel was the trademark of an airplane company. So to this date, there's been an argument about what is the right way to pronounce this language. Generally speaking, people in non-English speaking countries call it SQL. I'm used to calling it SQL because it's shorter and sweeter than SQL. But if you prefer to call it SQL, that's totally fine with me. I'm not going to get mad at you. So that's the history behind this language. But what about MySQL as a software product? Developers of this product prefer to call it MySQL rather than MySQL, but they don't mind if we call it MySQL. 
In this course, I'll be teaching you SQL with MySQL.
 
 
 
 
What is SQL:
------------

ğŸš€ Introduction to Databases:
A database is a structured collection of data that allows for easy access, management, and modification. To interact with databases, we use Database Management Systems (DBMS), which handle storing, retrieving, updating, and deleting data efficiently.




ğŸ“Œ Types of Database Management Systems:
1. Relational Databases (SQL-Based)
âœ” Data is stored in tables that are linked using relationships. 
âœ” Each table represents a specific entity (e.g., customers, orders, products). 
âœ” SQL (Structured Query Language) is used to query and modify data. 
âœ” Examples of Relational Database Management Systems (RDBMS):
MySQL (Open-source, widely used)
Microsoft SQL Server
PostgreSQL
Oracle Database
SQLite

ğŸ“Œ Example SQL Query to Retrieve Data:
sql
SELECT * 
FROM products 
WHERE category = 'food' 
ORDER BY price;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Retrieves all food products, ordered by price.




2. Non-Relational Databases (NoSQL)
âœ” Data is stored in flexible formats such as key-value pairs, documents, graphs, or columns. 
âœ” Does not use SQLâ€”each system has its own query language. 
âœ” Ideal for handling large, unstructured, or rapidly changing data. 
âœ” Examples of NoSQL Databases:
MongoDB (Document-based)
Redis (Key-value store)
Cassandra (Column-based)
Neo4j (Graph-based)

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” NoSQL databases offer flexibility, making them suitable for big data, real-time analytics, and large-scale applications.




ğŸ“Œ Understanding SQL vs NoSQL
Feature	SQL         (Relational)	                     NoSQL (Non-Relational)
Data Structure	    Tables (rows & columns)	             Flexible (JSON, key-value, graphs)
Relationships	    Uses Foreign Keys	                 No predefined relationships
Scalability	        Vertical (powerful server)	         Horizontal (distributed servers)
Query Language	    SQL	                                 NoSQL-specific syntax
Examples	        MySQL, PostgreSQL, SQL Server	     MongoDB, Redis, Cassandra

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” SQL is structured, best for transactions; NoSQL is flexible, ideal for handling large-scale dynamic data.





ğŸ“Œ History & Pronunciation of SQL
âœ” Originally called "SeQuEL" (Structured English Query Language). 
âœ” The name was changed to SQL after legal issues with an airplane company. 
âœ” Pronounced both "SQL" and "Sequel"â€”preferences vary globally.

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Both pronunciations are widely accepted, though MySQL developers prefer "MySQL" over "MySequel."




ğŸ“Œ Why MySQL?
âœ” MySQL is the most popular open-source database. 
âœ” Used by Facebook, Twitter, YouTube, and countless websites. 
âœ” Lightweight, fast, and reliable for handling structured data.

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” In this course, we'll use MySQL to learn SQL and database management.

ğŸš€ Installing MySQL on Mac & Windows:
-------------------------------------
This guide provides step-by-step instructions for installing MySQL on both Mac and Windows, setting up MySQL Workbench, and creating a connection to the MySQL database server.

ğŸ“Œ Installing MySQL on Mac
1. Download MySQL
âœ” Open your browser and go to MySQL Downloads. 
âœ” Scroll down and click on MySQL Community Edition (free version). 
âœ” Click MySQL Community Server â†’ Select MacOS DMG Archive. 
âœ” Click on "No thanks, just start my download" to download the file.



2. Install MySQL
âœ” Open the downloaded .dmg file. 
âœ” Double-click the package and follow the installation wizard:
Accept the license agreement.
Enter your Mac password (system login password).
Set a password for the MySQL root user. âœ” Click Finish, and MySQL is now installed.



3. Install MySQL Workbench
âœ” Go to MySQL Community Edition â†’ Find MySQL Workbench. 
âœ” Download the MacOS DMG Archive version. 
âœ” Open the .dmg file and drag MySQL Workbench into Applications.

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” You now have MySQL and MySQL Workbench installed!




ğŸ“Œ Setting Up MySQL Workbench on Mac:
1. Open MySQL Workbench
âœ” Press Command + Space â†’ Search MySQL Workbench â†’ Open it. 
âœ” If prompted, click "Open" to confirm security settings.



2. Create a Connection
âœ” If a default connection is available, use it. 
âœ” Otherwise, click on the + (plus) icon to create a new connection.

âœ” Fill in the following details:
Connection Name: Local Instance
Method: TCP/IP
Host: 127.0.0.1 (Local Machine)
Port: 3306 (Default MySQL Port)
Username: root

âœ” Click "Store in Keychain" â†’ Enter the root password set during installation. 
âœ” Click Test Connection â†’ Should show "Successful Connection".

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” MySQL Workbench is now connected to your database server.




ğŸ“Œ Installing MySQL on Windows
1. Download MySQL
âœ” Open MySQL Downloads â†’ Select MySQL Community Edition. 
âœ” Click MySQL Community Server â†’ Download MySQL Installer for Windows. 
âœ” Click on "No thanks, just start my download" to download the installer.



2. Install MySQL
âœ” Open the MySQL Installer file. 
âœ” Select Developer Default Setup and click Next. 
âœ” Follow the installation wizard and click Next:
If prompted, ignore the Python 3.7 warning (not required for MySQL). 
âœ” Click Execute â†’ Wait for installation (5-10 minutes). 
âœ” Click Next until prompted to set a password for the root user. 
âœ” Enter a secure password and proceed. 
âœ” Click Finish, and MySQL is installed!




3. Install MySQL Workbench
âœ” After installing MySQL Server, MySQL Workbench should be installed automatically. 
âœ” Open MySQL Workbench â†’ Create a new connection (similar to Mac setup):
Connection Name: Local Instance
Host: 127.0.0.1
Port: 3306
Username: root
âœ” Store the root password, test the connection, and click OK.

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” You have now successfully installed MySQL on Windows.

ğŸ“Œ Exploring MySQL Workbench
âœ” The navigator panel (left) lists all databases. 
âœ” The query editor (middle) is where we write SQL queries. 
âœ” The right panel contains snippets and SQL history.

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” You can now start writing SQL queries and managing databases!




ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” Installed MySQL on Mac & Windows. 
âœ” Set up MySQL Workbench to connect to the database server. 
âœ” Created a local connection to MySQL. 
âœ” Next steps: Creating databases and executing SQL queries!

Retrieving Data From a Single Table: 
------------------------------------

The SELECT Statement: 
---------------------
ğŸš€ Understanding the SELECT Statement in SQL:
In this tutorial, we will: 
âœ” Learn how to retrieve data from a single table using SQL's SELECT statement.
âœ” Understand how to filter (WHERE clause) and sort (ORDER BY clause) data. 
âœ” Explore SQL syntax rules and best practices.

ğŸ“Œ 1. Selecting a Database for Querying
Before executing SQL queries, we must select the database we want to query.

ğŸ“Œ Syntax:

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” The sql_store database is now the active database for our queries.




ğŸ“Œ 2. Retrieving All Data From a Table:

ğŸ“Œ Syntax to retrieve all records from customers table:

FROM customers;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Fetches all columns and rows from the customers table.

âœ” Use -- to disable a line of SQL code without deleting it.

ğŸ“Œ Example (Commenting Out WHERE Clause):

SELECT * 
FROM customers
-- WHERE customer_id = 1
ORDER BY first_name;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” The query executes without filtering, showing all customers. âœ” Useful for temporary testing/debugging.




ğŸ“Œ 6. Selecting Custom Values (Without a Table):
ğŸ“Œ Retrieving static values:

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Returns two columns named 1 and 2, each containing static values.

ğŸ“Œ 7. SQL Syntax Rules & Best Practices:
âœ” SQL is not case-sensitive, but use uppercase for keywords (SELECT, WHERE). 
âœ” Terminate statements with a semicolon (;) for clarity. 
âœ” Place each clause on a new line to improve readability:

SELECT * 
FROM customers

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Clear and structured queries for easier debugging.




ğŸ“Œ 8. Understanding Clause Order in SQL
âœ” The order of SQL clauses must follow a strict sequence:

SELECT â†’ FROM â†’ WHERE â†’ ORDER BY

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Ensures queries are properly structured to avoid errors.

ğŸ“Œ Example of Incorrect Clause Order (Throws Syntax Error):

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” This will fail because SELECT must come before FROM.

The SELECT Clause: 
------------------

ğŸš€ Understanding the SELECT Clause in SQL
The SELECT clause is one of the most fundamental parts of SQL, used for retrieving data from a table. This tutorial explores how to use it efficiently, apply arithmetic operations, use aliases, and remove duplicate values.

ğŸ“Œ 1. Retrieving Specific Columns vs. All Columns:
âœ” The simplest way to retrieve all data from a table is using an asterisk (*):

SELECT * 
FROM customers;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Returns all columns in the customers table.

ğŸ“Œ Retrieving Specific Columns âœ” Instead of fetching all data, we can select only the necessary columns:
SELECT first_name, last_name 
FROM customers;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Displays only the first_name and last_name columns, reducing load on the database.

ğŸ“Œ Changing Column Order âœ” Columns can be retrieved in any order:
SELECT last_name, first_name 
FROM customers;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” The order of columns in the output matches the order of selection.




ğŸ“Œ 2. Using Arithmetic Expressions in Queries:
âœ” SQL allows performing mathematical operations directly in queries.
ğŸ“Œ Example: Modifying Points Based on Customer Shopping
SELECT last_name, first_name, points + 10 
FROM customers;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Adds 10 to each customer's points, useful for calculating discounts.

ğŸ“Œ Complex Arithmetic Expression âœ” SQL supports multiple operators (+, -, *, /, %):
SELECT 
    last_name,
    first_name, 
    points * 10 + 100
FROM customers;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Multiplies points by 10, then adds 100 for a new discount calculation.




ğŸ“Œ 3. Understanding Operator Precedence in SQL
âœ” Just like in math, multiplication (*) and division (/) are processed before addition (+) and subtraction (-).

ğŸ“Œ Example Without Parentheses:
SELECT 
    last_name,
    first_name, 
    points + 10 * 100
FROM customers;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” First multiplies 10 * 100, then adds the result to points. âœ” If this is not what we want, use parentheses.

ğŸ“Œ Example With Parentheses (Changing the Order)

SELECT 
    last_name,
    first_name, 
    (points + 10) * 100 AS discount_factor
FROM customers;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” First adds 10 to points, then multiplies the result by 100.

ğŸ“Œ Visualization of Operator Precedence:
Without Parentheses:   points + (10 * 100)
With Parentheses:     (points + 10) * 100
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Parentheses ensure clarity and correct execution of calculations.




ğŸ“Œ 4. Using Aliases (AS) to Rename Columns
âœ” Column names in SQL output are based on expressions used in queries. âœ” We can make names more readable using AS.

ğŸ“Œ Assigning a Custom Name (Alias):
SELECT 
    last_name,
    first_name, 
    points * 10 + 100 AS discount_factor
FROM customers;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Renames the calculated column for better readability.

ğŸ“Œ Adding Spaces in Aliases (Using Quotes):
SELECT 
    last_name,
    first_name, 
    points * 10 + 100 AS 'Discount Factor'
FROM customers;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” If column names require spaces, use single or double quotes.




ğŸ“Œ 5. Removing Duplicate Values Using DISTINCT
âœ” The DISTINCT keyword removes duplicate entries from the result set.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Get Unique States from customers Table
SELECT DISTINCT state 
FROM customers;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Retrieves only unique states, eliminating duplicate entries.

ğŸ“Œ Why Use DISTINCT? âœ” Prevents redundancy when analyzing categorical data (e.g., unique cities, job titles).




ğŸ“Œ 6. SQL Exercise: Product Price Calculation
ğŸš€ Task: 
âœ” Retrieve product names and prices. 
âœ” Apply a 10% price increase. 
âœ” Use an alias (new_price) for better clarity.

ğŸ“Œ Expected Query Solution:
SELECT name, unit_price, unit_price * 1.1 AS new_price 
FROM products;

ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” SELECT retrieves specific columns or all columns (*). 
âœ” Arithmetic expressions allow calculations within queries. 
âœ” Operator precedence determines the order of executionâ€”use parentheses for clarity. 
âœ” Aliases (AS) rename columns, improving readability. 
âœ” DISTINCT removes duplicate values from results. 
âœ” SQL syntax must follow structured ordering (SELECT â†’ FROM â†’ WHERE â†’ ORDER BY).










The WHERE Clause: 
-----------------
ğŸš€ Understanding the WHERE Clause in SQL:
The WHERE clause in SQL allows us to filter data based on conditions, ensuring we retrieve only relevant records. This tutorial covers comparison operators, filtering string, numeric, and date values, and an exercise to solidify understanding.

ğŸ“Œ 1. Using the WHERE Clause to Filter Data:
Retrieve Customers With Points Greater Than 3000

SELECT * 
FROM Customers
WHERE points > 3000;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Filters records, returning only customers who have more than 3000 points.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:

âœ” The query execution engine checks each customer, evaluates the condition, and returns matching records.




ğŸ“Œ 2. Understanding Comparison Operators:
SQL provides several comparison operators for filtering data:

>, >=, <, <=, =, !=, <>

ğŸ“Œ Examples Using Comparison Operators 
âœ” Retrieve customers born after January 1st, 1990:

SELECT * 
FROM Customers
WHERE birth_date > '1990-01-01';

âœ” Retrieve customers from Virginia (VA):

SELECT * 
FROM Customers
WHERE state = 'VA';

âœ” Retrieve customers NOT from Virginia (VA):

SELECT * 
FROM Customers
WHERE state != 'VA';

âœ” Alternative syntax for NOT EQUAL (<>):

SELECT * 
FROM Customers
WHERE state <> 'VA';

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” All comparison operators work with numbers, text, and dates.




ğŸ“Œ 3. Working With String Filtering
âœ” Strings in SQL must be enclosed in quotes ('single' or "double"). 
âœ” SQL is not case-sensitive, so both queries below return the same result:

SELECT * 
FROM Customers
WHERE state = 'VA';

SELECT * 
FROM Customers
WHERE state = 'va';

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Retrieves all customers located in Virginia, regardless of uppercase/lowercase.




ğŸ“Œ 4. Filtering Data Using Date Values
âœ” SQL stores dates in the format YYYY-MM-DD. âœ” We can compare dates using comparison operators.

ğŸ“Œ Example Query: Customers Born After 1990:

SELECT * 
FROM Customers
WHERE birth_date > '1990-01-01';

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Retrieves only customers born after January 1, 1990.

The AND, OR and NOT Operators:
------------------------------
Combining Conditions in SQL Using AND, OR, and NOT Operators:
In this tutorial, we will learn how to filter data using multiple conditions, understand operator precedence, and apply logical operators (AND, OR, NOT) in SQL queries.

ğŸ“Œ 1. Using AND to Combine Conditions
The AND operator returns rows where all specified conditions are true.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Get Customers Born After 1990 & With More Than 1000 Points

SELECT * 
FROM Customers
WHERE birth_date > '1990-01-01' AND points > 1000;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Retrieves only customers who satisfy both conditions.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ CustomerIDâ”‚ FirstName â”‚ BirthDate â”‚ Points    â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ 5         â”‚ Clemmie   â”‚ 1991-09-04â”‚ 3675      â”‚

â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜
âœ” This query only returns customers who satisfy both conditions.




ğŸ“Œ 2. Using OR for Flexible Filtering:
The OR operator returns rows where at least one condition is true.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Get Customers Born After 1990 OR With More Than 1000 Points

SELECT * 
FROM Customers
WHERE birth_date > '1990-01-01' OR points > 1000;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Expands the result set, returning customers who satisfy at least one condition.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ CustomerIDâ”‚ FirstName â”‚ BirthDate â”‚ Points    â”‚

â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜
âœ” Customers are retrieved if at least one condition matches.




ğŸ“Œ 3. Combining AND & OR With Parentheses:
When combining multiple logical operators, order matters â€” use parentheses to control execution.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Customers Born After 1990 OR (Have 1000+ Points & Live in Virginia)

SELECT * 
FROM Customers
WHERE birth_date > '1990-01-01' OR 
      (points > 1000 AND state = 'VA');

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Customers who were born after 1990 OR who live in Virginia AND have over 1000 points.

ğŸ“Œ Understanding Precedence 
âœ” AND is evaluated first, then OR. 
âœ” Parentheses override default precedence and group conditions.


ğŸ“Œ Correct Precedence Order:
1. (points > 1000 AND state = 'VA') â†’ Evaluated first
2. birth_date > '1990-01-01' â†’ Evaluated after OR condition
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Ensures logical grouping and correct evaluation.




ğŸ“Œ 4. Using NOT to Negate Conditions:
The NOT operator reverses a condition, excluding matching rows.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Get Customers NOT Born After 1990 OR Who Don't Have More Than 1000 Points

SELECT * 
FROM Customers
WHERE NOT (birth_date > '1990-01-01' OR points > 1000);

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Retrieves customers born before 1990 & with 1000 or fewer points.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:

The IN Operator:
----------------
ğŸš€ Using the IN Operator in SQL:
The IN operator allows us to simplify multiple OR conditions, making SQL queries more concise and readable.


ğŸ“Œ 1. Understanding the Need for the IN Operator:
Using OR for Filtering Multiple Values
ğŸ“Œ Example: Retrieve Customers Located in Virginia, Georgia, or Florida

SELECT * 
FROM Customers
WHERE state = 'VA' OR state = 'GA' OR state = 'FL';

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Checks each row individually, leading to a long and repetitive query.

ğŸ“Œ Incorrect Syntax Attempt (What Doesnâ€™t Work)

FROM Customers
WHERE state = 'VA' OR 'GA' OR 'FL';  -- âŒ Incorrect syntax

ğŸš€ Why Doesn't This Work? 
âœ” SQL requires explicit conditions for each comparison. 
âœ” Strings cannot be combined with boolean expressions.




ğŸ“Œ 2. Simplifying with the IN Operator
Using IN for Cleaner Filtering

FROM Customers
WHERE state IN ('VA', 'GA', 'FL');

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Retrieves customers from Virginia, Georgia, or Florida. âœ” Shorter and easier to understand compared to using multiple OR conditions.

The BETWEEN Operator: 
---------------------

ğŸš€ Understanding the BETWEEN Operator in SQL:
The BETWEEN operator simplifies range-based filtering, making SQL queries more efficient and readable.

ğŸ“Œ 1. Filtering Data Using BETWEEN:
Using Comparison Operators to Retrieve Customers With Points Between 1000 and 3000

SELECT * 
FROM customers
WHERE points >= 1000 AND points <= 3000;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Retrieves customers whose points fall within the range of 1000 to 3000.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:

âœ” The query execution engine evaluates each row, returning matching records.




ğŸ“Œ 2. Simplifying Queries Using the BETWEEN Operator
âœ” Instead of using multiple comparison operators (>=, <=), BETWEEN provides a cleaner syntax.

ğŸ“Œ Equivalent Query Using BETWEEN:

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” The result is identical to using >= and <=, but the query is shorter and more readable.

ğŸ“Œ Understanding BETWEEN Behavior 

âœ” BETWEEN includes boundary values, meaning:

BETWEEN 1000 AND 3000 â†’ Equivalent to â‰¥ 1000 AND â‰¤ 3000
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Both the lower (1000) and upper (3000) limits are included.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Using BETWEEN With Date Values
âœ” The BETWEEN operator works with both numbers and dates.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Retrieve Customers Born Between 1990 and 2000:

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Retrieves customers born in the date range, including January 1, 1990 and January 1, 2000.

The LIKE Operator:
------------------
ğŸš€ Understanding the LIKE Operator in SQL:
The LIKE operator allows us to filter textual data based on patterns, enabling flexible searching in SQL queries.

ğŸ“Œ 1. Using LIKE for Pattern Matching
Example: Retrieve Customers Whose Last Name Starts With "B":

SELECT * 
FROM customers
WHERE last_name LIKE 'b%';

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Returns customers whose last name begins with "B", regardless of the number of following characters. âœ” Not case-sensitiveâ€”"B" matches both uppercase and lowercase.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”

â”‚ 3         â”‚ Freddi    â”‚ Boagey    â”‚ CO        â”‚

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Matches all last names beginning with "B".




ğŸ“Œ 2. Using Wildcards for Flexible Searching:
Wildcard Characters:
Wildcard	Function
%	        Matches any number of characters (including none)
_	        Matches exactly one character

ğŸ“Œ Example Queries: 
âœ” Retrieve last names that start with "Brush":
SELECT * 
FROM customers
WHERE last_name LIKE 'brush%';

âœ” Retrieve last names that contain "B" anywhere:
SELECT * 
FROM customers
WHERE last_name LIKE '%b%';

âœ” Retrieve last names ending with "Y":
SELECT * 
FROM customers
WHERE last_name LIKE '%y';
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” % ensures matches can be anywhere in the string.




ğŸ“Œ 3. Using _ (Underscore) for Exact Character Matching

âœ” Match last names that are exactly two characters long, ending with "Y":
SELECT * 
FROM customers
WHERE last_name LIKE '_y';
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Matches one-character last names followed by "Y" (if any exist).

âœ” Match last names with exactly five characters before "Y":
SELECT * 
FROM customers
WHERE last_name LIKE '_____y';
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Retrieves last names with exactly six characters, ending with "Y".

âœ” Match last names starting with "B" and having exactly four more characters before "Y":
SELECT * 
FROM customers
WHERE last_name LIKE 'b____y';
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Returns specific patterns with strict character matching.




ğŸ“Œ 4. Using LIKE With NOT for Excluding Patterns:
âœ” Retrieve customers whose phone numbers DO NOT end with "9":

SELECT * 
FROM customers
WHERE phone NOT LIKE '%9';
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Matches all phone numbers except those ending in "9".

FROM customers
WHERE last_name LIKE '%y';

SELECT * 
FROM customers
WHERE last_name LIKE '_y';

SELECT * 
FROM customers
WHERE last_name LIKE '_____y';

SELECT * 
FROM customers
WHERE last_name LIKE 'b____y';

	  address LIKE '%avenue%';

WHERE phone LIKE '%9';

ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” LIKE filters string patterns using % and _. 
âœ” % matches any number of characters, while _ matches exactly one character. 
âœ” NOT LIKE excludes specific patterns from results. 
âœ” SQL is case-insensitive, making searches flexible. 
âœ” Next steps: Learning how to filter data efficiently using REGEXP for advanced text matching.
















The REGEXP Operator: 
--------------------

ğŸš€ Using the REGEXP Operator in SQL:
The REGEXP operator allows for advanced text pattern matching in SQL, making searches more flexible and powerful than the LIKE operator.

ğŸ“Œ 1. Understanding Basic REGEXP Usage:
Using LIKE vs. REGEXP
ğŸ“Œ Using LIKE to Find Customers With "Field" in Last Name

SELECT * 
FROM customers
WHERE last_name LIKE '%field%';

ğŸ“Œ Using REGEXP for the Same Query

SELECT * 
FROM customers
WHERE last_name REGEXP 'field';

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” The results are identical, but REGEXP allows more advanced pattern matching.




ğŸ“Œ 2. Special Characters in REGEXP
Caret (^): Matches the Beginning of a String
âœ” Find customers whose last name starts with "Field":

SELECT * 
FROM customers
WHERE last_name REGEXP '^field';

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Retrieves records only where "Field" appears at the beginning.

Dollar Sign ($): Matches the End of a String
âœ” Find customers whose last name ends with "Field":

SELECT * 
FROM customers
WHERE last_name REGEXP 'field$';
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Matches records where "Field" appears at the end.

Pipe (|): OR Condition for Multiple Patterns
âœ” Find customers whose last name contains "Field", "Mac", or "Rose":

SELECT * 
FROM customers
WHERE last_name REGEXP 'field|mac|rose';
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Retrieves records matching any of the given patterns.

Combining Special Characters
âœ” Find customers whose last name starts with "Field" OR contains "Mac" OR "Rose":

SELECT * 
FROM customers
WHERE last_name REGEXP '^field|mac|rose';
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Ensures "Field" must be at the beginning, while "Mac" or "Rose" can be anywhere.




ğŸ“Œ 3. Using Square Brackets [ ] for Character Matching
âœ” Find customers whose last name contains "E", preceded by G, I, or M:

SELECT * 
FROM customers
WHERE last_name REGEXP '[gim]e';
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Ensures "E" is preceded by either "G", "I", or "M".

âœ” Find customers whose last name contains "E", followed by F, M, or Q:

SELECT * 
FROM customers
WHERE last_name REGEXP 'e[fmq]';
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Ensures "E" is followed by "F", "M", or "Q".




ğŸ“Œ 4. Using Ranges in Character Matching
âœ” Find customers whose last name contains "E", preceded by any letter from A to H:

SELECT * 
FROM customers
WHERE last_name REGEXP '[a-h]e';
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Searches for a last name containing "E", preceded by letters A-H.




ğŸ“Œ 5. Exercise: Using REGEXP for Advanced Searches
ğŸš€ Task: âœ” Retrieve customers based on the following conditions: 
1.First names should be Elka or Ambur. 
2.Last names should end with "EY" or "ON". 
3.Last names should start with "MY" or contain "SE". 
4.Last names should contain "B" followed by "R" or "U".

ğŸ“Œ Expected Query Solutions:

SELECT * 
FROM customers
WHERE first_name REGEXP 'elka|ambur';
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Matches Elka and Ambur.


SELECT * 
FROM customers
WHERE last_name REGEXP 'ey$|on$';
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Retrieves last names ending with "EY" or "ON".


SELECT * 
FROM customers
WHERE last_name REGEXP '^my|se';
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Retrieves last names starting with "MY" or containing "SE".


SELECT * 
FROM customers
WHERE last_name REGEXP 'b[ru]';
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Ensures "B" is followed by "R" or "U".

âœ” Alternative query using OR condition:


SELECT * 
FROM customers
WHERE last_name REGEXP 'br|bu';
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Matches exact phrases "BR" or "BU" following "B".





Queries in this lesson:

FROM customers
WHERE last_name REGEXP 'field'

SELECT * 
FROM customers
WHERE last_name REGEXP '^field'

SELECT * 
FROM customers
WHERE last_name REGEXP 'field$'

SELECT * 
FROM customers
WHERE last_name REGEXP 'field|mac|rose'

SELECT * 
FROM customers
WHERE last_name REGEXP 'field$|mac|rose'

SELECT * 
FROM customers
WHERE last_name REGEXP '[gim]e'

SELECT * 
FROM customers
WHERE last_name REGEXP '[a-h]e';

-- 	 first names are ELKA or AMBUR

SELECT * 
FROM customers

ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” REGEXP allows flexible pattern matching beyond LIKE. 
âœ” Special characters (^, $, |, [ ]) enhance text filtering precision. 
âœ” Ranges ([a-h]) provide a powerful way to search multiple characters efficiently. 
âœ” SQL regular expressions simplify complex searches, making queries concise. 
âœ” Next steps: Learning how to handle NULL values and missing data in SQL queries.











The IS NULL Operator: 
---------------------
ğŸš€ Understanding the IS NULL Operator in SQL:
The IS NULL operator is used to identify missing values in a database, allowing us to filter records where certain attributes are absent.

ğŸ“Œ 1. Identifying NULL Values in a Table:
âœ” NULL represents missing or unknown data. 
âœ” If a column contains NULL, it means no value has been assigned.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Retrieve Customers Without a Phone Number

SELECT * 
FROM customers
WHERE phone IS NULL;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Filters customers who do not have a phone number.

The ORDER BY Clause:
--------------------

ğŸš€ Understanding the ORDER BY Clause in SQL:
The ORDER BY clause in SQL allows us to sort query results based on one or more columns in ascending or descending order.

ğŸ“Œ 1. Default Sorting Behavior
âœ” By default, MySQL orders query results based on the primary key column (typically id). 
âœ” When we select all customers, they appear sorted by customer_id:

SELECT * 
FROM customers;

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ CustomerIDâ”‚ Name      â”‚ State         â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ 1         â”‚ Barbara   â”‚ VA            â”‚

âœ” The sorting is based on customer_id, as indicated by the primary key.




ğŸ“Œ 2. Sorting Data Using ORDER BY
âœ” We can change the sorting order using the ORDER BY clause.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Sorting Customers by First Name

SELECT * 
FROM customers 
ORDER BY first_name;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Customers are sorted alphabetically by first name in ascending order.

ğŸ“Œ Sorting in Descending Order âœ” To reverse the order, use DESC:

sql
SELECT * 
FROM customers 
ORDER BY first_name DESC;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Customers are sorted in reverse alphabetical order (Z-A).




ğŸ“Œ 3. Sorting By Multiple Columns
âœ” We can sort by multiple attributes, ensuring nested ordering.

ğŸ“Œ Example: First Sort By State, Then By First Name

SELECT * 
FROM customers 
ORDER BY state, first_name;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Customers are sorted alphabetically by state, and then by first name within each state.

ğŸ“Œ Mixing ASC & DESC for Multi-Level Sorting âœ” First sort by state in descending order, then by first name in ascending order:

SELECT * 
FROM customers 
ORDER BY state DESC, first_name ASC;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Data appears in reverse alphabetical order by state, but names remain in ascending order within each state.




ğŸ“Œ 4. Ordering Data by Non-Selected Columns
âœ” Unlike some database systems, MySQL allows sorting by columns not included in the SELECT clause.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Selecting Only Names, But Sorting By Birth Date

SELECT first_name, last_name 
FROM customers 
ORDER BY birth_date;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Only first_name and last_name are shown, but data is sorted by birth_date.




ğŸ“Œ 5. Sorting Using Aliases
âœ” We can sort by expressions or aliases.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Sorting By an Alias (points)

SELECT first_name, last_name, 10 AS points 
FROM customers 
ORDER BY points, first_name;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Even though points is not a real column, MySQL still allows sorting by its alias.




ğŸ“Œ 6. Avoiding Sorting By Column Positions
âœ” Some tutorials suggest sorting by column positions (ORDER BY 1, 2), but this is unreliable.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Sorting By Column Positions (Avoid This)

SELECT first_name, last_name, 10 AS points 
FROM customers 
ORDER BY 1, 2;

ğŸš€ Why Avoid This? 
âœ” If a new column is added before first_name, sorting behavior changes unexpectedly. 
âœ” Always sort by column names instead.

ğŸ“Œ Expected Query Solution

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Sorts items in order #2 by their total price, highest first.

The LIMIT Clause: 
-----------------
ğŸš€ Understanding the LIMIT Clause in SQL:
The LIMIT clause allows us to control the number of records returned in a SQL query. It is especially useful for pagination and retrieving top results.

ğŸ“Œ 1. Limiting Query Results
By default, when we execute a query like:

SELECT * 
FROM customers;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” It retrieves all records in the customers table. âœ” If the table contains 10 customers, the result will show all 10.

ğŸ“Œ Using LIMIT to Return Only the First 3 Customers:

SELECT * 
FROM customers
LIMIT 3;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Retrieves only the first 3 rows from the table.

ğŸ“Œ If LIMIT Exceeds Available Records:

SELECT * 
FROM customers
LIMIT 300;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Since there are only 10 customers, this query will return all 10 records. âœ” LIMIT will never exceed available data.




ğŸ“Œ 2. Using LIMIT With OFFSET for Pagination
âœ” The LIMIT clause allows us to skip records using OFFSET, ensuring efficient pagination.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Displaying Customers in Pages of 3

Page 1 â†’ Customers 1, 2, 3
Page 2 â†’ Customers 4, 5, 6
Page 3 â†’ Customers 7, 8, 9

ğŸ“Œ Retrieving Customers for Page 3

SELECT * 
FROM customers
LIMIT 6, 3;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Skips the first 6 records and retrieves the next 3 customers.

âœ” Alternative Syntax Using OFFSET

SELECT * 
FROM customers
LIMIT 3 OFFSET 6;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Ensures data is retrieved efficiently, helping websites paginate content properly.




ğŸ“Œ 3. Exercise: Retrieving the Top 3 Loyal Customers
ğŸš€ Task: âœ” Retrieve the top 3 customers with the highest points.

ğŸ“Œ Expected Query Solution:

SELECT * 
FROM customers
ORDER BY points DESC
LIMIT 3;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Customers are first sorted by points in descending order (DESC). âœ” Then, only the top 3 records are retrieved.

âœ” The most loyal customers appear first.

ğŸ“Œ Important SQL Clause Order âœ” The LIMIT clause must always come at the end of a query.

SELECT â†’ FROM â†’ WHERE â†’ ORDER BY â†’ LIMIT
âœ” Changing this order causes errors.

FROM customers
LIMIT 6 OFFSET 3;


Exercise: 
--Get the top three loyal customers

Solution: 
SELECT * 
FROM customers
ORDER BY points DESC
LIMIT 3;



ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” LIMIT controls the number of records returned. 
âœ” LIMIT with OFFSET enables pagination, helping display data in pages. 
âœ” Sorting must happen before limiting records, ensuring accurate results. 
âœ” SQL follows strict query structure (SELECT â†’ FROM â†’ WHERE â†’ ORDER BY â†’ LIMIT). 










Retrieving Data From Multiple Tables: 
-------------------------------------


Inner Joins:
------------
ğŸš€ Understanding INNER JOINS in SQL:
In real-world applications, data is spread across multiple tables, and JOIN operations allow us to retrieve related data efficiently. This tutorial covers INNER JOINS, their syntax, usage, and best practices.

ğŸ“Œ 1. Why Do We Need Joins?
âœ” Tables are structured to avoid redundancy and store related data separately. 
âœ” For example:
The orders table stores order details, but not customer information.
The customers table stores customer details, but not order details.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Orders Table:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”

â”‚ 1         â”‚ 3         â”‚ 2025-05-17â”‚
â”‚ 2         â”‚ 7         â”‚ 2025-05-16â”‚
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜
âœ” The customer_id column links orders to customers.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Customers Table:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”

âœ” To retrieve an order with customer details, we join both tables.




ğŸ“Œ 2. Using INNER JOIN to Merge Data:
âœ” INNER JOIN matches records between two tables based on a common key (e.g., customer_id).

ğŸ“Œ Retrieve Orders Along With Customer Names:

SELECT order_id, first_name, last_name
FROM orders
JOIN customers 
ON orders.customer_id = customers.customer_id;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Merges both tables, ensuring only matching records appear.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output

âœ” Now we see customer names instead of just IDs.




ğŸ“Œ 3. Handling Column Name Ambiguity:
âœ” If both tables contain a column with the same name (customer_id), MySQL requires explicit table prefixes.

ğŸ“Œ Example Query That Causes an Error:

SELECT order_id, customer_id, first_name, last_name
FROM orders
JOIN customers 
ON customer_id = customer_id;  -- âŒ Ambiguous!

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” This throws an errorâ€”MySQL doesn't know which customer_id to use.

ğŸ“Œ Correct Query Using Table Prefixes

SELECT order_id, orders.customer_id, first_name, last_name
FROM orders
JOIN customers 
ON orders.customer_id = customers.customer_id;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Explicit table names resolve ambiguity, making the query valid.




ğŸ“Œ 4. Using Table Aliases for Simplicity
âœ” To avoid repetition, use short aliases for tables.

ğŸ“Œ Assigning Aliases (o for orders, c for customers)

SELECT order_id, o.customer_id, first_name, last_name
FROM orders o
JOIN customers c
ON o.customer_id = c.customer_id;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Query remains clear, but avoids redundant table names.




ğŸ“Œ 5. Exercise: Joining Order Items with Products
ğŸš€ Task: âœ” Retrieve product details for each order item from order_items and products.

ğŸ“Œ Expected Query Solution:

SELECT order_id, oi.product_id, quantity, oi.unit_price

JOIN products p 
ON oi.product_id = p.product_id;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Displays product details alongside order quantities.

âœ” The product_id is retrieved along with quantity and unit price.




ğŸ“Œ 6. Understanding Price Data Storage:
âœ” The unit_price column exists in both order_items and products tables.

ğŸ“Œ Why Does unit_price Exist Twice? 
âœ” Products Table (products.unit_price) â†’ Stores current price. 
âœ” Order Items Table (order_items.unit_price) â†’ Stores historical price at time of order.

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Ensures accurate historical pricing, allowing proper sales calculations.

Joining Across Databases: 
-------------------------

ğŸš€ Joining Across Databases in SQL:
In real-world applications, developers and database administrators often work with multiple databases. This tutorial demonstrates how to join tables across different databases, ensuring proper data integration.

ğŸ“Œ 1. Why Join Tables Across Databases?
âœ” Large applications may store data in separate databases for organization and security. 
âœ” Sometimes, identical tables exist in multiple databases, but queries require combining them.

ğŸ“Œ Example Scenario: 
âœ” The sql_store database contains customer orders (order_items). 
âœ” The sql_inventory database contains product details (products). 
âœ” We need to retrieve order items from sql_store, along with product details from sql_inventory.




ğŸ“Œ 2. Writing Queries to Join Tables Across Databases
âœ” When a table exists in another database, it must be prefixed with the database name.

ğŸ“Œ Joining order_items (from sql_store) With products (from sql_inventory):

SELECT * 
FROM order_items oi
JOIN sql_inventory.products p 
ON oi.product_id = p.product_id;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Joins order items from sql_store with product details from sql_inventory.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:

â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤

ğŸ“Œ 3. Selecting a Different Database for Querying
âœ” The current database determines which tables need prefixing. âœ” The USE statement sets the active database.

ğŸ“Œ Changing the Active Database to sql_inventory:

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Sets sql_inventory as the current database, affecting queries.

ğŸ“Œ Key Takeaways
âœ” Tables from other databases must be prefixed with the database name (sql_inventory.products). 
âœ” If the table exists in the current database, no prefix is required. 
âœ” Changing the active database (USE database_name;) alters query requirements.

ğŸš€ Use cross-database joins to retrieve data seamlessly across systems!












Self Joins:
-----------
ğŸš€ Understanding Self Joins in SQL:
A self-join occurs when a table is joined with itself, often used to compare rows within the same table.

ğŸ“Œ 1. Why Use Self Joins?
âœ” Some tables reference themselves, such as hierarchical structures. 
âœ” In the sql_hr database, employees report to other employees, making a self-referencing relationship.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Employees Table:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ EmployeeIDâ”‚ FirstName â”‚ Job Title â”‚ Salary    â”‚ ReportsTo â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤

â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜
âœ” The ReportsTo column references EmployeeID within the same table.




ğŸ“Œ 2. Performing a Self Join:
âœ” To retrieve employees along with their managers, we join the employees table with itself.

ğŸ“Œ Joining Employees With Their Managers:

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Links each employee with their manager, forming a hierarchical structure.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:

Joining Multiple Tables:
------------------------

ğŸš€ Joining Multiple Tables in SQL:
In SQL, joining multiple tables allows us to combine related data from different sources into a single query result. This tutorial covers joining three tables, its practical use, and an exercise to reinforce learning.

ğŸ“Œ 1. Understanding Multi-Table Joins
âœ” The orders table contains order details but does not store customer or status names. 
âœ” The customers table contains customer details. 
âœ” The order_statuses table contains order status names.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Orders Table:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ OrderID   â”‚ CustomerIDâ”‚ Status    â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ 1         â”‚ 6         â”‚ 1         â”‚
â”‚ 2         â”‚ 3         â”‚ 2         â”‚
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜
âœ” The status column stores an order status ID, not a name.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Order Statuses Table:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ OrderStatusID â”‚ Name      â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ 1             â”‚ Processed â”‚
â”‚ 2             â”‚ Shipped   â”‚
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜
âœ” This table provides readable status names.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Writing a Multi-Table Join Query
ğŸ“Œ Retrieving Orders With Customer Names and Status:

USE sql_store;

SELECT 
    o.order_id, 
    o.order_date,
    c.first_name, 
    c.last_name,
    os.name AS status
FROM orders o
JOIN customers c 
    ON o.customer_id = c.customer_id
JOIN order_statuses os 
    ON o.status = os.order_status_id;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Retrieves order details along with customer names and order statuses.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ OrderID   â”‚ OrderDate â”‚ FirstName â”‚ LastName  â”‚ Status    â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤

âœ” Orders now display full customer names and status names.




ğŸ“Œ 3. How Multi-Table Joins Work
âœ” SQL executes joins sequentially, first linking orders â†’ customers, then orders â†’ order_statuses. 
âœ” Queries can join multiple tables in a single statement using additional JOIN clauses. 
âœ” Aliases (o, c, os) simplify queries for better readability.

ğŸ“Œ Query Breakdown:
JOIN customers connects orders with customers, matching customer IDs.
JOIN order_statuses connects orders with statuses, mapping the status ID.

Compound Join Conditions:
-------------------------

ğŸš€ Understanding Compound Join Conditions in SQL:
While many tables use a single column to uniquely identify rows (customer_id in customers), some require multiple columns for uniqueness. This tutorial covers composite primary keys and joining tables using multiple conditions.

ğŸ“Œ 1. Identifying Composite Keys in Tables:
âœ” Some tables cannot use a single column for unique identification. 
âœ” Example: order_items table contains repeated values in order_id and product_id.

ğŸ“Œ Example: order_items Table:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ OrderID   â”‚ ProductID â”‚ Quantity  â”‚ UnitPrice â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ 2         â”‚ 1         â”‚  2        â”‚  9.10     â”‚
â”‚ 2         â”‚ 4         â”‚  4        â”‚  1.66     â”‚
â”‚ 2         â”‚ 6         â”‚  2        â”‚  2.94     â”‚
â”‚ 6         â”‚ 1         â”‚  4        â”‚  8.65     â”‚
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜
âœ” Neither order_id nor product_id alone can uniquely identify records. 
âœ” The combination of order_id and product_id creates a unique identifier.

ğŸ“Œ Understanding Composite Primary Keys 
âœ” The primary key (PK) is a combination of multiple columns (order_id & product_id). 
âœ” This ensures each order item is uniquely tracked.




ğŸ“Œ 2. Joining Tables With a Composite Key:
âœ” When joining a table with a composite key, both key columns must be included in the join condition.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Joining order_items With order_item_notes:

SELECT * 
FROM order_items oi
JOIN order_item_notes oin 
	ON oi.order_id = oin.order_id 
	AND oi.product_id = oin.product_id;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Matches order items with their corresponding notes, ensuring precise record association.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ OrderID   â”‚ ProductID â”‚ Quantity  â”‚ NoteID    â”‚ Note       â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ 2         â”‚ 1         â”‚  2        â”‚ 1         â”‚ First note â”‚
â”‚ 2         â”‚ 1         â”‚  2        â”‚ 2         â”‚ Second noteâ”‚
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜
âœ” Each order item is correctly linked with its notes.




Queries in this discussion:

SELECT *
FROM order_items oi
JOIN order_item_notes oin
	ON oi.order_id = oin.order_id
	AND oi.product_id = oin.product_id




ğŸ“Œ Key Takeaways
âœ” Some tables require composite keys (multiple columns) for unique identification. 
âœ” Joining tables with composite keys requires multiple conditions (ON order_id AND product_id). 
âœ” Ensures precise data retrieval and accurate joins. 












Implicit Join Syntax:
---------------------

ğŸš€ Understanding Implicit Join Syntax in SQL:
MySQL allows two methods for joining tables: 
Explicit Join Syntax (Recommended âœ…) 
Implicit Join Syntax (Not Recommended âš ï¸)



ğŸ“Œ 1. Explicit vs. Implicit Joins:
âœ” Explicit joins use the JOIN keyword, ensuring clarity and structure. 
âœ” Implicit joins place multiple tables in the FROM clause and define join conditions in WHERE.

ğŸ“Œ Explicit Join (Recommended):

SELECT * 
FROM orders o
JOIN customers c 
ON o.customer_id = c.customer_id;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Directly links orders with customers, ensuring structured queries.


ğŸ“Œ Implicit Join (Not Recommended):
SELECT * 
FROM orders o, customers c 
WHERE o.customer_id = c.customer_id;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Produces the same result, but poses risks if the WHERE clause is omitted.




ğŸ“Œ 2. Risks of Implicit Join Syntax:
âœ” If the WHERE clause is mistakenly omitted, MySQL performs a CROSS JOIN, multiplying data unexpectedly.

ğŸ“Œ Example of Missing WHERE Clause (Cross Join):

SELECT * 
FROM orders o, customers c;  -- âš ï¸ No Join Condition!

ğŸš€ Effect: 
âœ” Every order combines with every customer, creating unexpected results. 
âœ” If the orders table contains 10 records and customers contains 10 records, the result produces 100 rows instead of 10.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output of a Cross Join:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ OrderID   â”‚ CustomerIDâ”‚ OrderDate â”‚ Customer  â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ 1         â”‚ 1         â”‚ 2025-05-17â”‚ Alice     â”‚
â”‚ 1         â”‚ 2         â”‚ 2025-05-17â”‚ Bob       â”‚
â”‚ 1         â”‚ 3         â”‚ 2025-05-17â”‚ Charlie   â”‚
â”‚ 2         â”‚ 1         â”‚ 2025-05-16â”‚ Alice     â”‚
â”‚ 2         â”‚ 2         â”‚ 2025-05-16â”‚ Bob       â”‚
â”‚ 2         â”‚ 3         â”‚ 2025-05-16â”‚ Charlie   â”‚
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜
âœ” Orders incorrectly combine with all customers, leading to data inconsistency.




ğŸ“Œ 3. Why Explicit Joins Are Safer
âœ” Explicit syntax forces the join condition, preventing unintended CROSS JOINS. 
âœ” If the join condition is missing, MySQL throws an error instead of generating incorrect results.

ğŸ“Œ Explicit Join Preventing Cross Join:

SELECT * 
FROM orders o
JOIN customers c;  -- âŒ ERROR (Join condition required)

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Ensures correctness by enforcing required conditions.





Queries in this discussion: 

SELECT *
FROM orders o
JOIN customers c
	ON o.customer_id = c.customer_id;
	
	
--Implicit Join Syntax
SELECT *
FROM orders o, customers c
WHERE o.customer_id = c.customer_id;




ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” Explicit joins (JOIN ON) ensure structured queries and prevent errors. 
âœ” Implicit joins (FROM table1, table2 WHERE) allow mistakes if the WHERE condition is forgotten. 
âœ” Avoid implicit joins to prevent accidental cross joins. 












Outer Joins: 
------------
ğŸš€ Understanding Outer Joins in SQL:
SQL joins allow us to combine data from multiple tables. So far, we've explored inner joins, which return only matching records between tables. However, outer joins retrieve all records from one table, even if there is no match in the other.

ğŸ“Œ 1. Difference Between Inner and Outer Joins
âœ” Inner Join: Returns only records that match in both tables. 
âœ” Outer Join: Returns all records from one table, even if there is no match in the second table.

ğŸ“Œ Inner Join Example:
SELECT c.customer_id, c.first_name, o.order_id
FROM customers c
JOIN orders o 
ON c.customer_id = o.customer_id
ORDER BY c.customer_id;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Only customers who have placed orders are retrieved.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output (Inner Join):
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ CustomerIDâ”‚ FirstName â”‚ OrderID   â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ 2         â”‚ Ines      â”‚ 4         â”‚
â”‚ 2         â”‚ Ines      â”‚ 7         â”‚
â”‚ 5         â”‚ Clemmie   â”‚ 12        â”‚

âœ” Customers without orders are missing.




ğŸ“Œ 2. Using LEFT JOIN to Retrieve All Customers
âœ” LEFT JOIN returns all records from the left table, even if no match exists in the right table.

ğŸ“Œ Retrieve All Customers, Including Those Without Orders:

SELECT c.customer_id, c.first_name, o.order_id
FROM customers c
LEFT JOIN orders o 
ON c.customer_id = o.customer_id
ORDER BY c.customer_id;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” All customers appear in the result, whether or not they have orders.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output (Left Join):
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”

â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ 1         â”‚ John      â”‚ NULL      â”‚ âŒ No orders

â”‚ 2         â”‚ Ines      â”‚ 7         â”‚ âœ… Has orders

âœ” Customers without orders display NULL values for order_id.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Using RIGHT JOIN to Retrieve All Orders
âœ” RIGHT JOIN returns all records from the right table, ensuring all orders appear, even if no matching customer exists.

ğŸ“Œ Retrieve All Orders, Including Those Without Customers:

SELECT c.customer_id, c.first_name, o.order_id
FROM orders o
RIGHT JOIN customers c 
ON c.customer_id = o.customer_id
ORDER BY c.customer_id;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” All orders appear, even if no customer exists.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output (Right Join)

â”‚ CustomerIDâ”‚ FirstName â”‚ OrderID   â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ 1         â”‚ John      â”‚ NULL      â”‚ âŒ No orders
â”‚ 2         â”‚ Ines      â”‚ 4         â”‚ âœ… Has orders
â”‚ 2         â”‚ Ines      â”‚ 7         â”‚ âœ… Has orders
â”‚ NULL      â”‚ NULL      â”‚ 10        â”‚ âŒ No matching customer
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜
âœ” Orders without customers display NULL values for customer_id.

ğŸ“Œ Understanding Table Order âœ” LEFT JOIN â†’ Ensures all customers appear. âœ” RIGHT JOIN â†’ Ensures all orders appear. âœ” Swapping tables affects results.




ğŸ“Œ 4. Exercise: Retrieve Products, Including Those Never Ordered
ğŸš€ Task: âœ” Retrieve all products, even those never ordered.

ğŸ“Œ Expected Query Solution:

SELECT p.product_id, p.name, oi.quantity 
FROM products p
LEFT JOIN order_items oi 
ON p.product_id = oi.product_id;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” All products appear, even if not ordered.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output (Left Join):

ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” INNER JOIN â†’ Only matching records from both tables. 
âœ” LEFT JOIN â†’ All records from left table, even if no match in right. 
âœ” RIGHT JOIN â†’ All records from right table, even if no match in left. 
âœ” OUTER keyword is optional (LEFT JOIN = LEFT OUTER JOIN). 













Outer Joins Between Multiple Tables: 
------------------------------------

ğŸš€ Using Outer Joins Between Multiple Tables in SQL
Just like inner joins, outer joins can be used to combine multiple tables, ensuring all records from one table appear, even if no match exists in another table.

ğŸ“Œ 1. Why Use Multiple Outer Joins?
âœ” Some tables contain missing values that can affect query results. 
âœ” Outer joins ensure all records from the primary table remain, even if some fields are NULL.

ğŸ“Œ Example Scenario 
âœ” The customers table contains customer details. 
âœ” The orders table contains customer orders, but not all customers have placed orders. 
âœ” The shippers table contains shipping companies, but not all orders have been shipped.

ğŸ“Œ 2. Combining Customers and Orders With a LEFT JOIN
âœ” If we join customers with orders using an INNER JOIN, only customers with orders appear.

ğŸ“Œ Inner Join (Excludes Customers Without Orders):
SELECT c.customer_id, c.first_name, o.order_id
FROM customers c
JOIN orders o 
ON c.customer_id = o.customer_id
ORDER BY c.customer_id;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Filters out customers who havenâ€™t placed orders, leading to missing data.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output (Inner Join):
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ CustomerIDâ”‚ FirstName â”‚ OrderID   â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤

â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜
âœ” Customers without orders are missing.


ğŸ“Œ Fixing Missing Data With LEFT JOIN:
SELECT c.customer_id, c.first_name, o.order_id
FROM customers c
LEFT JOIN orders o 
ON c.customer_id = o.customer_id
ORDER BY c.customer_id;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Ensures all customers appear, even those without orders.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output (Left Join):

âœ” NULL values indicate missing orders.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Adding Shipper Details With Another Left Join
âœ” Some orders havenâ€™t been shippedâ€”we must ensure all orders appear, even if no shipper exists.

ğŸ“Œ Joining orders With shippers Using LEFT JOIN:

SELECT c.customer_id, c.first_name, o.order_id, sh.name AS shipper

âœ” Orders without a shipper show NULL values.





ğŸ“Œ 4. Exercise: Retrieving Orders With Customer, Shipper, and Status
ğŸš€ Task: âœ” Retrieve all orders with their customer names, shipping companies, and order statuses.

ğŸ“Œ Expected Query Solution:

SELECT 
    o.order_id, 
    o.order_date,

ğŸ“Œ 5. Key Takeaways

Self Outer Joins:
-----------------

ğŸš€ Understanding Self Outer Joins in SQL
A self-join occurs when a table is joined with itself, often used to analyze hierarchical relationships such as employees and managers.

ğŸ“Œ 1. Why Use Self Joins With Outer Joins?
âœ” In the sql_hr database, the employees table stores manager relationships using the reports_to column. 
âœ” This column references the employee_id of the manager, linking employees within the same table.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Employees Table:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ EmployeeIDâ”‚ FirstName â”‚ Job Title â”‚ Salary    â”‚ ReportsTo â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ 37270     â”‚ John      â”‚ CEO       â”‚ 120000    â”‚ NULL      â”‚
â”‚ 33391     â”‚ Sarah     â”‚ Manager   â”‚ 75000     â”‚ 37270     â”‚
â”‚ 37851     â”‚ Alex      â”‚ Engineer  â”‚ 65000     â”‚ 33391     â”‚
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜
âœ” John is the CEO, meaning reports_to = NULL. âœ” Sarah reports to John, and Alex reports to Sarah.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Performing a Self Join
âœ” To retrieve employees along with their managers, we join the employees table with itself.

ğŸ“Œ Joining Employees With Their Managers (Using INNER JOIN)

USE sql_hr;

SELECT 
    e.employee_id,
    e.first_name, 
    m.first_name AS manager
FROM employees e
JOIN employees m 
ON e.reports_to = m.employee_id;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Lists only employees who have a manager.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output (Inner Join):
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ EmployeeIDâ”‚ FirstName â”‚ Manager   â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ 33391     â”‚ Sarah     â”‚ John      â”‚ âœ… Has manager
â”‚ 37851     â”‚ Alex      â”‚ Sarah     â”‚ âœ… Has manager
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜
âœ” John (CEO) is missing, because he has no manager.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Using LEFT JOIN to Include All Employees
âœ” Some employees donâ€™t have a manager (e.g., CEO)â€”these must still appear in the result. 
âœ” LEFT JOIN ensures all employees are displayed, even if they have no manager.

ğŸ“Œ Fixing Missing Data With LEFT JOIN:

SELECT 
    e.employee_id,
    e.first_name, 
    m.first_name AS manager
FROM employees e
LEFT JOIN employees m 
ON e.reports_to = m.employee_id;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” All employees appear, even those without a manager.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output (Left Join):
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ EmployeeIDâ”‚ FirstName â”‚ Manager   â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ 37270     â”‚ John      â”‚ NULL      â”‚ âŒ No manager (CEO)
â”‚ 33391     â”‚ Sarah     â”‚ John      â”‚ âœ… Has manager
â”‚ 37851     â”‚ Alex      â”‚ Sarah     â”‚ âœ… Has manager
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜
âœ” John (CEO) is now included with a NULL manager value.





Queries used in this discussion: 

USE sql_hr;

SELECT 
	e.employee_id,
	e.first_name, 
	m.first_name AS manager
FROM employees e
JOIN employees m 
	ON e.reports_to = m.employee_id;

	e.employee_id,
	e.first_name, 
	m.first_name AS manager
FROM employees e
LEFT JOIN employees m 
	ON e.reports_to = m.employee_id;







ğŸ“ŒKey Takeaways
âœ” Self-joins allow referencing rows within the same table, commonly used for hierarchical structures. 
âœ” Inner joins filter out records without matches, whereas left joins include all records. 
âœ” Explicit table aliases (e, m) simplify self-join queries. 
âœ” Next steps: Learning how to use FULL OUTER JOINS to retrieve all unmatched records from both tables.

ğŸš€ Use self outer joins for complete hierarchical relationships in SQL!



















The USING Clause:
-----------------

ğŸš€ Using the USING Clause in SQL Joins:
The USING clause in SQL simplifies join conditions when column names are identical across tables. This tutorial covers how to use USING, when to use ON instead, and best practices for multiple-column joins.

ğŸ“Œ 1. Understanding the USING Clause
âœ” The standard way to join tables uses the ON clause. 
âœ” If column names are identical, USING makes queries shorter and easier to read.

ğŸ“Œ Standard Join Condition Using ON:

SELECT 
    o.order_id, 
    c.first_name
FROM orders o
JOIN customers c
ON o.customer_id = c.customer_id;
ğŸ“Œ Equivalent Query Using USING


SELECT 
    o.order_id, 
    c.first_name
FROM orders o
JOIN customers c 
USING (customer_id);

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Both queries produce identical results, but the second is simpler.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Joining Multiple Tables With USING
âœ” USING works for multiple tables, provided column names match in all tables.

ğŸ“Œ Joining orders With shippers Using USING


SELECT 
    o.order_id, 
    c.first_name,
    sh.name AS shipper
FROM orders o
JOIN customers c 
USING (customer_id)
LEFT JOIN shippers sh 
USING (shipper_id);

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Retrieves orders, customers, and shippers, simplifying the query.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ OrderID   â”‚ Customer  â”‚ Shipper   â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ 1         â”‚ Alex      â”‚ FedEx     â”‚
â”‚ 2         â”‚ Maria     â”‚ NULL      â”‚ âŒ Not shipped
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜
âœ” Orders without shippers display NULL values.





ğŸ“Œ 3. When USING Cannot Be Used
âœ” USING only works when the column name is identical in both tables. 
âœ” If names are different, we must use ON instead.

ğŸ“Œ Example: 
The order_statuses Table âœ” In orders, the column name is status, but in order_statuses, itâ€™s order_status_id.

ğŸ“Œ Query Using ON Instead of USING

SELECT 
    o.order_id, 
    os.name AS status
FROM orders o
JOIN order_statuses os 
ON o.status = os.order_status_id;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” USING cannot be applied, because the column names do not match.





ğŸ“Œ 4. Using USING With Composite Keys
âœ” If a table has a composite primary key, we must join using multiple columns.

ğŸ“Œ Standard Join Using ON:
SELECT * 
FROM order_items oi
JOIN order_item_notes oin 
ON oi.order_id = oin.order_id 
AND oi.product_id = oin.product_id;

ğŸ“Œ Simplified Query Using USING:
SELECT * 
FROM order_items oi
JOIN order_item_notes oin 
USING (order_id, product_id);

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Avoids redundancy when joining tables with composite keys.





ğŸ“Œ 5. Exercise: Using USING With the Payments Table
ğŸš€ Task: âœ” Retrieve payments with client names and payment methods using USING where possible.

ğŸ“Œ Expected Query Solution:

SELECT 
    p.date, 
    c.name AS client,
    p.amount, 
    pm.name AS payment_method
FROM payments p
JOIN clients c USING (client_id)
JOIN payment_methods pm 
ON p.payment_method = pm.payment_method_id;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Uses USING for client_id, but ON for payment_method_id.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ Date      â”‚ Client    â”‚ Amount        â”‚ Method      â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ 2025-05-01â”‚ Vinte     â”‚ 250.00        â”‚ Credit Card â”‚

â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜
âœ” Payments are correctly linked to clients and payment methods.





Queries used in this discussion:

	o.order_id, 
	c.first_name
FROM orders o
JOIN customers c
	ON o.customer_id = c.customer_id;

	o.order_id, 
	c.first_name,
	sh.name AS shipper
FROM orders o
JOIN customers c
	USING (customer_id)
LEFT JOIN shippers sh
	USING (shipper_id);
	
		
SELECT * 
FROM order_items oi
JOIN order_item_notes oin 
	ON oi.order_id = oin.order_id 
	AND oi.product_id = oin.product_id;
	
	
SELECT * 
FROM order_items oi
JOIN order_item_notes oin 
	USING (order_id, product_id);

	ON p.payment_method = pm.payment_method_id
	
	





ğŸ“Œ Key Takeaways
âœ” USING simplifies join conditions, but only works when column names match. 
âœ” For different column names, use ON instead. 
âœ” Multiple columns can be joined using USING (col1, col2). 
âœ” Next steps: Learning how to optimize queries using indexes and query performance techniques.

ğŸš€ Use the USING clause for cleaner and more readable SQL joins!





	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
Natural Joins:
--------------

ğŸš€ Understanding Natural Joins in SQL
A Natural Join is a simplified way to join tables, where the database automatically detects matching columns. 
While easy to write, Natural Joins can be unpredictable, making explicit joins preferable.

ğŸ“Œ 1. How Natural Joins Work
âœ” Natural Joins automatically match columns with the same name in both tables. 
âœ” Unlike explicit joins (ON condition), the database engine determines the join condition.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Joining orders With customers Using a Natural Join

SELECT 
    o.order_id,
    c.first_name
FROM orders o
NATURAL JOIN customers c;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Automatically joins orders with customers, based on the customer_id column.

ğŸ“Œ Equivalent Explicit Join Using ON

SELECT 
    o.order_id,
    c.first_name
FROM orders o
JOIN customers c 
ON o.customer_id = c.customer_id;

âœ” Both queries produce the same result, but the explicit join ensures clarity.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Risks of Using Natural Joins
âœ” Loss of Control â†’ The database automatically determines the join columns, increasing the risk of accidental joins. 
âœ” Unexpected Results â†’ If tables contain multiple common columns, unintended joins may occur, leading to incorrect data retrieval.

ğŸ“Œ Why Explicit Joins Are Better? 
âœ” Explicit joins clearly define conditions, ensuring the intended result. 
âœ” Using explicit joins prevents accidental mismatches, unlike natural joins.





ğŸ“Œ 3. When to Avoid Natural Joins
âœ” If column names do not match across tables, natural joins cannot be used. 
âœ” If multiple common columns exist, the database may use unintended columns for joining.

ğŸ“Œ Example of a Potential Issue:
Orders Table â†’ customer_id, shipper_id
Customers Table â†’ customer_id, region_id

âœ” If both tables contain customer_id and region_id, the database may mistakenly join on both, causing data inconsistencies.




Queries in this discussion: 

SELECT 
	o.order_id,
	c.first_name
FROM orders o
NATURAL JOIN customers c






ğŸ“Œ Key Takeaways
âœ” Natural Joins automatically match common column names but should be used cautiously. 
âœ” Explicit joins (ON column1 = column2) ensure clarity, preventing unintended data mismatches. 
âœ” Avoid Natural Joins when multiple matching columns exist, as unexpected results may occur. 
âœ” Next steps: Learning cross joins and how they differ from natural joins.

ğŸš€ Use explicit joins for safe and predictable SQL queries!





















Cross Joins: 
------------
ğŸš€ Understanding Cross Joins in SQL
A Cross Join in SQL combines every record from the first table with every record from the second table, producing a Cartesian product.

ğŸ“Œ 1. What Is a Cross Join?
âœ” A Cross Join creates all possible combinations between two tables. 
âœ” Unlike other joins, there is no join condition (ON column = column). 
âœ” Each row from the first table combines with every row from the second table.

ğŸ“Œ Example Scenario âœ” We have customers and products, and we need to pair every customer with every product.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Explicit Cross Join Syntax
âœ” The explicit syntax uses the CROSS JOIN keyword.

ğŸ“Œ Query: Pairing Customers With Products

SELECT 
    c.first_name AS customer, 
    p.name AS product
FROM customers c
CROSS JOIN products p
ORDER BY c.first_name;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Each customer is paired with every product, producing a Cartesian product.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output (Cross Join):
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ Customer  â”‚ Product   â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ Amber     â”‚ Laptop    â”‚
â”‚ Amber     â”‚ Mouse     â”‚
â”‚ Amber     â”‚ Keyboard  â”‚
â”‚ Barbara   â”‚ Laptop    â”‚
â”‚ Barbara   â”‚ Mouse     â”‚
â”‚ Barbara   â”‚ Keyboard  â”‚
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜
âœ” Each customer is associated with every product.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Implicit Cross Join Syntax
âœ” Instead of using CROSS JOIN, we list both tables in the FROM clause.

ğŸ“Œ Query Using Implicit Syntax

SELECT 
    c.first_name AS customer, 
    p.name AS product
FROM customers c, products p
ORDER BY c.first_name;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Produces the same result, but explicit syntax is clearer.





ğŸ“Œ 4. When to Use Cross Joins?
âœ” Cross Joins are useful for generating all possible combinations between attributes.

ğŸ“Œ Example: 
Combining Sizes & Colors âœ” Assume we have a sizes table (small, medium, large) and a colors table (red, blue, green). âœ” A Cross Join will generate all possible combinations.

SELECT 
    s.size, 
    c.color
FROM sizes s
CROSS JOIN colors c;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Creates all color-size combinations, such as small-red, small-blue, large-green, etc.





ğŸ“Œ 5. Exercise: Cross Joining Shippers With Products

SELECT

FROM shippers sh, products p
ORDER BY sh.name;

ğŸ“Œ Expected Query Solution (Explicit Syntax)

    sh.name AS shipper, 
    p.name AS product 
FROM shippers sh
CROSS JOIN products p
ORDER BY sh.name;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Each shipper is paired with every product, creating a Cartesian result.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:

ğŸ“Œ Key Takeaways
âœ” Cross Joins create all possible record combinations, forming a Cartesian product. 
âœ” Explicit syntax (CROSS JOIN) is clearer than implicit syntax (FROM table1, table2). 
âœ” Useful for generating attribute combinations (e.g., size-color pairs).

Unions: 
-------
ğŸš€ Understanding UNION in SQL
SQL joins combine columns from multiple tables, but UNION allows combining rows from multiple tables. This is powerful for consolidating datasets, such as merging records from different time periods or tables.

ğŸ“Œ 1. What Is UNION in SQL?
âœ” UNION combines results from multiple queries into a single dataset. 
âœ” All queries must return the same number of columns and compatible data types. 
âœ” By default, UNION removes duplicates, but UNION ALL preserves all records.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Retrieve Orders, Labeling Them as Active or Archived

SELECT 
    order_id, 
    order_date, 
    'Active' AS status
FROM orders 
WHERE order_date >= '2019-01-01'
UNION 
SELECT 
    order_id, 
    order_date, 
    'Archived' AS status
FROM orders 
WHERE order_date < '2019-01-01';

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Retrieves all orders, marking current year orders as "Active" and past orders as "Archived".

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ OrderID   â”‚ OrderDate â”‚ Status    â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ 101       â”‚ 2025-03-10â”‚ Active    â”‚ âœ… Current Year
â”‚ 87        â”‚ 2024-08-22â”‚ Archived  â”‚ âŒ Previous Year
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜
âœ” Merges both datasets into one.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Using UNION Across Different Tables
âœ” UNION can merge results from entirely different tables.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Retrieve Names From Customers and Shippers

SELECT first_name FROM customers
UNION 
SELECT name FROM shippers;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Merges customer first names and shipper names into a single list.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”

â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤

â”‚ Bob       â”‚ âœ… Customer
â”‚ UPS       â”‚ âœ… Shipper
â”‚ FedEx     â”‚ âœ… Shipper
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜
âœ” Combines records while removing duplicates.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Union Errors: Mismatched Columns
âœ” The number of columns must match in both queries. 
âœ” Different column counts cause errors.

ğŸ“Œ Incorrect Query (Mismatched Columns):

SELECT first_name, last_name FROM customers
UNION 
SELECT name FROM shippers;  -- âš ï¸ Error! Only one column returned

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” MySQL throws an error, as the first query returns two columns, but the second query returns one.

ğŸ“Œ Fix: Matching Column Structure

SELECT name FROM shippers
UNION 
SELECT first_name FROM customers;
âœ” Now both queries return a single column, preventing errors.





ğŸ“Œ 4. Exercise: Classifying Customers Based on Points
ğŸš€ Task: âœ” Categorize customers into Bronze, Silver, and Gold, based on loyalty points.

ğŸ“Œ Expected Query Solution

SELECT 
    customer_id,

FROM customers 
WHERE points < 2000

    customer_id,
