Inserting, Updating and Deleting Data:
--------------------------------------

Column Attributes: 
------------------

ğŸš€ Understanding Column Attributes in SQL
Before working with inserting, updating, and deleting data, it's crucial to understand column attributes, which define data types, constraints, and behaviors within a table.

Column attributes in SQL define specific properties and constraints for a column in a table. These attributes help ensure data integrity, control how data is stored, and influence how queries interact with the column. Here are some key column attributes:

Data Type â€“ Specifies the type of data stored in the column (e.g., INT, VARCHAR, DATE, BOOLEAN).
DEFAULT â€“ Assigns a default value when no explicit value is provided in an INSERT operation.
NOT NULL â€“ Ensures that the column cannot store NULL values.
UNIQUE â€“ Guarantees that all values in the column are distinct.
PRIMARY KEY â€“ Uniquely identifies each row in the table; combines UNIQUE and NOT NULL.
FOREIGN KEY â€“ Establishes a relationship between tables by referencing a column in another table.
CHECK â€“ Defines a condition that values in the column must satisfy.
AUTO_INCREMENT â€“ Automatically generates sequential values for new rows (commonly used for primary keys).
INDEX â€“ Improves query performance by creating a searchable structure for the column.



ğŸ“Œ 1. Exploring Column Attributes in the customers Table:
âœ” Every column in a database table has a specific type and constraints. 
âœ” These attributes control what kind of data can be stored and how it behaves.

ğŸ“Œ Example: customers Table Schema
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ Column Name  â”‚ Data Type â”‚ Max Size â”‚ Default Valueâ”‚ PK   â”‚ NN   â”‚ AI   â”‚ Attributes     â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ customer_id  â”‚ INT       â”‚ -        â”‚ -            â”‚ âœ…   â”‚ âœ…    â”‚ âœ…   â”‚ Auto-Increment â”‚
â”‚ first_name   â”‚ VARCHAR   â”‚ 50       â”‚ -            â”‚      â”‚ âœ…    â”‚      â”‚ -              |
â”‚ last_name    â”‚ VARCHAR   â”‚ 50       â”‚ -            â”‚      â”‚ âœ…   â”‚      â”‚ -              |
â”‚ birth_date   â”‚ DATE      â”‚ -        â”‚ NULL         â”‚      â”‚      â”‚      â”‚ Nullable       |
â”‚ phone        â”‚ VARCHAR   â”‚ 15       â”‚ NULL         â”‚      â”‚      â”‚      â”‚ Nullable       â”‚ 
â”‚ points       â”‚ INT       â”‚ -        â”‚ 0            â”‚      â”‚ âœ…   â”‚      â”‚ Default Value  â”‚
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜

âœ” Primary Key (PK): Ensures unique identification of rows. 
âœ” Not Null (NN): Prevents empty values in columns. 
âœ” Auto Increment (AI): Automatically increments primary key values. 
âœ” Default Values: Assigns predefined values when none are supplied.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Understanding Data Types:
âœ” Each column must store specific types of data, defined by its data type.

ğŸ“Œ Common Data Types:

Data Type	Usage
INT	        Stores whole numbers (e.g., IDs, quantities).
VARCHAR(n)	Stores text up to n characters.
CHAR(n)	    Stores fixed-length text (n characters).
DATE	    Stores dates in YYYY-MM-DD format.

ğŸ“Œ VARCHAR vs. CHAR 
âœ” VARCHAR (variable-length) saves space by storing only required characters. 
âœ” CHAR (fixed-length) always reserves the full length, even for short values.

ğŸš€ Example Difference

VARCHAR(50) â†’ "John" (4 characters stored, space saved)
CHAR(50) â†’ "John                                 " (50 characters stored, wasted space)
âœ” Best Practice: Use VARCHAR for flexible text storage.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Auto Increment & Default Values
âœ” Auto Increment (AI) ensures automatic numbering for primary keys. 
âœ” Default Values prevent NULL values by assigning pre-defined values.

ğŸ“Œ Auto Increment Behavior Example:
customer_id â†’ [1, 2, 3, 4, â€¦]  â†’ Automatically increases for new rows.

âœ” Default Values Example
points â†’ Default = 0 â†’ If no value is given, '0' is stored.
birth_date â†’ Default = NULL â†’ If no value is given, 'NULL' is stored.

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Prevents inconsistent or missing data when inserting new records.





ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” Column attributes define data storage rules for each table. 
âœ” Primary keys (PK) ensure uniqueness, preventing duplicate records. 
âœ” Not Null (NN) ensures required values, preventing incomplete data. 
âœ” Auto Increment (AI) generates unique IDs, eliminating manual input. 
âœ” Use VARCHAR instead of CHAR for efficient storage. 
âœ” Default values prevent null issues, ensuring data integrity. 














Inserting a Row: 
----------------

ğŸš€ Inserting a Row in SQL
The INSERT INTO statement allows adding new records to a table. This tutorial covers standard insertions, handling auto-increment values, dealing with optional columns, and using default values.

ğŸ“Œ 1. Understanding the INSERT INTO Statement
âœ” The basic syntax for inserting data into a table is:

ğŸ“Œ Standard Insert Statement:

INSERT INTO customers
VALUES (
    DEFAULT,  -- Auto-incremented customer_id
    'John',   -- First name
    'Smith',  -- Last name
    '1990-01-01',  -- Birth date
    NULL,     -- Phone (optional)
    'address',-- Address
    'city',   -- City
    'CA',     -- State
    DEFAULT   -- Default points (0)
);

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Adds a new customer with automatically generated customer_id.

ğŸ“Œ Why Use DEFAULT Instead of a Number? 
âœ” If a specific customer_id is inserted manually, it may conflict with an existing ID, causing an error. 
âœ” Using DEFAULT, MySQL generates the next available ID automatically.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Handling Optional Columns
âœ” Some columns are optional (e.g., phone and birth_date). 
âœ” If a column is optional, we can insert a NULL or let MySQL handle it using DEFAULT.

ğŸ“Œ Setting an Optional Value to NULL:

INSERT INTO customers
VALUES (
    DEFAULT, 'Alice', 'Brown', '1985-07-20',
    NULL, 'address', 'city', 'CA', DEFAULT
);

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Stores NULL in the phone column, indicating no phone number available.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Using Column Lists for Flexible Inserts
âœ” Instead of inserting values for all columns, we can specify only required ones.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Specifying Only Certain Columns:

INSERT INTO customers (
    first_name, last_name, birth_date, address, city, state
) VALUES (
    'John', 'Smith', '1990-01-01', 'address', 'city', 'CA'
);

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Avoids inserting values for columns like customer_id, phone, and points. âœ” MySQL automatically assigns default values where applicable.

ğŸ“Œ Key Benefits 
âœ” Removes unnecessary NULL/default values from the statement. 
âœ” Allows column reordering, making queries more readable.

ğŸš€ Example With Reordered Columns

INSERT INTO customers (
    last_name, first_name, birth_date, address, city, state
) VALUES (
    'Smith', 'John', '1990-01-01', 'address', 'city', 'CA'
);

âœ” Swapping the order of columns is valid as long as values match column positions.





ğŸ“Œ 4. Verifying Inserted Data:
âœ” After executing an INSERT INTO statement, we can verify the new row in the table.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Checking the Last Inserted Customer

SELECT * FROM customers;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Displays all records, including the newly inserted row.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”

ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” Use INSERT INTO to add rows to a table, ensuring values match column attributes. 
âœ” DEFAULT auto-generates primary key values, preventing conflicts. 
âœ” Optional columns can use NULL or DEFAULT based on table rules. 
âœ” Specifying column names allows flexible and readable insert statements.

Inserting Hierarchial Rows:
---------------------------
ğŸš€ Inserting Hierarchical Rows in SQL
In many databases, an order consists of multiple items, meaning we need to insert data into multiple tables while maintaining relationships. This tutorial covers how to insert hierarchical data into parent-child tables.

ğŸ“Œ 1. Understanding Parent-Child Relationships
âœ” In relational databases, some tables depend on others. 
âœ” The orders table (parent) contains general order details, while the order_items table (child) contains specific ordered products.

ğŸ“Œ Example Relationship:
Orders Table â†’ Stores overall order details (ID, date, customer, status).
Order Items Table â†’ Stores individual products per order (ID, quantity, price).

âœ” Each order may have multiple items, meaning one order â†’ many items.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Inserting an Order Into the orders Table
âœ” First, we add an order without specifying the order_id, since itâ€™s auto-incremented.

ğŸ“Œ Inserting an Order

INSERT INTO orders (customer_id, order_date, status)
VALUES (1, '2019-01-02', 1);

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Adds a new order for customer_id = 1, with order status 1 (processed, shipped, etc.).

ğŸ“Œ Verifying the Inserted Order:
sql
SELECT * FROM orders;

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ OrderID   â”‚ CustomerIDâ”‚ OrderDate â”‚ Status    â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ 13        â”‚ 1         â”‚ 2019-01-02â”‚ 1         â”‚ âœ… New Order
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜
âœ” Order ID = 13 is auto-generated.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Inserting Items Into order_items Using LAST_INSERT_ID()
âœ” After inserting the order, we need its order_id to insert items. 
âœ” MySQL provides the LAST_INSERT_ID() function, which returns the ID of the last inserted record.

ğŸ“Œ Inserting Items Using Auto-Generated Order ID:
INSERT INTO order_items
VALUES 
    (LAST_INSERT_ID(), 1, 1, 2.95),
    (LAST_INSERT_ID(), 2, 1, 3.95);
	
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Adds two items to the latest order.

ğŸ“Œ Verifying the Inserted Items:
SELECT * FROM order_items;

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ OrderID   â”‚ ProductID â”‚ Quantity  â”‚ UnitPrice â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ 13        â”‚ 1         â”‚  1        â”‚ 2.95      â”‚ âœ… Product 1
â”‚ 13        â”‚ 2         â”‚  1        â”‚ 3.95      â”‚ âœ… Product 2
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜
âœ” Products correctly linked to Order 13.

ğŸ“Œ Why Use LAST_INSERT_ID()? 
âœ” Automatically retrieves the latest order_id, ensuring accurate order-item linkage. 
âœ” Prevents manual ID tracking, reducing errors and mismatches.





Queries used in this discussion: 

INSERT INTO orders (customer_id, order_date, status)
VALUES (1, '2019-01-02', 1);


INSERT INTO order_items
VALUES 
	(LAST_INSERT_ID(), 1, 1, 2.95)
	(LAST_INSERT_ID(), 2, 1, 3.95);
	




ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” Orders (parent) link to multiple items (child) using foreign keys (order_id). 
âœ” Insert the order first, then use LAST_INSERT_ID() to insert items. 
âœ” Auto-increment ensures unique IDs, avoiding manual entry errors. 
âœ” Next steps: Learning how to update hierarchical records efficiently.


	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	
	



Creating a Copy of a Table: 
---------------------------

ğŸš€ Creating a Copy of a Table in SQL:
SQL allows copying data from one table to another efficiently using subqueries and CREATE TABLE AS statements. This technique is useful for archiving, backups, and filtered data copies.

ğŸ“Œ 1. Using CREATE TABLE AS to Copy a Table
âœ” The CREATE TABLE AS statement creates a new table and copies data from an existing one.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Copying the orders Table to orders_archived

CREATE TABLE orders_archived AS 
SELECT * FROM orders;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Creates orders_archived and copies all records from orders.

ğŸ“Œ Verifying the Newly Created Table
SELECT * FROM orders_archived;

ğŸ“Œ Example Output
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ OrderID   â”‚ CustomerIDâ”‚ OrderDate â”‚ Status    â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ 1         â”‚ 5         â”‚ 2025-03-10â”‚ Processed â”‚
â”‚ 2         â”‚ 3         â”‚ 2024-08-22â”‚ Shipped   â”‚
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜
âœ” All data is copied from the orders table.

ğŸ“Œ Limitations of This Approach âœ” Primary keys and constraints are NOT copied, meaning auto-increment and uniqueness rules must be redefined.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Using INSERT INTO With a Subquery
âœ” Instead of copying an entire table, we can copy filtered records using a subquery in an INSERT INTO statement.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Copying Only Archived Orders:
INSERT INTO orders_archived 
SELECT * 
FROM orders
WHERE order_date < '2019-01-01';
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Copies only past orders into orders_archived.

ğŸ“Œ Verifying the Archived Orders:
SELECT * FROM orders_archived;

âœ” Orders placed before 2019 are retained, while new orders are ignored.

ğŸ“Œ Why Use This Approach? âœ” Optimizes storage by copying only relevant records. âœ” Maintains data integrity without manual filtering.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Exercise: Copying Filtered Invoices Into an Archive Table
ğŸš€ Task: âœ” Create a copy of the invoices table, replacing client_id with client names and filtering only paid invoices.

ğŸ“Œ Expected Query Solution:
USE sql_invoicing;

CREATE TABLE invoices_archived AS
SELECT 
    i.invoice_id, 
    i.number,
    c.name AS client,
    i.invoice_total, 
    i.payment_total, 
    i.invoice_date,
    i.payment_date,
    i.due_date
FROM invoices i
JOIN clients c 
USING (client_id)
WHERE payment_date IS NOT NULL;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Creates a filtered archive, ensuring only paid invoices are copied.

ğŸ“Œ Verifying the Archived Invoices:
SELECT * FROM invoices_archived;

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”

âœ” Invoices without payments are excluded.





ğŸ“Œ 4. Managing Existing Tables
âœ” If the archived table already exists, re-running the query causes an error. âœ” To recreate the archive, drop the table first.

ğŸ“Œ Dropping an Existing Archive Table:
DROP TABLE invoices_archived;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Removes the existing archive table, allowing fresh data copies.

ğŸ“Œ Recreating the Table:
CREATE TABLE invoices_archived AS 
SELECT ... ;  -- Re-run the archive query

âœ” Ensures the latest data is archived.





Queries used in this discussion: 

CREATE TABLE orders_archived AS 
SELECT * FROM orders


INSERT INTO orders_archived    --example of using a select statement as a sub query in an insert statement
SELECT * 
FROM orders
WHERE order_date < '2019-01-01';



Exercise: 

USE sql_invoicing; 

CREATE TABLE invoices_archived AS
SELECT 
	i.invoice_id, 
	i.number,
	c.name AS client,
	i.invoice_total, 
	i.payment_total, 
	i.invoice_date,
	i.payment_date,
	i.due_date
FROM invoices i
JOIN clients c
	USING (client_id)
WHERE payment_date IS NOT NULL; 







ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” CREATE TABLE AS copies a table but omits constraints. 
âœ” Subqueries in INSERT INTO allow selective data copying, optimizing storage. 
âœ” Using JOIN ensures relevant column replacements (e.g., client_id â†’ client_name). 
âœ” Dropping tables (DROP TABLE) allows rebuilding archive copies as needed. 



















Updating a Single Row: 
----------------------
ğŸš€ Updating a Single Row in SQL
The UPDATE statement in SQL allows us to modify existing records in a table. This tutorial covers how to update specific values, restore original data, and use expressions for calculated updates.

ğŸ“Œ 1. Understanding the UPDATE Statement
âœ” The UPDATE statement modifies existing records, targeting specific rows using the WHERE clause. 
âœ” The SET clause specifies which column(s) to update.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Updating Payment Information for an Invoice:
UPDATE invoices 
SET payment_total = 10, payment_date = '2019-03-01'
WHERE invoice_id = 1;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Sets payment_total = 10 and payment_date = '2019-03-01' for invoice ID 1.

ğŸ“Œ Verifying the Updated Data:
SELECT * FROM invoices WHERE invoice_id = 1;
ğŸ“Œ Example Output

â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ InvoiceID â”‚ PaymentTotal â”‚ PaymentDate â”‚ Status    â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ 1         â”‚ 10           â”‚ 2019-03-01  â”‚ Paid      â”‚ âœ… Updated
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜
âœ” Payment details are successfully updated.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Resetting Values Using NULL and DEFAULT
âœ” If an update was made incorrectly, we can restore values using NULL or DEFAULT.

ğŸ“Œ Resetting the Payment Details for Invoice 1:
UPDATE invoices 
SET payment_total = DEFAULT, payment_date = NULL
WHERE invoice_id = 1;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Resets payment_total to default (0) and payment_date to NULL, restoring original values.

ğŸ“Œ Why Use DEFAULT Instead of a Specific Value? 
âœ” Using DEFAULT ensures consistency, reflecting predefined table rules.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Using Expressions in Updates
âœ” Instead of inserting hardcoded values, we can use calculations.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Calculating 50% of Invoice Total for Invoice 3:
UPDATE invoices 
SET 
    payment_total = invoice_total * 0.5, 
    payment_date = due_date
WHERE invoice_id = 3;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Updates payment_total to 50% of invoice_total, ensuring accurate calculations.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ InvoiceID â”‚ PaymentTotal â”‚ PaymentDate â”‚ DueDate   â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ 3         â”‚ 73.5         â”‚ 2024-08-22  â”‚ 2024-08-22â”‚ âœ… Updated
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜
âœ” The payment total updates to 50% of the original invoice amount.


ğŸ“Œ Why Use Calculations Instead of Manual Updates? 
âœ” Ensures dynamic updates without manual entry errors. 
âœ” Helps with business logic operations (e.g., applying discounts, updating tax rates).





ğŸ“Œ 4. Handling Decimal Precision:
âœ” Some calculations truncate decimals automatically. 
âœ” When updating currency values, ensure proper data types to preserve decimal places.

ğŸ“Œ Solution for Decimal Truncation âœ” Using data type adjustments (DECIMAL(x, y)) ensures correct rounding.

ğŸš€ Example: Ensure Payment Total Maintains Precision
ALTER TABLE invoices 
MODIFY COLUMN payment_total DECIMAL(10,2);

âœ” Preserves decimal values properly.


The ALTER statement in SQL is used to modify an existing table structure. It allows you to add, modify, or delete columns and constraints without recreating the table. Since you're methodical in your learning approach, I'll break this down clearly:

Common Uses of ALTER
1. Adding a Column

ALTER TABLE employees 
ADD age INT;

This adds a new column (age) to the employees table.

2. Modifying a Column Data Type or Constraints

ALTER TABLE employees 
MODIFY age SMALLINT NOT NULL;

Changes age column type to SMALLINT and ensures it cannot contain NULL values.

3. Renaming a Column (Supported in Some Databases)

ALTER TABLE employees 
RENAME COLUMN age TO employee_age;

4. Dropping a Column

ALTER TABLE employees 
DROP COLUMN age;

This removes the age column from the table.

5. Adding a Constraint

ALTER TABLE employees 
ADD CONSTRAINT unique_email UNIQUE (email);

Ensures the email column has unique values.


Where is ALTER Used?
Database Maintenance: Modifying table structures without affecting stored data.
Schema Evolution: Adjusting columns and constraints as data needs evolve.
Index and Key Management: Ensuring data integrity and optimizing performance.





Queries used in this discussion: 

UPDATE invoices 
SET payment_total = 10, payment_date = '2019-03-01'
WHERE invoice_id = 1;


UPDATE invoices 
SET payment_total = DEFAULT, payment_date = NULL
WHERE invoice_id = 1;


UPDATE invoices 
SET 
	payment_total = invoice_total * 0.5, 
	payment_date = due_date
WHERE invoice_id = 3;





ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” Use UPDATE to modify existing records, targeting specific rows using WHERE. 
âœ” Reset values using NULL or DEFAULT to restore original data. 
âœ” Expressions (invoice_total * 0.5) allow calculated updates, preventing manual input errors. 
âœ” Next steps: Learning batch updates to modify multiple rows efficiently.












Updating Multiple Rows: 
-----------------------
ğŸš€ Updating Multiple Rows in SQL:
When modifying multiple records in a table, we use the same UPDATE statement, but with a broader condition in the WHERE clause. This tutorial covers how to update multiple rows efficiently, bypass safe update restrictions, and use advanced filtering techniques.

ğŸ“Œ 1. Using UPDATE for Multiple Records
âœ” The UPDATE statement modifies multiple rows when a general condition is used.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Updating All Invoices for Client ID 3:
UPDATE invoices 
SET 
    payment_total = invoice_total * 0.5, 
    payment_date = due_date
WHERE client_id = 3;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Updates payment_total to 50% of invoice_total for all invoices of client ID 3.

ğŸ“Œ Verifying the Updated Data:
SELECT * FROM invoices WHERE client_id = 3;

ğŸ“Œ 2. Safe Update Mode in MySQL Workbench
âœ” MySQL Workbench prevents updating multiple rows by default (to prevent accidental bulk changes). 
âœ” If you attempt an update on more than one row, you may receive a safe update error.

ğŸ“Œ Disabling Safe Update Mode 
 - Go to MySQL Workbench Menu â†’ Preferences. 
 - Click SQL Editor (left panel). 
 - Scroll down to "Safe Updates" and uncheck the box. 
 - Reconnect to MySQL instance to apply changes.

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Now you can safely update multiple rows without errors.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Using IN for More Flexible Updates:
âœ” If multiple clients need updates, use IN (value1, value2, â€¦) to target specific records.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Updating Invoices for Clients 3 and 4:
UPDATE invoices 
SET 
    payment_total = invoice_total * 0.5, 
    payment_date = due_date
WHERE client_id IN (3, 4);

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Expands the update to include invoices for clients 3 and 4, ensuring batch modifications.

Using Subqueries in updates:
----------------------------

ğŸš€ Using Subqueries in SQL Updates
Subqueries enhance SQL updates by dynamically retrieving values, making updates more precise and scalable. This tutorial covers how to use subqueries in UPDATE statements, handle multiple records, and follow best practices to prevent accidental updates.

ğŸ“Œ 1ï¸âƒ£ Understanding Subqueries in Updates
âœ” A subquery is a SELECT statement nested within another SQL statement. âœ” It retrieves dynamic values used in UPDATE conditions.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Updating Invoices for a Client Based on Name

sql
UPDATE invoices 
SET 
    payment_total = invoice_total * 0.5, 
    payment_date = due_date
WHERE client_id = 
        (SELECT client_id
        FROM clients
        WHERE name = 'Myworks');
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Finds the client ID for "Myworks" and updates all their invoices accordingly.

ğŸ“Œ Why Use a Subquery Instead of Hardcoding IDs? âœ” Eliminates manual entry errors. âœ” Ensures dynamic retrieval of IDs based on user input.

ğŸ“Œ 2ï¸âƒ£ Handling Multiple Records in Subqueries
âœ” If a subquery returns multiple results, we must use the IN operator instead of =.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Updating Invoices for Clients in Specific States

sql
UPDATE invoices 
SET 
    payment_total = invoice_total * 0.5, 
    payment_date = due_date
WHERE client_id IN 
        (SELECT client_id
        FROM clients
        WHERE state IN ('CA', 'NY'));
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Updates invoices for all clients located in California and New York.

ğŸ“Œ Verifying the Query Before Execution

sql
SELECT client_id 
FROM clients 
WHERE state IN ('CA', 'NY');
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Ensures correct records are targeted, preventing unintended updates.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output

â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ ClientID  â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ 1         â”‚ âœ… CA Client
â”‚ 3         â”‚ âœ… NY Client
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜
âœ” Using IN allows multi-client updates efficiently.

ğŸ“Œ 3ï¸âƒ£ Best Practice: Verifying Updates Before Execution
âœ” Always run a SELECT query first to preview affected rows.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Checking Invoices With No Payments Before Updating

sql
SELECT * FROM invoices WHERE payment_date IS NULL;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Displays records that will be updated, preventing mistakes.

ğŸ“Œ If Correct, Proceed With Update

sql
UPDATE invoices 
SET 
    payment_total = invoice_total * 0.5, 
    payment_date = due_date
WHERE payment_date IS NULL;
âœ” Ensures only unpaid invoices are updated.

ğŸ“Œ 4ï¸âƒ£ Exercise: Updating Orders for Gold Customers
ğŸš€ Task: âœ” Find customers with more than 3000 points and update order comments to "Gold Customer".

ğŸ“Œ Expected Query Solution

sql
UPDATE orders
SET comments = 'Gold Customer'
WHERE customer_id IN 
        (SELECT customer_id
        FROM customers 
        WHERE points > 3000);
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Updates orders for high-loyalty customers, marking them as Gold Customers.

â”‚ OrderID   â”‚ CustomerIDâ”‚ Comments  â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤

â”‚ 34        â”‚ 8        â”‚ Gold Customer â”‚ âœ… High Loyalty

âœ” Correct records are updated dynamically.







Queries used in this discussion:

ğŸ“Œ Summary

âœ” Replace hardcoded values with subqueries to retrieve data automatically. 
âœ” Use IN for multi-record updates when subqueries return multiple results. 
âœ” Always verify records before executing updates using a SELECT statement. 




		
		
		
		
		
		
		







Deleting Rows:
--------------
ğŸš€ Deleting Rows in SQL:
The DELETE FROM statement in SQL allows removing specific records from a table. This tutorial covers how to delete individual rows, bulk delete using conditions, and safely using subqueries to target related data.

ğŸ“Œ 1. Using DELETE FROM to Remove a Single Record
âœ” The basic syntax for deleting data from a table requires specifying a condition in WHERE.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Deleting Invoice with ID 1:
DELETE FROM invoices  
WHERE invoice_id = 1;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Removes the invoice with ID 1 from the table.

ğŸ“Œ Verifying the Deletion:
SELECT * FROM invoices WHERE invoice_id = 1;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” If the query returns no records, the deletion was successful.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Warning: Deleting All Records Without WHERE
âœ” If WHERE is omitted, ALL rows in the table are deleted. 
âœ” This cannot be undone unless backups exist.

ğŸ“Œ Example of a Dangerous Query:
DELETE FROM invoices;  -- âŒ Deletes ALL records!

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Removes all rows in the invoices table, wiping out all stored data.

ğŸ“Œ Best Practice âœ” Always double-check queries before execution to prevent accidental deletions.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Using Subqueries to Delete Rows Based on Related Data
âœ” Subqueries help dynamically retrieve conditions for deletion.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Deleting Invoices for a Specific Client (Using Subquery)
DELETE FROM invoices  
WHERE client_id = (
        SELECT client_id  
        FROM clients  
        WHERE name = 'MyWorks'
);

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Finds the client ID for "MyWorks" and deletes all their invoices.

ğŸ“Œ Verifying Before Deletion:
SELECT client_id  
FROM clients  
WHERE name = 'MyWorks';

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Ensures correct client is targeted, preventing unintended deletions.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output of Subquery:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ ClientID  â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ 2         â”‚ âœ… MyWorks
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜
âœ” Client ID 2 is dynamically retrieved, ensuring precise deletions.





Queries used in this discussion: 

DELETE FROM invoices  
WHERE invoice_id =1; 

-- without where statement, will delete all the records from the particular table


DELETE FROM invoices  
WHERE client_id = (
        SELECT * 
		FROM clients
		WHERE name = 'Myworks');





ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” DELETE FROM removes records using specific conditions. 
âœ” Always include WHERE to avoid deleting all rows unintentionally. 
âœ” Use subqueries for dynamic deletions, ensuring accuracy and efficiency.

After modifying databases through inserting, updating, and deleting data, restoring them to their original state ensures consistency for future operations.

âœ” Modifications made in this section (data insertions, updates, deletions) alter the datasets. 
âœ” If databases are not restored, queries in future lessons may produce different results. 
âœ” Resetting databases maintains a structured learning environment.

Summarizing Data:
-----------------

Aggregate Functions:
--------------------
ğŸš€ Understanding Aggregate Functions in SQL
Aggregate functions summarize data by performing calculations across multiple rows, helping businesses analyze sales, revenue, and performance trends.

ğŸ“Œ 1. What Are Aggregate Functions?
âœ” Aggregate functions process multiple rows to return a single summary value. 
âœ” These functions operate on numerical, date, and string data types.

ğŸ“Œ Common Aggregate Functions:
Function	Description
MAX()	    Returns the highest value in a column.
MIN()	    Returns the lowest value in a column.
AVG()	    Calculates the average of numeric values.
SUM()	    Computes the total sum of column values.
COUNT()	    Counts the number of records in a column.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Using Aggregate Functions in Queries
âœ” Apply aggregate functions to summarize invoices and payments.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Calculating Summary Statistics for Invoices

SELECT 
    MAX(invoice_total) AS highest, 
    MIN(invoice_total) AS lowest, 
    AVG(invoice_total) AS average,
    SUM(invoice_total) AS total,
    COUNT(invoice_total) AS number_of_invoices,
    COUNT(payment_date) AS count_of_payments,
    COUNT(*) AS total_records
FROM invoices;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Retrieves highest invoice amount, lowest invoice amount, average invoice, total sales, and record count.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ Highest   â”‚ Lowest    â”‚ Average   â”‚ Total     â”‚ Invoices  â”‚ Payments  â”‚ Records   â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ 189.00    â”‚ 72.50     â”‚ 150.20    â”‚ 2550.75   â”‚ 17        â”‚ 15        â”‚ 17        â”‚
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜
âœ” Total invoices processed: 17 âœ” Total payments recorded: 15 âœ” Total revenue: $2,550.75





ğŸ“Œ 3. Handling NULL Values in Aggregate Functions
âœ” Aggregate functions ignore NULL values unless explicitly handled.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Counting Only Non-NULL Payments:
SELECT COUNT(payment_date) AS count_of_payments 
FROM invoices;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Only invoices with recorded payment dates are counted.

ğŸ“Œ Alternative: Counting ALL Records, Including NULL Values:
SELECT COUNT(*) AS total_records 
FROM invoices;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Counts all invoices, regardless of payment status.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output Difference:
COUNT(payment_date) â†’ 15 (Only invoices with payments)
COUNT(*) â†’ 17 (Includes unpaid invoices)

âœ” Always verify how NULL values impact calculations.





ğŸ“Œ 4. Using Expressions in Aggregate Functions
âœ” We can apply mathematical operations within aggregate functions.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Calculating Total Sales With a 10% Tax Increase:
SELECT SUM(invoice_total * 1.1) AS total_sales_tax_included
FROM invoices;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Computes total revenue, including a 10% tax adjustment.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
SUM(invoice_total) â†’ $2,550.75
SUM(invoice_total * 1.1) â†’ $2,805.82 (After 10% increase)





ğŸ“Œ 5. Filtering Aggregate Results
âœ” Adding filters refines calculations, extracting insights from specific periods.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Calculating Sales Only for Second Half of the Year:
SELECT 
    SUM(invoice_total) AS total_sales
FROM invoices
WHERE invoice_date > '2019-07-01';

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Retrieves total sales only for invoices issued after July 1, 2019.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ Total Salesâ”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ 1350.25    â”‚ âœ… Revenue from second half of 2019
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜





ğŸ“Œ 6. Using DISTINCT With Aggregate Functions
âœ” Aggregate functions count ALL values, but use DISTINCT to filter unique entries.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Counting Unique Clients:
SELECT COUNT(DISTINCT client_id) AS unique_clients
FROM invoices
WHERE invoice_date > '2019-07-01';

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Ensures each client is counted only once, avoiding duplicates.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
COUNT(client_id) â†’ 7 (Counts all invoices with client IDs)
COUNT(DISTINCT client_id) â†’ 3 (Counts unique clients)





ğŸ“Œ 7. Exercise: Generating a Sales Report by Date Range
ğŸš€ Task: âœ” Create a report to summarize sales, payments, and expected revenue for:
First half of 2019
Second half of 2019
Full year total

ğŸ“Œ Expected Query Solution:

UNION 
SELECT

    SUM(invoice_total) AS total_sales, 
    SUM(payment_total) AS total_payments, 
    SUM(invoice_total - payment_total) AS expected_revenue
FROM invoices 
WHERE invoice_date BETWEEN '2019-07-01' AND '2019-12-31'
UNION 
SELECT 
    'Total' AS date_range, 
    SUM(invoice_total) AS total_sales, 
    SUM(payment_total) AS total_payments, 
    SUM(invoice_total - payment_total) AS expected_revenue
FROM invoices 
WHERE invoice_date BETWEEN '2019-01-01' AND '2019-12-31';

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Provides a structured sales analysis across different periods.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:

â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤

The GROUP BY Clause:
--------------------
ğŸš€ Understanding the GROUP BY Clause in SQL
Grouping data using the GROUP BY clause helps organize records into meaningful categories based on specific column values, allowing businesses to analyze sales, payments, and trends effectively.

ğŸ“Œ 1. What Does GROUP BY Do?
âœ” GROUP BY groups records with matching column values, applying aggregate functions to summarize each group. 
âœ” Used with SUM(), COUNT(), AVG(), MAX(), MIN(), and more.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Calculating Total Sales Using SUM():
SELECT SUM(invoice_total) AS total_sales
FROM invoices;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Returns a single total sales value.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ TotalSalesâ”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ 2550.75   â”‚ âœ… All invoices combined
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜
âœ” Useful but lacks detailed insights per client or region.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Grouping Data By a Single Column:
âœ” If we want total sales per client, we group by client_id.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Total Sales Per Client:
SELECT 
    client_id,
    SUM(invoice_total) AS total_sales
FROM invoices
GROUP BY client_id
ORDER BY total_sales DESC;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Groups invoices by client ID, summarizing their total spending.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ ClientID  â”‚ TotalSalesâ”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ 5         â”‚ 720.00    â”‚ âœ… Highest spender
â”‚ 3         â”‚ 610.50    â”‚
â”‚ 1         â”‚ 545.25    â”‚
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜
âœ” Now we know which clients generate the highest revenue.


ğŸ“Œ Adding a Filter Before Grouping âœ” We can limit the dataset before grouping using WHERE.

ğŸš€ Example: Filter for Second Half of the Year:
SELECT 
    client_id,
    SUM(invoice_total) AS total_sales
FROM invoices
WHERE invoice_date >= '2019-07-01'
GROUP BY client_id
ORDER BY total_sales DESC;

âœ” Summarizes sales per client, but only for invoices issued after July 1, 2019.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Grouping Data By Multiple Columns
âœ” Sometimes, we need a more detailed breakdownâ€”for example, sales per state and city.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Grouping by State and City:
SELECT 
    state,
    city,
    SUM(invoice_total) AS total_sales
FROM invoices i
JOIN clients USING (client_id)
GROUP BY state, city;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Displays total sales for each state and city combination.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ State     â”‚ City      â”‚ TotalSalesâ”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ CA        â”‚ San Diego â”‚ 520.00    â”‚
â”‚ NY        â”‚ Buffalo   â”‚ 450.75    â”‚
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜
âœ” Allows region-specific analysis.

ğŸ“Œ Why Use Multiple Columns? âœ” Provides granular breakdowns for deeper insights. âœ” Essential for regional sales comparisons.





ğŸ“Œ 4. Exercise: Summarizing Payments by Date and Payment Method
ğŸš€ Task: âœ” Generate a report showing total payments for each date and payment method.

ğŸ“Œ Expected Query Solution:
SELECT 
    date,
    pm.name AS payment_method,
    SUM(amount) AS total_payments
FROM payments p
JOIN payment_methods pm
    ON p.payment_method = pm.payment_method_id
GROUP BY date, payment_method 
ORDER BY date;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Displays how payments were received per day.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ Date      â”‚ Method    â”‚ TotalPaid â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ 2025-01-08â”‚ CreditCardâ”‚ 32.00     â”‚
â”‚ 2025-01-08â”‚ Cash      â”‚ 10.00     â”‚
â”‚ 2025-01-09â”‚ WireTrnsf â”‚ 25.50     â”‚
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜
âœ” Organizes payments efficiently for better financial tracking.





ğŸ“Œ 5. Understanding SQL Query Execution Order
âœ” Queries follow a strict order for execution.

ğŸ“Œ SQL Execution Order:
1.SELECT â†’ Retrieves data.
2.FROM â†’ Specifies the table.
3.WHERE â†’ Filters data.
4.GROUP BY â†’ Groups results into categories.
5.ORDER BY â†’ Sorts the final output.

âœ” Always place GROUP BY before ORDER BY, or else SQL throws a syntax error.





Queries used in this discussion: 

SELECT 
	client_id,
	SUM(invoice_total) AS total_sales
FROM invoices
GROUP BY client_id
ORDER BY DESC



SELECT 
	client_id,
	SUM(invoice_total) AS total_sales
FROM invoices
WHERE invoice_date >= '2019-07-01'
GROUP BY client_id
ORDER BY DESC;




SELECT 
	state,
	city
	SUM(invoice_total) AS total_sales
FROM invoices i
JOIN clients USING (client_id)
GROUP BY state, city;


Exercise:

	date,
	pm.name AS payment_method,
	SUM(amount) AS total_payments
FROM payments p
JOIN payment_methods pm
	ON p.payment_method = pm.payment_method_id
GROUP BY date, payment_method 
ORDER BY date;






ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” GROUP BY organizes data into meaningful groups, improving reporting. 
âœ” Use SUM() and COUNT() with GROUP BY to compute totals per group. 
âœ” Filter data first (WHERE), then group it (GROUP BY). 
âœ” Use multiple columns to refine summaries, like sales per state and city. 


















The HAVING Clause:
------------------
ğŸš€ Understanding the HAVING Clause in SQL
The HAVING clause filters grouped data, ensuring aggregated results meet specific conditions. This tutorial covers how HAVING differs from WHERE, using multiple conditions, and practical examples for real-world applications.

ğŸ“Œ 1. Difference Between WHERE and HAVING
âœ” WHERE filters records before grouping, while HAVING filters after grouping. 
âœ” HAVING is used with aggregate functions, whereas WHERE filters individual rows.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Filtering Clients With Sales Over $500:
SELECT 
    client_id,
    SUM(invoice_total) AS total_sales
FROM invoices
GROUP BY client_id
HAVING total_sales > 500;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Groups invoices by client ID, then filters out clients with less than $500 in total sales.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ ClientID  â”‚ TotalSalesâ”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ 5         â”‚ 720.00    â”‚ âœ… High-spending client
â”‚ 3         â”‚ 610.50    â”‚ âœ… High-spending client
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜

âœ” Client 2 is excluded because their total sales are below $500.

ğŸ“Œ Incorrect Use of WHERE:
SELECT 
    client_id,
    SUM(invoice_total) AS total_sales
FROM invoices
WHERE total_sales > 500  -- âŒ Error! Column not yet aggregated
GROUP BY client_id;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Returns an error because total_sales doesnâ€™t exist before aggregation. âœ” Use HAVING instead to filter grouped results.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Using Multiple Conditions in HAVING
âœ” Combine multiple conditions for refined queries.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Filtering Clients With High Sales and Multiple Invoices:
SELECT 
    client_id,
    SUM(invoice_total) AS total_sales,
    COUNT(*) AS number_of_invoices
FROM invoices
GROUP BY client_id
HAVING total_sales > 500 AND number_of_invoices > 5;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Filters clients with at least $500 in total sales AND more than 5 invoices.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ ClientID  â”‚ Sales     â”‚ Invoices  â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ 5         â”‚ 720.00    â”‚ 8         â”‚ âœ… High-spending & frequent buyer
â”‚ 3         â”‚ 610.50    â”‚ 6         â”‚ âœ… High-spending & frequent buyer
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜
âœ” Clients with fewer than 5 invoices are excluded.

ğŸ“Œ Rules for HAVING Conditions âœ” Every column in HAVING must be in SELECT, unlike WHERE. âœ” Ensure aggregation occurs first before filtering.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Exercise: Finding High-Spending Customers in Virginia
ğŸš€ Task: âœ” Retrieve customers in Virginia who have spent more than $100.

ğŸ“Œ Expected Query Solution:
USE sql_store; 

SELECT 
    c.customer_id,
    c.first_name,
    c.last_name,
    SUM(oi.quantity * oi.unit_price) AS total_sales  
FROM customers c
JOIN orders o USING (customer_id)
JOIN order_items oi USING (order_id)
WHERE state = 'VA'
GROUP BY 
    c.customer_id,
    c.first_name,
    c.last_name
HAVING total_sales > 100;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Identifies high-spending customers in Virginia.

The ROLLUP Operator:
--------------------
ğŸš€ Understanding the ROLLUP Operator in SQL
The ROLLUP operator in SQL extends aggregate functions by providing additional summary rows, making data analysis more efficient.

ğŸ“Œ 1. What Is the ROLLUP Operator?
âœ” ROLLUP automatically generates subtotal and grand total rows, summarizing data within groups. 
âœ” It works with GROUP BY to provide hierarchical totals.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Calculating Total Sales Per Client:
SELECT 
    client_id,
    SUM(invoice_total) AS total_sales
FROM invoices
GROUP BY client_id WITH ROLLUP;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Displays total sales for each client, plus a grand total row.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ ClientID  â”‚ TotalSalesâ”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ 1         â”‚ 500.00    â”‚ âœ… Individual Client Total
â”‚ 2         â”‚ 450.00    â”‚ âœ… Individual Client Total
â”‚ 3         â”‚ 900.00    â”‚ âœ… Individual Client Total
â”‚ NULL      â”‚ 1850.00   â”‚ âœ… Grand Total (ALL Clients)
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜
âœ” The last row represents the grand total, with NULL in client_id.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Grouping by Multiple Columns:
âœ” When multiple columns are grouped, ROLLUP provides subtotals for each group level.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Grouping by State and City:
SELECT 
    state,
    city,
    SUM(invoice_total) AS total_sales
FROM invoices i
JOIN clients c USING (client_id)
GROUP BY state, city WITH ROLLUP;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Computes total sales per city, total per state, and an overall grand total.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ State     â”‚ City      â”‚ TotalSalesâ”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ CA        â”‚ SanFran   â”‚ 820.00    â”‚ âœ… City Total
â”‚ CA        â”‚ NULL      â”‚ 820.00    â”‚ âœ… State Total (California)
â”‚ NY        â”‚ Buffalo   â”‚ 650.00    â”‚ âœ… City Total
â”‚ NY        â”‚ NULL      â”‚ 650.00    â”‚ âœ… State Total (New York)
â”‚ NULL      â”‚ NULL      â”‚ 1470.00   â”‚ âœ… Grand Total (All States)
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜
âœ” Each state has a total, and a grand total for all states is included.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Exercise: Summarizing Payments Using ROLLUP
ğŸš€ Task: âœ” Generate a payment report showing the total amount received for each method and the overall total.

ğŸ“Œ Expected Query Solution:
SELECT 
    pm.name AS payment_method,
    SUM(amount) AS total
FROM payments p
JOIN payment_methods pm
    ON p.payment_method = pm.payment_method_id
GROUP BY pm.name WITH ROLLUP;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Displays total payments per method, plus an overall total row.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ Method    â”‚ TotalPaid â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ CreditCardâ”‚ 320.00    â”‚ âœ… Individual Method Total
â”‚ Cash      â”‚ 150.00    â”‚ âœ… Individual Method Total
â”‚ WireTransfâ”‚ 90.50     â”‚ âœ… Individual Method Total
â”‚ NULL      â”‚ 560.50    â”‚ âœ… Grand Total (All Methods)
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜
âœ” The last row provides the overall total for all payment methods.

ğŸ“Œ Important Detail âœ” Column aliases (payment_method) cannot be used in GROUP BY WITH ROLLUP, so we must use actual column names (pm.name) instead.





Queries used in this discussion: 

SELECT 
	client_id,
	SUM(invoice_total) AS total_sales
FROM invoices
GROUP BY client_id WITH ROLLUP;


SELECT 
	state,
	city
	SUM(invoice_total) AS total_sales
FROM invoices i
JOIN clients c USING (client_id)
GROUP BY state, city WITH ROLLUP;



Exercise:

	pm.name AS payment_method,
	SUM(amount) AS total
FROM payments p
JOIN payment_methods pm
	ON p.payment_method = pm.payment_method_id
GROUP BY pm.name WITH ROLLUP;   -- when we use the rollup operator, we cannot use the column alias in the group by clause, we need to provide actual column name





ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” The ROLLUP operator adds subtotal and grand total rows, enhancing aggregated reports. 
âœ” Works with GROUP BY to structure hierarchical calculations. 
âœ” Provides automatic breakdowns across multiple columns, showing summary values at different levels. 
âœ” Next steps: Exploring advanced SQL grouping techniques like CUBE and GROUPING SETS.

Subqueries:
-----------
ğŸš€ Understanding Subqueries in SQL
A subquery is a SELECT statement nested within another SQL statement, allowing dynamic value retrieval. This tutorial covers how subqueries work, where they can be used, and practical examples.

ğŸ“Œ 1. What Is a Subquery?
âœ” A subquery is evaluated before the outer query executes. 
âœ” The result of the subquery is used as a condition or value in the main query.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Finding Products More Expensive Than "Lettuce":

SELECT * 
FROM products
WHERE unit_price > (
        SELECT unit_price
        FROM products 
        WHERE product_id = 3
);

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Retrieves all products priced higher than "Lettuce", dynamically comparing against its price.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ ProductID â”‚ Name      â”‚ Price     â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ 5         â”‚ Steak     â”‚ 15.99     â”‚ âœ… More expensive than Lettuce
â”‚ 7         â”‚ Salmon    â”‚ 22.50     â”‚ âœ… More expensive than Lettuce
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜
âœ” Only products exceeding the price of Lettuce (product_id = 3) are listed.

ğŸ“Œ How It Works: 
 - Subquery (SELECT unit_price FROM products WHERE product_id = 3) retrieves Lettuce's price. 
 - Outer query (WHERE unit_price > (â€¦)) filters all products priced higher than the retrieved value.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Types of Subquery Placement:
âœ” Subqueries can appear in different parts of SQL queries:

ğŸ“Œ Subquery in WHERE Clause âœ” Filters based on computed values.

SELECT * FROM employees
WHERE salary > (
        SELECT AVG(salary)
        FROM employees
);

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Retrieves employees earning above the average salary.


ğŸ“Œ Subquery in SELECT Clause âœ” Returns computed values alongside main query results.

SELECT name, 
    (SELECT AVG(salary) FROM employees) AS avg_salary
FROM employees;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Displays each employeeâ€™s name alongside the company-wide average salary.


ğŸ“Œ Subquery in FROM Clause âœ” Acts like a temporary table within the main query.

sql
SELECT e.name, s.avg_salary
FROM employees e
JOIN (
    SELECT AVG(salary) AS avg_salary FROM employees
) s;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Joins employee data with calculated average salary.





ğŸ“Œ3. Exercise: Finding Employees Who Earn Above Average

ğŸ“Œ Expected Query Solution

FROM employees
WHERE salary > (
        SELECT AVG(salary)
        FROM employees
);

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Dynamically filters employees based on computed average salary.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:

â”‚ EmpID     â”‚ Name      â”‚ Salary    â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ 6         â”‚ Alice     â”‚ 65,000    â”‚ âœ… Above average salary
â”‚ 9         â”‚ John      â”‚ 72,500    â”‚ âœ… Above average salary
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜

ğŸ“Œ Steps Used 
 - Subquery calculates the average salary (SELECT AVG(salary) FROM employees). 
 - Main query filters employees earning above this computed value.






Queries used in this discussion:

ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” Subqueries enable dynamic value retrieval, improving query flexibility.

The IN Operator:
----------------
ğŸš€ Using the IN Operator in SQL Subqueries
The IN operator allows filtering records based on a list of values, which can be dynamically generated using subqueries. This tutorial covers how to use IN for effective filtering, handling duplicates, and practical applications.

ğŸ“Œ 1. Understanding the IN Operator in Subqueries
âœ” The IN operator checks if a value exists within a list of specified values. 
âœ” A subquery inside IN dynamically generates the list, eliminating manual entry.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Finding Products That Have Never Been Ordered

USE sql_store;

SELECT * 
FROM products 
WHERE product_id NOT IN (
        SELECT DISTINCT product_id
        FROM order_items
);

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Retrieves all products that have never been included in any order.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ ProductID â”‚ Name      â”‚ Price     â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ 7         â”‚ Broccoli  â”‚ 2.50      â”‚ âœ… Never ordered
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜
âœ” Product ID = 7 is missing from order_items, so it appears in the results.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Steps to Implement IN With Subqueries
âœ” Step 1: Retrieve all ordered products from order_items.

SELECT DISTINCT product_id
FROM order_items;

âœ” Step 2: Use this result inside NOT IN to exclude ordered products.

SELECT * 
FROM products 
WHERE product_id NOT IN (
        SELECT DISTINCT product_id 
        FROM order_items
);

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” The subquery dynamically generates the product list, removing manual filtering.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Comparing Different Subquery Types
âœ” Single-value subquery:

Example: "Find employees earning above the company average"

SELECT * 
FROM employees 
WHERE salary > (
        SELECT AVG(salary) 
        FROM employees
);
âœ” List-based subquery (IN operator):

Example: "Find all clients with no invoices"

SELECT * 
FROM clients 
WHERE client_id NOT IN (
        SELECT DISTINCT client_id 
        FROM invoices
);
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Finds clients missing from the invoices table, identifying those who havenâ€™t made any purchases.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ ClientID  â”‚ Name      â”‚ Location  â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ 12        â”‚ GreenCorp â”‚ NY        â”‚ âœ… Never invoiced
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜

â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜
âœ” Helps businesses identify inactive clients.





Queries used in this discussion: 

-- Find the products that have never been ordered

USE sql_store;

FROM products 
WHERE product_id NOT IN (
		SELECT DISTINCT product_id
		FROM order_items
)

	SELECT DISTINCT client_id
	FROM invoices

ğŸ“Œ 5. Summary
âœ” Use IN to check if values exist within a dynamically generated list. 
âœ” NOT IN filters out values that appear in the subquery result. 
âœ” The DISTINCT keyword prevents duplicate values, optimizing query performance. 















Subqueries vs Joins:
--------------------
ğŸš€ Subqueries vs. Joins in SQL
Subqueries and joins both retrieve related data, but choosing between them depends on performance and readability. This tutorial covers how to rewrite subqueries as joins, when to use each method, and a practical comparison exercise.

ğŸ“Œ 1. Understanding the Difference Between Subqueries and Joins
âœ” Subqueries return data as part of another query, useful when filtering based on a single column or computed value. 
âœ” Joins combine multiple tables, allowing more intuitive relationships between datasets.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Finding Clients Without Invoices (Using a Subquery)

SELECT *
FROM clients
WHERE client_id NOT IN (
    SELECT DISTINCT client_id
    FROM invoices
);
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Retrieves clients who do not appear in the invoices table, meaning they have never made a purchase.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ ClientID  â”‚ Name      â”‚ Location  â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ 15        â”‚ GreenCorp â”‚ TX        â”‚ âœ… No invoices
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜





ğŸ“Œ 2. Rewriting a Subquery Using a Join
âœ” Instead of filtering clients based on a subquery, we can use a LEFT JOIN.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Finding Clients Without Invoices (Using a Join)

SELECT *
FROM clients
LEFT JOIN invoices USING (client_id)
WHERE invoice_id IS NULL;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Retrieves all clients, but only displays those without an invoice.

ğŸ“Œ Which Approach Is Better? 
âœ” If working with a simple exclusion (NOT IN), subqueries are more intuitive. 
âœ” If joining related tables (clients & invoices), LEFT JOIN is more readable.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Exercise: Finding Customers Who Ordered "Lettuce"
ğŸš€ Task: âœ” Retrieve customers who ordered "Lettuce" (product_id = 3), displaying customer ID, first name, and last name.

Solution Using a Subquery:
USE sql_store;

SELECT customer_id, first_name, last_name  
FROM customers 
WHERE customer_id IN (
    SELECT o.customer_id
    FROM order_items oi
    JOIN orders o USING (order_id)
    WHERE product_id = 3
);
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Retrieves customers who placed orders containing product ID 3.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ CustomerIDâ”‚ FirstName â”‚ LastName  â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ 8         â”‚ Alice     â”‚ Johnson   â”‚ âœ… Ordered Lettuce
â”‚ 2         â”‚ Bob       â”‚ Smith     â”‚ âœ… Ordered Lettuce
â”‚ 10        â”‚ Maria     â”‚ Roberts   â”‚ âœ… Ordered Lettuce
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜


Solution Using a Join:
SELECT DISTINCT customer_id, first_name, last_name 
FROM customers c
JOIN orders o USING (customer_id)
JOIN order_items oi USING (order_id)
WHERE oi.product_id = 3;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Uses a natural relationship between customers, orders, and order items to retrieve relevant results.


ğŸ“Œ Comparing Both Approaches 
âœ” Subquery is more direct, as it retrieves customer IDs from order records. 
âœ” Join is more readable, as it traces the natural flow of data across tables (customers â†’ orders â†’ order_items). 
âœ” In this case, the join is preferable, as it provides clear visibility into relationships.





Queries used in this discussion:

FROM clients
WHERE client_id NOT IN (
	SELECT DISTINCT client_id
	FROM invoices
)



SELECT *
FROM clients
LEFT JOIN invoices USING (client_id)
WHERE invoice_id IS NULL

ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” Subqueries filter data based on computed results, suitable for exclusion queries (NOT IN). 
âœ” Joins follow natural data relationships, making multi-table queries more readable. 
âœ” Choose based on performance and readabilityâ€”always analyze execution plans for efficiency. 















The ALL Keyword:
----------------
ğŸš€ Understanding the ALL Keyword in SQL Subqueries
The ALL keyword allows comparison against multiple values retrieved by a subquery, ensuring the condition applies to all results. This tutorial covers how ALL works, when to use it, and its relationship with aggregate functions.

ğŸ“Œ 1. Using a Subquery to Retrieve Invoices Larger Than All Invoices of Client 3
âœ” To find invoices larger than every invoice issued by Client 3, we need to:
 - Retrieve the highest invoice total of Client 3 (MAX(invoice_total))
 - Use this value in a subquery to filter invoices

ğŸ“Œ Approach Using MAX():

USE sql_invoicing;

SELECT *
FROM invoices 
WHERE invoice_total > (
    SELECT MAX(invoice_total)
    FROM invoices
    WHERE client_id = 3
);
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Filters invoices where invoice_total exceeds $167, which is Client 3's largest invoice.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ InvoiceID â”‚ ClientID  â”‚ InvoiceTotal â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ 18        â”‚ 1         â”‚ 185.00       â”‚ âœ… Larger than Client 3's highest invoice
â”‚ 20        â”‚ 5         â”‚ 190.50       â”‚ âœ… Larger than Client 3's highest invoice
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜
âœ” Only invoices with totals greater than Client 3's highest invoice are selected.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Using the ALL Keyword for Comparison Against Multiple Values
âœ” Instead of using MAX(), we can compare invoices against all invoices from Client 3.

ğŸ“Œ Approach Using ALL:
SELECT *
FROM invoices 
WHERE invoice_total > ALL(
    SELECT invoice_total
    FROM invoices
    WHERE client_id = 3
);
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Filters invoices greater than every invoice issued by Client 3.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ InvoiceID â”‚ ClientID  â”‚ InvoiceTotal â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ 18        â”‚ 1         â”‚ 185.00       â”‚ âœ… Larger than ALL of Client 3â€™s invoices
â”‚ 20        â”‚ 5         â”‚ 190.50       â”‚ âœ… Larger than ALL of Client 3â€™s invoices
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜
ğŸ“Œ How It Works 
 - Subquery retrieves all invoice totals from Client 3. 
 - Main query selects invoices where the total is greater than ALL subquery results.


ğŸ“Œ Technical Representation:
SELECT *
FROM invoices 
WHERE invoice_total > ALL(150, 130, 167,...);
âœ” The query compares each invoice against every value in the subquery list.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Comparing MAX() vs. ALL Approaches
Approach	Use Case	                                                   Comparison Type
MAX()	    When comparing against a single highest value	               WHERE invoice_total > (SELECT MAX(...))
ALL	        When ensuring values exceed every result in a subquery	       WHERE invoice_total > ALL(SELECT â€¦)

ğŸ“Œ Which Method Is Better? âœ” Both approaches are interchangeable, but:
Use MAX() if filtering against a single value.
Use ALL if filtering against multiple values dynamically.

âœ” In this case, ALL better aligns with the problem statement ("larger than all invoices of Client 3"), making it more intuitive.





Queries used in this discussion: 

-- Select invoices larger than all invoices of client 3

USE sql_invoicing;

SELECT *
FROM invoices 
WHERE invoice_total > (
	SELECT MAX(invoice_total)
	FROM invoices
	WHERE client_id = 3
)



SELECT *
FROM invoices 
WHERE invoice_total > ALL(
	SELECT invoice_total
	FROM invoices
	WHERE client_id = 3
)


The above subquery will be tecnically returned as:
SELECT *
FROM invoices 
WHERE invoice_total > ALL(150, 130, 167,...)





ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” Use MAX() for single-value comparisons. 
âœ” Use ALL to ensure a value exceeds ALL results in a subquery. 
âœ” Both approaches can be rewritten interchangeably, depending on readability and intent. 


















The ANY Keyword:
----------------
ğŸš€ Understanding the ANY Keyword in SQL Subqueries
The ANY keyword allows comparison against multiple values, ensuring a condition applies to at least one result from a subquery. This tutorial covers how ANY works, when to use it, and how it compares to the IN operator.

ğŸ“Œ 1. Difference Between ALL and ANY
âœ” ALL ensures a condition is met for every subquery result (> ALL = greater than all values). 
âœ” ANY ensures a condition is met for at least one subquery result (> ANY = greater than at least one value). 
âœ” SOME is an alias for ANY, meaning they can be used interchangeably.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Finding Invoices Larger Than Any Invoice of Client 3

SELECT *
FROM invoices 
WHERE invoice_total > ANY(
    SELECT invoice_total
    FROM invoices
    WHERE client_id = 3
);
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Selects invoices with totals greater than at least one invoice issued by Client 3.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ InvoiceID â”‚ ClientID  â”‚ InvoiceTotal â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ 18        â”‚ 1         â”‚ 160.00       â”‚ âœ… Greater than at least one invoice from Client 3
â”‚ 20        â”‚ 5         â”‚ 170.50       â”‚ âœ… Greater than at least one invoice from Client 3
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜
âœ” Unlike ALL, some invoices below the highest value of Client 3's invoices may still be selected.

ğŸ“Œ Technical Representation:

SELECT *
FROM invoices 
WHERE invoice_total > ANY(150, 130, 167,...);
âœ” The query compares each invoice against at least one value in the subquery list.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Using ANY to Select Clients With At Least Two Invoices
âœ” Instead of filtering invoices one-by-one, we can group them by client and count occurrences.

ğŸ“Œ Step 1: Retrieve Clients With At Least Two Invoices

SELECT client_id
FROM invoices 
GROUP BY client_id
HAVING COUNT(*) >= 2;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Retrieves clients who have issued at least two invoices.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ ClientID  â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ 1         â”‚ âœ… Issued 2+ invoices
â”‚ 3         â”‚ âœ… Issued 2+ invoices
â”‚ 5         â”‚ âœ… Issued 2+ invoices
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜





ğŸ“Œ 3. Using IN vs ANY to Select These Clients
âœ” Approach Using IN

SELECT *
FROM clients
WHERE client_id IN (
    SELECT client_id
    FROM invoices 
    GROUP BY client_id
    HAVING COUNT(*) >= 2
);
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Selects clients present in the subquery list.


âœ” Approach Using ANY (Equivalent to IN)

SELECT *
FROM clients
WHERE client_id = ANY(
    SELECT client_id
    FROM invoices 
    GROUP BY client_id
    HAVING COUNT(*) >= 2
);
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Selects clients if their ID matches any result from the subquery.


ğŸ“Œ Comparing IN vs ANY:

Approach	Use Case	                                                    Comparison Type
IN	        Matches exact values from subquery results	                    WHERE column IN (SELECT â€¦)
ANY	        Matches if condition applies to at least one subquery result	WHERE column = ANY(SELECT â€¦)

âœ” Both queries return the same results, but IN is more intuitive for matching exact values.




Queries used in this discussion:  

SELECT *
FROM invoices 
WHERE invoice_total > ANY(      --   you can also use 'SOME' 
	SELECT invoice_total
	FROM invoices
	WHERE client_id = 3
)


-- SELECT clients with at least two invoices 


SELECT *
FROM clients
WHERE client_id IN (
	SELECT client_id
	FROM invoices 
	GROUP BY client_id
	HAVING COUNT(*) >= 2
)


SELECT *
FROM clients
WHERE client_id = ANY(
	SELECT client_id
	FROM invoices 
	GROUP BY client_id
	HAVING COUNT(*) >= 2
)






ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” Use ALL to compare values against every subquery result. 
âœ” Use ANY to compare values against at least one subquery result. 
âœ” SOME is interchangeable with ANYâ€”use either based on preference. 
âœ” IN and = ANY are equivalentâ€”choose based on readability. 


















Correlated Subqueries:
----------------------

ğŸš€ Understanding Correlated Subqueries in SQL
A correlated subquery is a subquery that executes once for each row in the main query, allowing comparisons based on dynamic calculations. This tutorial covers how correlated subqueries work, their differences from uncorrelated subqueries, and performance considerations.

ğŸ“Œ 1. What Is a Correlated Subquery?
âœ” A correlated subquery references a value from the outer query, meaning each row triggers a subquery execution. 
âœ” Unlike regular subqueries, correlated subqueries depend on values in the main query.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Finding Employees With Salaries Above the Average in Their Office

USE sql_hr;

SELECT *
FROM employees e
WHERE salary > (
    SELECT AVG(salary)
    FROM employees
    WHERE office_id = e.office_id
);
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Retrieves employees who earn above the average salary within their respective offices.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ EmpID     â”‚ Name      â”‚ Salary    â”‚ OfficeID  â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ 6         â”‚ Alice     â”‚ 65,000    â”‚ 1         â”‚ âœ… Above average for Office 1
â”‚ 9         â”‚ John      â”‚ 72,500    â”‚ 2         â”‚ âœ… Above average for Office 2
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜
âœ” Average salary is calculated separately for each office, ensuring employee comparisons remain relevant.

ğŸ“Œ How It Works 
 - Outer query selects employees (SELECT * FROM employees e). 
 - Subquery calculates the average salary (SELECT AVG(salary) FROM employees WHERE office_id = e.office_id). 
 - Main query compares each employeeâ€™s salary against their office average (WHERE salary > (subquery)).





ğŸ“Œ 2. Comparing Correlated vs. Uncorrelated Subqueries
âœ” Uncorrelated subqueries execute ONCE, returning a fixed dataset. 
âœ” Correlated subqueries execute ONCE PER ROW, adapting results dynamically.

ğŸ“Œ Example of an Uncorrelated Subquery âœ” Find employees earning above the company-wide average

SELECT *
FROM employees
WHERE salary > (
    SELECT AVG(salary)
    FROM employees
);
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Compares salaries against a single company-wide average, rather than office-specific averages.

ğŸ“Œ Key Differences:

Type	        Execution Count	       Use Case
Uncorrelated	Once	               Returns a single result for filtering
Correlated	    Once per row	       Dynamically compares each row against computed values

âœ” Uncorrelated subqueries are generally faster, while correlated subqueries allow contextual comparisons.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Performance Considerations
âœ” Correlated subqueries execute repeatedly, increasing computational overhead. 
âœ” When handling large datasets, consider using joins or indexed queries for optimization.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Optimizing Using Joins Instead of Correlated Subqueries

sql
SELECT e.*
FROM employees e
JOIN (
    SELECT office_id, AVG(salary) AS avg_salary
    FROM employees
    GROUP BY office_id
) a ON e.office_id = a.office_id
WHERE e.salary > a.avg_salary;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Precomputes office averages once, improving query efficiency.





ğŸ“Œ 4. Exercise: Finding Invoices Larger Than the Clientâ€™s Average Invoice Amount
ğŸš€ Task: âœ” Retrieve invoices where the total amount exceeds the average invoice total for that specific client.

ğŸ“Œ Expected Query Solution

USE sql_invoicing;

SELECT *
FROM invoices i
WHERE invoice_total > (
    SELECT AVG(invoice_total)
    FROM invoices 
    WHERE client_id = i.client_id
);
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Retrieves only invoices exceeding the client's average invoice amount, ensuring a fair comparison.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ InvoiceID â”‚ ClientID  â”‚ InvoiceTotal â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ 2         â”‚ 1         â”‚ 320.00       â”‚ âœ… Above clientâ€™s average invoice
â”‚ 4         â”‚ 3         â”‚ 250.00       â”‚ âœ… Above clientâ€™s average invoice
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜
âœ” Ensures invoices are filtered based on dynamic client-specific averages.

ğŸ“Œ Steps Used 
 - Subquery calculates each clientâ€™s average invoice total (SELECT AVG(invoice_total) FROM invoices WHERE client_id = i.client_id). 
 - Main query selects invoices where total exceeds this average (WHERE invoice_total > (subquery)). 
 - Each invoice is compared dynamically against its clientâ€™s historical invoices.





Queries used in this discussion:

-- Select employees whose salary is 
-- above the average in their office

-- 		calculate the avg salary for employee.office
--  	return the employee if salary > avg



SELECT *
FROM employees e
WHERE salary > (
	SELECT AVG(salary)
	FROM employees
	WHERE office_id = e.office_id
)

	SELECT AVG(invoice_total)
	FROM invoices

ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” Correlated subqueries execute once per row, enabling contextual comparisons. 
âœ” Uncorrelated subqueries execute once, filtering against a static result set. 
âœ” Performance can degrade with large datasetsâ€”use indexed joins for optimization. 























The EXISTS Opertor:
-------------------

ğŸš€ Understanding the EXISTS Operator in SQL
The EXISTS operator efficiently checks whether a subquery returns any matching rows, making it useful for large datasets where performance matters.

ğŸ“Œ 1. What Does the EXISTS Operator Do?
âœ” EXISTS verifies if a subquery returns at least one matching row, without retrieving actual data. 
âœ” Unlike the IN operator, which returns a complete list, EXISTS checks for existence and stops once a match is found.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Finding Clients Who Have an Invoice Using IN

sql
SELECT * 
FROM clients 
WHERE client_id IN ( 
        SELECT DISTINCT client_id
        FROM invoices
);
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Retrieves clients whose IDs are present in the invoices table.


ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ ClientID  â”‚ Name      â”‚ Location  â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ 1         â”‚ Alice     â”‚ NY        â”‚ âœ… Has invoices
â”‚ 3         â”‚ John      â”‚ TX        â”‚ âœ… Has invoices
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜





ğŸ“Œ 2. Using EXISTS for More Efficient Filtering
âœ” Instead of returning a full list of client IDs, EXISTS checks for matching entries dynamically.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Finding Clients Who Have an Invoice Using EXISTS

SELECT * 
FROM clients c
WHERE EXISTS ( 
    SELECT client_id
    FROM invoices
    WHERE client_id = c.client_id
);
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Retrieves clients where at least one invoice exists, avoiding unnecessary full-list comparisons.

ğŸ“Œ Key Differences Between IN and EXISTS

Approach	Execution Behavior	                                     Performance Impact
IN	        Returns a full list of IDs, then filters records	     Slow with large datasets
EXISTS	    Checks for matches row-by-row, stopping at first match	 More efficient for large datasets

ğŸ“Œ When to Use EXISTS? 
âœ” Use EXISTS when the subquery produces a large dataset (millions of rows). 
âœ” Use IN when handling small lists (fewer than a few thousand rows).





ğŸ“Œ 3. Exercise: Finding Products That Have Never Been Ordered
ğŸš€ Task: âœ” Retrieve all products that do not appear in any order.

ğŸ“Œ Solution Using IN

USE sql_store; 

SELECT * 
FROM products 
WHERE product_id NOT IN (
    SELECT product_id 
    FROM order_items
);

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Selects products missing from order_items, meaning they have never been ordered.


ğŸ“Œ Optimized Solution Using EXISTS:

SELECT * 
FROM products p
WHERE NOT EXISTS (
    SELECT product_id 
    FROM order_items
    WHERE product_id = p.product_id 
);

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Dynamically checks each product for existence in order_items, improving efficiency.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ ProductID â”‚ Name      â”‚ Price     â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ 7         â”‚ Broccoli  â”‚ 2.50      â”‚ âœ… Never ordered
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜
âœ” Ensures only truly unpurchased products appear.





Queries used in this discussion:

-- Select clients that have an invoice 

SELECT * 
FROM clients 
WHERE client_id IN ( 
		SELECT DISTINCT client_id
		FROM invoices
)


SELECT * 
FROM clients c
WHERE  EXISTS ( 
	SELECT client_id
	FROM invoices
	WHERE client_id = c.client_id   -- more efficient if the IN operator returns a larger dataset 
)

	SELECT product_id 
	FROM order_items
)


SELECT *
