	SELECT product_id 
	FROM order_items
	WHERE product_id = p.product_id 
)






ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” Use EXISTS for efficient existence checks, avoiding full-list retrieval. 
âœ” IN works well for smaller datasets, but scales poorly for larger tables. 
âœ” Both approaches achieve the same result, but EXISTS is faster when subqueries return large datasets. 

















Subqueries in the SELECT Clause:
--------------------------------
ğŸš€ Using Subqueries in the SELECT Clause in SQL
Subqueries are not limited to WHERE clausesâ€”they can also be used in the SELECT clause to compute additional values dynamically. This technique is useful for reporting and analytics, enabling column-level calculations.

ğŸ“Œ 1. What Are Subqueries in the SELECT Clause?
âœ” Subqueries within SELECT retrieve computed values for each row. 
âœ” Used for dynamic calculations, such as totals, averages, and differences.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Creating an Invoice Report With Dynamic Columns 
âœ” We need four columns: 
 - Invoice ID 
 - Invoice Total 
 - Invoice Average (average of all invoices) 
 - Difference (invoice total minus invoice average)


ğŸ“Œ Query Solution:

SELECT 
    invoice_id, 
    invoice_total, 
    (SELECT AVG(invoice_total) FROM invoices) AS invoice_average,
    invoice_total - (SELECT AVG(invoice_total) FROM invoices) AS difference
FROM invoices;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Dynamically computes the invoice average for all records.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ InvoiceID â”‚ Total     â”‚ Average   â”‚ Differenceâ”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ 1         â”‚ 250.00    â”‚ 180.50    â”‚ 69.50     â”‚ âœ… Above average
â”‚ 2         â”‚ 150.00    â”‚ 180.50    â”‚ -30.50    â”‚ âŒ Below average
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜
âœ” Allows comparison between individual invoice totals and the company-wide average.

ğŸ“Œ Why Use a Subquery Instead of a Separate Computation? 
âœ” Ensures real-time dynamic calculation without requiring additional queries. 
âœ” Avoids repetitionâ€”AVG(invoice_total) does not need to be manually computed elsewhere.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Exercise: Generating a Client Sales Report
ğŸš€ Task: âœ” Retrieve total sales for each client, comparing it against the average sales across all clients.

ğŸ“Œ Expected Query Solution

SELECT 
    client_id, 
    name,
    (SELECT SUM(invoice_total)
        FROM invoices
        WHERE client_id = c.client_id) AS total_sales, 
    (SELECT AVG(invoice_total) FROM invoices) AS average, 
    (SELECT SUM(invoice_total)
        FROM invoices
        WHERE client_id = c.client_id) - (SELECT AVG(invoice_total) FROM invoices) AS difference
FROM clients c;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Computes total sales per client, compares against the company-wide average, and calculates the difference dynamically.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ ClientID  â”‚ Name      â”‚ TotalSalesâ”‚ Average   â”‚ Differenceâ”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ 1         â”‚ ABC Corp  â”‚ 650.00    â”‚ 500.00    â”‚ 150.00    â”‚ âœ… Above average
â”‚ 4         â”‚ Beta Ltd  â”‚ NULL      â”‚ 500.00    â”‚ NULL      â”‚ âŒ No invoices
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜
âœ” Client 4 has no invoices, resulting in NULL values for total sales and difference.

ğŸ“Œ Steps Used: 
 - Retrieve each client's total sales using a correlated subquery (SUM(invoice_total) WHERE client_id = c.client_id). 
 - Compute the company-wide average sales using an uncorrelated subquery (AVG(invoice_total) FROM invoices). 
 - Calculate the difference between total client sales and the company average.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Key Considerations When Using Subqueries in SELECT
âœ” Avoid aliasing inside expressions (SELECT total_sales - average is invalid). 
âœ” For correlated subqueries, prefix table aliases properly (c.client_id) to ensure accurate filtering. 
âœ” Check for NULL valuesâ€”if a client has no invoices, ensure the result handles empty data correctly.

ğŸ“Œ How to Handle NULL Values Gracefully âœ” Use COALESCE() to replace NULL values with default values

SELECT COALESCE((SELECT SUM(invoice_total) FROM invoices WHERE client_id = c.client_id), 0) AS total_sales
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Ensures clients without invoices show 0 instead of NULL.





Queries used in this discussion:

SELECT 
	invoice_id, 
	invoice_total, 
	(SELECT AVG(invoice_total)
		FROM invoices) AS invoice_average
	invoice_total - (SELECT invoice_average) AS difference 
FROM invoices 



Exercise:

SELECT 
	client_id, 
	name,
	(SELECT SUM(invoice_total)
		FROM invoices
		WHERE client_id = c.client_id) AS total_sales 
	(SELECT AVG(invoice_total) FROM invoices) AS average 
	(SELECT total_sales - average) AS difference 
FROM clients c





ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” Subqueries in SELECT dynamically compute additional columns, improving reporting accuracy. 
âœ” Correlated subqueries filter based on individual rows, allowing row-specific calculations. 
âœ” Uncorrelated subqueries return static values, such as overall averages.










Subqueries in the FROM clause:
------------------------------
ğŸš€ Using Subqueries in the FROM Clause in SQL
Subqueries can be used not only in WHERE and SELECT clauses but also in the FROM clause, enabling dynamic table creation for advanced SQL operations.

ğŸ“Œ 1. What Are Subqueries in the FROM Clause?
âœ” Subqueries in FROM behave like temporary tables, allowing further filtering, aggregation, and joining. 
âœ” They provide structured datasets, useful when the database lacks pre-existing summarized tables.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Generating a Sales Summary Table âœ” We need four columns: 
 - Client ID 
 - Client Name 
 - Total Sales (sum of all invoices per client) 
 - Difference from Average Sales

ğŸ“Œ Query Solution

SELECT *
FROM (
    SELECT 
        client_id, 
        name,
        (SELECT SUM(invoice_total)
            FROM invoices
            WHERE client_id = c.client_id) AS total_sales, 
        (SELECT AVG(invoice_total) FROM invoices) AS average, 
        (SELECT SUM(invoice_total)
            FROM invoices
            WHERE client_id = c.client_id) - (SELECT AVG(invoice_total) FROM invoices) AS difference 
    FROM clients c
) AS sales_summary;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Creates a temporary table (sales_summary) containing summarized sales data.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ ClientID  â”‚ Name      â”‚ TotalSalesâ”‚ Average   â”‚ Differenceâ”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ 1         â”‚ ABC Corp  â”‚ 650.00    â”‚ 500.00    â”‚ 150.00    â”‚ âœ… Above average
â”‚ 4         â”‚ Beta Ltd  â”‚ NULL      â”‚ 500.00    â”‚ NULL      â”‚ âŒ No invoices
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜
âœ” Client 4 has no invoices, resulting in NULL values for total sales and difference.

ğŸ“Œ Why Use This Approach? 
âœ” Subqueries create virtual tables, allowing filtering, grouping, and joining just like real tables. 
âœ” Prevents repetitive calculations, improving query efficiency.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Filtering Data From the Subquery
âœ” Instead of displaying all records, we can filter clients who have invoices.

ğŸ“Œ Adding a WHERE Clause to Remove Clients Without Sales

SELECT *
FROM (
    SELECT 
        client_id, 
        name,
        (SELECT SUM(invoice_total)
            FROM invoices
            WHERE client_id = c.client_id) AS total_sales, 
        (SELECT AVG(invoice_total) FROM invoices) AS average, 
        (SELECT SUM(invoice_total)
            FROM invoices
            WHERE client_id = c.client_id) - (SELECT AVG(invoice_total) FROM invoices) AS difference 
    FROM clients c
) AS sales_summary
WHERE total_sales IS NOT NULL;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Removes clients with no invoices, ensuring clean reporting.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ ClientID  â”‚ Name      â”‚ TotalSalesâ”‚ Average   â”‚ Differenceâ”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ 1         â”‚ ABC Corp  â”‚ 650.00    â”‚ 500.00    â”‚ 150.00    â”‚ âœ… Above average
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜
âœ” Only clients with recorded invoices appear.





ğŸ“Œ 3. When to Use Subqueries in the FROM Clause?
âœ” Use subqueries when creating temporary tables for further filtering or aggregation. 
âœ” Avoid them for complex queriesâ€”instead, use views to simplify queries.

ğŸ“Œ Alternative Approach Using Views âœ” Instead of writing complex subqueries repeatedly, store them as a view.

ğŸš€ Example: Creating a View for Sales Summary

CREATE VIEW sales_summary AS
SELECT 
    client_id, 
    name,
    (SELECT SUM(invoice_total)
        FROM invoices
        WHERE client_id = c.client_id) AS total_sales, 
    (SELECT AVG(invoice_total) FROM invoices) AS average, 
    (SELECT SUM(invoice_total)
        FROM invoices
        WHERE client_id = c.client_id) - (SELECT AVG(invoice_total) FROM invoices) AS difference 
FROM clients c;

ğŸ“Œ Querying the View Instead of Using Subqueries

SELECT * FROM sales_summary WHERE total_sales IS NOT NULL;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Simplifies future queries, improving readability and efficiency.






Queries used in this discussion:

SELECT *
FROM (
	SELECT 
		client_id, 
		name,
		(SELECT SUM(invoice_total)
			FROM invoices
			WHERE client_id = c.client_id) AS total_sales 
		(SELECT AVG(invoice_total) FROM invoices) AS average 
		(SELECT total_sales - average) AS difference 
	FROM clients c
) AS sales_summary



SELECT *
FROM (
	SELECT 
		client_id, 
		name,
		(SELECT SUM(invoice_total)
			FROM invoices
			WHERE client_id = c.client_id) AS total_sales 
		(SELECT AVG(invoice_total) FROM invoices) AS average 
		(SELECT total_sales - average) AS difference 
	FROM clients c
) AS sales_summary
WHERE total_sales IS NOT NULL 





ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” Subqueries in FROM act as temporary tables, allowing advanced data manipulation. 
âœ” Filtering subquery results is possible using WHERE in the main query. 
âœ” For complex queries, consider creating a view instead of repeating subqueries. 
âœ” Next steps: Exploring indexed views for database optimization.



















Essential MySQl Functions:
--------------------------


Numeric Functions:
------------------
ğŸš€ Understanding Numeric Functions in MySQL
MySQL provides built-in numeric functions that allow rounding, truncating, computing absolute values, and generating random numbers, making calculations precise and efficient.

ğŸ“Œ 1. Rounding and Truncating Numbers
âœ” ROUND(number, precision) â†’ Rounds a number to the nearest value, keeping optional decimal places.

ğŸ“Œ Examples:

SELECT ROUND(5.73);  -- Returns 6
SELECT ROUND(5.73, 1);  -- Returns 5.7
SELECT ROUND(5.7345, 2);  -- Returns 5.73
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Without precision, rounding gives the nearest integer. âœ” With precision, retains specified decimal places.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
ROUND(5.73) â†’ 6
ROUND(5.73, 1) â†’ 5.7
ROUND(5.7345, 2) â†’ 5.73


âœ” TRUNCATE(number, precision) â†’ Cuts off decimal places without rounding.

ğŸ“Œ Example

SELECT TRUNCATE(5.7345, 2);  -- Returns 5.73
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Removes extra decimal places, keeping only specified digits.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output

TRUNCATE(5.7345, 2) â†’ 5.73
âœ” Use when precision matters but rounding is not needed.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Controlling Decimal Values
âœ” CEILING(number) â†’ Returns the next highest integer. 
âœ” FLOOR(number) â†’ Returns the next lowest integer.

ğŸ“Œ Examples

SELECT CEILING(5.7);  -- Returns 6
SELECT FLOOR(5.2);  -- Returns 5
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” CEILING() moves upwardâ€”always rounds up. âœ” FLOOR() moves downwardâ€”always rounds down.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output

CEILING(5.7) â†’ 6
FLOOR(5.2) â†’ 5
âœ” Use when needing precise integer thresholds.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Computing Absolute Values
âœ” ABS(number) â†’ Always returns a positive number.

ğŸ“Œ Examples:

SELECT ABS(5.2);  -- Returns 5.2
SELECT ABS(-5.2);  -- Returns 5.2
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Removes negativity, ensuring non-negative values only.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output

ABS(5.2) â†’ 5.2
ABS(-5.2) â†’ 5.2
âœ” Useful in financial calculations where magnitude matters more than direction.





ğŸ“Œ 4. Generating Random Numbers
âœ” RAND() â†’ Generates a floating-point number between 0 and 1.

ğŸ“Œ Example
SELECT RAND();
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Each execution returns a unique random decimal between 0 and 1.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
RAND() â†’ 0.8524 (random)
RAND() â†’ 0.4521 (random)
âœ” Use for simulations, unique IDs, and random selection.





ğŸ“Œ 5. Exploring Additional Numeric Functions
âœ” MySQL offers more numeric functions beyond those listed here.

ğŸ“Œ Reference Link for Complete List ğŸ”— MySQL Numeric Functions

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Provides full documentation on numeric manipulation functions, including modulus operations and logarithmic calculations.




Functions and other notes used in this discussion:

ROUND(5.7345, 2) -- 5.73

ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” ROUND() and TRUNCATE() control decimal precision, determining rounding behavior. 
âœ” CEILING() and FLOOR() adjust integers upward/downward, ensuring range control. 
âœ” ABS() computes absolute values, keeping numbers positive. 
âœ” RAND() generates unpredictable values, assisting with randomness. 










String Functions: 
-----------------

ğŸš€ String Functions in MySQL
MySQL offers powerful string functions to manipulate text efficiently, including length calculation, case conversion, trimming spaces, extracting substrings, searching, replacing, and concatenation.

ğŸ“Œ 1.Measuring String Length
âœ” LENGTH(string) â†’ Returns the number of characters in a string.

ğŸ“Œ Examples:
SELECT LENGTH('Sky');  -- Returns 3
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Counts all characters, including spaces.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output
LENGTH('Sky') â†’ 3
âœ” Useful for validating string lengths.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Converting Case
âœ” UPPER(string) â†’ Converts text to uppercase. âœ” LOWER(string) â†’ Converts text to lowercase.

ğŸ“Œ Examples:
SELECT UPPER('Sky');  -- Returns 'SKY'
SELECT LOWER('Sky');  -- Returns 'sky'
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Ensures consistent formatting for case-sensitive applications.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
UPPER('Sky') â†’ SKY
LOWER('Sky') â†’ sky





ğŸ“Œ 3. Removing Spaces
âœ” LTRIM(string) â†’ Removes leading spaces. âœ” RTRIM(string) â†’ Removes trailing spaces. âœ” TRIM(string) â†’ Removes both leading and trailing spaces.

ğŸ“Œ Examples:
SELECT LTRIM('  Sky');  -- Returns 'Sky'
SELECT RTRIM('Sky  ');  -- Returns 'Sky'
SELECT TRIM('  Sky  ');  -- Returns 'Sky'
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Cleans up user input, ensuring consistent spacing.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
LTRIM('  Sky') â†’ 'Sky'
RTRIM('Sky  ') â†’ 'Sky'
TRIM('  Sky  ') â†’ 'Sky'





ğŸ“Œ 4. Extracting Substrings
âœ” LEFT(string, length) â†’ Extracts characters from the start. 
âœ” RIGHT(string, length) â†’ Extracts characters from the end. 
âœ” SUBSTRING(string, start, length) â†’ Extracts characters from anywhere in a string.

ğŸ“Œ Examples:
SELECT LEFT('Kindergarten', 4);  -- Returns 'Kind'
SELECT RIGHT('Kindergarten', 6);  -- Returns 'garten'
SELECT SUBSTRING('Kindergarten', 3, 5);  -- Returns 'nderg'
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Enables precise string extraction for structured data processing.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
LEFT('Kindergarten', 4) â†’ 'Kind'
RIGHT('Kindergarten', 6) â†’ 'garten'
SUBSTRING('Kindergarten', 3, 5) â†’ 'nderg'





ğŸ“Œ 5. Finding Text Positions
âœ” LOCATE(search_string, string) â†’ Finds the first occurrence position of a substring.

ğŸ“Œ Examples:
SELECT LOCATE('n', 'Kindergarten');  -- Returns 3
SELECT LOCATE('garden', 'Kindergarten');  -- Returns 7
SELECT LOCATE('x', 'Kindergarten');  -- Returns 0 (not found)
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Locates where text appears within a string.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
LOCATE('n', 'Kindergarten') â†’ 3
LOCATE('garden', 'Kindergarten') â†’ 7
LOCATE('x', 'Kindergarten') â†’ 0
âœ” Returns 0 if the text does not exist (unlike -1 in many programming languages).





ğŸ“Œ 6. Replacing Text
âœ” REPLACE(string, find, replacement) â†’ Replaces specific text within a string.

ğŸ“Œ Example:
SELECT REPLACE('Kindergarten', 'garten', 'garden');  -- Returns 'Kindergarden'
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Modifies text without changing its structure.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
REPLACE('Kindergarten', 'garten', 'garden') â†’ 'Kindergarden'





ğŸ“Œ 7. Concatenating Strings
âœ” CONCAT(string1, string2, ...) â†’ Joins multiple strings.

ğŸ“Œ Example:
SELECT CONCAT('First', ' ', 'Last');  -- Returns 'First Last'
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Merges multiple text fragments into a single value.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
CONCAT('First', ' ', 'Last') â†’ 'First Last'
âœ” Use for formatting names, addresses, and messages.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Combining First and Last Names:
SELECT CONCAT(first_name, ' ', last_name) AS full_name 
FROM customers;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Dynamically creates full names for each customer.





ğŸ“Œ 8. Exploring Additional String Functions
âœ” MySQL offers many more string functions beyond those listed here.

ğŸ“Œ Reference Link for Complete List ğŸ”— MySQL String Functions

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Provides full documentation on advanced string manipulation techniques.

ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” LENGTH() measures character count in a string. 
âœ” UPPER() and LOWER() standardize text casing. 
âœ” TRIM() removes unnecessary spaces, ensuring clean formatting. 
âœ” LEFT(), RIGHT(), and SUBSTRING() extract portions of text dynamically. 
âœ” LOCATE() finds text positions, enabling structured search operations. 
âœ” REPLACE() modifies text efficiently, making replacements within strings. 
âœ” CONCAT() merges text values, improving data readability. 

















Date Functions in MySQL:
------------------------
ğŸš€ Date Functions in MySQL
MySQL provides built-in functions to work with date and time values, enabling efficient extraction, formatting, and manipulation.

ğŸ“Œ 1. Retrieving Current Date and Time
âœ” NOW() â†’ Returns current date and time. âœ” CURDATE() â†’ Returns current date (without time). âœ” CURTIME() â†’ Returns current time (without date).

ğŸ“Œ Examples:
SELECT NOW();  -- Current date & time
SELECT CURDATE();  -- Current date only
SELECT CURTIME();  -- Current time only
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Captures real-time system timestamps, useful for tracking transactions.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
NOW() â†’ 2025-05-29 14:25:30
CURDATE() â†’ 2025-05-29
CURTIME() â†’ 14:25:30





ğŸ“Œ 2. Extracting Specific Components from Date-Time
âœ” YEAR(date) â†’ Returns year component. 
âœ” MONTH(date) â†’ Returns month component. 
âœ” DAY(date) â†’ Returns day component. 
âœ” HOUR(date) â†’ Extracts hour from a timestamp. 
âœ” MINUTE(date) â†’ Extracts minute component. 
âœ” SECOND(date) â†’ Extracts second component.

ğŸ“Œ Examples:
SELECT YEAR(NOW());  -- Extracts current year
SELECT MONTH(NOW());  -- Extracts current month
SELECT DAY(NOW());  -- Extracts current day
SELECT HOUR(NOW());  -- Extracts current hour
SELECT MINUTE(NOW());  -- Extracts current minute
SELECT SECOND(NOW());  -- Extracts current second
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Extracts granular date-time components, useful for calculations and filtering.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
YEAR(NOW()) â†’ 2025
MONTH(NOW()) â†’ 5
DAY(NOW()) â†’ 29
HOUR(NOW()) â†’ 14
MINUTE(NOW()) â†’ 25
SECOND(NOW()) â†’ 30





ğŸ“Œ 3. Extracting Day & Month Names
âœ” DAYNAME(date) â†’ Returns weekday name. 
âœ” MONTHNAME(date) â†’ Returns month name.

ğŸ“Œ Examples:
SELECT DAYNAME(NOW());  -- Returns the current day of the week
SELECT MONTHNAME(NOW());  -- Returns the current month name
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Converts numeric dates into readable formats, improving user-friendliness.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
DAYNAME(NOW()) â†’ Thursday
MONTHNAME(NOW()) â†’ May





ğŸ“Œ 4. Using the EXTRACT() Function
âœ” The EXTRACT() function is part of standard SQL, making queries portable across database systems.

ğŸ“Œ Syntax:
SELECT EXTRACT(YEAR FROM NOW());  -- Extracts current year
SELECT EXTRACT(DAY FROM NOW());  -- Extracts current day
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Similar to YEAR() and DAY(), but ensures compatibility across different SQL databases.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
EXTRACT(YEAR FROM NOW()) â†’ 2025
EXTRACT(DAY FROM NOW()) â†’ 29





ğŸ“Œ 5. Exercise: Retrieving Orders Placed in the Current Year
ğŸš€ Task: âœ” Modify the query to dynamically filter orders based on the current year.

ğŸ“Œ Incorrect Query Using Hardcoded Year:
SELECT * 
FROM orders
WHERE order_date >= '2019-01-01';  -- âŒ Not reliable
ğŸš€ Problem: âœ” The query does not update dynamically, meaning in 2026, it may return results for 2019, 2020, etc.

ğŸ“Œ Correct Query Using Dynamic Year Extraction:
SELECT * 
FROM orders
WHERE YEAR(order_date) = YEAR(NOW());
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Ensures the query always filters based on the current year, no matter when it runs.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:

ğŸ“Œ Key Takeaways

Formatting Dates and Times: 
---------------------------
ğŸš€ Formatting Dates and Times in MySQL
MySQL represents dates using the format YYYY-MM-DD, which is ideal for developers but may not be user-friendly in reports or applications. To customize date and time formats, MySQL offers DATE_FORMAT() and TIME_FORMAT() functions.

ğŸ“Œ 1. Understanding DATE_FORMAT()
âœ” DATE_FORMAT(date, format_string) â†’ Formats a date value using special specifiers.

ğŸ“Œ Examples:
SELECT DATE_FORMAT(NOW(), '%y');  -- Returns two-digit year (19)
SELECT DATE_FORMAT(NOW(), '%Y');  -- Returns full four-digit year (2019)
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Controls how dates appear, ensuring better readability.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
DATE_FORMAT(NOW(), '%y') â†’ 19
DATE_FORMAT(NOW(), '%Y') â†’ 2019





ğŸ“Œ 2. Formatting Month and Day Components
âœ” Use %m for numeric month, %M for month name. âœ” Use %d for day.

ğŸ“Œ Examples:
SELECT DATE_FORMAT(NOW(), '%m %y');  -- Returns '03 2019'
SELECT DATE_FORMAT(NOW(), '%M %Y');  -- Returns 'March 2019'
SELECT DATE_FORMAT(NOW(), '%M %d %y');  -- Returns 'March 11 2019'
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Displays dates in a readable format for users.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
DATE_FORMAT(NOW(), '%m %y') â†’ 03 2019
DATE_FORMAT(NOW(), '%M %Y') â†’ March 2019
DATE_FORMAT(NOW(), '%M %d %y') â†’ March 11 2019





ğŸ“Œ 3. Using TIME_FORMAT() for Time Formatting
âœ” TIME_FORMAT(time, format_string) â†’ Formats a time value with special specifiers.

ğŸ“Œ Examples:
SELECT TIME_FORMAT(NOW(), '%H:%i %p');  -- Returns '14:25 PM'
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Converts time formats, improving clarity for reports and logs.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
TIME_FORMAT(NOW(), '%H:%i %p') â†’ 14:25 PM
âœ” %H (24-hour format), %i (minutes), %p (AM/PM).





ğŸ“Œ 4. Reference Guide for Additional Format Specifiers
âœ” Instead of memorizing format codes, refer to MySQL's official documentation.

ğŸ“Œ Reference Link for Full Format Specifiers ğŸ”— MySQL Date & Time Formatting

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Provides a detailed list of format specifiers, ensuring customized outputs.





Queries and other notes in this discussion:
DATE_FORMAT(NOW(), '%y') -- 19
DATE_FORMAT(NOW(), '%Y') -- 2019


DATE_FORMAT(NOW(), '%m %y') -- 03 2019
DATE_FORMAT(NOW(), '%M %Y') -- March 2019 

DATE_FORMAT(NOW(), '%M %d %y') -- March 11 2019

ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” DATE_FORMAT() modifies date appearance, using %Y, %M, %d, etc. 
âœ” TIME_FORMAT() customizes time formats, making AM/PM formats easier to read. 
âœ” Refer to MySQL documentation for additional format options. 












Calculating Dates and Times:
-----------------------------
ğŸš€ Calculating Dates and Times in MySQL
MySQL provides powerful built-in functions for manipulating dates and times, enabling dynamic operations such as adding/subtracting intervals and computing differences between timestamps.

ğŸ“Œ 1. Adding and Subtracting Dates
âœ” DATE_ADD(date, INTERVAL x unit) â†’ Adds a specific interval to a date. 
âœ” DATE_SUB(date, INTERVAL x unit) â†’ Subtracts an interval from a date.

ğŸ“Œ Examples:
SELECT DATE_ADD(NOW(), INTERVAL 1 DAY);  -- Adds 1 day to the current date
SELECT DATE_ADD(NOW(), INTERVAL 1 YEAR);  -- Adds 1 year to the current date
SELECT DATE_ADD(NOW(), INTERVAL -1 YEAR);  -- Subtracts 1 year
SELECT DATE_SUB(NOW(), INTERVAL 1 YEAR);  -- Alternative way to subtract 1 year
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Enables date manipulation, useful for scheduling and forecasting.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
DATE_ADD(NOW(), INTERVAL 1 DAY) â†’ 2025-05-30
DATE_ADD(NOW(), INTERVAL 1 YEAR) â†’ 2026-05-29
DATE_ADD(NOW(), INTERVAL -1 YEAR) â†’ 2024-05-29
DATE_SUB(NOW(), INTERVAL 1 YEAR) â†’ 2024-05-29
âœ” Both DATE_ADD() and DATE_SUB() work similarly, with DATE_SUB() simplifying subtraction operations.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Calculating Date Differences
âœ” DATEDIFF(date1, date2) â†’ Returns the difference between two dates in days.

ğŸ“Œ Examples:
SELECT DATEDIFF('2019-01-05', '2019-01-01');  -- Returns 4 days
SELECT DATEDIFF('2019-01-05 09:00', '2019-01-01 17:00');  -- Ignores time, still returns 4 days
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Computes differences in days only, ignoring time components.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
DATEDIFF('2019-01-05', '2019-01-01') â†’ 4
DATEDIFF('2019-01-05 09:00', '2019-01-01 17:00') â†’ 4
âœ” Even if times are included, only the day difference is considered.

ğŸ“Œ Swapping Order of Dates 
âœ” If date1 is later than date2, the result is positive. 
âœ” If date1 is earlier than date2, the result is negative.


SELECT DATEDIFF('2019-01-01', '2019-01-05');  -- Returns -4
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Negative values indicate that date1 is before date2.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
DATEDIFF('2019-01-01', '2019-01-05') â†’ -4





ğŸ“Œ 3. Calculating Time Differences
âœ” TIME_TO_SEC(time) â†’ Converts time values into seconds since midnight.

ğŸ“Œ Examples:
SELECT TIME_TO_SEC('09:00');  -- Returns 32,400 seconds (9 hours)
SELECT TIME_TO_SEC('09:02') - TIME_TO_SEC('09:00');  -- Returns 120 seconds
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Enables time interval calculations, useful for scheduling tasks.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
TIME_TO_SEC('09:00') â†’ 32,400
TIME_TO_SEC('09:02') - TIME_TO_SEC('09:00') â†’ 120
âœ” Order Matters:

If time1 is earlier than time2, the result is negative.

SELECT TIME_TO_SEC('09:00') - TIME_TO_SEC('09:02');  -- Returns -120
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Helps track elapsed time in seconds, useful for performance metrics.





Queries and other notes in this discussion: 

SELECT DATE_ADD(NOW(), INTERVAL 1 DAY);
SELECT DATE_ADD(NOW(), INTERVAL 1 YEAR);
SELECT DATE_ADD(NOW(), INTERVAL -1 YEAR);
SELECT DATE_SUB(NOW(), INTERVAL 1 YEAR);
SELECT DATEDIFF('2019-01-05', '2019-01-01');
SELECT DATEDIFF('2019-01-05 09:00', '2019-01-01 17:00'); --only returns difference in days, even if we include time values

SELECT TIME_TO_SEC('09:00')
SELECT TIME_TO_SEC('09:02') - TIME_TO_SEC('09:00') -- 120 




ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” Use DATE_ADD() and DATE_SUB() to manipulate dates dynamically, adding or subtracting years, months, or days. 
âœ” Use DATEDIFF() to compute differences in days, ignoring time values. 
âœ” Use TIME_TO_SEC() for precise time interval calculations, converting hours, minutes, and seconds into seconds since midnight. 
















The IFNULL and COALESCE Functions: 
----------------------------------
ğŸš€ Using the IFNULL and COALESCE Functions in MySQL
Handling NULL values efficiently in MySQL ensures clean, user-friendly data representation. This tutorial covers how to replace NULL values dynamically using IFNULL and COALESCE.

ğŸ“Œ 1. Understanding IFNULL()
âœ” IFNULL(column, replacement_value) â†’ Replaces NULL with a specified alternative. 
âœ” Accepts two arguments:
First argument â†’ The column or expression to check.
Second argument â†’ The replacement value if the first argument is NULL.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Handling Missing Shipper Information âœ” In the orders table, some orders have NULL shipper IDs, meaning they havenâ€™t been assigned yet.

SELECT 
    order_id, 
    IFNULL(shipper_id, 'Not assigned') AS shipper
FROM orders;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Replaces NULL shipper IDs with "Not assigned", making the data readable.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ OrderID   â”‚ Shipper      â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ 1         â”‚ 103          â”‚ âœ… Assigned
â”‚ 2         â”‚ Not assigned â”‚ âŒ No shipper assigned
â”‚ 5         â”‚ 205          â”‚ âœ… Assigned
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜
âœ” Ensures missing shipper data is displayed clearly.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Understanding COALESCE()
âœ” COALESCE(value1, value2, ..., default_value) â†’ Returns the first non-NULL value from the list. âœ” More flexible than IFNULL(), since it can check multiple values.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Using COALESCE() to Handle Multiple Columns âœ” If shipper_id is NULL, retrieve the comments column instead. âœ” If both are NULL, display "Not assigned".

sql
SELECT 
    order_id, 
    COALESCE(shipper_id, comments, 'Not assigned') AS shipper
FROM orders;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Displays shipper ID if available. âœ” If shipper ID is missing, displays comments. âœ” If both are missing, displays "Not assigned".

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ OrderID   â”‚ Shipper          â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ 1         â”‚ 103              â”‚ âœ… Assigned
â”‚ 2         â”‚ Delayed Shipment â”‚ ğŸ“ Uses comment field
â”‚ 5         â”‚ Not assigned     â”‚ âŒ No data available
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜
âœ” Provides a fallback system to ensure meaningful output.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Exercise: Formatting Customer Information
ğŸš€ Task: âœ” Display customer full name (first + last name). âœ” If phone number is missing (NULL), display "Unknown".

ğŸ“Œ Expected Query Solution:
SELECT 
    CONCAT(first_name, ' ', last_name) AS customer, 
    COALESCE(phone, 'Unknown') AS phone
FROM customers;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Ensures readable customer data, replacing missing phone numbers with "Unknown".

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ Customer      â”‚ Phone     â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ Alice Johnson â”‚ 555-1234  â”‚ âœ… Has phone number
â”‚ Bob Smith     â”‚ Unknown   â”‚ âŒ Missing phone number
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜
âœ” Improves user experience by handling missing data effectively.

ğŸ“Œ Steps Used 
 - Used CONCAT() to merge first and last names into a single column (customer). 
 - Applied COALESCE() to replace missing phone values with "Unknown".





ğŸ“Œ 4. Comparing IFNULL and COALESCE

Function	                                     Use Case	                                              Flexibility
IFNULL(column, replacement)	                     Checks one column and replaces NULL	                  Limited
COALESCE(column1, column2, fallback_value)	     Checks multiple columns, using first non-NULL value	  More flexible

ğŸ“Œ When to Use Each? 
âœ” Use IFNULL() if replacing a single column value with a fallback. 
âœ” Use COALESCE() if selecting from multiple columns, ensuring the first available value is used.





Queries and other notes used in this discussion:

SELECT 
	order_id, 
	IF_NULL(shipper_id, 'Not assigned') AS shipper
FROM orders;


SELECT 
	order_id
	COALESCE(shipper_id, comments, 'Not assigned')
FROM orders;


Exercise:
SELECT 
	CONCAT(first_name, ' ', last_name) AS customer, 
	COALESCE(phone, 'Unknown') AS phone
FROM customers;






ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” IFNULL() replaces missing values with a fixed alternative, ensuring readable outputs. 
âœ” COALESCE() selects the first non-NULL value from multiple columns, providing fallbacks. 
âœ” Improves data clarity, making reports and user-facing data meaningful. 

















The IF Function:
----------------
ğŸš€ Understanding the IF Function in MySQL
The IF function in MySQL allows conditional evaluations, returning different values based on whether a condition evaluates to TRUE or FALSE. It is useful for categorizing data dynamically, reducing the need for multiple queries.

ğŸ“Œ 1. Using IF to Categorize Orders
âœ” Scenario:
We want to classify orders as Active or Archived.
Orders placed in the current year â†’ Active.
Older orders â†’ Archived.

âœ” Instead of using UNION between two queries, we use IF to streamline logic.

ğŸ“Œ Query Solution

SELECT
    order_id, 
    order_date, 
    IF(
        YEAR(order_date) = YEAR(NOW()),  
        'Active', 
        'Archived'
    ) AS category
FROM orders;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Returns order classification dynamically, ensuring real-time accuracy.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:

ğŸ“Œ 2. Exercise: Categorizing Product Order Frequency
ğŸš€ Task: âœ” Retrieve each product, displaying:
 - Number of times it was ordered (COUNT(*)).
 - Frequency Category (Many times or Once).

ğŸ“Œ Expected Query Solution:
SELECT 
    product_id, 
    name, 
    COUNT(*) AS orders,
    IF(COUNT(*) > 1, 'Many times', 'Once') AS frequency
FROM products
JOIN order_items USING (product_id)
GROUP BY product_id, name;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Counts the number of orders for each product and categorizes its frequency.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:

The CASE Operator:
------------------

ğŸš€ Using the CASE Operator in MySQL
The CASE operator in MySQL allows complex conditional evaluations, enabling multiple conditions within a single query. It is useful for categorizing data dynamically, reducing the need for multiple queries or UNION operations.

ğŸ“Œ 1. Understanding the CASE Operator
âœ” CASE tests multiple conditions, returning different values based on the result. âœ” It functions like an IF-ELSE structure in programming.

ğŸ“Œ Syntax:

CASE 
    WHEN condition1 THEN result1
    WHEN condition2 THEN result2
    ELSE default_result
END AS alias

âœ” Multiple conditions can be defined, ensuring accurate categorization.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Categorizing Orders Based on Placement Year
âœ” Scenario:
Orders placed in the current year â†’ Active.
Orders placed last year â†’ Last Year.
Older orders â†’ Archived.
Future orders â†’ Future.

ğŸ“Œ Query Solution:
SELECT 
    order_id, 
    CASE 
        WHEN YEAR(order_date) = YEAR(NOW()) THEN 'Active'
        WHEN YEAR(order_date) = YEAR(NOW()) - 1 THEN 'Last Year'
        WHEN YEAR(order_date) < YEAR(NOW()) - 1 THEN 'Archived'
        ELSE 'Future'
    END AS category
FROM orders;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Classifies orders dynamically, ensuring real-time accuracy.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ OrderID   â”‚ Category  â”‚ OrderDate  â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ 45        â”‚ Active    â”‚ 2025-05-10 â”‚ âœ… Placed this year
â”‚ 38        â”‚ Last Year â”‚ 2024-03-15 â”‚ ğŸ—‚ From last year
â”‚ 20        â”‚ Archived  â”‚ 2020-08-22 â”‚ ğŸ“ Older order
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜
âœ” Ensures categorized order tracking without separate queries.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Exercise: Categorizing Customers Based on Loyalty Points

Gold â†’ Above 3000 points.

ğŸ“Œ 4. Comparing IF vs. CASE:
Function	                                             Use Case	                      Flexibility
IF(condition, value_if_true, value_if_false)	         Single condition evaluation	  Limited
CASE WHEN condition THEN result ... ELSE default END	 Multiple conditions	          Highly flexible


âœ” Use IF for simple evaluations, use CASE for complex categorization.

Views:
------

Creating Views:
---------------
ğŸš€ Creating Views in MySQL
Views in MySQL simplify complex queries, making them reusable and more manageable. They act as virtual tables, allowing you to store SQL logic without duplicating code.

ğŸ“Œ 1. What Are Views in MySQL?
âœ” A view is a stored query that behaves like a table, allowing users to select, filter, and join data dynamically. 
âœ” Unlike tables, views do not store dataâ€”they only retrieve information from underlying tables.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Creating a View for Sales Per Client
ğŸš€ Scenario: âœ” Compute total sales per client to simplify future queries.

ğŸ“Œ Query to Get Sales Per Client:
USE sql_invoicing; 

CREATE VIEW sales_by_client AS
SELECT 
    c.client_id, 
    c.name, 
    SUM(invoice_total) AS total_sales
FROM clients c
JOIN invoices i USING (client_id)
GROUP BY client_id, name;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Stores the logic for retrieving total sales per client, making it reusable for multiple queries.

ğŸ“Œ Selecting Data from the View:
SELECT * FROM sales_by_client;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Retrieves total sales for each client, behaving like a regular table.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:

ğŸ“Œ 5. Exercise: Creating a View for Client Balances

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Stores client balances dynamically, simplifying accounting queries.

Altering or Dropping Views:
---------------------------
ğŸš€ Altering or Dropping Views in MySQL
Views in MySQL help simplify complex queries, but sometimes modifications are necessary. There are two primary ways to alter views: dropping and recreating, or using CREATE OR REPLACE for efficient updates.

ğŸ“Œ 1. Dropping and Recreating Views:
âœ” DROP VIEW view_name; â†’ Removes the view from the database.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Dropping a View:
DROP VIEW sales_by_client;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Completely removes the view, requiring it to be manually recreated.

ğŸ“Œ Recreating the View:
CREATE VIEW sales_by_client AS
SELECT 
    c.client_id, 
    c.name, 
    SUM(invoice_total) AS total_sales
FROM clients c
JOIN invoices i USING (client_id)
GROUP BY client_id, name;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Restores the view after dropping it, allowing further modifications.

ğŸ“Œ Drawbacks âœ” Manually dropping views is inefficient, especially when frequently updating queries.

ğŸ“Œ 2. Using CREATE OR REPLACE for Easier Modifications
âœ” Instead of dropping and recreating a view, use CREATE OR REPLACE VIEW.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Modifying the View Without Dropping It:
CREATE OR REPLACE VIEW sales_by_client AS
SELECT 
    c.client_id, 
    c.name, 
    SUM(invoice_total) AS total_sales
FROM clients c
JOIN invoices i USING (client_id)
GROUP BY client_id, name;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Replaces an existing view without manual deletion. âœ” Preserves queries dependent on the view, preventing disruptions.

ğŸ“Œ Advantages âœ” More efficientâ€”avoids unnecessary view deletion. âœ” Ensures continuity for applications relying on the view.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Best Practices for Managing Views
âœ” Save view definitions in SQL files to ensure reproducibility. 
âœ” Store views in a version-controlled repository (e.g., Git) for collaboration and tracking changes. 
âœ” Organize views into separate folders for structured management.

ğŸ“Œ Example Folder Structure for SQL Views

views/
   â”œâ”€â”€ sales_by_client.sql
   â”œâ”€â”€ client_balance.sql
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Enables easier tracking, sharing, and recovery.





ğŸ“Œ 4. Viewing and Editing Existing Views
âœ” MySQL stores views as metadata, allowing access via GUI or SQL commands.

ğŸ“Œ Manually Editing Views in MySQL Workbench 
âœ” Find the views panel, then click the edit icon. 
âœ” Modify the query, ensuring any necessary ORDER BY or WHERE conditions. 
âœ” Click Apply to commit changes.

ğŸ“Œ Adding Sorting to a View:
CREATE OR REPLACE VIEW sales_by_client AS
SELECT 
    c.client_id, 
    c.name, 
    SUM(invoice_total) AS total_sales
FROM clients c
JOIN invoices i USING (client_id)
GROUP BY client_id, name
ORDER BY total_sales DESC;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Ensures client sales are sorted in descending order.





Queries used in this discussion:

	c.client_id, 
	c.name, 
	SUM(invoice_total) AS total_sales

SELECT 
	c.client_id, 
	c.name, 
	SUM(invoice_total) AS total_sales
FROM clients c
JOIN invoices i USING (client_id)
GROUP BY client_id, name;





ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” Use DROP VIEW to remove views manually, followed by CREATE VIEW for recreation. 
âœ” Use CREATE OR REPLACE VIEW for efficient modifications without disruptions. 
âœ” Store SQL view files in version control systems (Git) for tracking and collaboration. 
âœ” Modify views directly in MySQL Workbench when necessary. 
















Updatable Views:
----------------
ğŸš€ Updatable Views in MySQL
Views in MySQL can be used in SELECT statements. But under certain conditions, they can also support INSERT, UPDATE, and DELETE operations. These are known as updatable views.

ğŸ“Œ 1. What Makes a View Updatable?
âœ” A view can be modified using INSERT, UPDATE, or DELETE if it does not contain:
DISTINCT keyword.
Aggregate functions (SUM(), AVG(), MAX(), etc.).
GROUP BY or HAVING clauses.
UNION operations.

ğŸ“Œ Updatable Views vs. Non-Updatable Views

View Type	          Supports Insert, Update, Delete?
Updatable View	      âœ… Yes (if it meets all conditions)
Non-Updatable View	  âŒ No (contains aggregate/grouping functions, unions, etc.)

âœ” If a view follows these rules, it can be modified like a regular table.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Creating an Updatable View:
ğŸš€ Scenario: âœ” The invoices table contains invoice_total and payment_total, but no balance column. âœ” Instead of recalculating the balance each time, create an updatable view to display and modify balances.

ğŸ“Œ Creating the View

CREATE OR REPLACE VIEW invoices_with_balance AS
SELECT 
    invoice_id, 
    number, 
    client_id, 
    invoice_total, 
    payment_total, 
    invoice_total - payment_total AS balance, 
    invoice_date, 
    due_date, 
    payment_date
FROM invoices 
WHERE (invoice_total - payment_total) > 0;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Creates a virtual table displaying invoice balances without modifying the original table structure.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ InvoiceID â”‚ ClientID  â”‚ Balance   â”‚ DueDate   â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ 101       â”‚ 5         â”‚ 150.75    â”‚ 2025-06-10â”‚ âœ… Open balance
â”‚ 204       â”‚ 3         â”‚ 75.00     â”‚ 2025-06-15â”‚ âœ… Pending payment
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜
âœ” Ensures only invoices with an outstanding balance are displayed.

ğŸ“Œ Important Note âœ” Aliases (AS balance) cannot be used in the WHERE clause, so use the full expression instead.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Updating and Deleting Data Using an Updatable View
âœ” Once a view is updatable, we can modify data just like a regular table.

ğŸ“Œ Deleting an Invoice:
DELETE FROM invoices_with_balance
WHERE invoice_id = 1;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Deletes the invoice with ID 1, just like a normal table operation.

ğŸ“Œ Updating an Invoice Due Date:
UPDATE invoices_with_balance
SET due_date = DATE_ADD(due_date, INTERVAL 2 DAY)
WHERE invoice_id = 2;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Extends the due date for invoice ID 2 by two days.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Due Date Changes

Before Update â†’ Due Date: 2025-06-01
After Update â†’ Due Date: 2025-06-03
âœ” Direct updates allow easy modifications through the view.





ğŸ“Œ 4. Inserting Data Through an Updatable View
âœ” Inserting data works if the view contains all required columns from the underlying table. 
âœ” If a column allows NULL values, inserting through the view may fail.

ğŸ“Œ Key Rule for Inserts âœ” Ensure required columns (invoice_date, client_id, etc.) exist in the view.






Queries and other notes in this discussion:

-- Aggregate Functions (MIN, MAX, SUM,..) 
-- GROUP BY/ HAVING
-- UNION 


CREATE OR REPLACE VIEW invoices_with_balance AS

	invoice_id, 
	number, 
	client_id, 
	invoice_total,

WHERE invoice_id = 1;

SET due_date = DATE_ADD(due_date, INTERVAL 2 DAY)
WHERE invoice_id = 2; 





ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” Updatable views allow INSERT, UPDATE, and DELETE operations, provided no aggregation, grouping, or unions exist. 
âœ” Views simplify complex calculations, making data handling more intuitive. 
âœ” Using UPDATE and DELETE on views modifies the original table, ensuring changes reflect correctly. 















The WITH OPTION CHECK Clause: 
-----------------------------
ğŸš€ Understanding the WITH CHECK OPTION Clause in MySQL Views
The WITH CHECK OPTION clause ensures that updates or deletions within a view do not remove rows from the view's result set. This prevents modifications that would cause records to disappear unexpectedly.

ğŸ“Œ 1. Default Behavior of Updatable Views
âœ” Views allow modifying data (INSERT, UPDATE, DELETE) under certain conditions. 
âœ” However, changes may cause rows to no longer meet the view's filter criteria, removing them from results.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Removing a Record by Updating a Value 
âœ” Suppose an invoice has an outstanding balance. 
âœ” If we update the payment_total to match invoice_total, the balance becomes 0, and the record disappears from the view.

ğŸ“Œ Updating Payment Total to Match Invoice Total:
UPDATE invoices_with_balance
SET payment_total = invoice_total 
WHERE invoice_id = 2;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Invoice disappears from the view, because it no longer matches the condition (balance > 0).

ğŸ“Œ Refreshing the View âœ” Before update::
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ InvoiceID â”‚ InvoiceTotal â”‚ Balance   â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ 2         â”‚ 450.00       â”‚ 50.00     â”‚ âœ… Balance exists
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜

âœ” After update:
(No resultsâ€”InvoiceID '2' is removed from the view)

âœ” Default behavior: rows disappear if modifications break view conditions.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Using WITH CHECK OPTION to Prevent Data Loss
âœ” Adding WITH CHECK OPTION prevents updates that would remove records from the view. 
âœ” Any attempt to modify a row such that it no longer meets the viewâ€™s criteria will result in an error.

ğŸ“Œ Updating the View Definition:
CREATE OR REPLACE VIEW invoices_with_balance AS
SELECT 
    invoice_id, 
    number, 
    client_id, 
    invoice_total, 
    payment_total, 
    invoice_total - payment_total AS balance, 
    invoice_date, 
    due_date, 
    payment_date
FROM invoices 
WHERE (invoice_total - payment_total) > 0
WITH CHECK OPTION;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Ensures modifications keep records within the view.

ğŸ“Œ Attempting an Update That Would Remove a Row:
UPDATE invoices_with_balance
SET payment_total = invoice_total 
WHERE invoice_id = 3;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Fails with an error:

ERROR 1369 (HY000): CHECK OPTION failed
âœ” Prevents removal of records due to modifications.





ğŸ“Œ 3. When to Use WITH CHECK OPTION?
âœ” Use WITH CHECK OPTION when you want to enforce view constraints, ensuring:
Data integrity is maintained.
Records do not unexpectedly disappear.
Users modifying data through views are restricted from breaking conditions.

ğŸ“Œ Best Practice âœ” Apply WITH CHECK OPTION on views with filtering conditions (WHERE, HAVING) to prevent unintended updates.





Queries used in this discussion: 

UPDATE invoices_with_balance
SET payment_total = invoice_total 
WHERE invoice_id = 2; 


CREATE OR REPLACE VIEW invoices_with_balance AS
SELECT 
	invoice_id, 
	number, 
	client_id, 
	invoice_total, 
	payment_total, 
	invoice_total - payment_total AS balance, 
	invoice_date, 
	due_date, 
	payment_date
FROM invoices 
WHERE (invoice_total - payment_total) > 0
WITH CHECK OPTION; 





ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” Updatable views allow modifications, but rows may disappear if updates break view conditions. 
âœ” Using WITH CHECK OPTION enforces constraints, preventing deletions due to updates. 
âœ” Unsuccessful updates will trigger an error, ensuring data integrity within views.









Other Benefits Of Views:
------------------------
ğŸš€ Key Benefits of Views in MySQL
Views in MySQL offer powerful advantages beyond simplifying queries. They help reduce the impact of database changes, provide data security, and improve maintainability.

ğŸ“Œ 1. Simplifying Queries
âœ” Views encapsulate complex queries, making them easier to manage and reuse. 
âœ” Instead of writing lengthy JOIN, GROUP BY, and filtering logic repeatedly, store these operations within a view. 
âœ” Example: Calculating Invoice Balances

CREATE VIEW invoices_with_balance AS
SELECT 
    invoice_id, 
    client_id, 
    invoice_total, 
    payment_total, 
    invoice_total - payment_total AS balance
FROM invoices;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Provides a structured way to retrieve balances, preventing repetition in multiple queries.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Reducing the Impact of Database Changes
âœ” Views create an abstraction layer, protecting queries from underlying table modifications. 
âœ” If a table is renamed or a column is moved, queries referencing the view remain unaffected.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Handling Column Changes 
âœ” If payment_total is renamed to total_paid, queries relying on invoices_with_balance would break. 
âœ” Instead of modifying every query, simply update the view definition.

CREATE OR REPLACE VIEW invoices_with_balance AS
SELECT 
    invoice_id, 
    client_id, 
    invoice_total, 
    total_paid AS payment_total,  -- Restoring old column name
    invoice_total - total_paid AS balance
FROM invoices;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Queries using the view remain unchanged, even if the original table column names change.





ğŸ“Œ Why It Matters? 
âœ” Reduces maintenance overheadâ€”only the view needs updating, not hundreds of queries. 
âœ” Ensures backward compatibility for applications and reports.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Restricting Access to Sensitive Data
âœ” Views can restrict access to certain columns or rows, enhancing data security. 
âœ” Instead of granting direct access to tables, users interact only with views, ensuring controlled visibility.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Restricting Sensitive Information 
âœ” Suppose the invoices table contains financial data that some users should not access. 
âœ” Instead of granting direct access, create a restricted view.

CREATE VIEW public_invoices AS
SELECT 
    invoice_id, 
    client_id, 
    invoice_total, 
    invoice_date 
FROM invoices;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Users can query public_invoices, but cannot see payment_total or balance.

ğŸ“Œ Additional Security Using WITH CHECK OPTION âœ” Prevent modifications that remove rows from a view.

CREATE VIEW invoices_with_balance AS
SELECT * FROM invoices WHERE invoice_total - payment_total > 0
WITH CHECK OPTION;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Ensures users cannot modify records in a way that removes them from the view.





ğŸ“Œ 4. Best Practices for Using Views
âœ” Use views for repeated queries, minimizing redundant code. 
âœ” Ensure views remain updatable if required, avoiding aggregation functions like SUM() and GROUP BY. 
âœ” Apply security controls to restrict access, improving data protection and compliance. 
âœ” Store view definitions in version control, making updates easier for teams.




Main points in this discussion:

Simplify Queries
Reduce the impact of changes
Restrict access to the data 


ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” Views simplify complex queries, making database management more efficient. 
âœ” They protect queries from schema changes, preventing query disruptions. 
âœ” They enhance security, limiting data exposure through controlled visibility. 




















Stored Procedures:
------------------
ğŸš€ Understanding Stored Procedures in MySQL
Stored procedures in MySQL help organize SQL code, making database operations efficient, secure, and easier to maintain.

ğŸ“Œ 1. What Are Stored Procedures?
âœ” Stored procedures are database objects that contain SQL logic, allowing structured execution. 
âœ” Instead of writing SQL queries in application code, procedures store queries within the database. 
âœ” Applications call stored procedures to retrieve, update, or modify data efficiently.

ğŸ“Œ Example Use Case: 
âœ” If building an application with Java, Python, or C#, embedding raw SQL within the application code makes it cluttered and hard to maintain. 
âœ” Instead of mixing SQL with application logic, store queries in a MySQL stored procedure.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Why Store SQL Code in Procedures?
âœ” Avoid Messy Code Integration

Mixing SQL with application logic (C#, Java, Python) makes debugging difficult. âœ” Prevent Recompilation Issues
Some languages require compilation. If queries change, entire code recompile and redeployment may be needed. âœ” Improve Performance
Most database systems optimize stored procedures, making execution faster. âœ” Enhance Security
Restrict direct table access, ensuring only stored procedures modify records.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Calling a Stored Procedure from an Application âœ” Instead of writing:
SELECT * FROM orders WHERE order_date > '2024-01-01';

âœ” Store it inside a procedure:
CREATE PROCEDURE GetRecentOrders()
BEGIN
    SELECT * FROM orders WHERE order_date > '2024-01-01';
END;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Allows applications to call GetRecentOrders(), simplifying query execution.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Performance and Security Benefits
âœ” Stored procedures improve execution speed by precompiling SQL logic. 
âœ” Restrict direct access to tables, preventing unauthorized modifications.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Enforcing Security with Stored Procedures 
âœ” Instead of granting direct table access, users only execute stored procedures.

GRANT EXECUTE ON PROCEDURE GetRecentOrders TO 'report_user';
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Limits query execution privileges, ensuring restricted database interaction.





Queries and other notes used in this discussion:

Store and organize SQL
Faster execution
Data security


ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” Stored procedures organize SQL code, reducing messiness in application logic. 
âœ” They enhance performance, as databases optimize procedure execution. 
âœ” They improve security, restricting direct table modifications. 

















Creating a Stored Procedure:
----------------------------
ğŸš€ Creating a Stored Procedure in MySQL
Stored procedures allow SQL logic to be stored within the database, ensuring organized, reusable, and efficient queries.

ğŸ“Œ 1.What Is a Stored Procedure?
âœ” A stored procedure is a database object that contains SQL statements, which can be executed repeatedly without rewriting queries. 
âœ” Instead of embedding SQL in application code, procedures store and execute queries within the database. 
âœ” Stored procedures improve performance, maintainability, and security.

ğŸ“Œ Syntax for Creating a Stored Procedure:

CREATE PROCEDURE procedure_name()
BEGIN
    SQL_statement;
END;
âœ” SQL statements within BEGIN ... END define the procedureâ€™s functionality.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Creating a Simple Stored Procedure
ğŸš€ Scenario: âœ” We need a procedure to retrieve all clients.

ğŸ“Œ Defining the Stored Procedure:

DELIMITER $$

CREATE PROCEDURE get_clients()
BEGIN 
    SELECT * FROM clients;
END$$

DELIMITER ;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Creates a stored procedure called get_clients(), which retrieves all client records.

ğŸ“Œ Why Change the Delimiter? 
âœ” MySQL uses ; as the default statement separator. 
âœ” Stored procedures contain multiple statements, requiring a different delimiter ($$) to prevent execution issues.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ ClientID  â”‚ Name      â”‚ City          â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ 1         â”‚ ABC Corp  â”‚ New York      â”‚ âœ… Client record
â”‚ 2         â”‚ Beta Ltd  â”‚ San Francisco â”‚ âœ… Client record
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜
âœ” Eliminates the need to rewrite queries for every request.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Executing a Stored Procedure
âœ” Stored procedures can be executed using the CALL statement.

ğŸ“Œ Executing get_clients()

CALL get_clients();
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Retrieves all clients without rewriting SELECT queries.

ğŸ“Œ Best Practice âœ” Most applications call stored procedures using C#, Java, Python, rather than directly in SQL.





ğŸ“Œ 4. Exercise: Creating a Stored Procedure for Invoice Balances
ğŸš€ Task: âœ” Define a stored procedure get_invoices_with_balance() to retrieve invoices with outstanding balances.

ğŸ“Œ Writing the Query:
SELECT * FROM invoices WHERE invoice_total - payment_total > 0;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Retrieves invoices where payment total is less than invoice total, meaning balance > 0.

ğŸ“Œ Creating the Stored Procedure:
DELIMITER $$

CREATE PROCEDURE get_invoices_with_balance()
BEGIN 
    SELECT * 
    FROM invoices_with_balance
    WHERE balance > 0;  
END$$

DELIMITER ;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Stores invoice retrieval logic inside the procedure, making it reusable.

ğŸ“Œ Executing the Procedure:
CALL get_invoices_with_balance();
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Returns all invoices with outstanding balances.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:

ğŸš€ Creating Stored Procedures Using MySQL Workbench

ğŸ“Œ 1. Using MySQL Workbench for Stored Procedures
âœ” MySQL Workbench allows procedure creation through its GUI, simplifying workflow.

ğŸ“Œ Steps for Creating a Procedure Using MySQL Workbench

2. Right-click the Stored Procedures folder in the navigator panel. 
3. Click "Create Stored Procedure" to open the procedure editor. 
4. Define the procedure name (e.g., get_payments). 
5. Write the SQL statements, ensuring each ends with a ;. 
6. Click "Apply" to save and execute.

ğŸ“Œ Example Procedure in Workbench:

BEGIN 
    SELECT * FROM payments; 
END;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Stores the query inside a procedure for easy execution.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Handling Delimiters in Workbench
âœ” Unlike manual SQL entry, MySQL Workbench automatically handles delimiters. âœ” Upon clicking "Apply," MySQL Workbench generates necessary SQL commands, ensuring smooth execution.

    SELECT * FROM payments;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Automatically sets delimiters, preventing manual adjustments.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Executing a Stored Procedure
âœ” Once created, stored procedures can be executed using CALL.

ğŸ“Œ Calling get_payments():

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Returns all payments from the payments table.

Dropping Stored Procedures:
---------------------------
ğŸš€ Dropping Stored Procedures in MySQL
Stored procedures help structure SQL logic within the database. However, when modifications are needed, procedures must be dropped and recreated. This guide covers how to safely drop stored procedures while maintaining version control.

ğŸ“Œ 1. Dropping a Stored Procedure:
âœ” The DROP PROCEDURE statement removes a stored procedure from the database. âœ” Attempting to drop a nonexistent procedure results in an error.

ğŸ“Œ Basic Syntax:
DROP PROCEDURE get_clients;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Removes get_clients from the database.

ğŸ“Œ Handling Errors When Dropping Procedures âœ” If a procedure does not exist, MySQL raises an error.
DROP PROCEDURE get_clients;
-- ERROR 1305 (42000): PROCEDURE get_clients does not exist

âœ” To prevent errors, use IF EXISTS.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Safely Dropping a Procedure Using IF EXISTS
âœ” Adding IF EXISTS prevents MySQL from raising an error if the procedure doesn't exist.

ğŸ“Œ Recommended Safe Dropping Syntax:
DROP PROCEDURE IF EXISTS get_clients;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Ensures smooth execution, avoiding unnecessary errors.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Best Practices for Managing Stored Procedures
âœ” Store procedure definitions in SQL files, ensuring organized version control. 
âœ” Use Git for tracking changes, allowing teams to maintain consistency.

ğŸ“Œ Example SQL File Structure:

Parameters:
----------
ğŸš€ Using Parameters in MySQL Stored Procedures
Stored procedures can accept parameters, allowing dynamic query execution. This tutorial covers how to define and use parameters in MySQL stored procedures.

ğŸ“Œ 1. What Are Parameters in Stored Procedures?
âœ” Parameters allow passing values into stored procedures, making queries flexible and reusable. 
âœ” They can be used for filtering data dynamically, instead of hardcoding values. 
âœ” Types of parameters:
CHAR(n) â†’ Fixed-length string (Example: "CA" for California).
VARCHAR(n) â†’ Variable-length string (Example: "California").
INT â†’ Whole numbers (Example: 1, 10, 250).





ğŸ“Œ 2. Creating a Stored Procedure with a Parameter
ğŸš€ Scenario: âœ” Create a stored procedure to retrieve clients by state.

ğŸ“Œ Defining the Procedure:
DROP PROCEDURE IF EXISTS get_clients_by_state;

DELIMITER $$

CREATE PROCEDURE get_clients_by_state(
    state CHAR(2)  -- Two-character state code
)
BEGIN 
    SELECT * FROM clients c
    WHERE c.state = state; 
END $$

DELIMITER ;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Defines a procedure get_clients_by_state(), which filters clients based on the passed state parameter.


ğŸ“Œ Executing the Procedure:
CALL get_clients_by_state('CA');
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Returns all clients in California (CA).


ğŸ“Œ Example Output:

Parameter With Default Value:
-----------------------------
ğŸš€ Using Default Values in Stored Procedure Parameters in MySQL
Stored procedures in MySQL can accept parameters with default values, allowing flexibility in execution. This tutorial covers how to assign default values, conditionally filter data, and optimize queries.

ğŸ“Œ 1. Why Use Default Values in Stored Procedures?
âœ” Allows procedures to execute even when parameters are missing. 
âœ” Enables flexible filtering, returning default results when parameters are null. 
âœ” Reduces redundant queries, making stored procedures efficient and reusable.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Assigning Default Values Using IF Statements
ğŸš€ Scenario: âœ” Modify get_clients_by_state() to return California (CA) by default when no state is provided.

ğŸ“Œ Procedure Definition:
DROP PROCEDURE IF EXISTS get_clients_by_state;

DELIMITER $$

CREATE PROCEDURE get_clients_by_state(
    state CHAR(2)  -- Two-character state code
)
BEGIN 
    IF state IS NULL THEN 
        SET state = 'CA';  -- Default state
    END IF;
    
    SELECT * FROM clients c
    WHERE c.state = state; 
END $$

DELIMITER ;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” If NULL is passed, procedures return clients from California (CA).

ğŸ“Œ Executing the Procedure:
CALL get_clients_by_state(NULL);

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Returns California clients when no state is supplied.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ ClientID  â”‚ Name      â”‚ State     â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ 1         â”‚ ABC Corp  â”‚ CA        â”‚ âœ… California-based client
â”‚ 2         â”‚ Beta Ltd  â”‚ CA        â”‚ âœ… California-based client
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜





ğŸ“Œ 3. Returning All Clients When No State is Provided
âœ” Instead of assigning CA as the default, return all clients if NULL is passed.

ğŸ“Œ Procedure Definition:
DROP PROCEDURE IF EXISTS get_clients_by_state;

DELIMITER $$

CREATE PROCEDURE get_clients_by_state(
    state CHAR(2) 
)
BEGIN 
    IF state IS NULL THEN 
        SELECT * FROM clients;  -- Return all clients
    ELSE
        SELECT * FROM clients c WHERE c.state = state;
    END IF;
END $$

DELIMITER ;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Returns all clients when no state is specified. âœ” When a state is provided, filters clients by state.

ğŸ“Œ Executing the Procedure:
CALL get_clients_by_state(NULL);  -- Returns all clients
CALL get_clients_by_state('NY');  -- Returns clients from New York

âœ” Efficient data filtering, avoiding unnecessary conditions.





ğŸ“Œ 4. Optimizing Queries Using IFNULL()
âœ” Instead of using IF-ELSE, use IFNULL() in the WHERE clause.

ğŸ“Œ Updated Procedure Definition:
DROP PROCEDURE IF EXISTS get_clients_by_state;

DELIMITER $$

CREATE PROCEDURE get_clients_by_state(
    state CHAR(2) 
)
BEGIN 
    SELECT * FROM clients c
    WHERE c.state = IFNULL(state, c.state);  
END $$

DELIMITER ;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” If state is NULL, it compares c.state = c.state (always true), returning all records. âœ” If state is specified, filters by state.

ğŸ“Œ Executing the Procedure:
CALL get_clients_by_state(NULL);  -- Returns all clients
CALL get_clients_by_state('TX');  -- Returns clients in Texas
âœ” Efficient and structured filtering using a single query.





ğŸ“Œ 5. Exercise: Creating a Flexible Payment Query
ğŸš€ Task: 
âœ” Define a stored procedure get_payments() with: 
 - client_id (INT) 
 - payment_method_id (TINYINT) 
âœ” If both parameters are NULL, return all payments. 
âœ” If only client_id is supplied, return payments for that client. 
âœ” If both are supplied, return payments matching both criteria.

ğŸ“Œ Procedure Definition:
DROP PROCEDURE IF EXISTS get_payments;

DELIMITER $$

CREATE PROCEDURE get_payments(
    client_id INT,
    payment_method_id TINYINT
)
BEGIN 
    SELECT * FROM payments p
    WHERE 
        p.client_id = IFNULL(client_id, p.client_id) AND 
        p.payment_method = IFNULL(payment_method_id, p.payment_method);
END $$

DELIMITER ;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Handles different filtering cases dynamically.

ğŸ“Œ Executing the Procedure:
CALL get_payments(NULL, NULL);  -- Returns all payments
CALL get_payments(5, NULL);  -- Returns payments for client ID 5
CALL get_payments(NULL, 2);  -- Returns payments using method ID 2
CALL get_payments(5, 2);  -- Returns payments for client ID 5 using method 2

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ PaymentID â”‚ ClientID  â”‚ Amount    â”‚ Method    â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤
â”‚ 10        â”‚ 5         â”‚ 250.00    â”‚ 2         â”‚ âœ… Payment for Client 5 using Method 2
â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜

âœ” Simplifies flexible payment queries using parameters with default values.

	state CHAR(2)

	SELECT * FROM clients c
	WHERE C.state = IFNULL(state, c.state);   -- if it is null, c.state = c.state, this condition is always true, returns all the rows

Parameter Validation:
---------------------
ğŸš€ Parameter Validation in MySQL Stored Procedures
Stored procedures can insert, update, and delete data, but without validation, they may store incorrect values. This guide covers parameter validation techniques to ensure data integrity and prevent errors.

ğŸ“Œ 1. Why Validate Parameters in Stored Procedures?
âœ” Prevents invalid data entry, ensuring only valid values are inserted or updated. 
âœ” Improves data integrity, avoiding negative, out-of-range, or null values. 
âœ” Ensures meaningful error handling, allowing procedures to return useful messages when validation fails.

ğŸ“Œ 2. Creating a Stored Procedure with Parameter Validation
ğŸš€ Scenario: âœ” Define a procedure make_payment() to update an invoiceâ€™s payment total and payment date. âœ” Ensure payment amount is valid (greater than 0).

ğŸ“Œ Initial Procedure Without Validation:
USE sql_invoicing;
DROP PROCEDURE IF EXISTS make_payment;

DELIMITER $$

CREATE PROCEDURE make_payment(
    invoice_id INT,
    payment_amount DECIMAL(9,2),
    payment_date DATE
)
BEGIN 
    UPDATE invoices i
    SET 
        i.payment_total = payment_amount,
        i.payment_date = payment_date
    WHERE i.invoice_id = invoice_id;
END $$

DELIMITER ;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Updates payment details for a given invoice, but does not prevent negative amounts.


ğŸ“Œ Executing the Procedure:
CALL make_payment(2, 100, '2019-01-01');
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Successfully updates invoice 2 with payment $100.


ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
InvoiceID | Payment Total | Payment Date
-----------------------------------------
2         | 100.00        | 2019-01-01
âœ” Works correctly for valid payment amounts.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Preventing Invalid Payments with Parameter Validation
âœ” Ensure payment amount is greater than 0. âœ” Use SIGNAL to raise an error if invalid values are passed.

ğŸ“Œ Updating the Procedure with Validation:
USE sql_invoicing;
DROP PROCEDURE IF EXISTS make_payment;

DELIMITER $$

CREATE PROCEDURE make_payment(
    invoice_id INT,
    payment_amount DECIMAL(9,2),
    payment_date DATE
)
BEGIN 
    IF payment_amount <= 0 THEN 
        SIGNAL SQLSTATE '22003' SET MESSAGE_TEXT = 'Invalid payment amount';
    END IF;

    UPDATE invoices i
    SET 
        i.payment_total = payment_amount,
        i.payment_date = payment_date
    WHERE i.invoice_id = invoice_id;
END $$

DELIMITER ;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Prevents invalid payments, ensuring only valid amounts are stored.


ğŸ“Œ Executing the Procedure with Invalid Payment:
CALL make_payment(2, -100, '2019-01-01');
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Triggers an error:

ERROR 22003 (HY000): Invalid payment amount
âœ” Prevents storing negative values in payment_total.


ğŸ“Œ Example: Refreshing Invoice Data
InvoiceID | Payment Total | Payment Date
-----------------------------------------
2        | 100.00        | 2019-01-01  âœ… Correct

âœ” Data integrity is maintainedâ€”negative values are rejected.





ğŸ“Œ 4. Understanding SQLSTATE Error Codes
âœ” Error codes classify validation failures, ensuring standardized error handling. 
âœ” 22003 is the SQL standard error code for "numeric value out of range".

ğŸ“Œ Finding SQL Error Codes âœ” Search for SQLSTATE error codes online to find standard errors. âœ” Use IBMâ€™s formatted SQL error code list for clarity.





ğŸ“Œ 5. Best Practices for Parameter Validation
âœ” Validate critical inputs (payment amounts, IDs, required fields) to prevent bad data entry. 
âœ” Use SIGNAL SQLSTATE to provide detailed error messages, improving troubleshooting. 
âœ” Avoid excessive validation within stored proceduresâ€”validate inputs in the application layer where possible.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Application-Level Validation âœ” Before calling the procedure, check input values in Java/Python/PHP:

if payment_amount <= 0:
    raise ValueError("Invalid payment amount")

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Catches errors before hitting the database, reducing query execution overhead.





Queries and other notes in this discussion:

DROP PROCEDURE IF EXISTS make_payment;

DELIMITER$$

(
	invoice_id INT,
	payment_amount DECIMAL(9,2),
	payment_date DATE
)
BEGIN 
	UPDATE invoices i
	SET 
		i.payment_total = payment_amount,
		i.payment_date = payment_date
	WHERE i.invoice_id = invoice_id;
END $$

DELIMITER ;

CALL make_payment(2, 100, '2019-01-01')
