DELIMITER$$
CREATE PROCEDURE make_payment
(
	invoice_id INT,
	payment_amount DECIMAL(9,2),
	payment_date DATE
)
BEGIN 
	IF payment_amount <=  0 THEN 
		SIGNAL SQLSTATE '22003' SET MESSAGE_TEXT = 'Invalid payment amount';
	END IF; 
	
	UPDATE invoices i
	SET 
		i.payment_total = payment_amount,
		i.payment_date = payment_date
	WHERE i.invoice_id = invoice_id;
END $$

DELIMITER ;

CALL make_payment(2, -100, '2019-01-01')



search for sqlstate errors 





ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” Stored procedures allow inserting, updating, and deleting data, but require validation to prevent errors. 
âœ” Use SIGNAL SQLSTATE to raise errors, ensuring invalid parameters are handled properly. 
âœ” SQLSTATE error codes standardize validation messages, improving debugging and user feedback. 
âœ” Keep validation minimal in stored procedures, relying on application-level checks where possible.













Output Parameters: 
------------------
ğŸš€ Using Output Parameters in MySQL Stored Procedures
Stored procedures in MySQL can accept input parameters, but they can also return values using output parameters. This tutorial covers how to define and retrieve output parameters in stored procedures.

ğŸ“Œ 1. What Are Output Parameters in Stored Procedures?
âœ” Input parameters allow passing values to a procedure. 
âœ” Output parameters allow returning values from a procedure. 
âœ” Useful for returning multiple values, making stored procedures more versatile.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Creating a Stored Procedure with Output Parameters
ğŸš€ Scenario: 
âœ” Define a procedure get_unpaid_invoices_for_client() to return: 
 - Total count of unpaid invoices (invoices_count) 
 - Sum of unpaid invoice amounts (invoices_total)

ğŸ“Œ Defining the Procedure:
DELIMITER $$

CREATE PROCEDURE get_unpaid_invoices_for_client(
    client_id INT,
    OUT invoices_count INT,    -- Output parameter for total count
    OUT invoices_total DECIMAL(9,2) -- Output parameter for total amount
)
BEGIN 
    SELECT COUNT(*), SUM(invoice_total)
    INTO invoices_count, invoices_total 
    FROM invoices i
    WHERE i.client_id = client_id AND payment_total = 0; 
END $$

DELIMITER ;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Stores calculated values in output parameters, ensuring dynamic result retrieval.


ğŸ“Œ Executing the Procedure:
CALL get_unpaid_invoices_for_client(3);

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Returns total unpaid invoices and their sum.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
Invoices Count | Invoices Total
--------------------------------
2              | 286.00





ğŸ“Œ 3. Retrieving Output Parameters Using Variables
âœ” Output parameters do not directly return values in MySQL. 
âœ” Use user-defined variables (@variable) to capture output parameter values.

ğŸ“Œ Defining Variables and Calling the Procedure:
SET @invoices_count = 0;
SET @invoices_total = 0;

CALL get_unpaid_invoices_for_client(3, @invoices_count, @invoices_total);

SELECT @invoices_count, @invoices_total;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Stores output values into user-defined variables, allowing retrieval.


ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
@invoices_count | @invoices_total
---------------------------------
2               | 286.00

âœ” Variables hold returned values, making them accessible for further calculations.





ğŸ“Œ 4. When to Use Output Parameters?
âœ” Useful for returning multiple computed values. 
âœ” Not necessary for simple queriesâ€”consider using SELECT instead. 
âœ” Preferred when integrating stored procedures with external applications (Java, Python, etc.).

ğŸ“Œ Alternative Approach Using SELECT (Avoiding Output Parameters) âœ” Instead of using output parameters, directly return values.

SELECT COUNT(*) AS invoices_count, SUM(invoice_total) AS invoices_total
FROM invoices
WHERE client_id = 3 AND payment_total = 0;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Directly retrieves results without needing variables.

ğŸ“Œ Why Avoid Output Parameters? 
âœ” Adds complexityâ€”using SELECT is simpler for direct queries. 
âœ” Requires extra steps (SET variables, CALL procedure, SELECT variables).






Queries and other notes in this discussion:

DELIMITER$$
CREATE PROCEDURE get_unpaid_invoices_for_client
(
	client_id INT
	OUT invoices_count INT
	OUT invoices_total DECIMAL(9,2)
)
BEGIN 
	SELECT COUNT(*), SUM(invoice_total)
	INTO invoices_count, invoices_total 
	FROM invoices i
	WHERE i.client_id = client_id AND payment_total = 0; 
	
END$$

DELIMITER ;


CALL get_unpaid_invoices_for_client(3); 


set @invoices_count = 0;
set @invoices_total = 0; 

CALL get_unpaid_invoices_for_client(3, @invoices_count, @invoices_total); 

select @invoices_count, @invoices_total;





ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” Stored procedures can return values using output parameters, but require additional steps to retrieve them. 
âœ” Use output parameters for complex procedures that need multiple values. 
âœ” For simple queries, prefer SELECT instead of output parameters. 














Variables:
----------
ğŸš€ Understanding Variables in MySQL Stored Procedures
MySQL supports two main types of variables: user (session) variables and local variables. These variables store values in memory, helping perform calculations and pass information within stored procedures.

ğŸ“Œ 1. User (Session) Variables
âœ” User variables persist throughout a session until the client disconnects. 
âœ” They are prefixed with @ and defined using the SET statement. 
âœ” Commonly used for retrieving values from stored procedures with output parameters.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Defining a User Variable
SET @invoices_count = 0;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Initializes @invoices_count with a value of 0, storing it for the session duration.


ğŸ“Œ Using User Variables in Stored Procedure Calls:
CALL get_unpaid_invoices_for_client(3, @invoices_count, @invoices_total);
SELECT @invoices_count, @invoices_total;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Stores output values in user variables, making them accessible across session queries.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
@invoices_count | @invoices_total
---------------------------------
2               | 286.00

âœ” Used for retrieving procedure results dynamically.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Local Variables
âœ” Defined inside stored procedures using the DECLARE statement. 
âœ” Exist only within the procedureâ€”disappear after execution. 
âœ” Often used for calculations, temporary storage, and transformations.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Declaring a Local Variable:
DECLARE risk_factor DECIMAL(9, 2) DEFAULT 0;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Creates a variable (risk_factor) for temporary calculations. âœ” Disappears after procedure execution.


ğŸ“Œ Data Types Used for Local Variables:
Type	        Usage
INT	            Whole numbers
DECIMAL(m, n)	Numbers with decimal points
VARCHAR(n)	    Text values
DATE	        Date storage





ğŸ“Œ 3. Using Local Variables in a Stored Procedure
ğŸš€ Scenario: âœ” Define a procedure get_risk_factor() to calculate a risk factor based on invoices.

ğŸ“Œ Procedure Definition:
CREATE PROCEDURE get_risk_factor()
BEGIN 
    -- Define local variables
    DECLARE risk_factor DECIMAL(9, 2) DEFAULT 0;
    DECLARE invoices_total DECIMAL(9, 2); 
    DECLARE invoices_count INT; 
    
    -- Fetch invoice data
    SELECT COUNT(*), SUM(invoice_total)
    INTO invoices_count, invoices_total
    FROM invoices; 
    
    -- Compute risk factor
    SET risk_factor = invoices_total / invoices_count * 5; 

    -- Return result
    SELECT risk_factor;
END;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Calculates risk_factor dynamically, making use of local variables.


ğŸ“Œ Executing the Procedure:
CALL get_risk_factor();

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Returns computed risk factor.


ğŸ“Œ Example Output
Risk Factor
-----------
777.75

âœ” Ensures structured calculations within the procedure.





ğŸ“Œ 4. Differences Between User and Local Variables
Variable Type	          Scope	                                 Prefix Used	                       Persistence
User (Session) Variable	  Exists throughout session	             @variable_name	                       Disappears upon disconnect
Local Variable	          Exists only inside stored procedures	 Declared with DECLARE (no prefix)	   Freed after procedure execution

ğŸ“Œ When to Use Each? 
âœ” Use user variables (@var) for passing results between queries. 
âœ” Use local variables (DECLARE var) for temporary calculations inside procedures.





Queries and other notes in this discussion:

-- user or session variables
SET @invoices_count = 0

-- Local variable

CREATE PROCEDURE get_risk_factor()
BEGIN 
-- risk_factor = invoices_total / invoices_count * 5
	DECLARE risk_factor DECIMAL(9, 2) DEFAULT 0;
	DECLARE invoices_total DECIMAL(9, 2); 
	DECLARE invoices_count INT; 
	
	SELECT COUNT(*), SUM(invoice_total)
	INTO invoices_count, invoices_total
	FROM invoices; 
	
	SET risk_factor = invoices_total / invoices_count * 5; 

	SELECT risk_factor;
END 





ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” User variables (@var) persist during a session, useful for storing output parameters. 
âœ” Local variables (DECLARE var) exist only inside stored procedures, ideal for temporary calculations. 
âœ” Stored procedures use variables to store, manipulate, and return results dynamically. 












Functions:
----------
ğŸš€ Creating and Using Functions in MySQL
MySQL allows custom functions, enabling structured calculations within queries. Unlike stored procedures, functions return a single value, making them ideal for specific computations.

ğŸ“Œ 1. What Are Functions in MySQL?
âœ” Functions return a single value, unlike stored procedures that can return multiple rows/columns. 
âœ” Useful for specific calculations, such as tax calculations, order totals, or risk assessments. 
âœ” Can be used in SELECT statements, just like built-in MySQL functions (SUM(), COUNT(), ROUND()).





ğŸ“Œ 2. Syntax for Creating Functions
âœ” Functions use the CREATE FUNCTION statement, defining parameters, return type, and SQL logic.

ğŸ“Œ Basic Structure:
CREATE FUNCTION function_name(param1 datatype, param2 datatype) 
RETURNS return_datatype
DETERMINISTIC   -- Function behavior attribute
BEGIN
    -- Function logic here
    RETURN value;
END;

âœ” Every function must return a value using RETURN.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Creating a Function for Risk Factor Calculation
ğŸš€ Scenario: âœ” Define a function get_risk_factor_for_client() to calculate risk factor based on invoices.

ğŸ“Œ Defining the Function:
CREATE FUNCTION get_risk_factor_for_client(client_id INT) 
RETURNS DECIMAL(9,2)
READS SQL DATA 
BEGIN
    -- Declare variables
    DECLARE risk_factor DECIMAL(9, 2) DEFAULT 0;
    DECLARE invoices_total DECIMAL(9, 2); 
    DECLARE invoices_count INT; 
    
    -- Retrieve invoice details
    SELECT COUNT(*), SUM(invoice_total)
    INTO invoices_count, invoices_total
    FROM invoices i 
    WHERE i.client_id = client_id;
    
    -- Compute risk factor
    SET risk_factor = invoices_total / invoices_count * 5;
    
    -- Ensure NULL values default to 0
    RETURN IFNULL(risk_factor, 0);
END;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Stores logic for risk factor calculation within the function. âœ” Prevents NULL errors using IFNULL(), ensuring clean calculations.

ğŸ“Œ Attributes Used:
Attribute	        Usage
DETERMINISTIC	    Ensures consistent results for identical inputs
READS SQL DATA	    Allows function to fetch data from tables
MODIFIES SQL DATA	Allows function to modify tables (Not used here)

ğŸ“Œ Why Remove DETERMINISTIC? 
âœ” The function reads dynamic data (invoices), meaning results can change. 
âœ” A client's risk factor may update due to invoice changes, making it non-deterministic.





ğŸ“Œ 4. Using the Function in a Query
âœ” Functions integrate seamlessly into SELECT queries, just like built-in MySQL functions.

ğŸ“Œ Retrieving Risk Factors for Clients:
SELECT 
    client_id, 
    name, 
    get_risk_factor_for_client(client_id) AS risk_factor
FROM clients; 

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Calculates risk factor dynamically for each client.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
ClientID | Name      | Risk Factor
----------------------------------
1        | ABC Corp  | 777.75
2        | Beta Ltd  | 0.00  -- No invoices

âœ” Clients without invoices return 0 instead of NULL.





ğŸ“Œ 5. Handling NULL Values in Calculations
âœ” If a client has no invoices, SUM(invoice_total) returns NULL, affecting computations. âœ” Using IFNULL(value, default_value) ensures NULL values default to 0.

ğŸ“Œ Example Update:
SET risk_factor = IFNULL(invoices_total / invoices_count * 5, 0);

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Prevents calculation errors, ensuring consistent results.





ğŸ“Œ 6. Dropping a Function
âœ” Functions can be removed using DROP FUNCTION.

ğŸ“Œ Deleting the Function:
DROP FUNCTION IF EXISTS get_risk_factor_for_client;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Removes the function from the database, allowing recreation or updates.





Queries and other notes in this discussion:

CREATE FUNCTION function_name(param1 datatype, param2 datatype) 
RETURNS return_datatype
DETERMINISTIC   -- Other attributes are READS SQL DATA, MODIFIES SQL DATA 
BEGIN
    -- Function logic here
    RETURN value;
END;


CREATE FUNCTION get_rist_factor_for_client(client_id INT) 
RETURNS INTEGER
READS SQL DATA 
BEGIN
	DECLARE risk_factor DECIMAL(9, 2) DEFAULT 0;
	DECLARE invoices_total DECIMAL(9, 2); 
	DECLARE invoices_count INT; 
	
	SELECT COUNT(*), SUM(invoice_total)
	INTO invoices_count, invoices_total
	FROM invoices i 
	WHERE i.client_id = client_id;
	
	SET risk_factor = invoices_total / invoices_count * 5; 
    RETURN IFNULL(risk_factor, 0);
END;




SELECT 
	client_id, 
	name, 
	get_rist_factor_for_client(client_id) AS risk_factor
FROM clients; 


DROP FUNCTION IF EXISTS get_rist_factor_for_client; 





ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” Functions return a single value, making them ideal for structured calculations. 
âœ” Use RETURN to specify the result, ensuring seamless execution. 
âœ” Functions integrate directly into queries, improving readability and efficiency. 
âœ” Handling NULL values prevents computation errors, ensuring clean results. 











Other Conventions:
------------------
ğŸš€ Naming Conventions in MySQL Development:
Every organization adopts different naming conventions for stored procedures, functions, and delimiters. There is no universally best conventionâ€”the key is consistency.

ğŸ“Œ 1. Naming Conventions for Stored Procedures and Functions
âœ” Different organizations follow different approaches for naming procedures and functions. 
âœ” Some developers prefer prefixes like:
fn_ â†’ Functions
proc_ â†’ Stored procedures

âœ” Camel Case vs. Underscore Naming:

Convention	                                Example
Prefix-based (procedures)	                proc_get_risk_factor
Prefix-based (functions)	                fn_calculate_tax
Camel Case (procedures)	                    procGetRiskFactor
Camel Case (functions)	                    getRiskFactor
Underscore-based (procedures/functions)	    get_risk_factor

ğŸš€ Effect: 
âœ” Some teams prefer proc_ or fn_ prefixes, while others omit them entirely. 
âœ” Some prefer camel case, while others use underscores for readability.

ğŸ“Œ Advice: Stick to Your Teamâ€™s Existing Convention 
âœ” If working on an existing project, follow the organizationâ€™s standard naming. 
âœ” Do not introduce new conventions without consensus, as it can cause inconsistency.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Conventions for Changing MySQL Delimiters
âœ” Different teams use different delimiters to define stored procedures and functions. 
âœ” Some commonly used delimiters include:

Delimiter Type	             Example Syntax
Double Dollar Signs ($$)	 DELIMITER $$ ... END $$ DELIMITER ;
Double Slashes (//)	D        ELIMITER // ... END // DELIMITER ;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Both work identically, but teams adopt different preferences.

ğŸ“Œ Advice: Use the Existing Convention in Your Organization âœ” If existing procedures use $$, stick with $$. âœ” If existing procedures use //, stick with //.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Why Adopting Existing Conventions Matters
âœ” Consistency across the project prevents unnecessary confusion. 
âœ” Easier code readabilityâ€”teams quickly recognize standard patterns. 
âœ” Maintains uniformity in large applications, avoiding fragmented naming styles.

ğŸ“Œ Best Practice for New Projects 
âœ” When starting a project from scratch, choose a convention that aligns with industry standards. 
âœ” Ensure all developers agree on the naming standard before beginning development.




Queries and other notes in this discussion:

'fn_', 'proc_', 'procGetRiskFactor', 'getRiskFactor', 'get_risk_factor'

DELIMITER $$
DELIMITER //





ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” Stored procedures and functions follow different naming conventions, based on organizational preferences. 
âœ” Prefixes (proc_, fn_), camel case (getRiskFactor), or underscores (get_risk_factor) are all valid approaches. 
âœ” Delimiters ($$ vs. //) vary, but consistency within a project is key. 
âœ” Stick to the existing conventions in your organization, rather than arguing for changes. 




















Triggers and Events:
--------------------

Triggers:
---------
ğŸš€ Understanding Triggers in MySQL
Triggers in MySQL are blocks of SQL code that automatically execute before or after an INSERT, UPDATE, or DELETE operation. They help maintain data integrity and automate database updates.

ğŸ“Œ 1. What Are Triggers?
âœ” Triggers execute automatically when specific database actions occur. 
âœ” Common use cases include updating related tables or enforcing data consistency. 
âœ” They fire for every row affected, ensuring consistent behavior across multiple records.

ğŸ“Œ Example Use Case 
âœ” In the invoices table, payment_total should equal the sum of all payments in the payments table. 
âœ” A trigger can ensure payment_total' updates automatically when new payments are added or removed.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Creating a Trigger for Payments (INSERT)
ğŸš€ Scenario: âœ” When inserting a new payment, update payment_total in invoices.

ğŸ“Œ Trigger Definition:
DELIMITER $$

CREATE TRIGGER payments_after_insert 
    AFTER INSERT ON payments 
    FOR EACH ROW 
BEGIN
    UPDATE invoices  
    SET payment_total = payment_total + NEW.amount 
    WHERE invoice_id = NEW.invoice_id; 
END $$

DELIMITER ;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Whenever a payment is inserted into payments, this trigger automatically updates payment_total in invoices.

ğŸ“Œ Executing an Insert Operation:
INSERT INTO payments 
VALUES (DEFAULT, 5, 3, '2019-01-01', 10, 1);
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Adds a $10 payment to Invoice ID 3.

ğŸ“Œ Verifying Invoice Update:
SELECT invoice_id, payment_total FROM invoices WHERE invoice_id = 3;


ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
InvoiceID | Payment Total
-------------------------
3        | 10.00 âœ… Updated!

âœ” Ensures payment data updates automatically.

ğŸ“Œ Key Concepts 
âœ” NEW.column_name retrieves values from the newly inserted row. 
âœ” FOR EACH ROW ensures the trigger fires for every affected row. 
âœ” Triggers execute SQL operations automatically, reducing manual updates.






ğŸ“Œ 3. Creating a Trigger for Payments (DELETE)
ğŸš€ Scenario: âœ” When deleting a payment, subtract its amount from payment_total in invoices.

ğŸ“Œ Trigger Definition:
DELIMITER $$

CREATE TRIGGER payments_after_delete 
    AFTER DELETE ON payments 
    FOR EACH ROW 
BEGIN
    UPDATE invoices  
    SET payment_total = payment_total - OLD.amount 
    WHERE invoice_id = OLD.invoice_id; 
END $$

DELIMITER ;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Ensures invoice totals decrease automatically when payments are removed.


ğŸ“Œ Deleting a Payment:
DELETE FROM payments WHERE payment_id = 10;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Removes the payment for Invoice 3.

ğŸ“Œ Verifying Invoice Update:
SELECT invoice_id, payment_total FROM invoices WHERE invoice_id = 3;

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
InvoiceID | Payment Total
-------------------------
3        | 0.00 âœ… Updated!

âœ” Ensures payment total decreases when payments are deleted.

ğŸ“Œ Key Concepts 
âœ” OLD.column_name retrieves values from the deleted row. 
âœ” Triggers prevent manual corrections, maintaining data integrity automatically.





ğŸ“Œ 4. Important Considerations for Triggers
âœ” MySQL does not support table-level triggersâ€”triggers fire for each row, not once per operation. 
âœ” Avoid modifying the same table within its trigger, as this can cause infinite loops. 
âœ” Ensure efficient indexing on affected tables, preventing performance issues in high-transaction environments.





Queries and other notes in this discussion:

A block of SQL code that automatically gets executed before or after an insert, update or delete statement.

	AFTER INSERT ON payments 
	FOR EACH ROW 
BEGIN
	UPDATE invoices  
	SET payment_total = payment_total + NEW.amount 
	WHERE invoice_id = NEW.invoice_id; 
END $$

DELIMITER ;

VALUES (DEFAULT, 5, 3, '2019-01-01', 10, 1);

END $$

DELETE 
FROM payments 
WHERE payment_id = 10; 






ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” Triggers automate updates and enforce data integrity, eliminating manual changes. 
âœ” Use NEW.column_name for inserted records and OLD.column_name for deleted records. 
âœ” Ensure triggers do not modify their own table to prevent infinite loops.

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Retrieves all triggers, displaying details such as:

âœ” Allows users to verify triggers currently defined in the database.

Dropping Triggers:
------------------
ğŸš€ Dropping Triggers in MySQL
Triggers automatically execute SQL statements before or after inserts, updates, or deletions. When modifications are required, triggers can be dropped and recreated.

ğŸ“Œ 1. How to Drop a Trigger
âœ” Use the DROP TRIGGER statement to remove a trigger from the database. 
âœ” Best Practice: Use IF EXISTS to prevent errors when the trigger doesnâ€™t exist.

ğŸ“Œ Dropping a Trigger:
DROP TRIGGER IF EXISTS payments_after_insert;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Removes the payments_after_insert trigger from the database.

ğŸ“Œ Avoiding Errors âœ” If the trigger does not exist, MySQL raises an error. âœ” Using IF EXISTS prevents errors, ensuring smooth execution.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Best Practice: Storing Triggers in SQL Files
âœ” Maintain trigger definitions in a source control repository (Git). 
âœ” Include both DROP and CREATE statements in the same script file, ensuring consistent database deployment.

ğŸ“Œ Example SQL File Structure:
triggers/
   â”œâ”€â”€ payments_after_insert.sql
   â”œâ”€â”€ payments_after_delete.sql
   
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Allows developers to recreate the database with all triggers consistently. âœ” Enables tracking history of changes to database triggers.






ğŸ“Œ 3. Recreating a Trigger After Dropping
âœ” After dropping a trigger, ensure it is correctly recreated. 
âœ” Include the DROP TRIGGER statement before defining a new trigger.

ğŸ“Œ Recreating the Payments INSERT Trigger:
DELIMITER $$

DROP TRIGGER IF EXISTS payments_after_insert; 

CREATE TRIGGER payments_after_insert 
    AFTER INSERT ON payments 
    FOR EACH ROW 
BEGIN
    UPDATE invoices  
    SET payment_total = payment_total + NEW.amount 
    WHERE invoice_id = NEW.invoice_id; 
END $$

DELIMITER ;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Ensures payment totals update automatically when new payments are inserted.






ğŸ“Œ 4. Exercise: Dropping and Recreating the Payments DELETE Trigger
ğŸš€ Task: âœ” When a payment is deleted, subtract its amount from payment_total in invoices.

ğŸ“Œ Dropping and Recreating the DELETE Trigger:
DELIMITER $$

DROP TRIGGER IF EXISTS payments_after_delete;

CREATE TRIGGER payments_after_delete 
    AFTER DELETE ON payments 
    FOR EACH ROW 
BEGIN
    UPDATE invoices  
    SET payment_total = payment_total - OLD.amount 
    WHERE invoice_id = OLD.invoice_id; 
END $$

DELIMITER ;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Ensures invoice payment totals reduce automatically when payments are deleted.

ğŸ“Œ Verifying Changes:

SELECT invoice_id, payment_total FROM invoices WHERE invoice_id = 3;
âœ” Ensures payment removal reflects correctly in the invoices table.

ğŸ“Œ Summary

Using Triggers for Auditing:
----------------------------

ğŸš€ Using Triggers for Auditing in MySQL
Triggers in MySQL can log changes to a database, providing an audit trail for tracking insertions and deletions. This is particularly useful for security, compliance, and data integrity.

ğŸ“Œ 1.Why Use Triggers for Auditing?
âœ” Automatically record changes when rows are inserted, updated, or deleted. 
âœ” Track who made what changes and when, helping with compliance. 
âœ” Ensure historical records of deleted data, making rollback possible.

ğŸ“Œ Example Use Case âœ” When inserting or deleting records in the payments table, store details in payments_audit.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Creating an Audit Table
âœ” Audit tables store historical records of changes, including:
Client ID
Date
Payment Amount
Action Type (INSERT, DELETE)
Timestamp (NOW())

ğŸ“Œ Creating the Audit Table:

USE sql_invoicing; 

CREATE TABLE payments_audit
(
    client_id       INT             NOT NULL, 
    date            DATE            NOT NULL,
    amount          DECIMAL(9, 2)   NOT NULL,
    action_type     VARCHAR(50)     NOT NULL,
    action_date     DATETIME        NOT NULL
);
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Stores historical payment changes, ensuring an accurate audit trail.

â”‚ ClientID  â”‚ Date      â”‚ Amount    â”‚ ActionTypeâ”‚ ActionDateâ”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤

ğŸ“Œ 3. Creating a Trigger for Payment Insertions
ğŸš€ Scenario: âœ” When a new payment is added, log the change in payments_audit.

ğŸ“Œ Defining the INSERT Trigger:
DELIMITER $$

DROP TRIGGER IF EXISTS payments_after_insert; 

CREATE TRIGGER payments_after_insert 
    AFTER INSERT ON payments 
    FOR EACH ROW 
BEGIN
    UPDATE invoices  
    SET payment_total = payment_total + NEW.amount 
    WHERE invoice_id = NEW.invoice_id; 

    INSERT INTO payments_audit 
    VALUES (NEW.client_id, NEW.date, NEW.amount, 'Insert', NOW());
END $$

DELIMITER ;

ğŸš€ Effect: 
âœ” Updates payment_total for the invoice. 
âœ” Logs insert operation into payments_audit, ensuring a record of changes.

ğŸ“Œ Executing an Insert Operation:

VALUES (DEFAULT, 5, 3, '2025-06-10', 250, 1);
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Logs the new payment automatically in the payments_audit table.






ğŸ“Œ 4. Creating a Trigger for Payment Deletions
ğŸš€ Scenario: âœ” When a payment is deleted, log the change in payments_audit.

ğŸ“Œ Defining the DELETE Trigger:
DELIMITER $$

    AFTER DELETE ON payments 
    FOR EACH ROW 
BEGIN
    UPDATE invoices  
    SET payment_total = payment_total - OLD.amount 
    WHERE invoice_id = OLD.invoice_id; 
    
    INSERT INTO payments_audit 
    VALUES (OLD.client_id, OLD.date, OLD.amount, 'Delete', NOW());
END $$

DELIMITER ;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Reduces payment_total for the invoice. âœ” Logs the deletion operation into payments_audit, maintaining an audit trail.

ğŸ“Œ Executing a Deletion Operation:

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Logs the deleted payment automatically in the payments_audit table.






ğŸ“Œ 5. Viewing Audit Logs
âœ” Retrieve audit records using a SELECT query.

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Displays insertions and deletions, providing a clear audit trail.

Events:
-------

ğŸš€ Understanding Events in MySQL
Events in MySQL allow scheduled execution of SQL tasks, automating database maintenance. This guide covers how to enable, create, and schedule events efficiently.

ğŸ“Œ 1. What Are Events in MySQL?
âœ” Events execute SQL tasks automatically based on a defined schedule. âœ” Common use cases include:
Deleting stale data
Archiving old records
Generating reports periodically âœ” Runs once or on a recurring schedule, ensuring automated database management.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Removing Old Audit Records âœ” Schedule an event to delete records older than one year.






ğŸ“Œ 2. Checking and Enabling the MySQL Event Scheduler
âœ” MySQL uses an event scheduler to track scheduled tasks. âœ” Check if the event scheduler is enabled.

ğŸ“Œ Checking the Event Scheduler Status:
SHOW VARIABLES LIKE 'event%';
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Displays event-related system variables, including event_scheduler.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
Variable Name   | Value
----------------------
event_scheduler | ON âœ… Enabled

âœ” If event_scheduler is OFF, enable it using SET GLOBAL:
SET GLOBAL event_scheduler = ON;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Ensures scheduled events execute automatically.

ğŸ“Œ Turning Off the Event Scheduler (Optional):
SET GLOBAL event_scheduler = OFF;

âœ” Stops background event execution, saving system resources.






ğŸ“Œ 3. Creating a Scheduled Event
ğŸš€ Scenario: âœ” Delete audit records older than one year, ensuring clean database management.

ğŸ“Œ Creating the Event:
DELIMITER $$

CREATE EVENT yearly_delete_stale_audit_rows 
ON SCHEDULE 
    EVERY 1 YEAR STARTS '2019-01-01' ENDS '2029-01-01'
DO BEGIN
    DELETE FROM payments_audit
    WHERE action_date < NOW() - INTERVAL 1 YEAR; 
END $$

DELIMITER ;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Deletes audit records older than one year, ensuring data remains relevant.

ğŸ“Œ Alternative Ways to Calculate One Year Ago:
DELETE FROM payments_audit WHERE action_date < DATE_ADD(NOW(), INTERVAL -1 YEAR);
or

DELETE FROM payments_audit WHERE action_date < DATE_SUB(NOW(), INTERVAL 1 YEAR);
âœ” All approaches produce the same result.

ğŸ“Œ Optional Start and End Time âœ” Define when the event starts and stops:
STARTS '2019-01-01' ENDS '2029-01-01'

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Controls execution timeframe, preventing unnecessary future runs.






ğŸ“Œ 4. Naming Conventions for Events
âœ” Follow a standard naming format to organize events efficiently. 
âœ” Prefix event names with their execution frequency:

Event Name	                   Execution Interval
hourly_clean_temp_data	       Every hour
daily_backup_users	           Every day
monthly_generate_report	       Every month
once_process_archive	       Executes once
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Makes filtering events easier, simplifying management.

ğŸ“Œ Finding Events Based on Frequency:

SHOW EVENTS WHERE Name LIKE 'monthly%';
âœ” Lists all monthly scheduled events.






Queries and other notes in this discussion:

A task (or block of SQL code) that gets executed according to a schedule

SHOW VARIABLES LIKE 'event%'; 


event_scheduler -- set it to 'ON'

SET GLOBAL event_scheduler = ON; 




DELIMITER $$

CREATE EVENT yearly_delete_stale_audit_rows 
ON SCHEDULE 
	-- AT '2019-05-01'
	EVERY 1 YEAR STARTS '2019-01-01' ENDS '2029-01-01'
DO BEGIN
	DELETE FROM payments_audit
	WHERE  action_date < NOW() - INTERVAL 1 YEAR;   -- or DATEADD(NOW(), INTERVAL -1 YEAR) or DATESUB(NOW(), INTERVAL 1 YEAR)
END $$

DELIMITER ;






ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” Events automate database tasks, ensuring structured data maintenance. 
âœ” Use SHOW VARIABLES LIKE 'event%' to check event scheduler status. 
âœ” Enable scheduler with SET GLOBAL event_scheduler = ON to activate scheduled tasks. 
âœ” Define events using CREATE EVENT with a scheduled execution time. 



















Viewing, Dropping and Altering Events:
--------------------------------------
ğŸš€ Managing Events in MySQL: Viewing, Dropping, and Altering Events
Events in MySQL allow scheduled execution of SQL tasks, ensuring automated database maintenance. This tutorial covers how to view, drop, and modify events efficiently.

ğŸ“Œ 1. Viewing Events in MySQL
âœ” Use the SHOW EVENTS statement to retrieve all events in the current database. âœ” Provides details such as:

ğŸ“Œ Query to View All Events:

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Retrieves all scheduled events, displaying execution frequency and details.

ğŸ“Œ Filtering Events by Interval Using LIKE âœ” Naming events systematically allows filtering easily.

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Lists all yearly scheduled events.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
â”Œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¬â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”
â”‚ Event Name                     â”‚ Frequency â”‚ Start Date â”‚ End Date  â”‚ Status    â”‚
â”œâ”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¼â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”¤

â””â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”´â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”€â”˜
âœ” Allows searching for specific event schedules efficiently.






ğŸ“Œ 2. Dropping an Event
âœ” Use the DROP EVENT statement to remove an event from the database. âœ” Always use IF EXISTS to prevent errors when the event doesnâ€™t exist.

ğŸ“Œ Dropping an Event Safely:
DROP EVENT IF EXISTS yearly_delete_stale_audit_rows;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Removes the scheduled event, ensuring database cleanup.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Altering an Event
âœ” Use the ALTER EVENT statement to modify an existing event, instead of dropping and recreating it. 
âœ” Change execution frequency or modify SQL logic.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Modifying Event Execution:
DELIMITER $$

ALTER EVENT yearly_delete_stale_audit_rows 
ON SCHEDULE 
    EVERY 1 YEAR STARTS '2019-01-01' ENDS '2029-01-01'
DO BEGIN
    DELETE FROM payments_audit
    WHERE action_date < NOW() - INTERVAL 1 YEAR;  
END $$

DELIMITER ;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Reschedules the event, ensuring data cleanup at the correct intervals.






ğŸ“Œ 4. Enabling or Disabling an Event
âœ” Temporarily deactivate or reactivate events using ALTER EVENT.

ğŸ“Œ Disabling an Event:
ALTER EVENT yearly_delete_stale_audit_rows DISABLE;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Prevents execution while maintaining the event definition.

ğŸ“Œ Reactivating an Event:

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Resumes scheduled execution.

ğŸ“Œ Example Status Output:

------------------------------------------
yearly_delete_stale_audit_rows | DISABLED âŒ Paused
yearly_clean_temp_data         | ENABLED âœ… Active

âœ” Ensures proper control over scheduled events.






Queries and other notes in this discussion:

DELIMITER $$

ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” Use SHOW EVENTS to list scheduled database tasks, ensuring visibility. 
âœ” Filter events efficiently using SHOW EVENTS LIKE 'interval%' (e.g., yearly%).

âœ” Modify execution intervals and logic using ALTER EVENT, preventing unnecessary recreation.

Transactions and Concurrency:
-----------------------------

Transactions:
-------------

ğŸš€ Understanding Transactions in MySQL
Transactions in MySQL represent a single unit of work, ensuring multiple SQL operations succeed together or fail entirely. This guarantees data integrity and prevents inconsistencies.

ğŸ“Œ 1. What Is a Transaction?
âœ” A transaction is a group of SQL statements that must be executed together. 
âœ” If all statements succeed, the transaction is committed. 
âœ” If any statement fails, the transaction rolls back, ensuring database consistency.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Bank Transaction âœ” If transferring $10 from one account to another: 1.Deduct $10 from Account A  2.Add $10 to Account B

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Either both operations succeed, or none should be executed, ensuring accurate financial records.

ğŸ“Œ Potential Failure Scenario 
âœ” If deduction succeeds but addition fails (server crash, error, etc.), Account A loses money but Account B doesnâ€™t gain it. 
âœ” Transaction ensures rollback, preventing data inconsistencies.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Why Are Transactions Important?
âœ” Transactions ensure multiple related operations execute reliably. 
âœ” Prevent database inconsistencies, like orders without items or incomplete financial records.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Order and Order Items âœ” When processing an order: 1.Insert order details (orders table) 2.Insert order items (order_items table)

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” If one step fails, the order is incompleteâ€”transactions ensure rollback.





ğŸ“Œ 3.ACID Properties of Transactions
âœ” Transactions follow ACID properties to ensure data reliability.

ğŸ“Œ ACID Principles:
Property	 Description
Atomicity	 Ensures transactions execute as a single unitâ€”either all succeed or none.
Consistency	 Maintains database integrity, preventing invalid states. or The database remains valid after transactions.
Isolation	 Transactions do not interfere with each other â€” locks prevent conflicts.
Durability	 Once committed, changes are permanent, even after system failures.

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Transactions protect data from errors, failures, and concurrency issues.




Queries and other notes in this discussion:

A group of SQL statements that represent a single unit of work 


properties of Transactions:(ACID)

Atomicity 
Consistency
Isolation 
Durability




ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” Transactions ensure multiple SQL operations succeed together or fail entirely. 
âœ” Follow ACID principles, ensuring atomicity, consistency, isolation, and durability. 
âœ” Prevent data inconsistencies, ensuring structured database execution. 
















Creating Transactions:
-----------------------
ğŸš€ Creating Transactions in MySQL:
Transactions in MySQL ensure multiple SQL operations execute together, maintaining data integrity. This guide covers how to create transactions, rollback failures, and manage automatic commits.

ğŸ“Œ 1. What Is a Transaction?
âœ” A transaction is a set of SQL statements that must succeed or fail together. 
âœ” Ensures consistencyâ€”either all operations complete, or none are applied.

ğŸ“Œ Example Use Case: 
Order Processing âœ” When creating an order: 1.Insert order details into the orders table 2. Insert order items into the order_items table

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Prevents incomplete ordersâ€”ensuring order details and items are always stored together.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Creating a Transaction
âœ” Use START TRANSACTION to begin a transaction. 
âœ” Use COMMIT to finalize changes, ensuring database updates. 
âœ” If an error occurs, MySQL automatically rolls back unfinished changes.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Storing an Order:
USE sql_store;

START TRANSACTION; 

INSERT INTO orders (customer_id, order_date, status)
VALUES(1, '2019-01-01', 1);

INSERT INTO order_items
VALUES (LAST_INSERT_ID(), 1, 1, 1);

COMMIT;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Creates an order and its item within the same transaction. âœ” Ensures both operations complete together.

ğŸ“Œ Verifying Data:
SELECT * FROM orders ORDER BY order_id DESC;
SELECT * FROM order_items ORDER BY order_id DESC;
âœ” Confirms order and items were successfully committed.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Simulating a Transaction Failure
âœ” If a failure occurs before COMMIT, MySQL rolls back changes automatically. 
âœ” To manually test rollback, disconnect before committing.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Breaking a Transaction 
1.Execute the INSERT INTO orders statement. 
2.Execute the INSERT INTO order_items statement. 
3.Disconnect the database BEFORE committing. 
4.Reconnect and check data.

ğŸš€ Expected Behavior: âœ” Order was NOT stored, ensuring no incomplete data is saved.

ğŸ“Œ Verifying Transaction Status:
SELECT * FROM orders ORDER BY order_id DESC;
âœ” The order should NOT appear, confirming rollback success.





ğŸ“Œ 4. Using ROLLBACK to Undo Changes
âœ” Instead of committing changes, use ROLLBACK to cancel the transaction.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Rolling Back a Transaction:
USE sql_store;

START TRANSACTION; 

INSERT INTO orders (customer_id, order_date, status)
VALUES(1, '2019-01-01', 1);

INSERT INTO order_items
VALUES (LAST_INSERT_ID(), 1, 1, 1);

ROLLBACK;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Cancels order creation, removing partial updates.

ğŸ“Œ Verifying Rollback Success:
SELECT * FROM orders ORDER BY order_id DESC;
SELECT * FROM order_items ORDER BY order_id DESC;
âœ” No new records should exist, confirming rollback success.





ğŸ“Œ 5. Understanding Auto-Commit in MySQL
âœ” By default, MySQL automatically commits every INSERT, UPDATE, or DELETE statement. 
âœ” Controlled via autocommit system variable.

ğŸ“Œ Checking Auto-Commit Status:
SHOW VARIABLES LIKE 'autocommit';
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Returns current auto-commit setting (usually ON).

ğŸ“Œ Example Output

Variable Name   | Value
----------------------
autocommit      | ON âœ… Auto-commit enabled

âœ” If enabled, every single statement commits automatically.

ğŸ“Œ Disabling Auto-Commit for Manual Transactions:
SET autocommit = OFF;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Prevents automatic commits, requiring explicit COMMIT or ROLLBACK.

Extra Notes: 



Difference Between COMMIT and ROLLBACK in Transactions

In SQL transactions, 'COMMIT' and 'ROLLBACK' are crucial commands that determine whether changes made during a transaction are permanently applied to the database or undone.


1. COMMIT
A 'COMMIT' statement is used to permanently save all changes made within a transaction to the database.


Key Characteristics:
- Ensures changes are applied and visible to other users.
- Cannot be undone once executed.
- Locks held during the transaction are released after commit.
- Improves consistency by transitioning the database to a stable state.



Example Usage:
'''sql
START TRANSACTION;

INSERT INTO orders (customer_id, order_date, status)
VALUES(1, '2019-01-01', 1);

INSERT INTO order_items
VALUES (LAST_INSERT_ID(), 1, 1, 1);

COMMIT;  -- Saves changes permanently
'''
ğŸ‘‰ After 'COMMIT', the inserted records remain in the database and cannot be undone.



2. ROLLBACK
A 'ROLLBACK' statement undoes all changes made within a transaction, returning the database to the state before the transaction started.

Key Characteristics:
- Used when an error occurs or when partial updates must be prevented.
- Ensures atomicity, meaning either all or none of the changes are applied.
- Helps maintain database integrity by avoiding inconsistencies.
- Locks held during the transaction are released after rollback.


Example Usage:
'''sql
START TRANSACTION;

INSERT INTO orders (customer_id, order_date, status)
VALUES(1, '2019-01-01', 1);

-- Simulating an error
INSERT INTO order_items
VALUES (NULL, 1, 1, 1);  -- Fails due to NULL violation

ROLLBACK;  -- Undoes all changes
'''
ğŸ‘‰ Since the second 'INSERT' fails, 'ROLLBACK' ensures none of the transaction changes persist.





Key Differences Between COMMIT and ROLLBACK:

| Feature                   | COMMIT                    | ROLLBACK                                  |
|---------------------------|---------------------------|-------------------------------------------|
| Effect                    | Saves changes permanently | Undoes changes made in the transaction    |
| Undo Possible?            | No                        | Yes, before COMMIT                        |
| Used When                 | Transaction is successful | Errors occur or data integrity is at risk |
| Locks Released            | Yes, after commit         | Yes, after rollback                       |
| Prevents Partial Updates? | No, changes persist       | Yes, all changes are reverted             |






ğŸ“Œ Summary:
âœ” Transactions ensure multiple SQL operations succeed together, preventing partial updates. 
âœ” Use START TRANSACTION and COMMIT to finalize successful operations. 
âœ” Use ROLLBACK to cancel transactions when needed. 
âœ” Auto-commit automatically commits each SQL statementâ€”disable for full transaction control. 


















Concurrency and Locking:
------------------------

ğŸš€ Understanding Concurrency and Locking in MySQL
In real-world applications, multiple users may access and modify the same data simultaneously, leading to potential concurrency issues. MySQL uses locking mechanisms to prevent conflicts, ensuring data integrity and consistency.

ğŸ“Œ 1. What Is Concurrency in Databases?
âœ” Concurrency occurs when multiple users or processes attempt to access or modify the same data simultaneously. 
âœ” MySQL handles concurrency using locks, preventing data corruption or inconsistencies.

ğŸ“Œ Example Concurrency Issue: 
âœ” Two users attempt to update customer points simultaneously: 
1.User 1 starts a transaction and increases points by 10. 
2.User 2 starts a transaction and tries to increase points by 10 at the same time. 
âœ” Without locking, conflicts could occurâ€”one user's update may override the other's.

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” MySQL prevents simultaneous updates by locking the row until the first transaction completes.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Simulating Concurrency with Two Sessions
âœ” Opening two separate database connections simulates multiple users modifying the same data.

ğŸ“Œ Step 1: Open Two Sessions in MySQL Workbench âœ” Each session represents a different user accessing the database.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Testing Concurrency with Transactions
ğŸš€ Scenario: âœ” Two users attempt to update points for Customer ID 1 simultaneously.

ğŸ“Œ Session 1: Start Transaction and Update Points

USE sql_store;
START TRANSACTION; 

UPDATE customers
SET points = points + 10 
WHERE customer_id = 1;

COMMIT;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Updates points for Customer ID 1, locking the row until committed.


ğŸ“Œ Session 2: Start Another Transaction
USE sql_store;
START TRANSACTION; 

UPDATE customers
SET points = points + 10 
WHERE customer_id = 1;

COMMIT;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Session 2 must wait for Session 1 to complete before modifying the row.





ğŸ“Œ 4. Understanding Locking Behavior
âœ” When Session 1 updates a row, MySQL locks it, preventing simultaneous modifications. 
âœ” Session 2 attempts to update the same row, but it must wait until Session 1 commits or rolls back. 
âœ” If Session 1 never commits, Session 2 times out, preventing deadlocks.

ğŸ“Œ Example of Timeout in Session 2
ERROR 1205 (HY000): Lock wait timeout exceeded; try restarting transaction
âœ” This happens when Session 1 holds the lock for too long.

ğŸ“Œ Resolving Lock Timeout âœ” Session 1 should commit or rollback as soon as possible:
COMMIT;  -- Finalize changes

ğŸ“Œ Refreshing Data After Commit âœ” After both transactions complete, verify points were updated correctly:
SELECT customer_id, points FROM customers WHERE customer_id = 1;

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Customer points increase by 20, confirming both updates were applied sequentially.

ğŸ“Œ Example Output:
Customer ID | Points
--------------------
1           | 2293 âœ… Updated correctly

âœ” Ensures transactions are executed safely, without interference.





ğŸ“Œ 5. MySQLâ€™s Default Locking Behavior
âœ” By default, MySQL automatically locks rows when transactions modify them. 
âœ” Other transactions must wait until the lock is released. 
âœ” This prevents concurrency problems in most cases.

ğŸ“Œ When Default Behavior Isnâ€™t Enough 
âœ” Some applications require customized transaction isolation levels. 
âœ” Next steps: Exploring solutions to handle special concurrency scenarios.



Queries and other notes in this discussion:

SET points = points + 10 
WHERE customer_id = 1;
COMMIT; 


connection 2: 

USE sql_store;
START TRANSACTION; 
UPDATE customers
SET points = points + 10 
WHERE customer_id = 1;
COMMIT; 





ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” Concurrency occurs when multiple users modify the same data simultaneously. 
âœ” MySQL prevents conflicts using row-level locks, ensuring data integrity. 
âœ” Transactions lock rows until committed, preventing other modifications. 
âœ” Use COMMIT or ROLLBACK to finalize changes quickly and avoid timeouts. 
âœ” Next steps: Learning advanced isolation levels and concurrency handling techniques.

















Concurrency Problems: 
---------------------
ğŸš€ Concurrency Problems in MySQL
Concurrency issues arise when multiple transactions access and modify the same data simultaneously. Without proper controls, such simultaneous actions can lead to data inconsistencies. Below is a detailed discussion of the common concurrency problems along with examples and solutions.

1. Lost Updates:
Definition: Lost updates occur when two transactions update the same data concurrently without proper locking. The later-committing transaction can overwrite the changes made by an earlier one, resulting in lost information.

Example Scenario: Imagine a customer record for John in New York with 10 points. Two transactions occur:
Transaction A: Updates the state for John.
Transaction B: Updates the points for John.

Both transactions read the same original record. If Transaction A and Transaction B run concurrently and Transaction B commits last, the changes made by Transaction A (e.g., updating the state) will be overwritten. Consequently, the update made by Transaction A is lost.

Solution: Use appropriate locking mechanisms. MySQLâ€™s default behavior often locks the rows being updated, ensuring that such updates occur in sequence. This prevents lost updates as one transaction will wait for the other to complete before proceeding.





2. Dirty Reads
Definition: A dirty read happens when a transaction reads data that has been modified by another transaction but not yet committed. If the modifying transaction later rolls back, the data read is invalid.

Example Scenario:
Transaction A: Changes a customer's points from 10 to 20 but hasn't committed the change.
Transaction B: Reads the updated points (20) and, based on the new value, gives a $1 discount per point, thereby offering a $20 discount.

If Transaction A rolls back, Customer's points revert to 10, meaning that Transaction Bâ€™s discount was based on data that never actually existed.

Solution: Set the transaction isolation level to "Read Committed." With this setting, transactions only read committed data, preventing the occurrence of dirty reads.





3. Non-Repeatable Reads
Definition: Non-repeatable reads occur when a transaction reads the same row more than once and sees different values each time because another transaction modified the data between the reads.

Example Scenario:
Transaction A: Reads a customer's points and sees 10.
Transaction B: Updates the customer's points to 0 and commits.
Transaction A (again): Reads the customer's points a second time and now sees 0 instead of the original 10.

This inconsistency can lead to incorrect business decisions if Transaction A's operations assume a static snapshot of data.

Solution: Use the "Repeatable Read" isolation level to ensure that once data is read, it remains consistent throughout the transaction. Alternatively, in business scenarios where the most current data is preferred, accepting non-repeatable reads might be acceptable.






4. Phantom Reads
Definition: Phantom reads occur when a transaction retrieves a set of rows that match a condition and then re-queries the same condition within the same transaction, only to find additional (or missing) rows due to inserts or deletions by other transactions.

Example Scenario:
Transaction A: Selects all customers with more than 10 points to offer them a discount.
Transaction B: Updates a customer's points, making them now eligible for the discount after Transaction Aâ€™s query has executed.

As a result, Transaction A misses this customer, leading to a â€œphantomâ€ read where data appears or disappears unexpectedly.

Solution: To guard against phantom reads, use the "Serializable" isolation level. This level ensures that transactions are completely isolated from changes made by other concurrent transactions, though it may reduce concurrent throughput and impact performance.




5. Summary & Best Practices:
Lost Updates: Prevent by using locks so that concurrent transactions update the same row in sequence.
Dirty Reads: Avoid by using an isolation level like Read Committed, ensuring only committed data is read.
Non-Repeatable Reads: Resolve with Repeatable Read isolation so that data snapshots remain consistent during the transaction.
Phantom Reads: Eliminate or minimize by using Serializable isolationâ€”this can impact performance and should be reserved for critical operations.

Understanding and selecting the appropriate transaction isolation level (Read Committed, Repeatable Read, Serializable) based on the specific business requirements is vital to prevent these concurrency issues while balancing performance and consistency.


Additional Information:
Transaction Isolation Levels: The SQL standard defines four levels of transaction isolation. Familiarize yourself with them to choose the best level for your application:
Read Uncommitted
Read Committed
Repeatable Read
Serializable

Locking Mechanisms: MySQL automatically locks rows during updates. This default behavior plays a critical role in avoiding lost updates and ensuring that concurrent transactions are properly serialized.

Monitoring Concurrency: Always test your application under load and simulate concurrent transactions to ensure that the chosen isolation levels and locking mechanisms work as expected.

Common Concurrency Problems Recap:
Lost Updates
Dirty Reads
Non-Repeatable Reads
Phantom Reads

Transaction Isolation Levels:
-----------------------------

ğŸš€ Transaction Isolation Levels in MySQL
Transactions ensure that a series of SQL statements execute as a single unit. To prevent concurrency issues when multiple transactions access or modify the same data, MySQL offers several transaction isolation levels. These levels determine how much one transaction is isolated from the changes made by other transactions.

1. Common Concurrency Problems:
Before reviewing the isolation levels, itâ€™s important to understand the basic concurrency issues they aim to solve:
Lost Updates: Occurs when two transactions update the same row without proper locking. The transaction that commits last overwrites the earlier change, resulting in lost data.
Dirty Reads: Happen when a transaction reads data that has been modified by another transaction but not yet committed. If the modifying transaction rolls back, the reading transaction has based its logic on data that never became permanent.
Non-Repeatable Reads: Occur when a transaction reads the same row twice but sees different values because another transaction modified the data between the two reads.
Phantom Reads: Happen when a transaction re-executes a query that returns a set of rows and finds that the set has changed (rows have been added or removed) due to another transactionâ€™s modifications.





2. Standard Transaction Isolation Levels:
MySQL supports four standard transaction isolation levels, each offering different guarantees and trade-offs:

a. Read Uncommitted
Behavior: No isolationâ€”you can read uncommitted changes from other transactions.
Protection: None. It does not prevent dirty reads, lost updates, non-repeatable reads, or phantom reads.
Use Case: Rarely used because it offers high concurrency at the expense of accuracy.

b. Read Committed
Behavior: A transaction sees only data that has been committed. It prevents dirty reads.
Protection: Prevents dirty reads but still allows non-repeatable reads and phantom reads.
Use Case: Suitable when you need to exclude uncommitted dataâ€”for example, in bulk reporting scenarios where perfect repeatability isnâ€™t critical.

c. Repeatable Reads (Default in MySQL)
Behavior: Once data is read, it remains consistent throughout the transaction. This prevents non-repeatable reads.
Protection: Prevents dirty reads and non-repeatable reads. However, phantom reads can still occur.
Use Case: The default level in MySQL since it offers a good balance between consistency and performance. It works well for most scenarios except where phantom reads need to be completely avoided.

d. Serializable
Behavior: The highest isolation level. Transactions are completely isolated from each other.
Protection: Prevents dirty reads, non-repeatable reads, and phantom reads. Ensures that transactions execute as if they were completely sequential.
Trade-Off: Introduces significant locking overhead which can reduce performance and scalability.
Use Case: Use only in critical situations where complete isolation is required, for example when phantom reads must be prevented.





3. Trade-Offs Between Isolation Levels:
Lower Isolation Levels (Read Uncommitted, Read Committed):
Pros: Higher concurrency and performance due to fewer locks.
Cons: Increased risk of inconsistency (dirty reads, non-repeatable reads, or phantom reads).

Higher Isolation Levels (Repeatable Reads, Serializable):
Pros: Better data consistency and integrity.
Cons: Reduced performance due to extensive locking and resource use. Serializable, in particular, can severely limit concurrency.
MySQLâ€™s default is Repeatable Reads, which provides a good balance by preventing most concurrency problems while retaining relatively high performance. For scenarios where precise consistency is less criticalâ€”like bulk reportingâ€”you might consider using Read Committed or even Read Uncommitted. Use Serializable only when necessary due to its performance cost.





4. Setting the Transaction Isolation Level
You can view and modify the transaction isolation level in MySQL:

a. Viewing the Current Isolation Level:
SHOW VARIABLES LIKE 'transaction_isolation';
Effect: Reveals the current isolation level (default is typically REPEATABLE-READ in MySQL).

b. Setting the Isolation Level for a Transaction
For the next transaction only:
SET TRANSACTION ISOLATION LEVEL SERIALIZABLE;

For the current session:
SET SESSION TRANSACTION ISOLATION LEVEL SERIALIZABLE;

Globally (new sessions):
SET GLOBAL TRANSACTION ISOLATION LEVEL SERIALIZABLE;

Note for Application Developers: Typically, you change the isolation level for a specific connection in your application just before executing a transaction. This local change does not affect other sessions or the global configuration.





Queries and other notes in this discussion:


SHOW VARIABLES LIKE 'transaction_isolation'; 

SET TRANSACTION ISOLATION LEVEL SERIALIZABLE;

SET SESSION TRANSACTION ISOLATION LEVEL SERIALIZABLE;

SET GLOBAL TRANSACTION ISOLATION LEVEL SERIALIZABLE;










5. Summary
Concurrency Problems:
Lost Updates, Dirty Reads, Non-Repeatable Reads, Phantom Reads.

Isolation Levels:
Read Uncommitted: No protections.
Read Committed: Prevents dirty reads.
Repeatable Reads: Prevents dirty and non-repeatable reads (default in MySQL).
Serializable: Prevents all, including phantom reads (at a performance cost).
Trade-Off: Lower isolation offers higher performance but less consistency, while higher isolation provides greater consistency at the expense of performance and scalability.
Setting Levels: Use appropriate SET TRANSACTION ISOLATION LEVEL statements to tailor isolation for individual transactions, sessions, or globally.









READ UNCOMMITTED Isolation Level: 
---------------------------------
ğŸš€ Understanding READ UNCOMMITTED Isolation Level in MySQL
The READ UNCOMMITTED isolation level allows transactions to read uncommitted changes made by other transactions. This leads to dirty reads, where a transaction sees data that might never be committed, resulting in potential inconsistencies.

ğŸ“Œ 1. Simulating READ UNCOMMITTED Isolation Level
ğŸš€ Scenario: âœ” Two transactions interact with the customer points record: 
1.Session 1: Reads customer points before another transaction modifies it. 
2.Session 2: Starts a transaction, updates the points, but does not commit immediately.

ğŸ“Œ Expected Behavior Under READ UNCOMMITTED: âœ” Session 1 will read the uncommitted update. âœ” If Session 2 rolls back, Session 1â€™s read becomes invalidâ€”this is a dirty read.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Steps to Demonstrate Dirty Reads
ğŸš€ Opening Two Sessions âœ” Open two MySQL connections to simulate multiple clients accessing the database.

ğŸ“Œ Session 1: Setting Isolation Level and Reading Data
USE sql_store; 
SET TRANSACTION ISOLATION LEVEL READ UNCOMMITTED;

SELECT points 
FROM customers 
WHERE customer_id = 1;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Reads uncommitted data that might change.


ğŸ“Œ Session 2: Updating Points Without Committing Immediately
USE sql_store; 
START TRANSACTION;

UPDATE customers 
SET points = 20
WHERE customer_id = 1;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Customer's points are now 20, but the transaction isnâ€™t committed yet.

ğŸ“Œ Session 1: Reading Uncommitted Changes
SELECT points FROM customers WHERE customer_id = 1;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Session 1 sees 20 points, even though the update isnâ€™t committed.

ğŸ“Œ Session 2: Rolling Back Instead of Committing
ROLLBACK;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” The update is undone, restoring original points.

ğŸ“Œ Session 1â€™s Dirty Read Issue âœ” Session 1 made a decision based on 20 points, but that value never officially existed.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Why READ UNCOMMITTED Is Risky?
âœ” Allows reading temporary, uncommitted changes. 
âœ” Data might roll back, leading to invalid business decisions. 
âœ” Results in all concurrency problems, including lost updates, dirty reads, non-repeatable reads, and phantom reads.

ğŸ“Œ Typical Use Cases for READ UNCOMMITTED 
âœ” Rarely used in production due to data inconsistencies. 
âœ” Might be acceptable for bulk reporting where data precision isnâ€™t critical.




Queries and other notes in this discussion:

connection 1:

USE sql_store; 
SET TRANSACTION ISOLATION LEVEL READ UNCOMMITTED;
SELECT points 
FROM customers 
WHERE customer_id = 1; -- reads the uncommitted data which is points = 20


connection 2:
USE sql_store; 
START TRANSACTION;
UPDATE customers 
SET points = 20
WHERE customer_id = 1; 
COMMIT; 


USE sql_store; 
START TRANSACTION;
UPDATE customers 
SET points = 20
WHERE customer_id = 1; 
ROLLBACK;   -- say if we rolls back for some reason, and connection 1 has read it as points as 20 which is not true now 






ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” READ UNCOMMITTED is the lowest isolation level, allowing dirty reads. 
âœ” Transactions read uncommitted changes, leading to potentially invalid decisions. 
âœ” ROLLBACK in one session cancels changes, making previously read data incorrect. 
âœ” Typically avoided in production, except in rare cases where consistency is less important.















READ COMMITTED Isolation Level: 
-------------------------------
ğŸš€ Understanding READ COMMITTED Isolation Level in MySQL
The READ COMMITTED isolation level ensures that transactions only read data that has been committed by other transactions. This prevents dirty reads, ensuring that queries do not see uncommitted changes. However, non-repeatable reads can still occur, where a query returns different results when executed multiple times within the same transaction.

ğŸ“Œ 1. Simulating READ COMMITTED Isolation Level
ğŸš€ Scenario: âœ” Two transactions interact with customer points: 
1.Session 1: Reads customer points before and after another transaction modifies them. 
2.Session 2: Starts a transaction, updates the points, and commits the change.

ğŸ“Œ Expected Behavior Under READ COMMITTED: 
âœ” Session 1 does not see uncommitted updates. 
âœ” Once Session 2 commits, Session 1 sees the changes. 
âœ” Non-repeatable reads can occur if data is updated mid-transaction.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Steps to Demonstrate Read Committed Behavior
ğŸš€ Opening Two Sessions âœ” Open two MySQL connections to simulate concurrent database access.

ğŸ“Œ Session 1: Setting Isolation Level and Reading Data
USE sql_store; 
SET TRANSACTION ISOLATION LEVEL READ COMMITTED;

SELECT points 
FROM customers 
WHERE customer_id = 1;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Reads only committed changes.


ğŸ“Œ Session 2: Updating Points Without Committing Immediately
USE sql_store; 
START TRANSACTION;

UPDATE customers 
SET points = 20
WHERE customer_id = 1;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” The update is made but not committedâ€”Session 1 should not see this change.


ğŸ“Œ Session 1: Reading Committed Data
SELECT points FROM customers WHERE customer_id = 1;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Returns previously committed points instead of the uncommitted update.


ğŸ“Œ Session 2: Committing the Change
COMMIT;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” The change becomes permanent, and now Session 1 will see the updated value.

ğŸ“Œ Session 1: Reading Updated Points
SELECT points FROM customers WHERE customer_id = 1;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Now returns 20, reflecting the committed update.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Identifying Non-Repeatable Reads
âœ” A transaction using READ COMMITTED may see different values when reading the same row multiple times.

ğŸ“Œ Session 1: Executing Two Consecutive Reads
USE sql_store; 
SET TRANSACTION ISOLATION LEVEL READ COMMITTED;
START TRANSACTION;

SELECT points FROM customers WHERE customer_id = 1; -- Reads 20 initially


ğŸ“Œ Session 2: Updating the Same Record
USE sql_store; 
START TRANSACTION;

UPDATE customers 
SET points = 30
WHERE customer_id = 1;

COMMIT;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” The update is committed, making Session 1â€™s previous read outdated.


ğŸ“Œ Session 1: Second Read Within the Same Transaction
SELECT points FROM customers WHERE customer_id = 1; -- Reads 30 now
COMMIT;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” First read saw 20 points, second read saw 30â€”this is a non-repeatable read.





ğŸ“Œ 4. Key Takeaways
âœ” READ COMMITTED prevents dirty reads, ensuring queries see only committed changes. 
âœ” Transactions may experience non-repeatable reads, meaning subsequent reads may return different values. 
âœ” Best used when queries must read only finalized data but donâ€™t require repeatable results.

ğŸ“Œ Use Cases âœ” Standard for most applications needing reliable reads (avoiding dirty data). âœ” Not ideal for situations requiring consistent snapshot reads (consider Repeatable Read instead).

ğŸ“Œ Next Steps: âœ” For repeatable reads, increase the isolation level to REPEATABLE READ to prevent inconsistencies.

SET points = 30
WHERE customer_id = 1; 
COMMIT; 





ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” READ COMMITTED ensures transactions only see committed data, preventing dirty reads. 
âœ” Transactions may experience non-repeatable reads, where the same row returns different values in different queries. 
âœ” Suitable for applications requiring consistency without full transaction snapshots. 
âœ” For higher data consistency, consider REPEATABLE READ isolation level.

















REPEATABLE READ Isolation Level:
--------------------------------

ğŸš€ Understanding REPEATABLE READ Isolation Level in MySQL
The REPEATABLE READ isolation level ensures that data remains consistent throughout a transaction, preventing non-repeatable reads. However, phantom reads can still occur when new rows are inserted or updated by other transactions during execution.

ğŸ“Œ 1. What Is REPEATABLE READ Isolation Level?
âœ” Ensures consistent data retrievalâ€”once a transaction reads a row, subsequent reads within the same transaction return the same value, even if other transactions modify it. 
âœ” Prevents non-repeatable reads, meaning queries in a transaction consistently see the same snapshot of data. 
âœ” Does not prevent phantom reads, where new rows appear or disappear due to updates in another transaction.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Demonstrating REPEATABLE READ Behavior
ğŸš€ Scenario: âœ” Two transactions interact with customer points: 
1.Session 1: Reads customer points before and after another transaction modifies them. 
2.Session 2: Starts a transaction, updates the points, and commits the change.

ğŸ“Œ Expected Behavior Under REPEATABLE READ: 
âœ” Session 1 maintains consistent reads, even if Session 2 modifies data. 
âœ” Session 1 does not see committed changes until it completes.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Steps to Prevent Non-Repeatable Reads
ğŸš€ Opening Two Sessions âœ” Open two MySQL connections to simulate concurrent transactions.

ğŸ“Œ Session 1: Setting Isolation Level and Reading Data Consistently
USE sql_store; 
SET TRANSACTION ISOLATION LEVEL REPEATABLE READ;
START TRANSACTION;

SELECT points FROM customers WHERE customer_id = 1; -- Reads 30 points
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Establishes a consistent snapshot of data for the transaction.


ğŸ“Œ Session 2: Updating the Same Record
USE sql_store; 
START TRANSACTION;

UPDATE customers 
SET points = 40
WHERE customer_id = 1;

COMMIT;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” The update is committed, but Session 1 will NOT see the change yet.


ğŸ“Œ Session 1: Second Read Within the Same Transaction
SELECT points FROM customers WHERE customer_id = 1; -- Still reads 30 points
COMMIT;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” REPEATABLE READ maintains the original snapshot until the transaction completes.

ğŸ“Œ After Commit in Session 1
SELECT points FROM customers WHERE customer_id = 1; -- Now reads 40 points
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Session 1 now sees updated values after committing.





ğŸ“Œ 4. Identifying Phantom Reads
âœ” Phantom reads occur when a query retrieves rows that change mid-transaction due to another transaction inserting or modifying data.

ğŸ“Œ Session 1: Selecting Customers in Virginia
USE sql_store; 
SET TRANSACTION ISOLATION LEVEL REPEATABLE READ;
START TRANSACTION;

SELECT * FROM customers WHERE state = 'VA';  -- Customer ID 1 not included
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Session 1 captures a snapshot of customers in Virginia.


ğŸ“Œ Session 2: Updating Customer State
USE sql_store; 
START TRANSACTION;

UPDATE customers 
SET state = 'VA'
WHERE customer_id = 1;

COMMIT;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Now Customer ID 1 is in Virginia, but Session 1 does not see it yet.


ğŸ“Œ Session 1: Re-executing the Same Query
SELECT * FROM customers WHERE state = 'VA'; -- Still shows only the original snapshot
COMMIT;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Even after the state change, Session 1 does not see new rows during the transaction.


ğŸ“Œ After Commit in Session 1
SELECT * FROM customers WHERE state = 'VA'; -- Now shows Customer ID 1
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Session 1 now includes the updated data after committing.






ğŸ“Œ 5. Key Takeaways
âœ” REPEATABLE READ ensures data consistency during a transaction, preventing non-repeatable reads. 
âœ” Transactions maintain their initial snapshot until completion, ignoring updates from other transactions. 
âœ” Does not prevent phantom reads, where new records appear or disappear between queries. 
âœ” To prevent phantom reads, use the SERIALIZABLE isolation level.

ğŸ“Œ Use Cases âœ” Suitable for applications that require consistent snapshot reads during transactions. âœ” Recommended as the default isolation level in MySQL, balancing consistency and performance.

ğŸ“Œ Next Steps: âœ” For complete isolation, switch to SERIALIZABLE isolation level to prevent phantom reads.






Queries and other notes in this discussion:

connection 1:
USE sql_store; 
SET TRANSACTION ISOLATION LEVEL REPEATABLE READ;
START TRANSACTION;
SELECT points FROM customers WHERE customer_id = 1; -- reads 30
SELECT points FROM customers WHERE customer_id = 1; -- reads 30, because our reads are repeatable and consistent 
COMMIT; 



connection 2:
USE sql_store; 
START TRANSACTION;
UPDATE customers 
SET points = 40
WHERE customer_id = 1; 
COMMIT; 



problem with phantom reads:
connection 1:
USE sql_store; 
SET TRANSACTION ISOLATION LEVEL REPEATABLE READ;
START TRANSACTION;
SELECT * FROM customers WHERE state = 'VA';  -- we won't get to see the customer_id 1
COMMIT; 


connection 2:
USE sql_store; 
START TRANSACTION;
UPDATE customers 
SET state = 'VA'
WHERE customer_id = 1; 
COMMIT; 






ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” REPEATABLE READ prevents non-repeatable reads, ensuring consistent data retrieval within a transaction. 
âœ” Transactions do not see changes from other transactions until committed. 
âœ” Phantom reads still occur, meaning inserts or updates may modify query results mid-transaction. 
âœ” For complete consistency, SERIALIZABLE isolation level must be used.




















SERIALIZABLE Isolation Level:
-----------------------------

ğŸš€ Understanding SERIALIZABLE Isolation Level in MySQL
The SERIALIZABLE isolation level ensures the highest level of data consistency by executing transactions in sequence, eliminating concurrency issues. Transactions do not overlap, behaving as if they were processed in a single-user system.

ğŸ“Œ 1. What Is SERIALIZABLE Isolation Level?
âœ” Transactions execute one after another, preventing interference. 
âœ” Ensures strict data consistency, solving all concurrency problems:
Prevents lost updates
Eliminates dirty reads
Guarantees repeatable reads
Prevents phantom reads âœ” Transactions must wait for others to complete before executing, reducing concurrency.

ğŸ“Œ Expected Behavior Under SERIALIZABLE: 
âœ” Transactions do not see changes made by other transactions until fully committed. 
âœ” If a transaction is modifying data, other transactions must wait before reading or modifying the same data.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Demonstrating SERIALIZABLE Isolation Level
ğŸš€ Scenario: âœ” Two transactions interact with customer records in Virginia: 
1.Session 1: Reads customers in Virginia before another transaction modifies them. 
2.Session 2: Updates the state of a new customer to Virginia.

ğŸ“Œ Expected Behavior: 
âœ” Session 1 waits for Session 2 to complete before executing its query. 
âœ” Ensures accurate data retrieval without phantom reads.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Steps to Prevent Phantom Reads
ğŸš€ Opening Two Sessions âœ” Open two MySQL connections to simulate concurrent transactions.

ğŸ“Œ Session 1: Setting Isolation Level and Selecting Customers:
USE sql_store; 
SET TRANSACTION ISOLATION LEVEL SERIALIZABLE;
START TRANSACTION;

SELECT * FROM customers WHERE state = 'VA'; -- Only existing Virginia customers seen
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Establishes a snapshot of customers before new updates.


ğŸ“Œ Session 2: Updating a Customerâ€™s State:
USE sql_store; 
START TRANSACTION;

UPDATE customers 
SET state = 'VA'
WHERE customer_id = 3;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Customer 3 is now located in Virginia, but changes are not yet committed.


ğŸ“Œ Session 1: Attempting to Read Customers
SELECT * FROM customers WHERE state = 'VA'; -- Transaction is blocked, waiting...
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Session 1's transaction cannot proceed until Session 2 completes. âœ” Prevents phantom reads by ensuring updates finalize before queries execute.


ğŸ“Œ Session 2: Committing Changes
COMMIT;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Session 1 resumes execution, now including Customer 3 in results.


ğŸ“Œ Session 1: Query After Transaction Completion
COMMIT;
SELECT * FROM customers WHERE state = 'VA'; -- Now includes Customer 3
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Ensures all customers in Virginia are correctly retrieved.





ğŸ“Œ 4. Key Takeaways
âœ” SERIALIZABLE completely eliminates concurrency issues, ensuring transactions execute sequentially. 
âœ” Prevents phantom reads, guaranteeing that transactions see all changes affecting the query results. 
âœ” Transactions must wait for others to complete before proceeding, potentially reducing system performance.

ğŸ“Œ Use Cases 
âœ” Critical financial transactions where data consistency is mandatory. 
âœ” Avoiding phantom reads when data integrity is crucial. 
âœ” Not recommended for high-concurrency systems, as it limits performance.

ğŸ“Œ Next Steps: âœ” For improved concurrency, use REPEATABLE READ instead unless phantom reads must be prevented.





Queries and other notes in this discussion:

connection 1:
USE sql_store; 
SET TRANSACTION ISOLATION LEVEL SERIALIZABLE;
START TRANSACTION;
SELECT * FROM customers WHERE state = 'VA';  -- this transaction should be waiting for the transaction to finish and then reads customers along with customer_id 3 
COMMIT; 


connection 2:
USE sql_store; 
START TRANSACTION;
UPDATE customers 
SET state = 'VA'
WHERE customer_id = 3; 
COMMIT; 





ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” SERIALIZABLE ensures complete transaction isolation, solving all concurrency problems. 
âœ” Transactions execute sequentially, removing interference from concurrent updates. 
âœ” Prevents phantom reads, ensuring consistent query results. 
âœ” Use sparingly, as it may reduce performance due to transaction blocking.



















Deadlocks:
----------
ğŸš€ Understanding Deadlocks in MySQL
Deadlocks occur in databases when multiple transactions hold locks that the other transaction needs, preventing either from proceeding. Deadlocks can block execution indefinitely, requiring MySQL to resolve them by rolling back one transaction.

ğŸ“Œ 1. What Is a Deadlock?
âœ” A deadlock happens when two or more transactions wait for each other to release a lock, preventing completion. 
âœ” Each transaction locks some records while waiting to access others, creating circular dependency.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Two Transactions 
âœ” Transaction 1 locks customers, then tries to update orders. 
âœ” Transaction 2 locks orders, then tries to update customers. 
âœ” Each transaction waits for the other to complete, causing a deadlock.

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Neither transaction can proceed, forcing MySQL to resolve the conflict by rolling back one of them.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Simulating a Deadlock in MySQL
ğŸš€ Opening Two Sessions âœ” Open two MySQL connections to simulate concurrent transactions.

ğŸ“Œ Session 1: Updating Customers First
USE sql_store; 
START TRANSACTION; 

UPDATE customers SET state = 'VA' WHERE customer_id = 1;
UPDATE orders SET status = 1 WHERE order_id = 1;

COMMIT;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Locks the customers record first, then tries to update orders.



ğŸ“Œ Session 2: Updating Orders First (Reverse Order)
USE sql_store; 
START TRANSACTION; 

UPDATE orders SET status = 1 WHERE order_id = 1;
UPDATE customers SET state = 'VA' WHERE customer_id = 1;

COMMIT;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Locks the orders record first, then tries to update customers.

ğŸ“Œ Deadlock Occurs When Both Transactions Execute âœ” Each transaction holds a lock on its first update and waits for the other. âœ” Neither can complete, causing MySQL to detect a deadlock.

ğŸ“Œ Expected Output (Session 1 or 2)
ERROR 1213 (40001): Deadlock found when trying to get lock
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” MySQL selects one transaction as the victim and rolls it back.





ğŸ“Œ 3. How to Minimize Deadlocks?
âœ” Follow consistent order when updating multiple records across transactions. 
âœ” Keep transactions short to minimize lock duration. 
âœ” Avoid long-running transactions on large tables, as these increase chances of collision. 
âœ” Schedule large updates during non-peak hours to reduce contention.

ğŸ“Œ Best Practices âœ” If deadlocks happen frequently, review SQL execution order. âœ” If a transaction is rolled back due to a deadlock, the application should retry execution.





Queries and other notes in this discussion:


Session 1:
USE sql_store; 
START TRANSACTION; 
UPDATE customers SET state = 'VA' WHERE customer_id = 1; 
UPDATE orders SET status = 1 WHERE order_id = 1; 
COMMIT; 



Session 2:
USE sql_store; 
START TRANSACTION; 
UPDATE orders SET status = 1 WHERE order_id = 1; 
UPDATE customers SET state = 'VA' WHERE customer_id = 1; 
COMMIT; 






ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” Deadlocks occur when transactions block each other, preventing execution. 
âœ” MySQL resolves deadlocks by rolling back one transaction, allowing others to proceed. 
âœ” Reduce deadlocks by following consistent execution order and minimizing transaction duration.
