String Types:
-------------

ğŸš€ Understanding String Types in MySQL:
MySQL provides various string data types for handling textual data, each optimized for different use cases. Choosing the right type ensures efficient storage and indexing while maintaining data integrity.

ğŸ“Œ 1. Common String Data Types:
âœ” MySQL supports multiple string types, including:
CHAR(x) â†’ Fixed-length strings.
VARCHAR(x) â†’ Variable-length strings.
TEXT Variants (TINYTEXT, TEXT, MEDIUMTEXT, LONGTEXT) â†’ Large text storage.

ğŸ“Œ Key Differences Between CHAR and VARCHAR

Type	    Description	                                                                           Max Storage
CHAR(x)	    Fixed-length string, ideal for short, constant-size text (e.g., abbreviations).	       Up to 255 bytes
VARCHAR(x)	Variable-length string, suitable for varying-sized data (e.g., usernames, emails).	   Up to 65,535 characters (~64KB)

ğŸš€ Effect: 
âœ” CHAR(x) always reserves memory for its length (e.g., CHAR(10) reserves 30 bytes for Chinese characters). 
âœ” VARCHAR(x) saves space by using only the necessary storage for the string.

ğŸ“Œ When to Use CHAR vs. VARCHAR âœ” Use CHAR(x) for fixed-size text (state abbreviations, gender codes). âœ” Use VARCHAR(x) for dynamic-length text (names, passwords, emails).





ğŸ“Œ 2. Choosing VARCHAR Lengths for Optimization
âœ” Standardized lengths improve maintainability. âœ” Recommended choices:
VARCHAR(50) â†’ For short strings (usernames, passwords).
VARCHAR(255) â†’ For longer strings (emails, addresses).

ğŸ“Œ Why Stick to These Lengths? âœ” Consistency across tables simplifies maintenance. âœ” Prevents unnecessary storage allocation, reducing index size.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Handling Large Text (TEXT Variants)
âœ” When storing very large textual data, use TEXT types:

Type	    Max Storage	              Use Case
TINYTEXT	255 characters	          Short descriptions
TEXT	    64KB	                  Articles, summaries
MEDIUMTEXT	16MB	                  JSON objects, logs
LONGTEXT	4GB	                      Books, extensive logs

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Allows storing large unstructured textual data while maintaining flexibility.

ğŸ“Œ Why Choose VARCHAR Over TEXT? âœ” Indexes work better with VARCHARâ€”TEXT types cannot be fully indexed. âœ” Use TEXT when data exceeds VARCHAR's 64KB limit.





ğŸ“Œ 4. Handling International Characters
âœ” MySQL allocates storage based on character encoding.

Language	                Storage per Character
English	                    1 byte
European, Middle Eastern	2 bytes
Asian (Chinese, Japanese)	3 bytes

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” If using CHAR(10) for Chinese characters, MySQL reserves 30 bytes, ensuring proper encoding storage.

ğŸ“Œ Why Consider Character Encoding? âœ” Use UTF-8 for global support. âœ” Choose VARCHAR wisely to avoid wasted storage.





Queries and other notes in this discussion:

VARCHAR(x) -- max: 65, 535 characters (~64KB)

VARCHAR(50) for short strings 
VARCHAR(255) for medium-length strings

LONGTEXT: max: 4GB

TINYTEXT: max: 255 bytes 
TEXT: max: 64KB


Bytes: 
English = 1 byte, 
European, Middle-eastern = 2 bytes
Asian = 3 bytes 





ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” Use CHAR(x) for fixed-length data and VARCHAR(x) for variable-length text. 
âœ” Optimize VARCHAR usage by sticking to 50 or 255 characters where possible. 
âœ” For large text storage, use TEXT, MEDIUMTEXT, or LONGTEXT, but be aware of indexing limitations. 
âœ” International characters require more storage, so plan column types accordingly.
















Integer Types:

ğŸš€ Understanding Integer Types in MySQL
Integer types in MySQL store whole numbers without decimal points, with different types providing varying storage sizes and ranges. Choosing the appropriate integer type ensures efficient storage and query performance.

ğŸ“Œ 1. MySQL Integer Types and Their Ranges
âœ” MySQL provides five integer types, each differing in storage size and range:

Type	    Storage Size    Signed Range	          Unsigned Range

ğŸš€ Effect: 
âœ” Smaller types like TINYINT use less storage, improving performance in queries and indexing. 
âœ” Use UNSIGNED for positive-only values, extending storage range.

ğŸ“Œ Use Cases 
âœ” TINYINT UNSIGNED â†’ Best for small positive values (age, ratings). 
âœ” INT â†’ Standard choice for primary keys and counters. 
âœ” BIGINT â†’ Used for huge numerical data (financial, large-scale indexing).





ğŸ“Œ 2. Understanding Unsigned Values
âœ” By default, integers in MySQL are signed, meaning they can store both negative and positive values. 
âœ” Adding UNSIGNED limits values to positive-only, increasing storage capacity.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Using UNSIGNED for Age Storage
age TINYINT UNSIGNED
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Prevents negative values, ensuring data integrity.

ğŸ“Œ When to Use UNSIGNED? âœ” Use for inherently positive values (age, counters). âœ” Avoid for values requiring negative ranges (temperature, balances).





ğŸ“Œ 3. Handling Out-of-Range Values
âœ” MySQL restricts values to column type ranges. 
âœ” If an insert exceeds the type limit, MySQL throws an "Out of Range" error.

ğŸ“Œ Example:

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Invalid values are rejected, preserving data integrity.

ğŸ“Œ Preventing Range Errors âœ” Choose appropriate data types during design. âœ” Use constraints or validations in applications.





ğŸ“Œ 4. Using ZEROFILL for Padding Numbers
âœ” ZEROFILL ensures numbers display with leading zeros. 
âœ” This does not affect stored valuesâ€”only presentation.

ğŸ“Œ Example:

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Displays 1 as 0001 while keeping actual value as 1.

ğŸ“Œ When to Use ZEROFILL? âœ” Used for formatting purposes (e.g., invoice numbers). âœ” Not recommended for mathematical operations.





ğŸ“Œ 5. Best Practices
âœ” Use the smallest data type that suits your needs for optimal performance. 
âœ” Use UNSIGNED when values will never be negative, maximizing storage range. 
âœ” Keep integer columns compact to improve query execution speed. 
âœ” Optimize storage by avoiding unnecessary large types (BIGINT for small numbers).

Fixed-point and Floating-point Types:
-------------------------------------

ğŸš€ Understanding Fixed-Point and Floating-Point Types in MySQL
MySQL provides three data types for storing numbers with decimal points. These are optimized for either precise calculations (e.g., currency) or scientific computations (e.g., approximations for extremely large or small numbers).

ğŸ“Œ 1. Fixed-Point Numbers (DECIMAL)
âœ” Used for exact values, such as monetary amounts. 
âœ” Has a fixed number of decimal places, preventing rounding errors. 
âœ” Requires specifying precision (p) and scale (s).

ğŸ“Œ Syntax:
DECIMAL(p, s)  
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” p (Precision) â†’ Maximum number of digits (total). âœ” s (Scale) â†’ Number of digits after the decimal point.

ğŸ“Œ Example:
DECIMAL(9, 2)  -->  1234567.89  âœ…
âœ” Stores up to 9 digits, with 2 decimal places (7 digits before the decimal point).

ğŸ“Œ Synonyms for DECIMAL âœ” DEC, NUMERIC, and FIXED are identical to DECIMALâ€”stick to DECIMAL for consistency.

ğŸ“Œ Use Case âœ” Best for currency, precise values requiring accurate representation.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Floating-Point Numbers (FLOAT and DOUBLE)
âœ” Used for scientific calculations, where exact precision is not required. 
âœ” Stored as approximations, making them ideal for large or small numbers.

ğŸ“Œ Type Comparison:
Type	Storage Size	   Behavior
FLOAT	4 bytes	           Approximate storage, lower precision
DOUBLE	8 bytes	           Higher precision, can store larger numbers
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” FLOAT â†’ Uses less storage, but values may round unpredictably. âœ” DOUBLE â†’ More precise for scientific calculations requiring larger storage.



ğŸ“Œ Example Calculation Issue:
SELECT 0.1 + 0.2 = 0.3; -- May return FALSE due to floating-point rounding
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Floating-point types may introduce small rounding errors in calculations.

ğŸ“Œ Use Case âœ” Best for scientific computations where exact precision isnâ€™t necessary. âœ” Avoid for financial or monetary valuesâ€”use DECIMAL instead.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Choosing the Right Type
âœ” Use DECIMAL for fixed precision (currency, accounting). 
âœ” **Use FLOAT or DOUBLE when exact precision isnâ€™t required (scientific data). 
âœ” Avoid floating-point types for financial calculations due to rounding errors.

ğŸ“Œ Best Practices 
âœ” Stick to DECIMAL when precision matters, ensuring reliable arithmetic. 
âœ” Use floating-point types sparingly, knowing they store approximate values.

ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” Fixed-point (DECIMAL) ensures accuracy, ideal for currency & financial applications.

âœ” Always use DECIMAL for monetary values, avoiding floating-point rounding errors.



















Boolean Types:
---------------
ğŸš€ Understanding Boolean Types in MySQL
Boolean types store binary values, representing true/false conditions in a database. MySQL does not have a dedicated Boolean type but instead uses TINYINT(1) as a synonym.

ğŸ“Œ 1. What Is a Boolean in MySQL?
âœ” MySQL represents boolean values using TINYINT(1). 
âœ” TRUE is stored as 1, and FALSE is stored as 0. 
âœ” BOOLEAN or BOOL is simply an alias for TINYINT(1).

ğŸ“Œ Example Boolean Column Definition:
CREATE TABLE posts (
    id INT PRIMARY KEY AUTO_INCREMENT,
    title VARCHAR(255) NOT NULL,
    is_published BOOL
);
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” BOOL is stored as TINYINT(1), where TRUE = 1 and FALSE = 0.

ğŸ“Œ Alternative Representation âœ” Instead of TRUE and FALSE, 1 and 0 can be used directly:
UPDATE posts SET is_published = 1;  -- Equivalent to TRUE
UPDATE posts SET is_published = 0;  -- Equivalent to FALSE
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Boolean values can be stored explicitly using 1 or 0.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Using Boolean Conditions in Queries
âœ” Boolean columns are useful in conditional checks.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Retrieving Published Posts:
SELECT * FROM posts WHERE is_published = TRUE;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Filters all posts where is_published = 1.

ğŸ“Œ Alternative: Using 1 for Clarity:
SELECT * FROM posts WHERE is_published = 1;
âœ” Equivalent representation without using TRUE keyword.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Boolean Type Limitations
âœ” MySQL lacks a dedicated Boolean typeâ€”it treats it as TINYINT(1). âœ” Boolean columns accept any integer (not restricted to 0 or 1).

ğŸ“Œ Preventing Invalid Values âœ” Use a CHECK constraint to ensure only 0 or 1 values:
ALTER TABLE posts ADD CONSTRAINT chk_is_published CHECK (is_published IN (0,1));
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Restricts the column to valid Boolean values only.





ğŸ“Œ 4. Key Takeaways
âœ” Boolean values are stored as TINYINT(1) (where TRUE=1 and FALSE=0). 
âœ” MySQL does not enforce strict Boolean constraintsâ€”use checks for integrity. 
âœ” Use BOOL or BOOLEAN for readability but understand they are aliases for TINYINT(1).

ğŸ“Œ Use Cases 
âœ” Best for flags, status indicators, and yes/no values (is_active, is_deleted). 
âœ” Use explicit constraints to prevent invalid entries.





Queries and other notes in this discussion:

BOOL 

BOOLEAN 

UPDATE posts
SET is_published = 1 





ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” MySQL stores Boolean values using TINYINT(1)â€”no dedicated Boolean type exists. 
âœ” TRUE is represented as 1, FALSE as 0, making queries straightforward. 
âœ” Use BOOL or BOOLEAN for readability, but they function as TINYINT(1). 
âœ” Add constraints to ensure data integrity when working with Boolean values.
















Enum and Set Types:
-------------------
ğŸš€ Understanding ENUM and SET Types in MySQL
MySQL provides ENUM and SET types for restricting column values to predefined lists. While they offer some convenience, they also have significant drawbacks that make them less practical for scalable database designs.


ğŸ“Œ 1. What Is the ENUM Type?
âœ” ENUM restricts column values to a predefined list of strings, ensuring only valid values are inserted. âœ” Common use case: Storing fixed categories, such as clothing sizes (small, medium, large).

ğŸ“Œ Example: Defining an ENUM Column
CREATE TABLE products (
    id INT PRIMARY KEY AUTO_INCREMENT,
    name VARCHAR(255) NOT NULL,
    size ENUM('small', 'medium', 'large')
);
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Only "small", "medium", or "large" can be stored in the size column. âœ” Invalid entries will be rejected.


ğŸ“Œ Updating a Value
UPDATE products SET size = 'medium' WHERE id = 1;
âœ” Works as expected, storing only predefined values.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Why ENUM Is Problematic
While ENUM seems useful, it has significant drawbacks:

âœ” Table Rebuilds Are Expensive
If you add, remove, or rename ENUM values, MySQL rebuilds the entire table.
This is costly for large datasets.

âœ” Cannot Add Attributes to ENUM Members
What if "small" has specific measurements?
ENUM does not support additional attributes, making it inflexible.

âœ” Not Easily Reusable
If another table needs the same values, ENUM must be manually redefined, creating duplication.
Any change must be applied across multiple tables manually, increasing maintenance efforts.

ğŸ“Œ Better Alternative: Lookup Tables âœ” Instead of using ENUM, create a separate table (sizes) for better scalability and reusability:
CREATE TABLE sizes (
    id INT PRIMARY KEY AUTO_INCREMENT,
    name VARCHAR(50) NOT NULL UNIQUE,
    measurement VARCHAR(50) NOT NULL
);
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Easy updatesâ€”no table rebuild needed. âœ” Can store additional attributes (e.g., "small" â†’ "36 inches"). âœ” Reusable across multiple tables.





ğŸ“Œ 3. What Is the SET Type?
âœ” SET allows multiple values in one column, meaning it can store a list of predefined options. âœ” Example Use Case: A column called categories allowing multiple tags per product.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Defining a SET Column
CREATE TABLE products (
    id INT PRIMARY KEY AUTO_INCREMENT,
    name VARCHAR(255) NOT NULL,
    categories SET('electronics', 'furniture', 'clothing')
);
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Allows multiple predefined values per row ('electronics,furniture').


ğŸ“Œ Inserting Multiple SET Values
INSERT INTO products (name, categories) VALUES ('Laptop', 'electronics,furniture');
âœ” Stores both values together.





ğŸ“Œ 4. Why SET Is Problematic
âœ” Like ENUM, modifying the list requires a table rebuild, making changes expensive. âœ” Data is stored as a comma-separated string, limiting relational integrity. âœ” Cannot easily query individual SET valuesâ€”filtering specific options is cumbersome.

ğŸ“Œ Better Alternative: Join Tables âœ” Use a separate table for storing multiple categories per product, ensuring proper relational structure.
CREATE TABLE product_categories (
    product_id INT,
    category_id INT,
    PRIMARY KEY (product_id, category_id)
);
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Supports flexible querying (find all products under "electronics"). âœ” Can add or remove categories easily.





Queries and other notes in this discussion: 
ENUM('small', 'medium', 'large')
SET(...)




ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” ENUM restricts values to a predefined list but has severe update limitations. 
âœ” SET allows multiple values but is not scalable or efficient for querying. 
âœ” Better alternatives exist:
Use lookup tables instead of ENUM for flexible updates and attributes.
Use join tables instead of SET for efficient querying and relational integrity.














Date and Time Types:
--------------------
ğŸš€ Understanding Date and Time Types in MySQL
MySQL provides several date and time data types for storing various timestamp values efficiently. Choosing the right type ensures accurate storage, retrieval, and indexing.

ğŸ“Œ 1. Overview of Date and Time Data Types
âœ” MySQL offers five primary date and time types:

Type	   Storage Size	            Usage
DATE	   3 bytes	                Stores only date, without a time component (YYYY-MM-DD).
TIME	   3 bytes	                Stores only time, without a date component (HH:MM:SS).
DATETIME   8 bytes	                Stores both date and time, valid beyond 2038 (YYYY-MM-DD HH:MM:SS).
TIMESTAMP  4 bytes	                Stores date and time, valid only up to 2038 (YYYY-MM-DD HH:MM:SS).
YEAR	   1 byte	                Stores only a four-digit year (YYYY).
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Use TIMESTAMP for tracking row creation/modification timestamps, but it only works until 2038. âœ” For future-proofing, use DATETIME, which stores larger date ranges.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Choosing Between DATETIME and TIMESTAMP
âœ” DATETIME supports years beyond 2038, while TIMESTAMP is limited to 2038. 
âœ” TIMESTAMP consumes less storage (4 bytes vs. 8 bytes) but has restrictions.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Storing Event Timestamps:
CREATE TABLE events (
    id INT PRIMARY KEY AUTO_INCREMENT,
    event_name VARCHAR(255) NOT NULL,
    event_time TIMESTAMP DEFAULT CURRENT_TIMESTAMP
);
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Automatically stores the current timestamp upon insertion.

ğŸ“Œ Year 2038 Problem âœ” TIMESTAMP only supports dates up to 2038 due to internal storage limitations.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Using DATE and TIME Separately
âœ” If time and date donâ€™t need to be stored together, using DATE and TIME separately saves storage.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Storing Birthdate and Login Time Separately
CREATE TABLE users (
    id INT PRIMARY KEY AUTO_INCREMENT,
    birth_date DATE NOT NULL,
    last_login TIME NOT NULL
);
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Reduces storage usage, keeping data structured.





ğŸ“Œ 4. Key Takeaways
âœ” Use TIMESTAMP for tracking row insertion/updating, but beware of 2038 limitations. 
âœ” Use DATETIME for flexibility and long-term storage beyond 2038. 
âœ” Use DATE and TIME separately when exact timestamps are unnecessary. 
âœ” Use YEAR only for storing standalone four-digit years.

ğŸ“Œ Use Cases 
âœ” TIMESTAMP â†’ Best for logging creation/update times. 
âœ” DATETIME â†’ Best for future-proofing large time-sensitive records. 
âœ” DATE / TIME â†’ Efficient when date/time tracking is needed separately.




Queries and other notes in this discussion: 
DATE
TIME
DATETIME  8b
TIMESTAMP 4b (up to 2038)
YEAR





ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” DATE and TIME store independent values efficiently. 
âœ” DATETIME provides long-term compatibility beyond 2038. 
âœ” TIMESTAMP is lightweight but expires in 2038. 
âœ” Use YEAR only for storing standalone years. 
âœ” Choosing wisely improves query performance and database efficiency.



















Blob Types: 
-----------
ğŸš€ Understanding BLOB Types in MySQL
MySQL provides Binary Large Object (BLOB) types for storing binary data, such as images, videos, PDFs, and other multimedia files. While BLOB types allow database storage for such files, storing binary data in a relational database is generally discouraged due to performance concerns.

ğŸ“Œ 1. Overview of BLOB Types
âœ” MySQL supports four BLOB types, each varying in maximum storage capacity:

Type	      Storage Size	             Use Case
TINYBLOB	  255 bytes	                 Small binary data (icons, thumbnails).
BLOB	      65 KB	                     Medium-sized documents (PDFs, audio snippets).
MEDIUMBLOB	  16 MB	                     Larger images or videos.
LONGBLOB	  4 GB	                     High-resolution images, large multimedia files.
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Larger BLOB types allow more storage, but consume significant database space.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Challenges of Storing Files in a Database
âœ” While MySQL supports storing binary data, using databases for file storage has drawbacks.

ğŸ“Œ Problems with Database-Based File Storage 
âœ” Increased database size â†’ Large files inflate database storage, making backups slower. 
âœ” Slower backups â†’ Database backup and restoration take longer due to bulk storage. 
âœ” Performance issues â†’ Retrieving images from a database is slower than fetching from a file system. 
âœ” Extra coding required â†’ Reading and writing binary files requires additional logic in queries.

ğŸ“Œ Best Alternative âœ” Store files in a separate storage system (file server, cloud storage) and save file paths in the database. âœ” Example approach:
CREATE TABLE products (
    id INT PRIMARY KEY AUTO_INCREMENT,
    name VARCHAR(255) NOT NULL,
    image_path VARCHAR(255) NOT NULL  -- Stores file path instead of binary data
);
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Keeps database compact, allowing faster retrieval via file paths. âœ” Improves scalability, preventing unnecessary performance bottlenecks.





ğŸ“Œ 3. When to Use BLOB Types?
âœ” Only consider BLOB storage if database-level access is essential, such as:
Strict security requirements (where storing files separately is not viable).
Frequent transactions requiring file retrieval within queries.

ğŸ“Œ Use Cases Where BLOB Might Be Needed 
âœ” Medical records requiring strict access control (X-ray images). 
âœ” Financial institutions managing encrypted documents directly within a database.





ğŸ“Œ 4. Key Takeaways
âœ” MySQL offers four BLOB types for storing binary data (ranging from TINYBLOB to LONGBLOB). 
âœ” Storing files in a database increases size, slows backups, and impacts performance. 
âœ” Saving file paths instead of binary data is recommended for scalability. 
âœ” Use BLOB types only when direct database storage is necessary.





Queries and other notes in this discussion: 


TINYBLOB      255b
BLOB          65KB
MEDIUM BLOB   16MB
LONGBLOB      4GB


Problems with storing files in database: 

Increased database size
Slower backups
Performance problems 
More code to read/write images




ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” BLOB types store binary data, but using databases for file storage is often inefficient. 
âœ” Save file paths in a database instead, fetching actual files from a separate storage system. 
âœ” Only use BLOB types for highly secure, database-dependent storage. 
âœ” Optimizing data storage ensures faster queries and efficient backups.











JSON Type:
----------

ğŸš€ Working with JSON in MySQL
JSON (JavaScript Object Notation) is a lightweight format for storing and transferring structured data, commonly used in web and mobile applications. MySQL provides built-in JSON functions to efficiently store, manipulate, and query JSON data.

ğŸ“Œ 1. Storing JSON Data in MySQL
âœ” JSON is stored in MySQL using the JSON data type, allowing structured key-value storage.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Updating a Product's Properties (Standard JSON Format):
UPDATE products 
SET properties = '
{
    "dimensions": [1, 2, 3],
    "weight": 10,
    "manufacturer": { "name": "sony" }
}'
WHERE product_id = 1;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Stores dimensions as an array, weight as a number, and manufacturer as a nested object.


ğŸ“Œ Alternative Approach Using MySQL JSON Functions:
UPDATE products 
SET properties = JSON_OBJECT(
    'weight', 10, 
    'dimensions', JSON_ARRAY(1, 2, 3),
    'manufacturer', JSON_OBJECT('name', 'sony')
)
WHERE product_id = 1;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Uses MySQL JSON functions (JSON_OBJECT, JSON_ARRAY) for structured insertion.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Querying JSON Data in MySQL
âœ” Extract specific JSON values using JSON_EXTRACT() or column path operators (->, ->>).

ğŸ“Œ Retrieving a JSON Value (weight) Using JSON_EXTRACT:
SELECT product_id, JSON_EXTRACT(properties, '$.weight') AS weight
FROM products
WHERE product_id = 1;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Extracts the value of "weight" from the JSON column.


ğŸ“Œ Alternative Using Column Path Operator (->)
SELECT product_id, properties -> '$.weight' AS weight
FROM products
WHERE product_id = 1;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Equivalent to JSON_EXTRACT, but shorter syntax.


ğŸ“Œ Accessing Array Elements Inside JSON (dimensions[0])
SELECT product_id, properties -> '$.dimensions[0]'
FROM products
WHERE product_id = 1;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Retrieves the first element of the dimensions array.


ğŸ“Œ Retrieving Nested JSON Objects (manufacturer.name)
SELECT product_id, properties ->> '$.manufacturer.name'
FROM products
WHERE product_id = 1;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Extracts manufacturer.name while removing surrounding double quotes.


ğŸ“Œ Filtering Data Based on JSON Properties
SELECT product_id, properties ->> '$.manufacturer.name'
FROM products
WHERE properties ->> '$.manufacturer.name' = 'sony';
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Filters products where manufacturer.name is "sony".





ğŸ“Œ 3. Updating JSON Values in MySQL
âœ” Use JSON_SET() to modify existing properties or add new values.

ğŸ“Œ Updating the weight Value and Adding age:
UPDATE products 
SET properties = JSON_SET(
    properties,
    '$.weight', 20,
    '$.age', 10
)
WHERE product_id = 1;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Updates weight to 20 and adds age = 10.





ğŸ“Œ 4. Removing JSON Properties
âœ” Use JSON_REMOVE() to delete a specific key from the JSON object.

ğŸ“Œ Removing the age Property:
UPDATE products 
SET properties = JSON_REMOVE(
    properties,
    '$.age'
)
WHERE product_id = 1;
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Removes the age property while keeping other values unchanged.





ğŸ“Œ 5. Key Takeaways
âœ” Use MySQL JSON functions (JSON_OBJECT, JSON_ARRAY) for structured storage. 
âœ” Extract JSON data using JSON_EXTRACT() or -> operators. 
âœ” Modify JSON values using JSON_SET() and remove values with JSON_REMOVE(). 
âœ” Indexing JSON fields improves query performance (covered in advanced techniques).




Queries and other notes in this discussion:

json
{
 "key" : value
}



UPDATE products 
SET properties = '
{
	"dimensions" : [1, 2, 3],

}

FROM products
WHERE product_id = 1;

FROM products
WHERE product_id = 1; 


SELECT product_id, properties -> '$.dimensions[0]'
FROM products
WHERE product_id = 1; 

SELECT product_id, properties ->> '$.manufacturer.name'
FROM products
WHERE product_id = 1;

FROM products
WHERE properties ->> '$.manufacturer.name' = 'sony';

	properties,

WHERE product_id = 1;

SET properties = JSON_REMOVE(
	properties,
	'$.age'
)
WHERE product_id = 1;






ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” MySQL supports structured JSON storage and efficient queries using built-in functions. 
âœ” Use JSON_EXTRACT() or -> for retrieving values, JSON_SET() for updates, and JSON_REMOVE() for deletions. 
âœ” JSON data structure ensures flexible storage without unnecessary table modifications. 
âœ” Mastering MySQL JSON operations improves dynamic data management capabilities.
















Designing Databases:

So far we've been writing our queries against existing databases. But as a software engineer or database administrator, there are times when you will need to design a new database from scratch or add new tables to an existing database. And that's what you're going to learn in this section. I'm going to walk you through a simple step-by-step process to design well-structured databases. Remember, a well-designed database requires a bit of planning at the beginning and it may take a bit of time, but it plays a critical role in the success of your applications. If your database is designed properly, you can easily extend it to support new business requirements, you can easily query to extract useful information and your queries will execute quickly. A poorly designed database requires a lot of maintenance and the cost of maintaining it increases over time to the extent that it will eventually prevent you from supporting new business requirements. 
In one of the companies that I worked at, we had a poorly designed database and believe it or not, some of our stored procedures were almost a thousand lines of code because the structure of the database was overly complex. So extracting data was really difficult. Also, some of our queries took several minutes to execute. So in this section, I'm going to walk you through a systematic approach to design well-structured databases. Are you ready? Let's get started.




Data Modelling:
---------------
ğŸš€ Understanding Data Modeling in MySQL
Data modeling is the process of designing the structure of data that will be stored in a database. It ensures efficient data storage, retrieval, and integrity. The process follows four key steps to refine raw business requirements into a structured database design.

ğŸ“Œ 1. Steps in Data Modeling
âœ” Data modeling involves four structured phases:

Step	                               Purpose
Understanding Requirements	           Identify business needs and data relationships.
Conceptual Model	                   Define entities and their relationships.
Logical Model	                       Structure tables, columns, and constraints, independent of technology.
Physical Model	                       Implement database-specific data types, primary keys, and stored procedures.

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Ensures structured and optimized database design, preventing inefficient data storage.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Step 1: Understanding Business Requirements
âœ” Before designing tables, fully understand business objectives. âœ” Interview stakeholders, domain experts, and users to analyze existing records.

ğŸ“Œ Sources for Business Data Analysis âœ” Forms, documents, spreadsheets, applications, existing databases. âœ” Identifying business relationships and dependencies to ensure completeness.

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Prevents incorrect database structuring, ensuring all essential data points are captured.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Step 2: Building the Conceptual Model
âœ” Defines business entities and relationships. 
âœ” Visual representation used to communicate with stakeholders.

ğŸ“Œ Example Conceptual Model for an E-Commerce Database âœ” Entities:
Customers (id, name, email)
Orders (order_id, date, customer_id)
Products (product_id, name, price)

âœ” Relationships:
A customer places multiple orders.
Each order contains multiple products.

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Ensures logical structure, avoiding redundant or missing relationships.





ğŸ“Œ 4. Step 3: Refining to a Logical Model
âœ” Transforms conceptual model into structured tables and columns. âœ” Abstract data definition, independent of database technology.

ğŸ“Œ Example Logical Model

Table	          Column Definitions
Customers	      customer_id (PK), name, email
Orders	          order_id (PK), date, customer_id (FK)
Products	      product_id (PK), name, price

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Lays the foundation for database implementation.





ğŸ“Œ 5. Step 4: Creating the Physical Model
âœ” Implements the logical model using database-specific details. 
âœ” Defines exact data types, constraints, indexes, and relationships.

ğŸ“Œ Example Physical Model for MySQL:
CREATE TABLE customers (
    customer_id INT PRIMARY KEY AUTO_INCREMENT,
    name VARCHAR(255) NOT NULL,
    email VARCHAR(255) NOT NULL UNIQUE
);

CREATE TABLE orders (
    order_id INT PRIMARY KEY AUTO_INCREMENT,
    date DATETIME NOT NULL,
    customer_id INT,
    FOREIGN KEY (customer_id) REFERENCES customers(customer_id)
);

CREATE TABLE products (
    product_id INT PRIMARY KEY AUTO_INCREMENT,
    name VARCHAR(255) NOT NULL,
    price DECIMAL(10, 2) NOT NULL
);
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Includes database-specific optimizations (primary keys, indexing). âœ” Ensures high performance and efficient querying.





ğŸ“Œ 6. Key Takeaways
âœ” Data modeling follows four steps: requirements analysis, conceptual model, logical model, and physical model. 
âœ” Skipping requirement analysis leads to inefficient database designs. 
âœ” Conceptual models visualize business entities and relationships before structuring tables. 
âœ” Logical models define tables and columns independently of database technology. 
âœ” Physical models specify database-specific details like primary keys, indexing, and optimization.





Queries and other notes in this discussion:

Understand the requirements 
Build a Conceptual Model 
Build a Logical Model 
Build a Physical Model 





ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” Data modeling is critical for structuring databases efficiently. 
âœ” Follow structured modeling steps to design a scalable, high-performance database. 
âœ” Logical models serve as a bridge between conceptual ideas and technical implementations. 
âœ” Mastering data modeling ensures robust and optimized data storage.











Conceptual Models:
------------------
ğŸš€ Understanding Conceptual Models in MySQL Data Modeling
A conceptual model is the first step in data modeling, providing a high-level overview of business entities and their relationships. It helps business stakeholders and developers align on requirements before diving into technical implementation.

ğŸ“Œ 1. What Is a Conceptual Model?
âœ” Represents business entities and their relationships without focusing on technical details. âœ” Used to communicate with stakeholders and ensure clarity before designing tables. 
âœ” Examples of entities:
People â†’ Students, instructors.
Events â†’ Course enrollments.
Locations â†’ Online platforms.

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Defines a visual structure to prevent misalignment in requirements.

ğŸ“Œ Benefits of Conceptual Modeling 
âœ” Helps organize business rules and data flow. 
âœ” Ensures agreement before investing in logical or physical models.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Example: Conceptual Model for an Online Course Platform
âœ” Users enroll in courses uploaded by instructors. 
âœ” Courses have tags for categorization.

ğŸ“Œ Identified Entities âœ” Student â†’ Has name, email, registration_date. âœ” Course â†’ Has title, price, instructor, tags.

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Entities define core objects in the system, ensuring structured relationships.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Visualizing Conceptual Models
âœ” Entity Relationship Diagrams (ERDs) and UML diagrams are common for visualization.

ğŸ“Œ Tools for Creating Diagrams âœ” Microsoft Visio (Windows). âœ” Draw.io / Lucidcharts (Web-based).

ğŸ“Œ Defining Relationships âœ” Use many-to-many relationships:

Student  -- enrolls -- Course

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Defines how students interact with courses, ensuring proper structure.





ğŸ“Œ 4. Conceptual Model vs. Logical & Physical Models
âœ” Conceptual Model: High-level overview of entities and relationships. 
âœ” Logical Model: Defines tables, attributes, and constraints. 
âœ” Physical Model: Implements the structure within a specific database technology (MySQL).

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Helps bridge business requirements into technical database solutions.





ğŸ“Œ 5. Key Takeaways
âœ” Conceptual models define business structure, ensuring alignment before database implementation. 
âœ” Entities represent core business concepts, such as students, courses, and enrollments. 
âœ” Relationships visualize interactions, guiding database design decisions. 
âœ” Using ERDs or UML diagrams improves clarity and communication with stakeholders.





Queries and other notes in this discussion:
