Entity Relationship, UML - visually expressing the concepts 

conceptual model - identifying the entities or things or concepts in the business and their relationships with each other. A conceptual model is just a visual representation of these concepts that you use to communicate with the stakeholders so you know you're both on the same page.






ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” Conceptual models help organize business data without technical complexities. 
âœ” Identifying entities ensures structured relationships before defining tables. 
âœ” Using ERDs or UML diagrams improves visualization and stakeholder communication. 
âœ” Refining conceptual models leads to accurate logical and physical database designs.


















Logical Models:
---------------
ğŸš€ Understanding Logical Models in MySQL Data Modeling
A logical model defines the structure of data in a database without being tied to a specific database technology. It refines a conceptual model by specifying attributes, data types, and relationships, preparing for actual implementation.

ğŸ“Œ 1. What Is a Logical Model?
âœ” Refines a conceptual model by specifying data types and relationships. 
âœ” Independent of database technologiesâ€”focuses on abstract data modeling. 
âœ” Provides a structured blueprint for tables before physical implementation.

ğŸ“Œ Logical Model vs. Conceptual Model

Feature	              Conceptual Model	                                   Logical Model
Focus	              High-level business entities and relationships	   Structured tables, attributes, and relationships
Technical Details	  No data types or constraints	                       Includes attributes, data types, relationships
Independence	      Business-level abstraction	                       Abstract database schema (not tech-specific)

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Bridges business requirements to database implementation, ensuring clear structuring.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Refining a Conceptual Model Into a Logical Model
âœ” Step 1: Define Entity Attributes âœ” Step 2: Specify Data Types âœ” Step 3: Identify Relationships

ğŸ“Œ Example Logical Model for an Online Course Platform:
Entity	   Attributes
Student	   first_name (String), last_name (String), email (String), date_registered (Datetime)
Course	   title (String), price (Float), instructor (String), tags (String)

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Precise attribute definition ensures structured data storage.

ğŸ“Œ Splitting Composite Attributes âœ” Breaking attributes into smaller fields ensures better querying:
Full Name  â†’  First Name + Last Name
Address    â†’  Street + City + Zip Code + Country

âœ” Improves sorting, filtering, and indexing efficiency.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Defining Relationships in a Logical Model
âœ” Relationships define how entities interact.

ğŸ“Œ Three Primary Relationship Types:
Type	              Example
One-to-One	          A person has one passport.
One-to-Many	          A customer places multiple orders.
Many-to-Many	      A student enrolls in multiple courses.

ğŸ“Œ Handling Many-to-Many Relationships âœ” Students enroll in multiple courses. âœ” Courses have multiple students.

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Direct many-to-many relationships complicate querying. âœ” Introducing an Enrollment Entity simplifies data storage.





ğŸ“Œ 4. Resolving Many-to-Many Relationships Using an Intermediate Entity
âœ” Step 1: Introduce an Enrollment entity. 
âœ” Step 2: Define its attributes. 
âœ” Step 3: Convert Many-to-Many into Two One-to-Many relationships.

ğŸ“Œ Updated Logical Model:
Entity	     Attributes
Student	     first_name, last_name, email, date_registered
Course	     title, price, instructor, tags
Enrollment	 date (Datetime), price (Float)


ğŸ“Œ Updated Relationships:
Student 1:M Enrollment 1:M Course

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Converts Many-to-Many into two One-to-Many relationships, simplifying querying.

ğŸ“Œ Why Store Price in Enrollment? âœ” Courses may have varying prices over time. âœ” Price should reflect what the student paid during enrollment.





ğŸ“Œ 5. Logical Model Attribute Refinement
âœ” Ensure attributes align with structured data representation.

ğŸ“Œ Updated Course Attributes

Attribute	Data Type
Title	    String
Price	    Float
Instructor	String
Tags	    String
ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Tags stored as a comma-separated string (temporary approach). âœ” Normalization techniques will later refine tag storage.





ğŸ“Œ 6. Key Takeaways
âœ” Logical models refine conceptual models by specifying data types and structured relationships. 
âœ” Splitting attributes improves query efficiency (e.g., first_name, last_name instead of name). 
âœ” Introducing an intermediate entity (Enrollment) simplifies many-to-many relationships. 
âœ” Logical models remain independent of database technologies, preparing for implementation.




Note: 
logical model is independent of database technologies




ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” Logical models provide structured data modeling before technical implementation. 
âœ” Clearly defining relationships ensures scalable database design. 
âœ” Resolving many-to-many relationships using an intermediate entity enhances query efficiency. 
âœ” Logical models serve as a blueprint for database creation.

















Physical Models:
----------------
ğŸš€ Understanding Physical Models in MySQL Data Modeling
A physical model is the actual implementation of a logical model in a specific database technology (e.g., MySQL). It defines tables, columns, data types, constraints, and relationships, making the abstract model ready for real-world use.

ğŸ“Œ 1. What Is a Physical Model?
âœ” A physical model is the detailed implementation of a logical model in MySQL. 
âœ” Includes exact MySQL data types, constraints (NOT NULL, DEFAULT values, PRIMARY KEY). 
âœ” Defines how data will be stored and retrieved efficiently.

ğŸ“Œ Logical vs. Physical Model

Feature	                    Logical Model	                     Physical Model
Database-Independent?	    Yes	                                 No (specific to MySQL)
Attribute Data Types?	    General types (String, Float)	     MySQL-specific types (VARCHAR, DECIMAL)
Relationships?	            Abstract relationships	             Implemented using foreign keys
Primary Keys, Defaults?	    Not specified	                     Defined explicitly

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Translates abstract design into actual database tables.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Creating a Physical Model in MySQL Workbench
âœ” Step 1: Open MySQL Workbench â†’ File â†’ New Model. 
âœ” Step 2: Rename Database Schema â†’ Default is mydb, change to school. 
âœ” Step 3: Add Tables & Columns.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Creating the students Table

Column	        Data Type	     Constraints
student_id	    INT	             PRIMARY KEY, AUTO_INCREMENT
first_name	    VARCHAR(50)	     NOT NULL
last_name	    VARCHAR(50)	     NOT NULL
email	        VARCHAR(255)	 NOT NULL, UNIQUE
date_registered	DATETIME	     NOT NULL

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Defines structured storage, ensuring efficient querying.

ğŸ“Œ Best Practices for Naming Conventions âœ” Use plural table names (students, courses). âœ” Use underscores (first_name) for column names instead of camelCase.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Implementing Other Tables
âœ” Define courses and enrollments tables based on logical model.

ğŸ“Œ Example: courses Table
Column	      Data Type	      Constraints
course_id	  INT	          PRIMARY KEY, AUTO_INCREMENT
title	      VARCHAR(255)	  NOT NULL
price	      DECIMAL(5,2)	  NOT NULL
instructor	  VARCHAR(255)	  NOT NULL
tags	      VARCHAR(255)	  NULLABLE (Normalization will later refine this)


ğŸ“Œ Example: enrollments Table:

Column	          Data Type	       Constraints
enrollment_id	  INT	           PRIMARY KEY, AUTO_INCREMENT
student_id	      INT	           FOREIGN KEY REFERENCES students(student_id)
course_id	      INT	           FOREIGN KEY REFERENCES courses(course_id)
date	          DATETIME	       NOT NULL
price	          DECIMAL(5,2)	   NOT NULL

ğŸš€ Effect: âœ” Stores enrollment details, linking students to courses using foreign keys.

ğŸ“Œ Avoiding Redundant Column Naming âœ” Use date, not enrollment_date â†’ Column already belongs to enrollments.





ğŸ“Œ 4. Key Takeaways
âœ” Physical models implement logical designs using database-specific details. 
âœ” Use exact MySQL data types (VARCHAR, DECIMAL, DATETIME) for efficiency. 
âœ” Define primary keys and foreign keys to establish relationships between tables. 
âœ” Avoid redundant column names (date, price instead of enrollment_date, enrollment_price).

ğŸš€ Next steps: Adding primary keys and defining relationships between tables.





ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” Physical models translate abstract logical models into MySQL database tables. 
âœ” Proper naming conventions enhance query efficiency and database clarity. 
âœ” Using FOREIGN KEY constraints ensures referential integrity across tables. 
âœ” Optimizing storage by choosing appropriate data types improves performance.



















Primary Keys:
-------------
ğŸš€ Understanding Primary Keys in MySQL
A primary key uniquely identifies each record in a table, ensuring data integrity and preventing duplicate entries. Proper primary key selection is essential for efficient database relationships and indexing.




ğŸ“Œ 1. What Is a Primary Key?
âœ” A primary key is a unique identifier for each row in a table.  
âœ” Must contain unique values and cannot be NULL.  
âœ” Used to establish relationships between tables (via foreign keys).

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Ensures fast lookups and prevents duplicate records.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Choosing the Right Primary Key
âœ” The primary key should be a field that remains unchanged.  
âœ” Avoid natural attributes that may change, such as:
   - First name + Last name â†’ Not unique ('John Smith' can exist multiple times).
   - Email â†’ Unique but changeable, making it unreliable.

ğŸ“Œ Best Approach: Using an 'ID' Column
âœ” Introduce a dedicated 'id' column.
âœ” Use an integer type ('INT') for efficiency.
âœ” Enable 'AUTO_INCREMENT' to automatically generate unique values.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Adding Primary Key to 'students' Table:
CREATE TABLE students (
    student_id INT PRIMARY KEY AUTO_INCREMENT,
    first_name VARCHAR(50) NOT NULL,
    last_name VARCHAR(50) NOT NULL,
    email VARCHAR(255) NOT NULL UNIQUE,
    date_registered DATETIME NOT NULL
);

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Ensures each student has a unique identifier ('student_id').  
âœ” Prevents duplication of lengthy text fields ('email') in related tables.





ğŸ“Œ3. Defining a Primary Key in MySQL Workbench
âœ” Step 1: Open MySQL Workbench â†’ Table Design View.  
âœ” Step 2: Add 'student_id' as the first column.  
âœ” Step 3: Set 'INT' as the data type.  
âœ” Step 4: Mark as 'PRIMARY KEY' and enable 'AUTO_INCREMENT'.

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” MySQL automatically generates unique IDs, simplifying insertion.

ğŸ“Œ Ordering the Primary Key First
âœ” Place primary keys at the top for clarity.  
âœ” Use 'Move Up' option in MySQL Workbench to reorder.





ğŸ“Œ 4. Adding Primary Keys to Other Tables
âœ” Each table requires a primary key for data consistency.

ğŸ“Œ Example: 'courses' Table:

CREATE TABLE courses (
    course_id INT PRIMARY KEY AUTO_INCREMENT,
    title VARCHAR(255) NOT NULL,
    price DECIMAL(5,2) NOT NULL,
    instructor VARCHAR(255) NOT NULL,
    tags VARCHAR(255)
);

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Ensures courses are uniquely identified using 'course_id', preventing duplicates.

ğŸ“Œ Example: 'enrollments' Table:
CREATE TABLE enrollments (
    enrollment_id INT PRIMARY KEY AUTO_INCREMENT,
    student_id INT NOT NULL,
    course_id INT NOT NULL,
    enrollment_date DATETIME NOT NULL,
    price DECIMAL(5,2) NOT NULL,
    FOREIGN KEY (student_id) REFERENCES students(student_id),
    FOREIGN KEY (course_id) REFERENCES courses(course_id)
);

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Converts enrollment into a structured entity, ensuring unique course-student relations.





ğŸ“Œ 5. Key Takeaways
âœ” Always use dedicated 'id' columns as primary keys ('INT AUTO_INCREMENT').  
âœ” Avoid using changeable attributes like 'email' as primary keys.  
âœ” Place primary keys at the top of the table for clarity.  
âœ” Each table should have a unique identifier for efficient relationships.  

ğŸš€ Next steps: Defining foreign keys to establish table relationships.





ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” Primary keys uniquely identify records, ensuring efficient indexing and lookups.  
âœ” Using integer-based 'AUTO_INCREMENT' keys simplifies data management.  
âœ” Avoid using fields that may change as primary keys ('name', 'email').  
âœ” Defining structured primary keys lays the foundation for strong relational database design.


















Foreign Keys:
-------------
ğŸš€ Understanding Foreign Keys in MySQL

Foreign keys establish relationships between tables, ensuring referential integrity. They connect records from one table to another, preventing orphaned data and improving query efficiency.



ğŸ“Œ 1. What Is a Foreign Key?
âœ” A foreign key is a column that references a primary key in another table.  
âœ” Used to enforce relationships between tables, ensuring data consistency.  
âœ” Parent Table ('Primary Key Table') â†’ Contains the original unique records.  
âœ” Child Table ('Foreign Key Table') â†’ References the primary key from the parent table.

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Ensures valid relationships, preventing orphaned data.

ğŸ“Œ Example Foreign Key Relationship
âœ” A student enrolls in multiple courses:
Student (Primary Table) 1:M Enrollment (Foreign Table)





ğŸ“Œ 2. Creating Foreign Key Relationships in MySQL Workbench
âœ” Step 1: Identify parent and child tables.  
âœ” Step 2: Select foreign key table first, then the parent table.  
âœ” Step 3: Ensure MySQL Workbench auto-generates the foreign key column.

ğŸ“Œ Adding Foreign Keys to 'Enrollments' Table
âœ” Student ID ('student_id') â†’ Links to 'students(student_id)'.  
âœ” Course ID ('course_id') â†’ Links to 'courses(course_id)'.

ğŸ“Œ Renaming Foreign Key Columns
âœ” Default naming may be redundant ('students_student_id').  
âœ” Rename to simpler format ('student_id', 'course_id').

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Improves readability, keeping naming conventions clean.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Choosing a Primary Key for 'Enrollments' Table
âœ” Two possible approaches:
1.Composite Primary Key ('student_id + course_id').  
2.Dedicated 'enrollment_id' column ('INT AUTO_INCREMENT').

ğŸ“Œ Using a Composite Primary Key ('student_id, course_id')
âœ” Prevents duplicate enrollments (same student cannot enroll twice in the same course).  
âœ” Ensures uniqueness in relationships.

ğŸ“Œ Using a Dedicated 'enrollment_id' Primary Key
âœ” Simplifies future relationshipsâ€”only 'enrollment_id' needs repetition in child tables.  
âœ” More scalable if additional tables reference 'enrollments'.

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” For the current design, a composite primary key is preferred (no child table exists yet).  
âœ” Future changes can introduce 'enrollment_id' if needed.

ğŸ“Œ Final Structure
CREATE TABLE enrollments (
    student_id INT,
    course_id INT,
    enrollment_date DATETIME NOT NULL,
    price DECIMAL(5,2) NOT NULL,
    PRIMARY KEY (student_id, course_id),
    FOREIGN KEY (student_id) REFERENCES students(student_id),
    FOREIGN KEY (course_id) REFERENCES courses(course_id)
);

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Prevents duplicate enrollments, enforcing relational integrity.





ğŸ“Œ 4. Key Takeaways
âœ” Foreign keys reference primary keys in another table, ensuring relational integrity.  
âœ” Using composite primary keys prevents duplicate enrollments.  
âœ” Dedicated primary keys ('enrollment_id') offer better scalability for complex relationships.  
âœ” Future design changes can adjust primary key strategy if required.  

ğŸš€ Next steps: Implementing foreign key constraints and cascading options in MySQL.



Note: parent or the primary key table, child or the foreign key table 




ğŸ“Œ 5.Summary
âœ” Foreign keys enforce structured data relationships, linking tables efficiently.  
âœ” A composite primary key ('student_id + course_id') ensures unique enrollments.  
âœ” Alternative: Using 'enrollment_id' as a primary key for future flexibility.  
âœ” Optimizing primary key selection improves database scalability and performance.
















Foreign Key Constraints:
------------------------
ğŸš€ Understanding Foreign Key Constraints in MySQL:
Foreign key constraints ensure referential integrity by defining rules for updates and deletions in parent-child relationships. They prevent orphaned records and unintended data loss, making relational databases more reliable.



ğŸ“Œ 1. What Are Foreign Key Constraints?
âœ” Foreign key constraints define rules for handling updates and deletions in a referenced table.  
âœ” Prevents corrupt or orphaned data by enforcing relational integrity.  
âœ” Each foreign key must have a unique name within the database.

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Ensures structured data management, avoiding inconsistencies.

ğŸ“Œ Foreign Key Example
âœ” 'student_id' in 'enrollments' references 'student_id' in 'students'.  
âœ” 'course_id' in 'enrollments' references 'course_id' in 'courses'.



ğŸ“Œ 2. Managing Foreign Key Constraints
âœ” Define behavior when parent records change or are deleted.

ğŸ“Œ Handling Foreign Key Updates
âœ” What if 'student_id' changes from '1' to '2' in 'students'?
   - CASCADE â†’ Automatically updates 'student_id' in 'enrollments'.
   - RESTRICT / NO ACTION â†’ Prevents changes to 'student_id'.
   - SET NULL â†’ Sets 'student_id' to NULL (results in orphan records).

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Using 'CASCADE' ensures consistent references without manual updates.

ğŸ“Œ Handling Foreign Key Deletions
âœ” What happens if a student is deleted?
   - CASCADE â†’ Deletes related enrollments (not ideal for financial data).
   - RESTRICT / NO ACTION â†’ Prevents student deletion if enrollments exist.
   - SET NULL â†’ Makes 'student_id' NULL, creating orphan records.

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Prevent deletion ('RESTRICT / NO ACTION') when financial data exists.

ğŸ“Œ Example Foreign Key Constraints for 'enrollments'

ALTER TABLE enrollments 
ADD CONSTRAINT fk_enrollments_students 
FOREIGN KEY (student_id) REFERENCES students(student_id) 
ON UPDATE CASCADE 
ON DELETE RESTRICT;

ALTER TABLE enrollments 
ADD CONSTRAINT fk_enrollments_courses 
FOREIGN KEY (course_id) REFERENCES courses(course_id) 
ON UPDATE CASCADE 
ON DELETE NO ACTION;

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Ensures valid updates but prevents unwanted deletions.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Business Considerations for Foreign Key Constraints
âœ” Always align constraints with business needs.  
âœ” For financial records, prevent automatic deletions to retain sales history.  
âœ” For non-critical data (e.g., reminders), cascading deletions may be acceptable.

ğŸ“Œ Example Decision Scenarios
âœ” Prevent course deletion if students enrolled â†’ Use 'RESTRICT / NO ACTION'.  
âœ” Auto-delete reminders when user deletes account â†’ Use 'CASCADE'.

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Custom rules maintain business integrity while allowing structured data flow.





ğŸ“Œ 4. Key Takeaways
âœ” Foreign key constraints enforce data integrity and prevent inconsistencies.  
âœ” Use 'CASCADE' for updates to maintain references automatically.  
âœ” For financial records, prevent deletion to retain transaction history ('RESTRICT').  
âœ” Align constraints with business rules for optimal database management.  

ğŸš€ Next steps: Implementing foreign keys in table creation and MySQL Workbench.





ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” Foreign keys establish relationships while constraints define update/delete behavior.  
âœ” Using 'CASCADE' ensures synchronized updates across tables.  
âœ” Prevent unintended deletions ('RESTRICT / NO ACTION') when necessary.  
âœ” Choosing the right constraint avoids data loss while maintaining referential integrity.



















Normalization: 
--------------
ğŸš€ Understanding Normalization in MySQL Data Modeling
Normalization is the process of optimizing database design to eliminate redundant data and ensure consistency. It minimizes storage usage, simplifies insert/update/delete operations, and prevents inconsistent records.




ğŸ“Œ 1. Why Is Normalization Important?
âœ” Reduces redundancy, making updates more efficient.  
âœ” Ensures data integrity, preventing inconsistencies in reports.  
âœ” Simplifies database structure, improving scalability.

ğŸ“Œ Example Problem Without Normalization
âœ” A student's name is stored in multiple tables â†’ If they change their name, multiple updates are needed, leading to possible inconsistencies.

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Maintaining a single source of truth ensures accuracy and efficiency.




ğŸ“Œ 2. What Are Normal Forms?
âœ” Normalization follows seven rules, known as "Normal Forms".  
âœ” Each normal form builds upon the previous one.

ğŸ“Œ Key Normal Forms for Practical Applications
âœ” First Normal Form (1NF) â†’ Ensures atomicity, meaning each column should store a single value.  
âœ” Second Normal Form (2NF) â†’ Eliminates partial dependencies, ensuring each attribute depends entirely on the primary key.  
âœ” Third Normal Form (3NF) â†’ Removes transitive dependencies, meaning no column should depend on non-primary key attributes.

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Applying the first three normal forms solves 99% of real-world database issues.

ğŸ“Œ Beyond 3NF
âœ” Higher normal forms (BCNF, 4NF, 5NF, etc.) handle complex relationships but are rarely needed in standard applications.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Steps to Normalize a Database
âœ” Step 1: Review the schema to identify redundant data.  
âœ” Step 2: Apply the first three normal forms, ensuring structured relationships.  
âœ” Step 3: Verify that data dependencies align with primary keys.  
âœ” Step 4: Refactor tables when necessary, creating new entities for repeated attributes.

ğŸ“Œ Example Refinements
âœ” If a student's name appears in multiple tables â†’ Move names to a separate 'students' table and reference them via 'student_id'.  
âœ” If courses have multiple tags stored in a single column â†’ Create a separate 'course_tags' table instead of storing tags as a comma-separated string.

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Prevents duplication, enhances data integrity, and optimizes querying.





ğŸ“Œ 4. Key Takeaways
âœ” Normalization eliminates redundant data, ensuring consistency and efficiency.  
âœ” The first three normal forms solve most real-world database issues.  
âœ” Higher normal forms (4NF, 5NF) are rarely needed in standard applications.  
âœ” Structured relationships prevent incorrect updates and unnecessary duplication.  

ğŸš€ Next steps: Understanding First Normal Form (1NF) and how to implement it.





ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” Normalization optimizes database design, ensuring structured, scalable data storage.  
âœ” Applying 1NF, 2NF, and 3NF eliminates redundant data and improves integrity.  
âœ” Refactoring tables prevents inconsistencies and simplifies data retrieval.  
âœ” Mastering normalization ensures efficient database performance and maintainability.


















1NF-First Normal Form:
----------------------
ğŸš€ First Normal Form (1NF) in MySQL Normalization:
First Normal Form (1NF) ensures that each cell in a table contains a single value and that columns are not duplicated. It eliminates data redundancy and ensures atomicity, making databases more scalable and easier to maintain.





ğŸ“Œ 1. Understanding 1NF Rules
âœ” Each column must store atomic values (no lists or multiple values).  
âœ” Columns should not be repeated (e.g., 'tag_1', 'tag_2', 'tag_3').

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Prevents poor scalability, avoiding the need to modify table structure when new values are added.

ğŸ“Œ Example Violation in the 'courses' Table
âœ” A 'tags' column storing multiple values separated by commas:
Tags â†’ "frontend, backend, JavaScript"

ğŸš€ Problem:  
âœ” Hard to query and filter individual tags.  
âœ” Cannot guarantee consistent formatting across records.  
âœ” Adding a new tag requires modifying the schema.

ğŸ“Œ Alternative Poor Design: Using Multiple Columns:
Tag_1 â†’ "frontend"
Tag_2 â†’ "backend"
Tag_3 â†’ "JavaScript"

ğŸš€ Problem:  
âœ” Limits flexibilityâ€”future additions require schema changes.  
âœ” Hard to sort and filter efficiently.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Fixing the 'tags' Column: Creating a Separate 'tags' Table
âœ” Step 1: Remove the 'tags' column from 'courses'.  
âœ” Step 2: Create a new table for 'tags'.  
âœ” Step 3: Establish a many-to-many relationship between 'courses' and 'tags'.

ğŸ“Œ Creating the 'tags' Table:
CREATE TABLE tags (
    tag_id TINYINT PRIMARY KEY AUTO_INCREMENT,
    tag_name VARCHAR(50) NOT NULL
);


ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Stores tags separately, making querying easier.  
âœ” Allows dynamic growth without altering schema.

ğŸ“Œ Choosing the Right Data Type for 'tag_id'
âœ” If tags are manually created by admins â†’ Use 'TINYINT' (max ~255 values).  
âœ” If tags are dynamically created by users â†’ Use 'INT' (larger range).

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” TINYINT conserves storage space, assuming 50-60 predefined tags.

ğŸ“Œ Handling Data Type Bugs in MySQL Workbench
âœ” Sometimes, MySQL Workbench automatically changes 'TINYINT' back to 'INT'.  
âœ” To fix this, manually set 'TINYINT' in the column properties, removing unnecessary parentheses.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Creating a Many-to-Many Relationship Between 'courses' and 'tags'
âœ” Step 1: Introduce a junction table ('course_tags').  
âœ” Step 2: Reference 'course_id' from 'courses' and 'tag_id' from 'tags'.  

ğŸ“Œ Defining the 'course_tags' Table:

CREATE TABLE course_tags (
    course_id INT NOT NULL,
    tag_id TINYINT NOT NULL,
    PRIMARY KEY (course_id, tag_id),
    FOREIGN KEY (course_id) REFERENCES courses(course_id),
    FOREIGN KEY (tag_id) REFERENCES tags(tag_id)
);


ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Ensures each course can have multiple tags without duplication.  
âœ” Allows filtering courses by tags efficiently.





ğŸ“Œ 4. Key Takeaways
âœ” First Normal Form (1NF) eliminates duplicate columns and ensures atomic values.  
âœ” Storing multiple values in a single cell (comma-separated data) violates 1NF.  
âœ” Moving related attributes into separate tables prevents redundancy.  
âœ” Using a 'course_tags' table establishes a scalable many-to-many relationship.  

ğŸš€ Next steps: Implementing a structured many-to-many relationship using foreign keys.





ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” 1NF ensures that columns hold single values and prevents duplicate column names.  
âœ” Replacing comma-separated data with structured tables optimizes queries and scalability.  
âœ” Many-to-many relationships prevent data repetition while maintaining relational integrity.  
âœ” Following 1NF prevents unnecessary schema modifications and inefficient queries.














Link Tables:
------------

ğŸš€ Understanding Link Tables in MySQL Data Modeling
Since relational databases do not support direct many-to-many relationships, link tables (junction tables) act as intermediary tables to connect two entities via two one-to-many relationships.



ğŸ“Œ 1. What Is a Link Table?
âœ” Used to implement many-to-many relationships in relational databases.  
âœ” Contains foreign keys referencing primary keys in both related tables.  
âœ” Ensures structured data relationships without redundancy.

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Optimizes many-to-many relationships, making querying efficient.

ğŸ“Œ Example Many-to-Many Relationship: Courses & Tags
âœ” A course can have multiple tags, and a tag can belong to multiple courses.
âœ” Solution: Create a 'course_tags' table to manage the relationship.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Implementing the Link Table ('course_tags')
âœ” Step 1: Create 'course_tags' table.  
âœ” Step 2: Define foreign keys ('course_id', 'tag_id').  
âœ” Step 3: Establish two one-to-many relationships (Courses â†’ CourseTags, Tags â†’ CourseTags).  

ğŸ“Œ Creating the 'course_tags' Table:
CREATE TABLE course_tags (
    course_id INT NOT NULL,
    tag_id TINYINT NOT NULL,
    PRIMARY KEY (course_id, tag_id),
    FOREIGN KEY (course_id) REFERENCES courses(course_id),
    FOREIGN KEY (tag_id) REFERENCES tags(tag_id)
);



ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Prevents duplicate course-tag relationships via composite primary key.  
âœ” Improves flexibilityâ€”courses can have unlimited tags without schema modifications.

ğŸ“Œ Defining Foreign Key Relationships
âœ” Courses â†’ CourseTags â†’ A course can have multiple tags.  
âœ” Tags â†’ CourseTags â†’ A tag can be assigned to multiple courses.

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Ensures valid referencesâ€”deleting a course automatically removes its tags if 'CASCADE' is set.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Removing Redundant Data
âœ” Eliminate the 'tags' column from 'courses' (previously stored comma-separated values).  
âœ” Refactor database to rely on 'course_tags' instead of manual tag storage.

ğŸ“Œ Deleting the 'tags' Column:
ALTER TABLE courses DROP COLUMN tags;

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Ensures proper normalization, preventing data duplication.

ğŸ“Œ Key Benefits of Using a Link Table
âœ” Centralized management of tags â†’ Modifications only require updating one row.  
âœ” Prevents redundant storage â†’ No need to repeat tag data in multiple places.  
âœ” Optimizes updates â†’ Changing a tag does not lock multiple course records.





ğŸ“Œ 4. Key Takeaways:
âœ” Link tables establish many-to-many relationships without redundancy.  
âœ” Using a junction table ('course_tags') ensures structured tagging for courses.  
âœ” Removing the 'tags' column from 'courses' improves scalability.  
âœ” Applying link tables adheres to 'First Normal Form (1NF)', eliminating repeated data.  

ğŸš€ Next steps: Implementing Second Normal Form (2NF) for further optimization.





ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” Link tables serve as intermediary structures for managing many-to-many relationships.  
âœ” Creating a 'course_tags' table prevents redundant tag storage and improves query efficiency.  
âœ” Proper normalization ensures structured and scalable database design.  
âœ” Following 1NF principles keeps databases efficient and free from repeated columns and values.















2NF-Second Normal Form:
-----------------------
ğŸš€ Second Normal Form (2NF) in MySQL Normalization
Second Normal Form (2NF) ensures that each table has a single purpose and every column must describe the entity that the table represents. It eliminates partial dependencies, preventing unnecessary data duplication.



ğŸ“Œ 1. Understanding 2NF Rules
âœ” Tables must first satisfy First Normal Form (1NF) (atomic values, no duplicate columns).  
âœ” Every column should directly relate to the table's primary entity.  
âœ” Eliminate attributes that belong to a different entity, ensuring proper table separation.

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Reduces data redundancy, making updates simpler and more efficient.

ğŸ“Œ Example Violation: Courses Table
âœ” The 'instructor' column exists in 'courses', but instructors can teach multiple courses.
âœ” Duplicating instructor names leads to update issues (changing a name requires multiple updates).

ğŸ“Œ Example Violation: Orders Table
âœ” Storing 'customer_name' in 'orders' violates 2NF.
âœ” A customer may place multiple orders, leading to name duplication.

ğŸš€ Solution:  
âœ” Move 'instructor' to a separate 'instructors' table.  
âœ” Move 'customer_name' to a separate 'customers' table.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Implementing 2NF in MySQL
âœ” Step 1: Create separate tables for related entities.  
âœ” Step 2: Remove columns that do not belong.  
âœ” Step 3: Establish one-to-many relationships using foreign keys.

ğŸ“Œ Creating the 'instructors' Table:
CREATE TABLE instructors (
    instructor_id SMALLINT PRIMARY KEY AUTO_INCREMENT,
    name VARCHAR(50) NOT NULL
);


ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Stores instructor details in a single location, preventing duplication.

ğŸ“Œ Updating the 'courses' Table
âœ” Remove 'instructor' column.
âœ” Introduce 'instructor_id' as a foreign key.

ALTER TABLE courses DROP COLUMN instructor;

ALTER TABLE courses ADD COLUMN instructor_id SMALLINT NOT NULL;
ALTER TABLE courses ADD CONSTRAINT fk_courses_instructors 
FOREIGN KEY (instructor_id) REFERENCES instructors(instructor_id)
ON UPDATE CASCADE 
ON DELETE NO ACTION;


ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Links courses to instructors via IDs, enabling scalable relationships.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Establishing a One-to-Many Relationship
âœ” Each instructor teaches multiple courses, but each course belongs to one instructor.

ğŸ“Œ Creating One-to-Many Relationship:
Instructor (Primary Table) 1:M Courses (Foreign Table)

âœ” Courses reference 'instructor_id', ensuring data consistency.

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Prevents redundant instructor names across multiple courses.  
âœ” Modifying an instructorâ€™s name updates all associated records without duplication.





ğŸ“Œ 4. Key Takeaways
âœ” 2NF ensures each table stores only relevant attributes.  
âœ” If an attribute belongs to another entity, create a separate table.  
âœ” Introducing a foreign key maintains structured relationships.  
âœ” Use cascading updates to ensure consistent records across relationships.  

ğŸš€ Next steps: Implementing Third Normal Form (3NF) for further optimization.



ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” Second Normal Form eliminates partial dependencies and organizes data efficiently.  
âœ” Refactoring tables prevents unnecessary duplication.  
âœ” Using foreign keys ensures structured entity relationships.  
âœ” Applying 2NF improves database scalability and maintainability.
















3NF-Third Normal Form:
----------------------
ğŸš€ Third Normal Form (3NF) in MySQL Normalization
Third Normal Form (3NF) ensures that no column in a table should be derived from other columns. It eliminates transitive dependencies, preventing unnecessary data duplication and ensuring consistency.



ğŸ“Œ 1. Understanding 3NF Rules
âœ” A table must first satisfy Second Normal Form (2NF) (attributes must belong directly to the entity).  
âœ” No attribute should be dependent on a non-primary attribute (transitive dependency).  
âœ” Remove calculable values and store only raw data.

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Eliminates inconsistent and redundant data, improving update reliability.

ğŸ“Œ Example Violation: Invoices Table
âœ” The 'balance' column is derived from 'invoice_total - payment_total':
balance = invoice_total - payment_total

ğŸš€ Problem:  
âœ” If 'invoice_total' or 'payment_total' changes, 'balance' must be updated manually.  
âœ” Forgetting to update 'balance' leads to inconsistent records.

ğŸ“Œ Solution: Remove 'balance' Column
âœ” Balance should be calculated dynamically in queries instead of stored explicitly.
SELECT invoice_total - payment_total AS balance FROM invoices;


ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Prevents inconsistent records and ensures real-time accuracy.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Identifying Transitive Dependencies
âœ” Transitive dependency: A column depends on another non-primary attribute, rather than directly on the primary key.

ğŸ“Œ Example Violation: Full Name in Customers Table
âœ” If the 'customers' table contains 'first_name', 'last_name', and 'full_name':
full_name = CONCAT(first_name, ' ', last_name)

ğŸš€ Problem:  
âœ” Full name is derived from first and last name, creating unnecessary duplication.  
âœ” Updating 'first_name' or 'last_name' requires updating 'full_name' manually.

ğŸ“Œ Solution: Remove 'full_name' and use dynamic queries:
SELECT CONCAT(first_name, ' ', last_name) AS full_name FROM customers;

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Reduces storage usage and ensures consistency.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Applying 3NF Across Tables
âœ” Remove any column that can be calculated using other columns.  
âœ” Ensure all attributes depend only on the primary key, not other non-primary attributes.

ğŸ“Œ Common Fixes Using 3NF
âœ” Remove computed values (e.g., balance, full name).  
âœ” Ensure attributes belong directly to the entity's primary key.  
âœ” Use queries instead of storing derivable data.

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Prevents redundant updates, ensures data consistency, and reduces storage usage.





ğŸ“Œ 4. Key Takeaways
âœ” 3NF eliminates transitive dependencies, ensuring structured data relationships.  
âœ” Derived values should be removed and calculated dynamically in queries.  
âœ” Removing redundant attributes improves database maintainability.  
âœ” Applying 3NF prevents inconsistencies, ensuring accurate reporting.  

ğŸš€ Next steps: Implementing best practices for indexing and optimizing MySQL queries.





ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” Third Normal Form ensures that no column is dependent on non-primary attributes.  
âœ” Transitive dependencies must be eliminated to prevent update issues.  
âœ” Using dynamic queries rather than stored derived values optimizes accuracy and efficiency.  
âœ” Applying 3NF improves database integrity and reduces unnecessary storage usage.
















My Pragmatic Advice:
--------------------
ğŸš€ Pragmatic Approach to Normalization in MySQL
Normalization helps optimize databases by eliminating redundancy and ensuring structured relationships. However, memorizing normalization rules isn't necessaryâ€”the key is focusing on practical database design principles.



ğŸ“Œ 1. Focus on Removing Redundancy
âœ” Avoid worrying about specific normal forms (1NF, 2NF, 3NF, etc.)â€”instead,  
âœ” Identify duplicate values that are not foreign keys and eliminate unnecessary repetition.  
âœ” When redundant data appears, rethink table structure and logical relationships.

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Ensures efficient storage, faster queries, and simplified updates.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Example: Fixing a Poorly Designed Customers Table
ğŸš¨ Problem: Storing Multiple Shipping Addresses in a Single Table
| Customer_ID  | Name   | Email           | Shipping_Address |
|--------------|--------|-----------------|------------------|
| 101          | Alice  | alice@email.com | 123 Main St      |
| 101          | Alice  | alice@email.com | 456 Elm St       |

ğŸš€ Issue:  
âœ” Customer data is duplicated whenever they have multiple shipping addresses.  
âœ” Updating customer details (e.g., email) requires multiple changes.

ğŸ“Œ Solution: Split into 'customers' and 'addresses' tables
âœ” Recognize logical entities â†’ Customers and Addresses.  
âœ” Define relationships â†’ Customers can have multiple addresses (one-to-many relationship).

ğŸ“Œ Creating Separate Tables:
CREATE TABLE customers (
    customer_id INT PRIMARY KEY AUTO_INCREMENT,
    name VARCHAR(50) NOT NULL,
    email VARCHAR(255) NOT NULL UNIQUE
);

CREATE TABLE addresses (
    address_id INT PRIMARY KEY AUTO_INCREMENT,
    customer_id INT NOT NULL,
    shipping_address VARCHAR(255) NOT NULL,
    FOREIGN KEY (customer_id) REFERENCES customers(customer_id)
);

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Prevents customer duplication, ensuring accurate and efficient updates.  
âœ” Addresses are managed separately, avoiding redundant information storage.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Start with Logical Entities, Not Normalization Rules
âœ” Instead of memorizing normalization rules, focus on logical relationships.  
âœ” Understand the real-world constraints before designing database tables.  
âœ” Skipping proper entity identification leads to messy, inefficient designs.

ğŸ“Œ Logical Modeling Approach
âœ” Identify core entities â†’ Define what represents unique data points.  
âœ” Establish relationships â†’ Avoid forcing unnecessary data into a table.

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Results in a cleaner, scalable database schema without blindly following rules.





ğŸ“Œ 4. Consider Business Requirements Before Applying Normalization
âœ” Every design decision should align with business needs.  
âœ” Some redundancy may be acceptable if requirements dictate it.  
âœ” Donâ€™t overcomplicate designs just to follow normalization blindly.

ğŸ“Œ Example: Supporting Multiple Shipping Addresses
âœ” If required, use separate 'addresses' table.  
âœ” If not needed, storing a single address per customer is acceptable.

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Database design should fit requirements, not arbitrary normalization rules.





ğŸ“Œ 5. Key Takeaways
âœ” Forget rigid normalization rulesâ€”focus on removing redundancy.  
âœ” Recognizing logical entities ensures scalable database design.  
âœ” Requirements dictate structureâ€”don't model unnecessary complexity.  
âœ” Efficient table relationships prevent duplicate data and optimize storage.  

ğŸš€ Next steps: Avoiding overcomplication by not modeling unnecessary relationships.




ğŸ“ŒSummary
âœ” Eliminate duplicate data by understanding entity relationships instead of memorizing normalization forms.  
âœ” Separate data into logical tables only when necessary based on business needs.  
âœ” Always start with a conceptual model before defining tables.  
âœ” Build databases that align with real-world applications, avoiding unnecessary complexity.
















Don't Model the Universe:
-------------------------
ğŸš€ Avoid Overcomplicating Database Models â€“ Donâ€™t Model the Universe
One of the most common mistakes in database design is over-generalizing models to anticipate future requirements. While flexibility is important, creating overly complex structures based on hypothetical scenarios leads to inefficiency and unnecessary complexity.



ğŸ“Œ 1. The Problem with Over-Generalized Models
âœ” Many data modelers attempt to future-proof their designs, anticipating requirements that may never happen.  
âœ” Instead of focusing on the current scope of the project, they create overly complex models, making maintenance difficult.  
âœ” A complex, abstract database model can become nearly impossible to understand, leading to poor query performance.

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Designing for imaginary future use cases results in unnecessary complexity.  
âœ” Projects become difficult to scale due to overcomplicated structures.





ğŸ“Œ 2. The Consequences of Over-Engineering
âœ” A real-world example of an overly complex data model:
- The architect designed a universal database with only three tables: 'entities', 'attributes', and 'values'.
- His argument: The system would be flexible enough to store any kind of data without requiring schema changes.

ğŸš€ Result:  
âœ” Queries were painfully slowâ€”even simple lookups required multiple joins.  
âœ” Stored procedures were excessively long, sometimes exceeding 1000 lines of code.  
âœ” Basic operations took minutes, making real-time queries impractical.

ğŸ“Œ Lessons Learned
âœ” Avoid abstracting database models beyond practical use.  
âœ” A system that is â€œtoo flexibleâ€ often lacks efficiency and clarity.  
âœ” A relational database should reflect structured real-world relationships, not attempt to replace them.



ğŸ“Œ 3. Focus on Current Requirements, Not Hypotheticals
âœ” Database models should be designed for todayâ€™s requirements, not hypothetical future needs.  
âœ” Real-world data changes (e.g., customers move, change names), but handling this complexity should be based on business needs, not theoretical concerns.  

ğŸ“Œ Example Mistakes
âœ” Adding unnecessary attributes like 'previous_name' for all customers, assuming users might legally change their names.  
âœ” Supporting unlimited addresses when only the current address is needed.  

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Over-designing creates unnecessary maintenance burdens.  
âœ” Complexity increases without adding meaningful business value.

ğŸ“Œ Practical Approach
âœ” Model based on real-world requirements, not speculation.  
âœ” Future changes will happenâ€”adjustments to the database schema are inevitable.  
âœ” Migrations can always be scripted when needed instead of making assumptions.





ğŸ“Œ 4. Keep Database Models Simple and Understandable
âœ” Complexity should be justified by actual business needs, not theoretical possibilities.  
âœ” Design with clarityâ€”your model should be easy for others to understand and maintain.  
âœ” Performance and usability should take priority over excessive flexibility.

ğŸ“Œ Guiding Principles
âœ” Simplicity is the ultimate sophistication.  
âœ” Great design simplifies complexity rather than adding to it.  
âœ” Structured models perform better and require less maintenance.

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Efficient queries, easy schema adjustments, and a model everyone can understand.  
âœ” Future requirements can be integrated logically rather than anticipated blindly.





ğŸ“Œ 5. Key Takeaways
âœ” Donâ€™t build for hypothetical scenariosâ€”design for whatâ€™s needed today.  
âœ” A database should reflect business needs, not abstract concepts.  
âœ” Overcomplicated models lead to poor query performance and difficult maintenance.  
âœ” Embrace changeâ€”database migration scripts allow for future adjustments when necessary.  

ğŸš€ Next steps: Optimizing query performance and maintaining scalability without over-engineering.






ğŸ“Œ 6. Summary
âœ” Resist the temptation to create overly flexible database modelsâ€”focus on practical implementation.  
âœ” Structure your database around current business needs instead of hypothetical future changes.  
âœ” Simplifying complexity improves database performance and maintainability.  
âœ” Making informed adjustments when changes arise is far more effective than excessive anticipation.













Forward Engineering a Model:
----------------------------
ğŸš€ Forward Engineering a Model in MySQL Workbench:
Forward engineering converts a physical data model into a real MySQL database. This process automatically generates SQL scripts to create tables, views, and other database objects.



ğŸ“Œ 1. What Is Forward Engineering?
âœ” It transforms a structured model into an actual database, eliminating manual table creation.  
âœ” Uses MySQL Workbenchâ€™s built-in wizard to automate database deployment.  

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Ensures database structure aligns with the designed model while reducing human errors.




ğŸ“Œ 2. Steps to Forward Engineer a Model
âœ” Step 1: Open MySQL Workbench with your physical model.  
âœ” Step 2: Click Database â†’ Forward Engineer.  
âœ” Step 3: Select your MySQL connection.

ğŸ“Œ Selecting a Connection
âœ” By default, your active connection appears.  
âœ” If not, manually select the correct database server.  
âœ” Enter your password if required.

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Connects MySQL Workbench to the target database, ensuring proper schema creation.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Configuring Forward Engineering Settings
âœ” Leave default settings unless customization is needed.  
âœ” Next step: Select database objects to include in the script.

ğŸ“Œ Choosing Objects to Forward Engineer
âœ” Tables (mandatory).  
âœ” Views (optional).  
âœ” Stored procedures, triggers, and functions (optional).  
âœ” Apply filters to exclude unnecessary tables.

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Ensures only relevant structures are generated in the SQL script.





ğŸ“Œ 4. Reviewing Generated SQL Script
âœ” MySQL Workbench generates the schema creation SQL code.  
âœ” Typical commands include:

CREATE SCHEMA school;
CREATE TABLE students (
    student_id INT PRIMARY KEY AUTO_INCREMENT,
    first_name VARCHAR(50) NOT NULL,
    last_name VARCHAR(50) NOT NULL,
    email VARCHAR(255) NOT NULL UNIQUE,
    date_registered DATETIME NOT NULL
);


ğŸ“Œ Options Available
âœ” Save to File â†’ Store script for version control.  
âœ” Copy to Clipboard â†’ Paste into MySQL Workbench manually.  
âœ” Click Continue â†’ Automatically create the database and tables.

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Allows manual verification before executing changes on the database.





ğŸ“Œ 5. Finalizing Forward Engineering
âœ” Step 1: Click Continue to execute the script.  
âœ” Step 2: Refresh the Navigator Panel in MySQL Workbench.  
âœ” Step 3: Verify that all tables now exist in the database.

ğŸ“Œ Verifying the Database
âœ” Navigate to Local Instance â†’ Expand Databases â†’ Check for school database.  
âœ” Ensure tables match the designed physical model.

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Database is successfully created, ready for data insertion and application integration.





ğŸ“Œ 6. Key Takeaways
âœ” Forward engineering automates database creation, reducing manual work.  
âœ” Selecting the correct objects ensures accurate schema generation.  
âœ” Generated SQL scripts can be stored in version control for tracking changes.  
âœ” Refreshing the database navigator confirms successful table creation.  

ğŸš€ Next steps: Populating tables with initial data and optimizing queries.




ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” Forward engineering transforms a physical model into an actual MySQL database.  
âœ” Using MySQL Workbench ensures structured table creation without manual intervention.  
âœ” Saving generated scripts improves maintainability and version tracking.  
âœ” Successful execution results in fully defined tables ready for data management.














Synchronizing a Model with a Database:
--------------------------------------

ğŸš€ Synchronizing a Model with a Database in MySQL Workbench

Synchronization allows developers to update an existing database structure based on changes made to the physical data model. This process ensures consistency across environments (development, staging, testing, production) and eliminates manual table modifications.



ğŸ“Œ 1. Why Is Synchronization Important?
âœ” Ensures database structure remains aligned with changes in the model.  
âœ” Prevents inconsistent schemas across environments (development, testing, production).  
âœ” Automatically applies changes without manual intervention.

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Streamlines schema updates, making collaboration in teams more efficient.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Making Changes to the Model
âœ” Step 1: Open MySQL Workbench, select the physical model.  
âœ” Step 2: Modify tables in the design mode (e.g., adding columns).  
âœ” Step 3: Exampleâ€”Adding a 'coupon' column in the 'enrollments' table:
ALTER TABLE enrollments ADD COLUMN coupon VARCHAR(50) NULL;

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Stores coupon codes for student enrollments, maintaining flexibility.

ğŸ“Œ Ensuring Nullable Values
âœ” Set column as NULL â†’ Not all students use coupons.  
âœ” VARCHAR(50) â†’ Consistent with other short string attributes.



ğŸ“Œ 3. Synchronizing Model with the Database
âœ” Step 1: Navigate to Database â†’ Synchronize Model.  
âœ” Step 2: Select the correct database connection:
   - Local for personal use.
   - Testing/Staging/Production for organization-wide synchronization.

ğŸ“Œ Selecting the Correct Environment
âœ” Development â†’ Local development database.  
âœ” Testing â†’ QA database where changes are tested.  
âœ” Staging â†’ Pre-production database used for final validation.  
âœ” Production â†’ Live application database (only after validation).

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Prevents accidental modifications to production databases.





ğŸ“Œ 4. Detecting Affected Tables
âœ” MySQL Workbench detects dependencies:
   - Enrollments table modified â†’ Affects related 'courses' table.  
   - Courses table modified â†’ Affects 'course_tags' due to relationships.

ğŸ“Œ Handling Foreign Key Dependencies
âœ” MySQL temporarily drops foreign key constraints, then recreates them:
'''sql
ALTER TABLE courses DROP FOREIGN KEY fk_courses_enrollments;
ALTER TABLE enrollments ADD COLUMN coupon VARCHAR(50);
ALTER TABLE courses ADD CONSTRAINT fk_courses_enrollments FOREIGN KEY (course_id) REFERENCES enrollments(course_id);

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Ensures smooth schema updates without violating constraints.





ğŸ“Œ 5. Reviewing and Executing the Synchronization
âœ” MySQL Workbench generates an SQL script for all changes.  
âœ” Options available:
   - Save to File â†’ Keep a backup for version control (e.g., Git).  
   - Copy to Clipboard â†’ Manually execute SQL in another environment.  
   - Execute Now â†’ Apply changes immediately.

ğŸ“Œ Best Practice: Using Version Control
âœ” Store scripts in Git repositories â†’ Track database changes.  
âœ” Allows replication of the same changes across multiple servers.

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Prevents undocumented schema changes, making updates repeatable and traceable.



ğŸ“Œ 6. Finalizing Database Synchronization
âœ” Step 1: Click Execute to apply changes.  
âœ” Step 2: Refresh Navigator Panel to confirm updates.  
âœ” Step 3: Verify that the new column 'coupon' appears in 'enrollments'.

ğŸ“Œ Verifying the Updated Schema
DESCRIBE enrollments;

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Confirms successful synchronization between model and database.





ğŸ“Œ 7. Key Takeaways
âœ” Synchronization automates schema updates across database environments.  
âœ” Foreign key dependencies are managed to prevent schema conflicts.  
âœ” Using version control (Git) ensures traceable and replicable changes.  
âœ” Refreshing the navigator verifies successful updates.

ğŸš€ Next steps: Populating updated tables with relevant data for validation.




ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” Synchronizing a model ensures the database remains consistent with structural changes.  
âœ” Automatically detecting dependencies simplifies schema modification.  
âœ” Tracking schema updates using SQL scripts prevents accidental inconsistencies.  
âœ” MySQL Workbench efficiently handles foreign key constraints during updates.











Reverse Engineering a Database:
-------------------------------
ğŸš€ Reverse Engineering a Database in MySQL Workbench:
Reverse engineering allows developers to create a model from an existing database, making it easier to visualize relationships, identify design flaws, and apply future changes systematically.



ğŸ“Œ 1. Why Reverse Engineer a Database?
âœ” Some databases exist without a formal data modelâ€”reverse engineering generates one.  
âœ” Helps understand existing database structures, especially when working with inherited systems.  
âœ” Identifies missing relationships and schema inconsistencies, improving data integrity.

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Creates a structured model, making future modifications more manageable.




ğŸ“Œ 2. Steps to Reverse Engineer a Database
âœ” Step 1: Open MySQL Workbench, close any existing models.  
âœ” Step 2: Navigate to Database â†’ Reverse Engineer.  
âœ” Step 3: Select the correct MySQL connection.  

ğŸ“Œ Why Close Existing Models?
âœ” MySQL Workbench adds reverse-engineered databases to open models, which may cause unwanted merging.  
âœ” Keeping separate models for different databases improves organization.

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Ensures clean database modeling, avoiding unintended mix-ups.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Selecting the Target Database
âœ” Step 1: Choose the correct database from available connections.  
âœ” Step 2: If multiple databases exist, select only those that are highly related.  
âœ” Step 3: Proceed to the next step.

ğŸ“Œ Handling Multiple Databases
âœ” Selecting multiple databases is useful for related systems that share entities.  
âœ” Unrelated databases should remain in separate models.

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Keeps models organized, preventing confusion when making changes.





ğŸ“Œ 4. Importing Tables into the Model
âœ” Step 1: MySQL Workbench detects all tables in the selected database.  
âœ” Step 2: Filter tables if needed, or import everything.  
âœ” Step 3: Execute the reverse engineering process.

ğŸ“Œ Filtering Tables
âœ” Exclude tables that donâ€™t need modeling to keep the model clean.  
âœ” For comprehensive modeling, include all tables.

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Ensures complete visualization of database relationships and dependencies.





ğŸ“Œ 5. Reviewing the Generated Diagram
âœ” MySQL Workbench generates a full ER diagram, showing tables and their relationships.  
âœ” Key benefits of the diagram:
   - Easily visualize entity connections (e.g., 'products' â†’ 'order_items' one-to-many relationship).  
   - Detect missing relationships that may impact data integrity.  

ğŸ“Œ Example Issue: 'order_item_notes' Table
âœ” This table lacks relationships, meaning 'order_id' values aren't validated.  
âœ” Without foreign key constraints, invalid data can be inserted.

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Identifies missing constraints, ensuring proper referential integrity.





ğŸ“Œ 6. Making Changes After Reverse Engineering
âœ” Step 1: Modify the model using MySQL Workbench's design tools.  
âœ” Step 2: Add missing relationships to enforce data consistency.  
âœ” Step 3: Generate SQL scripts to apply changes across MySQL databases.

ğŸ“Œ Advantages of Reverse Engineering
âœ” Improves schema understanding, making modifications easier.  
âœ” Enforces relational integrity, preventing accidental data corruption.  
âœ” Provides a clear roadmap for future optimizations.

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Database becomes structured, scalable, and easier to maintain.



ğŸ“Œ 7. Key Takeaways
âœ” Reverse engineering creates a model for databases that lack formal documentation.  
âœ” Generated ER diagrams help visualize relationships and identify design flaws.  
âœ” Missing foreign key constraints can be detected and corrected.  
âœ” Changes can be scripted and executed across different environments.

ğŸš€ Next steps: Synchronizing reverse-engineered models with live databases.



ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” Reverse engineering simplifies database management, allowing structured visualization.  
âœ” Generated diagrams help identify missing relationships and inconsistencies.  
âœ” Fixing schema issues ensures referential integrity and prevents invalid data entry.  
âœ” Reverse-engineered models facilitate future modifications in a systematic way.















Project: Flight Booking System

Alright, here's a project for you. I want you to design a database for a flight booking system. Download the PDF I've attached to this lecture. In this PDF, you can see an example of a ticket that this system should generate. Based on the information you see here, design a database for this system. You will see my solution next.


Details in the attached pdf: 
Flight Booking System.pdf:
Los Angeles, CA -> San Francisco, CA

ğŸš€ Flight Booking System Database Design
A flight booking system manages flight details, passengers, bookings, tickets, and pricing. Based on the provided sample ticket, we need to design a structured relational database to capture key information efficiently.



ğŸ“Œ Identified Entities
âœ” Flights â†’ Stores flight details (departure, arrival, timing, airline).  
âœ” Passengers â†’ Stores traveler details (name, ticket number).  
âœ” Bookings â†’ Links passengers to flights via booking records.  
âœ” Airports â†’ Stores standardized airport details.  
âœ” Pricing â†’ Manages ticket costs associated with each passenger.  

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Organizes data logically, preventing duplication and ensuring consistency.



ğŸ“Œ 2. Tables and Their Attributes
âœ” Step 1: Define tables and attributes.  
âœ” Step 2: Establish relationships via foreign keys.

ğŸ“Œ Creating the 'flights' Table

CREATE TABLE flights (
    flight_id INT PRIMARY KEY AUTO_INCREMENT,
    airline VARCHAR(100) NOT NULL,
    flight_number VARCHAR(10) NOT NULL UNIQUE,
    departure_airport_code VARCHAR(10) NOT NULL,
    arrival_airport_code VARCHAR(10) NOT NULL,
    departure_datetime DATETIME NOT NULL,
    arrival_datetime DATETIME NOT NULL,
    duration TIME NOT NULL,
    distance_miles INT NOT NULL,
    class VARCHAR(50) NOT NULL
);

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Stores flight details uniquely, making retrieval efficient.  

ğŸ“Œ Creating the 'passengers' Table

CREATE TABLE passengers (
    passenger_id INT PRIMARY KEY AUTO_INCREMENT,
    first_name VARCHAR(50) NOT NULL,
    last_name VARCHAR(50) NOT NULL,
    ticket_number VARCHAR(20) NOT NULL UNIQUE
);

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Eliminates passenger redundancy, tracking individual ticket numbers.

ğŸ“Œ Creating the 'bookings' Table

CREATE TABLE bookings (
    booking_id INT PRIMARY KEY AUTO_INCREMENT,
    passenger_id INT NOT NULL,
    flight_id INT NOT NULL,
    confirmation_number VARCHAR(10) NOT NULL UNIQUE,
    FOREIGN KEY (passenger_id) REFERENCES passengers(passenger_id),
    FOREIGN KEY (flight_id) REFERENCES flights(flight_id)
);

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Links passengers to flights, ensuring valid bookings.

ğŸ“Œ Creating the 'airports' Table
CREATE TABLE airports (
    airport_code VARCHAR(10) PRIMARY KEY,
    airport_name VARCHAR(100) NOT NULL,
    city VARCHAR(50) NOT NULL
);

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Standardizes airport information, preventing duplication.

ğŸ“Œ Creating the 'pricing' Table
CREATE TABLE pricing (
    price_id INT PRIMARY KEY AUTO_INCREMENT,
    booking_id INT NOT NULL,
    ticket_price DECIMAL(6,2) NOT NULL,
    FOREIGN KEY (booking_id) REFERENCES bookings(booking_id)
);

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Tracks financial records for individual travelers, preventing inconsistencies.




ğŸ“Œ 3. Establishing Relationships
âœ” Passengers book flights â†’ Linked via 'bookings'.  
âœ” Flights depart and arrive at airports â†’ Referenced via airport codes.  
âœ” Pricing depends on booking records â†’ Ensures accurate costs.  

ğŸ“Œ Table Relationships Summary
| Entity       | Relation Type | Linked Entity |
|--------------|---------------|------------|
| 'passengers' | One-to-Many   | 'bookings' |
| 'flights'    | One-to-Many   | 'bookings' |
| 'bookings'   | One-to-One    | 'pricing' |
| 'flights'    | Many-to-One   | 'airports' |

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Ensures structured data access, avoiding duplication.



ğŸ“Œ 4. Key Takeaways
âœ” Structured database ensures logical flight booking data management.  
âœ” Foreign keys establish relationships between passengers, flights, and pricing.  
âœ” Airport codes standardize location references, ensuring accuracy.  
âœ” Following normalization eliminates redundancy, simplifying updates.  

ğŸš€ Next steps: Enhancing queries for efficient booking retrieval.




ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” Flight booking systems require structured tables to store passengers, flights, bookings, airports, and pricing.  
âœ” Using relational tables improves efficiency and data integrity.  
âœ” Foreign keys establish relationships ensuring reliable queries.  
âœ” Normalization prevents duplication, making retrieval seamless.
















Solution - Conceptual Model:
----------------------------
ğŸš€ Conceptual Model for a Flight Booking System
A conceptual model defines the key entities and their relationships within a flight booking system. It provides a high-level overview of the system before transitioning to a more detailed logical model.



ğŸ“Œ 1. Identified Entities
âœ” Passenger â†’ Represents travelers booking flights.  
âœ” Flight â†’ Defines flight details (departure, arrival, airline).  
âœ” Airport â†’ Stores airport codes and location details.  
âœ” Booking â†’ Tracks ticket purchases, prices, and flight classes.  

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Ensures structured relationships between flights, passengers, and bookings.



ğŸ“Œ 2. Defining Entities and Attributes
âœ” Step 1: Define entities and their key attributes.  
âœ” Step 2: Establish relationships between them.

ğŸ“Œ Passenger Entity
| Attribute  | Description    |
|------------|----------------|
| Name       | Travelerâ€™s full name (e.g., John Smith). |

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Stores basic passenger details needed for booking a flight.


ğŸ“Œ Flight Entity
| Attribute          | Description |
|--------------------|----------------|
| Flight Number      | Unique identifier for a flight (e.g., Flight 1490). |
| Departure DateTime | Scheduled departure time. |
| Arrival DateTime   | Scheduled arrival time. |
| Duration           | Flight duration (e.g., 1h 15m). |
| Distance           | Flight distance (e.g., 236 miles). |
| Airline            | Operator of the flight (e.g., Alaska Airlines). |

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Stores detailed flight attributes, ensuring structured tracking.


ğŸ“Œ Airport Entity
| Attribute  | Description |
|------------|----------------|
| IATA Code  | Three-letter airport code (e.g., LAX for Los Angeles Intl Airport). |
| Name       | Full airport name (e.g., Los Angeles Intl Airport). |
| City       | City where the airport is located. |
| State      | State where the airport is located. |

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Ensures airport standardization, avoiding duplication.


ğŸ“Œ Booking Entity
| Attribute           | Description |
|---------------------|----------------|
| Ticket Number       | Unique identifier per booking (e.g., 0177200658). |
| Price               | Amount paid by the passenger (e.g., $357.60). |
| Flight Class        | Cabin class (Economy, Business, First Class). |
| Confirmation Number | Airline-issued booking confirmation (e.g., TAEGKX). |

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Links passengers to flights, storing pricing and ticket details.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Establishing Relationships
âœ” Passengers purchase flight tickets, connecting bookings to travelers.  
âœ” Flights depart and arrive at specific airports, ensuring accurate tracking.  
âœ” Bookings are linked to both passengers and flights, maintaining structured reservations.

ğŸ“Œ Defining Relationships
| Entity | Relationship | Linked Entity |
|------------|---------------|------------|
| 'Passenger' | Purchases | 'Booking' |
| 'Booking' | Issued for | 'Flight' |
| 'Flight' | Departs/Arrives at | 'Airport' |

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Structured mapping enables efficient query execution.





ğŸ“Œ 4. Key Takeaways
âœ” Conceptual models define entities before transitioning to logical models.  
âœ” Ensuring structured relationships prevents redundant attributes.  
âœ” Airline, airport, and flight details must be stored independently for scalability.  
âœ” Following a conceptual model makes database development systematic.  

ğŸš€ Next steps: Refining this conceptual model into a logical model with normalized tables.



ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” Flight booking systems require structured conceptual modeling.  
âœ” Passengers, flights, bookings, and airports define essential entities.  
âœ” Relationships ensure proper data structuring and avoid redundancy.  
âœ” Conceptual modeling is the foundation for transitioning into logical database design.












Solution - Logical Model:
-------------------------
ğŸš€ Logical Model for a Flight Booking System
A logical model refines the conceptual model by specifying relationships, data types, and entity attributes, preparing for physical database implementation.



ğŸ“Œ 1. Refining Relationships
âœ” Step 1: Adjust relationships for correct one-to-many structures.  
âœ” Step 2: Ensure entities reflect logical associations.

ğŸ“Œ Updated Relationships
| Entity      | Relationship Type | Linked Entity |
|-------------|-------------------|------------|
| 'Passenger' | One-to-Many       | 'Ticket' |
| 'Ticket'    | One-to-Many       | 'Flight' |
| 'Flight'    | One-to-Many       | 'Airline' |
| 'Flight'    | Many-to-Many      | 'Airport' |
| 'Ticket'    | One-to-Many       | 'Flight Class' |

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Ensures passengers book multiple tickets while flights manage multiple passengers.



ğŸ“Œ 2. Refining Attributes and Data Types
âœ” Step 1: Assign correct data types for attributes.  
âœ” Step 2: Enhance naming conventions for clarity.

ğŸ“Œ Entity Attributes
| Entity      | Attributes                               | Data Type |
|-------------|------------------------------------------|------------|
| Passenger   | 'first_name', 'last_name'                | 'VARCHAR(50)' |
| Ticket      | 'ticket_number'                          | 'VARCHAR(20)' |
| Booking     | 'confirmation_number'                    | 'VARCHAR(10)' |
| Flight      | 'flight_number'                          | 'VARCHAR(10)' |
| Flight      | 'departure_datetime', 'arrival_datetime' | 'DATETIME' |
| Flight      | 'duration_minutes'                       | 'INT' |
| Flight      | 'distance_miles'                         | 'INT' |
| Airline     | 'name'                                   | 'VARCHAR(100)' |
| Airport     | 'iata_code', 'name', 'city', 'state' | 'VARCHAR' |

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Proper data types enhance query efficiency and ensure structured data.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Handling Normalization vs. Denormalization
âœ” Step 1: Prevent excessive joins by denormalizing airport attributes.  
âœ” Step 2: Ensure maintainability without unnecessary fragmentation.

ğŸ“Œ Why Denormalize?
âœ” Joining multiple tables for airport lookups slows queries.  
âœ” Few airports exist globally, so minor duplication isn't an issue.  
âœ” Keeping 'city' and 'state' in 'airports' improves performance.

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Optimizes airport lookups without impacting database scalability.




ğŸ“Œ 4. Adjusting Many-to-Many Relationships
âœ” Step 1: Recognize relational database constraintsâ€”only one-to-one or one-to-many exist.  
âœ” Step 2: Convert many-to-many (Flight & Airport) into two one-to-many relationships.

ğŸ“Œ Updated Flight-Airport Relationship
âœ” Each flight involves exactly two airports (departure & arrival).  
âœ” Using a link table allows unnecessary associations with multiple airports.

ğŸ“Œ Solution: Use Attributes Instead of a Link Table
ALTER TABLE flights ADD COLUMN departure_airport_id INT NOT NULL;
ALTER TABLE flights ADD COLUMN arrival_airport_id INT NOT NULL;

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Maintains correct logicâ€”each flight has exactly two airports.




ğŸ“Œ 5. Key Takeaways
âœ” Logical models refine conceptual designs, ensuring optimized relationships.  
âœ” Denormalization improves query performance when dealing with limited datasets.  
âœ” Handling relational constraints requires breaking many-to-many relationships properly.  
âœ” Each solution has trade-offsâ€”database designs evolve based on business needs.  

ğŸš€ Next steps: Building the physical model for actual implementation.




ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” Logical models define structured table relationships, enhancing query efficiency.  
âœ” Adjusting relationships ensures scalability while preventing redundant data storage.  
âœ” Denormalization balances performance when excessive joins impact lookup speed.  
âœ” Following correct relational structures ensures clarity and maintainability.

Vidly - Requirements:
Weâ€™re going to build a desktop application called Vidly. This application will be used at a video rental store. We need different levels of permissions for different users.
The store manager should be able to add/update/delete the list of movies. They will be in charge of setting the stock for each movie as well as the daily rental rate. Cashiers should have a read-only view of the list of movies. They should be able to manage the list of customers and the movies they rent. 

At check out, a customer brings one or more movies. The cashier looks up a customer by their phone number. If the customer is a first-time customer, the cashier asks their full name, email and phone number, and then registers them in the system. The cashier then scans the movies the customer has brought to check out and records them in the system. Each movie has a 10 digit barcode printed on the cover.


When the customer returns to the store, theyâ€™ll bring the movies they rented. If a movie is lost, the customer should be charged 5 times the daily rental rate of the movie. The cashier should mark the movie as lost and this will reduce the stock. There is no need to keep track of the lost movies. All we need to know is the the number of movies in stock and how much the customer was charged.


For other movies, the customer should be charged based on the number of days and the daily rental rate.

We issue discount coupons from time to time. The customer can bring a coupon when returning the movies.

It is possible that a customer returns the movies theyâ€™ve rented in multiple visits.

We need to be able to track the
- top movies
- top customers
- revenue per day, month and year





ğŸš€ Designing a Data Model for Vidly â€“ A Video Rental Application
Vidly is a desktop application for a video rental store, requiring structured customer management, movie rentals, pricing, and reporting. This data model ensures efficient operations and accurate tracking.



ğŸ“Œ 1. Key Functional Requirements
âœ” User Permissions
   - Store manager: Full access to manage movies (add/update/delete stock, set rental rate).
   - Cashier: Read-only access to movies, full access to customer and rental management.

âœ” Customer Registration
   - Look up customers by phone number.
   - First-time customers must provide full name, email, and phone number.

âœ” Movie Rentals
   - Each movie has a barcode (10-digit identifier).
   - Customers rent one or more movies at checkout.
   - Return process tracks charges based on rental duration or loss.

âœ” Pricing and Lost Movies
   - Rental charge based on number of days rented and daily rental rate.
   - Lost movies incur 5x the daily rental rate.
   - Lost movies reduce stock but donâ€™t need separate tracking.

âœ” Discount Coupons
   - Customers can use coupons upon returning movies.

âœ” Tracking & Reporting
   - Top movies rented.
   - Top customers based on rentals.
   - Revenue tracking per day, month, and year.




ğŸ“Œ 2. Identified Entities
âœ” Users â†’ Manages permissions for store managers and cashiers.  
âœ” Customers â†’ Stores customer details.  
âœ” Movies â†’ Contains movie details (barcode, stock, rental rate).  
âœ” Rentals â†’ Tracks transactions when customers rent movies.  
âœ” Returns â†’ Stores return details, calculating rental charges.  
âœ” Coupons â†’ Stores issued discount coupons.  
âœ” Reports â†’ Tracks top movies, top customers, and revenue.

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Ensures structured data relationships, preventing redundant information.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Tables and Their Attributes
âœ” Step 1: Define tables and attributes.  
âœ” Step 2: Establish relationships via foreign keys.

ğŸ“Œ Creating the 'users' Table
'''sql
CREATE TABLE users (
    user_id INT PRIMARY KEY AUTO_INCREMENT,
    full_name VARCHAR(100) NOT NULL,
    email VARCHAR(255) NOT NULL UNIQUE,
    phone VARCHAR(15) NOT NULL UNIQUE,
    role ENUM('manager', 'cashier') NOT NULL
);
'''
ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Manages user roles, ensuring controlled access permissions.

ğŸ“Œ Creating the 'customers' Table
'''sql
CREATE TABLE customers (
    customer_id INT PRIMARY KEY AUTO_INCREMENT,
    full_name VARCHAR(100) NOT NULL,
    email VARCHAR(255) NOT NULL UNIQUE,
    phone VARCHAR(15) NOT NULL UNIQUE
);
'''
ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Tracks customers efficiently, allowing lookup by phone number.

ğŸ“Œ Creating the 'movies' Table
'''sql
CREATE TABLE movies (
    movie_id INT PRIMARY KEY AUTO_INCREMENT,
    title VARCHAR(255) NOT NULL,
    barcode VARCHAR(10) NOT NULL UNIQUE,
    stock INT NOT NULL DEFAULT 0,
    daily_rental_rate DECIMAL(5,2) NOT NULL
);
'''
ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Manages available stock and pricing for rentals.

ğŸ“Œ Creating the 'rentals' Table
'''sql
CREATE TABLE rentals (
    rental_id INT PRIMARY KEY AUTO_INCREMENT,
    customer_id INT NOT NULL,
    movie_id INT NOT NULL,
    rental_date DATETIME NOT NULL DEFAULT CURRENT_TIMESTAMP,
    expected_return_date DATETIME NOT NULL,
    FOREIGN KEY (customer_id) REFERENCES customers(customer_id),
    FOREIGN KEY (movie_id) REFERENCES movies(movie_id)
);
'''
ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Tracks active rentals, storing expected return dates.

ğŸ“Œ Creating the 'returns' Table
'''sql
CREATE TABLE returns (
    return_id INT PRIMARY KEY AUTO_INCREMENT,
    rental_id INT NOT NULL,
    actual_return_date DATETIME NOT NULL DEFAULT CURRENT_TIMESTAMP,
    late_days INT NOT NULL DEFAULT 0,
    charge DECIMAL(6,2) NOT NULL,
    lost_movie BOOLEAN DEFAULT FALSE,
    FOREIGN KEY (rental_id) REFERENCES rentals(rental_id)
);
'''
ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Tracks return process, calculates late charges.

ğŸ“Œ Creating the 'coupons' Table
'''sql
CREATE TABLE coupons (
    coupon_id INT PRIMARY KEY AUTO_INCREMENT,
    code VARCHAR(50) NOT NULL UNIQUE,
    discount_percentage DECIMAL(5,2) NOT NULL,
    valid_until DATE NOT NULL
);
'''
ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Enables coupon-based discounts on movie rentals.

ğŸ“Œ Creating the 'reports' Table
'''sql
CREATE TABLE reports (
    report_id INT PRIMARY KEY AUTO_INCREMENT,
    report_type ENUM('top_movies', 'top_customers', 'revenue') NOT NULL,
    generated_on DATETIME DEFAULT CURRENT_TIMESTAMP
);
'''
ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Stores statistical insights for business tracking.




ğŸ“Œ 4. Establishing Relationships
âœ” Customers rent multiple movies â†’ Linked via 'rentals'.  
âœ” Movies have rental histories â†’ Ensured through 'rentals' and 'returns'.  
âœ” Lost movies reduce stock â†’ Updated in the 'returns' process.  
âœ” Coupons apply discounts â†’ Referenced during 'returns' processing.  
âœ” Reports summarize revenue & rental data â†’ Generated dynamically.

ğŸ“Œ Defining Relationships
| Entity | Relationship | Linked Entity |
|------------|---------------|------------|
| 'Customers' | One-to-Many | 'Rentals' |
| 'Rentals' | One-to-One | 'Returns' |
| 'Movies' | One-to-Many | 'Rentals' |
| 'Returns' | One-to-One | 'Coupons' (optional) |
| 'Reports' | Summarizes | 'Movies', 'Customers', 'Revenue' |

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Ensures scalable management of rentals and revenue tracking.




ğŸ“Œ 5. Key Takeaways
âœ” Structured relationships ensure accurate rental tracking.  
âœ” Coupon discounts are applied during the return process.  
âœ” Lost movie charges dynamically affect stock management.  
âœ” Data reporting enables business insights into rentals and revenue trends.  

ğŸš€ Next steps: Building the physical model for implementation.




ğŸ“Œ 6. Summary
âœ” Vidlyâ€™s database model efficiently tracks customers, movies, rentals, returns, and reports.  
âœ” Establishing foreign key relationships ensures structured data retrieval.  
âœ” Normalization optimizes stock management and rental transactions.  
âœ” Reports provide insights into business performance and revenue trends.

âœ” Movie â†’ Contains movie details (stock, barcode, rental rate).

âœ” Ensures structured relationships between users, movies, customers, and transactions.
