âœ” They outperform traditional 'LIKE' queries in efficiency and flexibility.  
âœ” Advanced search techniques like Boolean mode and relevance ranking improve results.  
âœ” Using full-text indexing ensures scalable and high-performance text searches.














Composite Indexes: 
------------------
ğŸš€ Composite Indexes in MySQL for Optimized Query Performance
A composite index allows MySQL to index multiple columns, improving search efficiency for queries with multiple filtering conditions. Composite indexes help avoid unnecessary table scans and speed up complex searches.



ğŸ“Œ 1. Why Use Composite Indexes?
âœ” Single-column indexes only optimize part of a query, requiring table scans for additional filters.  
âœ” Composite indexes optimize queries with multiple filtering conditions, reducing read time.  
âœ” Efficient indexing ensures MySQL retrieves records without scanning unnecessary rows.

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Speeds up filtering on multiple criteria while minimizing storage overhead.

ğŸ“Œ Example Query Without a Composite Index
'''sql
EXPLAIN SELECT customer_id FROM customers 
WHERE state = 'CA' AND points > 1000;
'''
ğŸš€ Issue:  
âœ” MySQL picks a single-column index ('state') but must scan all customers in California manually for 'points > 1000'.  
âœ” Scanning 112 rows slows performance when dealing with large datasets.





ğŸ“Œ 2. Creating a Composite Index
âœ” Step 1: Identify frequently used filtering columns.  
âœ” Step 2: Create a composite index on those columns.

ğŸ“Œ Creating a Composite Index on 'state' and 'points'
'''sql
CREATE INDEX idx_state_points ON customers (state, points);
'''
ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Optimizes searches for customers located in a given state with specific points criteria.

ğŸ“Œ Verifying Query Improvement
'''sql
EXPLAIN SELECT customer_id FROM customers 
WHERE state = 'CA' AND points > 1000;
'''
ğŸš€ Result:  
âœ” Reduces scanned rows from '112' to '58', improving efficiency.  





ğŸ“Œ 3. Why Composite Indexes Are Better Than Single-Column Indexes
âœ” MySQL picks only one index per query, ignoring other single-column indexes.  
âœ” A composite index ensures MySQL optimally searches across multiple conditions instead of performing table scans.  
âœ” Single-column indexes waste space when used redundantly, slowing write operations ('INSERT', 'UPDATE', 'DELETE').  

ğŸ“Œ Dropping Inefficient Single-Column Indexes
'''sql
DROP INDEX idx_state ON customers;
DROP INDEX idx_points ON customers;
'''
ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Reduces storage usage while optimizing multi-filter queries.



ğŸ“Œ 4. Determining the Optimal Number of Columns in a Composite Index
âœ” MySQL supports up to '16' columns per composite index (highly excessive in most cases).  
âœ” Best practice suggests '4â€“6 columns' for balanced performance and storage.  
âœ” Always test composite indexes on real queries to determine effectiveness.

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Prevents unnecessary indexing while ensuring optimal search speeds.





ğŸ“Œ 5. Key Takeaways
âœ” Composite indexes optimize multi-condition queries, preventing slow table scans.  
âœ” Dropping redundant single-column indexes improves storage efficiency.  
âœ” Balancing the number of indexed columns prevents excessive overhead.  
âœ” Always test indexes using 'EXPLAIN' to confirm improvements.  





Queries and other notes in this discussion: 


USE sql_store; 

SHOW INDEXES IN customers; 

EXPLAIN select customer_id FROM customers 
WHERE state = 'CA' AND points > 1000;


CREATE INDEX idx_state_points ON customers (state, points);

EXPLAIN select customer_id FROM customers 
WHERE state = 'CA' AND points > 1000;


DROP INDEX idx_state ON customers; 
DROP INDEX idx_points ON customers; 




ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” Composite indexes outperform single-column indexes in multi-filter searches.  
âœ” Using 'EXPLAIN' helps verify index effectiveness and query improvements.  
âœ” Choosing an optimal number of indexed columns enhances search speed.  
âœ” Efficient indexing strategies prevent performance bottlenecks in large databases.














Order of Columns in Composite Indexes:
--------------------------------------
ğŸš€ Optimizing Column Order in Composite Indexes in MySQL
Composite indexes improve query efficiency, but the order of columns plays a crucial role in optimization. Choosing the right column order ensures faster filtering and minimal table scans.



ğŸ“Œ 1. Guidelines for Column Order in Composite Indexes
âœ” Frequently used columns should come first.  
âœ” Columns with higher cardinality (more unique values) should be prioritized.  
âœ” Always test indexes based on query performance rather than general rules.  

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Optimizes query execution by reducing unnecessary scans.




ğŸ“Œ 2. Understanding Cardinality in Indexing
âœ” Cardinality represents the number of unique values in an indexed column.  
âœ” Higher cardinality means better filtering capabilities.  

ğŸ“Œ Checking Cardinality of Columns
'''sql
SELECT 
    COUNT(DISTINCT state) AS state_cardinality,
    COUNT(DISTINCT last_name) AS last_name_cardinality
FROM customers;
'''
ğŸš€ Example Result:
| Column      | Unique Values (Cardinality) |
|-------------|----------------|
| 'state'     | '48' |
| 'last_name' | '996' |

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Although 'last_name' has higher cardinality, query patterns must be considered.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Testing Composite Index Order
âœ” First, create a composite index with 'last_name' first.

ğŸ“Œ Creating Composite Index ('last_name' â†’ 'state')
'''sql
CREATE INDEX idx_lastname_state ON customers (last_name, state);
'''

ğŸ“Œ Evaluating Performance Using 'EXPLAIN'
'''sql
EXPLAIN SELECT customer_id 
FROM customers
WHERE state = 'CA' AND last_name LIKE 'A%';
'''
ğŸš€ Result:  
âœ” MySQL scans '40' rows, filtering by 'last_name' first, then 'state'.

âœ” Second, create a composite index with 'state' first.

ğŸ“Œ Creating Composite Index ('state' â†’ 'last_name')
'''sql
CREATE INDEX idx_state_lastname ON customers (state, last_name);
'''

ğŸ“Œ Evaluating Performance Using 'EXPLAIN'
'''sql
EXPLAIN SELECT customer_id 
FROM customers
WHERE state = 'CA' AND last_name LIKE 'A%';
'''
ğŸš€ Result:  
âœ” MySQL scans only '7' rowsâ€”better optimization.  

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Putting 'state' first reduced scans significantly, proving it is the better index for this query.  





ğŸ“Œ 4. Forcing MySQL to Use a Specific Index
âœ” Even though MySQL selects the most efficient index, users can force an alternative index.  

ğŸ“Œ Explicitly Selecting an Index
'''sql
EXPLAIN SELECT customer_id 
FROM customers
USE INDEX (idx_lastname_state)
WHERE state = 'NY' AND last_name LIKE 'A%';
'''
ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Forces MySQL to use 'idx_lastname_state', even if another index is more optimal.  





ğŸ“Œ 5. Handling Queries with Multiple Range Filters
âœ” If a query includes a range filter ('LIKE', '<', '>'), column order can impact efficiency.  

ğŸ“Œ Example Query Using Two Range Filters
'''sql
EXPLAIN SELECT customer_id 
FROM customers
WHERE state LIKE 'A%' AND last_name LIKE 'A%';
'''
ğŸš€ Result Comparison:
- Using 'idx_lastname_state' â†’ Scans '40' rows.  
- Using 'idx_state_lastname' â†’ Scans '51' rows.  

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” For range-based queries, column order must be tested case-by-case.  
âœ” However, range queries are rare in real-world applications, so focus indexing on most common queries.  






ğŸ“Œ 6. Dropping Unnecessary Indexes
âœ” After testing, drop inefficient indexes to free storage.

ğŸ“Œ Removing the Less Efficient Index
'''sql
DROP INDEX idx_lastname_state ON customers;
'''
ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Keeps only the best-performing index for filtering customers efficiently.  





ğŸ“Œ 7. Key Takeaways
âœ” Column order in composite indexes affects query performance significantly.  
âœ” Higher cardinality columns typically come firstâ€”but query logic matters more.  
âœ” Always use 'EXPLAIN' to test query execution before finalizing an index order.  
âœ” Removing redundant indexes prevents storage waste and optimizes MySQL performance.  





Queries and other notes in this discussion: 

order of columns: 
put the most frequently used columns first 
put the columns with a higher cardinality first 
Take your queries into account


cardinality represents the number of unique values in the index.

SELECT 
	COUNT(DISTINCT state),
	COUNT(DISTINCT last_name)
FROM customers; 


CREATE INDEX idx_lastname_state ON customers (last_name, state);

EXPLAIN SELECT customer_id 
FROM customers
WHERE state = 'CA' AND last_name LIKE 'A%';  -- 40 rows scanned


CREATE INDEX idx_state_lastname ON customers (state, last_name);

EXPLAIN SELECT customer_id 
FROM customers
WHERE state = 'CA' AND last_name LIKE 'A%'; -- 7 rows scanned 


EXPLAIN SELECT customer_id 
FROM customers
WHERE state = 'NY' AND last_name LIKE 'A%';


EXPLAIN SELECT customer_id 
FROM customers
USE INDEX (idx_lastname_state)  -- forcing MySQL to use the mentioned index 
WHERE state = 'NY' AND last_name LIKE 'A%';


EXPLAIN SELECT customer_id 
FROM customers
USE INDEX (idx_lasname_state)    -- mysql had to scan 40 records
WHERE state LIKE 'A%' AND last_name LIKE 'A%';


EXPLAIN SELECT customer_id 
FROM customers
USE INDEX (idx_state_lastname)   -- mysql had to scan 51 records 
WHERE state LIKE 'A%' AND last_name LIKE 'A%';



DROP INDEX idx_lastname_state ON customers;







ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” Composite index order should be based on query logic, NOT just cardinality.  
âœ” Query analysis using 'EXPLAIN' helps determine the most efficient index order.  
âœ” Dropping redundant indexes improves storage efficiency.  
âœ” Optimized composite indexing ensures high-speed query execution for large datasets.



















When Indexes are Ignored:
-------------------------
ğŸš€ When Indexes Are Ignored in MySQL Queries
Indexes significantly enhance query performance, but there are scenarios where indexes may be ignored or fail to optimize queries. Understanding these cases helps refine query execution strategies.



ğŸ“Œ 1. Why Indexes Might Be Ignored
âœ” Using 'OR' Conditions prevents optimal index utilization.  
âœ” Columns inside expressions limit MySQL's ability to use indexes efficiently.  
âœ” Poor index selection leads to full index scans instead of filtered lookups.

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” May cause MySQL to perform unnecessary scans, slowing query execution.



ğŸ“Œ 2. Case 1: Index Ignored Due to 'OR' Conditions
âœ” Query using 'OR' forces MySQL to scan the entire index, impacting performance.  

ğŸ“Œ Query With 'OR' Operator
'''sql
EXPLAIN SELECT customer_id FROM customers 
WHERE state = 'CA' OR points > 1000;
'''
ğŸš€ Issue:  
âœ” MySQL performs a full index scan, scanning '1010' rows instead of filtering efficiently.  

ğŸ“Œ Optimizing with 'UNION' Instead of 'OR'
âœ” Break the query into two separate selections and combine using 'UNION'.  

'''sql
CREATE INDEX idx_points ON customers (points);

EXPLAIN
SELECT customer_id FROM customers 
WHERE state = 'CA' 
UNION 
SELECT customer_id FROM customers 
WHERE points > 1000;
'''
ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Breaks the query into smaller searches, utilizing appropriate indexes efficiently.  
âœ” Reduces scanned rows from '1010' to '640' for improved performance.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Case 2: Index Ignored Due to Column Inside an Expression
âœ” Using arithmetic operations in a 'WHERE' clause prevents index utilization.  

ğŸ“Œ Inefficient Query
'''sql
EXPLAIN SELECT customer_id FROM customers 
WHERE points + 10 > 2010;
'''
ğŸš€ Issue:  
âœ” MySQL scans all '1010' rows despite having an index on 'points'.  

ğŸ“Œ Optimizing by Isolating the Column
âœ” Rewrite the query to remove the arithmetic operation inside the condition.  

'''sql
EXPLAIN SELECT customer_id FROM customers 
WHERE points > 2000;
'''
ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Reduces scanned rows from '1010' to '3', enabling MySQL to use the index optimally.  





ğŸ“Œ 4. Best Practices to Ensure Index Utilization
âœ” Avoid placing indexed columns inside expressions ('points + 10 > X').  
âœ” Rewrite 'OR' conditions as 'UNION' queries to optimize filtering with multiple indexes.  
âœ” Use 'EXPLAIN' to analyze query execution and confirm index usage.  
âœ” Ensure indexes align with query logic rather than general assumptions.  

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Guarantees efficient index usage, minimizing database resource consumption.





ğŸ“Œ 5. Key Takeaways
âœ” Indexes improve query performance but can be ignored under certain conditions.  
âœ” Using 'OR' conditions prevents efficient index utilizationâ€”use 'UNION' instead.  
âœ” Columns inside expressions disable index filteringâ€”rewrite queries to isolate columns.  
âœ” Optimized query structure reduces unnecessary index scans, improving performance.  





Queries and other notes in this discussion: 


EXPLAIN SELECT customer_id FROM customers 
WHERE state = 'CA' OR points > 1000;


CREATE INDEX idx_points ON customers (points);
EXPLAIN
	SELECT customer_id FROM customers 
	WHERE state = 'CA' 
	UNION 
	SELECT customer_id FROM customers 
	WHERE points > 1000


EXPLAIN SELECT customer_id FROM customers
WHERE points + 10 > 2010;


EXPLAIN SELECT customer_id FROM customers
WHERE points > 2000;








ğŸ“Œ 6. Summary
âœ” MySQL indexes can be ignored due to certain query conditions (like 'OR' or expressions).  
âœ” Breaking queries into optimized structures ensures efficient index utilization.  
âœ” Using 'EXPLAIN' helps diagnose indexing problems and refine queries.  
âœ” Proper query design ensures fast and scalable database performance.





















Using Indexes for Sorting:
--------------------------
ğŸš€ Using Indexes for Sorting in MySQL
Indexes not only speed up filtering queries but can also significantly improve sorting performance. Proper indexing strategies help avoid costly sorting operations and enhance database efficiency.



ğŸ“Œ 1. How Indexes Help with Sorting
âœ” MySQL automatically stores indexed column values in sorted order.  
âœ” If a query orders data based on an indexed column, MySQL retrieves the records directly without extra sorting.  
âœ” Sorting using indexes avoids expensive 'filesort' operations, improving efficiency.

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Enhances query speed by eliminating unnecessary computation costs.

ğŸ“Œ Example Query: Sorting by an Indexed Column ('state')
'''sql
EXPLAIN SELECT customer_id FROM customers ORDER BY state;
'''
ğŸš€ Result:  
âœ” Uses an index scan ('Using index'), efficiently sorting the data.  

ğŸ“Œ Example Query: Sorting by a Non-Indexed Column ('first_name')
'''sql
EXPLAIN SELECT customer_id FROM customers ORDER BY first_name;
'''
ğŸš€ Issue:  
âœ” Triggers a full table scan ('Using filesort'), making the operation costly.  





ğŸ“Œ 2. Analyzing Query Cost Using 'SHOW STATUS'
âœ” Use 'SHOW STATUS LIKE 'last_query_cost'' to measure query execution cost.  
âœ” Sorting on indexed columns costs significantly less than sorting using 'filesort'.

ğŸ“Œ Measuring Query Cost
'''sql
SHOW STATUS LIKE 'last_query_cost';
'''
ğŸš€ Example Cost Comparison:
- Sorting by 'state' (indexed) â†’ Query cost ~'100'
- Sorting by 'first_name' (non-indexed) â†’ Query cost ~'1112'

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Sorting using indexes is ~10x more efficient than using 'filesort' on large tables.  





ğŸ“Œ 3. Sorting Multiple Columns Using a Composite Index
âœ” Sorting works best when index columns match the 'ORDER BY' clause exactly.  

ğŸ“Œ Sorting by 'state' and 'points' (matching composite index)
'''sql
EXPLAIN SELECT customer_id FROM customers ORDER BY state, points;
'''
ğŸš€ Result:  
âœ” Uses index scan ('Using index'), ensuring efficient sorting.  

ğŸ“Œ Adding an unrelated column ('first_name') into sorting
'''sql
EXPLAIN SELECT customer_id FROM customers ORDER BY state, first_name, points;
'''
ğŸš€ Issue:  
âœ” Triggers a full table scan ('Using filesort'), reducing efficiency.  





ğŸ“Œ 4. Sorting in Different Directions
âœ” Sorting mixed directions ('ASC' and 'DESC') prevents full index usage, forcing MySQL to use 'filesort'.  

ğŸ“Œ Sorting by 'state' and 'points' in descending order
'''sql
EXPLAIN SELECT customer_id FROM customers ORDER BY state DESC, points DESC;
'''
ğŸš€ Result:  
âœ” Uses backward index scan ('Using index'), maintaining efficiency.  

ğŸ“Œ Sorting by 'state ASC, points DESC' (mixing directions)
'''sql
EXPLAIN SELECT customer_id FROM customers ORDER BY state ASC, points DESC;
'''
ğŸš€ Issue:  
âœ” Triggers 'filesort', making query costly.  

ğŸš€ Rule:  
âœ” Indexes work for sorting when both columns use the same direction ('ASC' or 'DESC').  





ğŸ“Œ 5. Sorting the Second Column of an Index
âœ” Sorting by a second column ('points') alone without filtering by the first column ('state') forces MySQL to perform a 'filesort'.  

ğŸ“Œ Sorting by 'points' alone
'''sql
EXPLAIN SELECT customer_id FROM customers ORDER BY points;
'''
ğŸš€ Issue:  
âœ” Uses 'filesort', making query expensive.  

ğŸ“Œ Optimizing by filtering the first column ('state')
'''sql
EXPLAIN SELECT customer_id FROM customers WHERE state = 'CA' ORDER BY points;
'''
ğŸš€ Result:  
âœ” No 'filesort' neededâ€”MySQL retrieves pre-sorted data efficiently.  

ğŸš€ Rule:  
âœ” Sorting by a second column within a specific first-column filter allows index usage.  





ğŸ“Œ 6. Best Practices for Index-Based Sorting
âœ” Avoid sorting unless necessaryâ€”sorting is computationally expensive.  
âœ” Ensure sorting columns match an existing composite index to prevent 'filesort' operations.  
âœ” When filtering, include the first indexed column to allow sorting optimization.  
âœ” Use 'EXPLAIN' to check whether an index is being used for sorting.  

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Prevents unnecessary full table scans and improves query speed.  





ğŸ“Œ 7. Key Takeaways
âœ” Indexes help MySQL sort data efficiently without performing costly 'filesort' operations.  
âœ” Queries should match index column order to avoid performance degradation.  
âœ” Sorting mixed directions ('ASC', 'DESC') can force MySQL to ignore indexes.  
âœ” Filtering the first column in a composite index allows optimized sorting for the second column.  




Queries and other notes in this discussion: 

EXPLAIN SELECT customer_id FROM customers ORDER BY state; 

EXPLAIN SELECT customer_id FROM customers ORDER BY first_name;

SHOW STATUS LIKE 'last_query_cost'; -- Last_query_cost 1112.749000

EXPLAIN SELECT customer_id FROM customers ORDER BY state, points DESC -- Using index 

EXPLAIN SELECT customer_id FROM customers ORDER BY state, points DESC; --Using index, Using filesort

EXPLAIN SELECT customer_id FROM customers ORDER BY state DESC, points DESC;  -- Backward index scan, using index 


EXPLAIN SELECT customer_id FROM customers ORDER BY points;  -- Using index, Using filsort

EXPLAIN SELECT customer_id FROM customers WHERE state = 'CA' ORDER BY points; -- using where, using index 


-- (a, b)
-- a
-- a, b
-- a DESC, b DESC

-- a, c, b (results in full table scan)
-- b (results in full scan








ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” Using indexes for sorting improves efficiency and prevents resource-intensive table scans.  
âœ” Matching index order with sorting columns ensures optimal performance.  
âœ” Sorting operations should be carefully structured to avoid unnecessary computation costs.  
âœ” Strategic index design enhances scalability for complex database queries.  











Covering Indexes:
-----------------
ğŸš€ Covering Indexes in MySQL for Optimal Query Performance
A covering index is an index that contains all columns required to satisfy a query, allowing MySQL to retrieve data solely from the index without accessing the full table.



ğŸ“Œ 1. What Is a Covering Index?
âœ” A covering index includes all necessary columns for filtering, sorting, and selection.  
âœ” Queries using a covering index execute entirely within the index, avoiding costly table scans.  
âœ” These indexes improve performance by reducing disk I/O operations.

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Fastest possible query execution since MySQL does not access the full table.




ğŸ“Œ 2. Example Query Using a Covering Index
âœ” Selecting 'customer_id' while sorting by 'state' allows MySQL to use an index efficiently.

ğŸ“Œ Using 'EXPLAIN' to Analyze the Query
'''sql
EXPLAIN SELECT customer_id FROM customers ORDER BY state;
'''
ğŸš€ Result:  
âœ” MySQL performs an index scan ('Using index'), meaning no table access is needed.  

ğŸ“Œ Checking Query Execution Cost
'''sql
SHOW STATUS LIKE 'last_query_cost';
'''
ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Low query cost confirms efficient execution.





ğŸ“Œ 3. Issue with Selecting All Columns ('SELECT *')
âœ” Selecting all columns forces MySQL to read the entire table, invalidating the covering index.  
âœ” Even though an index exists for 'state', MySQL cannot use it to retrieve all columns efficiently.

ğŸ“Œ Query That Causes a Full Table Scan
'''sql
EXPLAIN SELECT * FROM customers ORDER BY state;
'''
ğŸš€ Issue:  
âœ” Triggers a full table scan ('Using filesort'), increasing query cost dramatically.  





ğŸ“Œ 4. Components of a Covering Index
âœ” Primary key ('customer_id') â†’ Automatically included in secondary indexes.  
âœ” Filtering columns ('WHERE' conditions) â†’ Helps narrow down search results.  
âœ” Sorting columns ('ORDER BY' conditions) â†’ Allows efficient sorting within the index.  
âœ” Selected columns ('SELECT clause') â†’ Must be included for a true covering index.

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Enables MySQL to fully satisfy the query from the index without reading the table.





ğŸ“Œ 5. Best Practices for Designing Covering Indexes
âœ” Ensure filtering columns are included in the index to optimize searches.  
âœ” Sort data using indexed columns to avoid expensive 'filesort' operations.  
âœ” Include necessary selection columns in the index to create a covering index.  
âœ” Use 'EXPLAIN' to validate whether an index fully satisfies a query.  

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Balances performance optimization and storage efficiency.





ğŸ“Œ 6. Key Takeaways
âœ” Covering indexes prevent unnecessary table access, improving query performance.  
âœ” Using 'SELECT *' forces MySQL to scan the full table, negating index benefits.  
âœ” Filtering, sorting, and selecting indexed columns ensure efficient query execution.  
âœ” Checking query cost ('SHOW STATUS') confirms index optimization success.  




Queries and other notes in this discussion:


EXPLAIN SELECT customer_id FROM customers ORDER BY state; 

SHOW STATUS LIKE 'last_query_cost';

EXPLAIN SELECT * FROM customers ORDER BY state; -- full table scan





ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” Covering indexes enable efficient query execution by eliminating table access.  
âœ” Strategic index design improves filtering, sorting, and data retrieval.  
âœ” Avoiding unnecessary full table scans enhances database speed and scalability.  
âœ” Proper index management ensures sustainable performance across large datasets.  










Index Maintenance:
------------------
ğŸš€ Index Maintenance in MySQL: Managing Duplicate and Redundant Indexes
Indexes significantly improve query performance, but too many or improperly managed indexes can lead to unnecessary storage usage and slow write operations. Duplicate and redundant indexes should be eliminated for optimal database efficiency.



ğŸ“Œ 1. What Are Duplicate Indexes?
âœ” Duplicate indexes occur when two indexes have the exact same columns in the same order.  
âœ” MySQL does not prevent duplicate indexes, causing wasted storage and unnecessary maintenance.  

ğŸ“Œ Example of Duplicate Indexes
| Index Name   | Column Order | Duplicate? |
|--------------|----------------|----------------|
| 'idx_abc'    | ('A, B, C') | âŒ No |
| 'idx_duplicate' | ('A, B, C') | âœ… Yes |

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Duplicate indexes consume storage unnecessarily and slow database performance.

ğŸ“Œ Best Practice:  
âœ” Check existing indexes before creating new ones to avoid duplication.





ğŸ“Œ 2. What Are Redundant Indexes?
âœ” Redundant indexes happen when an index contains all columns of another index in the same order.  
âœ” If an index exists on ('A, B'), a separate index on 'A' is redundant because the composite index covers queries on 'A'.  

ğŸ“Œ Example of Redundant Indexes
| Index Name | Column Order | Redundant? |
|------------|-------------|------------|
| 'idx_ab' | ('A, B') | âŒ No |
| 'idx_a' | ('A') | âœ… Yes (redundant) |
| 'idx_ba' | ('B, A') | âŒ No |
| 'idx_b' | ('B') | âŒ No |

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Creates unnecessary index maintenance overhead, impacting write operations.  

ğŸ“Œ Best Practice:  
âœ” Extend existing indexes instead of creating redundant ones.  





ğŸ“Œ 3. Index Maintenance Best Practices
âœ” Regularly review indexes using 'SHOW INDEXES'.  
âœ” Eliminate duplicate indexes to save storage and improve performance.  
âœ” Remove redundant indexes and consolidate where possible.  
âœ” Monitor unused indexesâ€”drop them if they no longer serve query optimization.  

ğŸ“Œ Checking Existing Indexes
'''sql
SHOW INDEXES FROM customers;
'''
ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Provides insight into existing indexes, helping eliminate unnecessary duplicates.  





ğŸ“Œ 4. Key Takeaways
âœ” Duplicate indexes waste space and should be removed.  
âœ” Redundant indexes slow updates and should be consolidated.  
âœ” Always check existing indexes before creating new ones.  
âœ” Optimizing index maintenance improves MySQL performance and reduces storage overhead.  





Queries and other notes in this discussion:


Duplicate indexes:

(A, B, C)
(A, B, C) --duplicate

Redundant Indexes: 
(A, B)
(A) --  redundant 
(B, A) -- not redundant
(B)   -- not redundant




ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” Proper index management ensures a fast and efficient database.  
âœ” Duplicate indexes should be avoided to prevent unnecessary maintenance.  
âœ” Redundant indexes should be consolidated to reduce storage consumption.  
âœ” Regular index analysis improves query execution and database health.  









Performance Best Pratices:
--------------------------
Here is a summary of all the performance best practices we discussed in this
course. This list is not complete by any means, but it contains guidelines that can
help solve a lot of performance problems. If youâ€™re interested in learning more
about this topic, take a look at the additional resources later in this document.

1. Smaller tables perform better. Donâ€™t store the data you donâ€™t need. Solve todayâ€™s
problems, not tomorrowâ€™s future problems that may never happen.
2. Use the smallest data types possible. If you need to store peopleâ€™s age, a
TINYINT is sufficient. No need to use an INT. Saving a few bytes is not a big
deal in a small table, but has a significant impact in a table with millions of
records.
3. Every table must have a primary key.
4. Primary keys should be short. Prefer TINYINT to INT if you only need to store a
hundred records.
5. Prefer numeric types to strings for primary keys. This makes looking up records
by the primary key faster.
6. Avoid BLOBs. They increase the size of your database and have a negative
impact on the performance. Store your files on disk if you can.
7. If a table has too many columns, consider splitting it into two related tables
using a one-to-one relationship. This is called vertical partitioning. For
example, you may have a customers table with columns for storing their
address. If these columns donâ€™t get read often, split the table into two tables
(users and user_addresses).
8. In contrast, if you have several joins in your queries due to data fragmentation,
you may want to consider denormalizing data. Denormalizing is the opposite of
normalization. It involves duplicating a column from one table in another table
(to reduce the number of joins) required
9. Consider creating summary/cache tables for expensive queries. For example, if
the query to fetch the list of forums and the number of posts in each forum is
expensive, create a table called forums_summary that contains the list of
forums and the number of posts in them. You can use events to regularly refresh
the data in this table. You may also use triggers to update the counts every time
there is a new post.
10.Full table scans are a major cause of slow queries. Use the EXPLAIN statement
and look for queries with type = ALL. These are full table scans. Use indexes to
optimize these queries.
11. When designing indexes, look at the columns in your WHERE clauses first.
Those are the first candidates because they help narrow down the searches.
Next, look at the columns used in the ORDER BY clauses. If they exist in the
index, MySQL can scan your index to return ordered data without having to
perform a sort operation (filesort). Finally, consider adding the columns in the
SELECT clause to your indexes. This gives you a covering index that covers
everything your query needs. MySQL doesnâ€™t need to retrieve anything from
your tables.
12.Prefer composite indexes to several single-column index.
13. The order of columns in indexes matter. Put the most frequently used columns
and the columns with a higher cardinality first, but always take your queries
into account.
14.Remove duplicate, redundant and unused indexes. Duplicate indexes are the
indexes on the same set of columns with the same order. Redundant indexes are
unnecessary indexes that can be replaced with the existing indexes. For
example, if you have an index on columns (A, B) and create another index on
column (A), the latter is redundant because the former index can help.
15. Donâ€™t create a new index before analyzing the existing ones.
16. Isolate your columns in your queries so MySQL can use your indexes.
17. Avoid SELECT *. Most of the time, selecting all columns ignores your indexes
and returns unnecessary columns you may not need. This puts an extra load on
your database server.
18.Return only the rows you need. Use the LIMIT clause to limit the number of
rows returned.
19.Avoid LIKE expressions with a leading wildcard (eg LIKE â€˜%nameâ€™).
20.If you have a slow query that uses the OR operator, consider chopping up the
query into two queries that utilize separate indexes and combine them using the
UNION operator.














Securing Databases:
-------------------

Throughout this course, we have been using MySQL on our own computer. And for this very reason, we didn't have to worry about security in terms of who can access our data and how much access they should have. However, in a real world scenario, you typically have a database somewhere on a server, and if you don't take security seriously, people can access and misuse your data. So in this section, we're going to talk about user accounts and privileges for securing databases. Are you ready? Let's get started.





Creating a User:
----------------
ğŸš€ Creating a User in MySQL
Creating user accounts in MySQL allows controlled database access, ensuring security and privilege management in production environments.



ğŸ“Œ 1. Why Create Additional Users?
âœ” Limit access for applications â†’ Prevent altering the database structure.  
âœ” Grant administrative privileges â†’ Allow database management for authorized users.  
âœ” Restrict connections â†’ Ensure users can only access MySQL from specific locations.  

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Enhances security by preventing unauthorized modifications to the database.  





ğŸ“Œ 2. Creating a User
âœ” Use the 'CREATE USER' statement to define a new MySQL user.  
âœ” Specify optional connection restrictions based on IP or hostname.  
âœ” Set strong passwords for security (avoid simple passwords like '1234').  

ğŸ“Œ Creating a User
'''sql
CREATE USER john IDENTIFIED BY 'SecurePassword123!';
'''
ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Creates 'john' with a secure password, allowing unrestricted access from any location.  






ğŸ“Œ 3. Restricting User Connections
âœ” Limit access to a specific IP address ('127.0.0.1') â†’ Ensures local connections only.  
âœ” Use 'localhost' to restrict access to the same machine.  
âœ” Specify a domain to allow access from multiple locations within an organization.  

ğŸ“Œ Examples of User Restrictions
'''sql
CREATE USER john@127.0.0.1 IDENTIFIED BY 'SecurePassword123!';
CREATE USER john@localhost IDENTIFIED BY 'SecurePassword123!';
CREATE USER john@'codewithmosh.com' IDENTIFIED BY 'SecurePassword123!';
CREATE USER john@'%.codewithmosh.com' IDENTIFIED BY 'SecurePassword123!';
'''
ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Controls where 'john' can connect from, preventing unauthorized remote access.  





ğŸ“Œ 4. Best Practices for User Management
âœ” Use strong passwords to prevent unauthorized logins.  
âœ” Regularly review user permissions to ensure proper access levels.  
âœ” Restrict connections to trusted IPs or domains for enhanced security.  
âœ” Check existing users before creating new ones to prevent unnecessary duplication.  

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Ensures proper security measures while maintaining database integrity.  





ğŸ“Œ 5. Key Takeaways
âœ” Creating MySQL users allows controlled access management.  
âœ” Restricting connections prevents unauthorized database interactions.  
âœ” Strong passwords and privilege management enhance security.  
âœ” Always verify existing users before adding new accounts.  





Queries and other notes in this discussion: 

CREATE USER john@127.0.0.1

CREATE USER john@localhost

CREATE USER john@codewithmosh.com

CREATE USER john@'%.codewithmosh.com'

CREATE USER john

CREATE USER john IDENTIFIED BY '1234';





ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” Adding user accounts prevents direct access with the root user.  
âœ” Restricting connections improves security by limiting access points.  
âœ” Implementing strong passwords prevents unauthorized logins.  
âœ” Managing MySQL users ensures database protection and controlled interactions.

--------------
ğŸš€ Viewing Users in MySQL
Understanding how to list MySQL user accounts ensures proper access control and user management within the database server.



ğŸ“Œ 1. Methods to View Users in MySQL

âœ” Ensures visibility into database users and their connection permissions.

âœ” MySQL stores user account details in the 'mysql.user' system table.  

ğŸ“Œ Querying User Accounts
'''sql

'''
ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Displays all existing user accounts, including their host restrictions and privileges.  

ğŸ“Œ Understanding Key Columns in 'mysql.user'
| Column | Description |
|------------|----------------|

| 'host' | Specifies where the user can connect from. |

| Various permission columns | Determine database privileges. |

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Helps identify user access levels and security configurations.  





ğŸ“Œ 3. Host Restrictions in MySQL Users
âœ” Users can be restricted to connect only from certain IPs or hosts.  
âœ” Wildcards ('%') allow unrestricted access from any location.  

ğŸ“Œ Example: Viewing Host Restrictions
| User | Host | Access Scope |
|-----------|-----------|----------------|
| 'john' | '%' | Can connect from any location. |
| 'root' | 'localhost' | Can only connect from this computer. |

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Prevents unauthorized remote access for sensitive user accounts like 'root'.  





ğŸ“Œ 4. Viewing Users in MySQL Workbench
âœ” Step 1: Open the Administration Panel.  
âœ” Step 2: Navigate to Users and Privileges.  
âœ” Step 3: View and manage user accounts visually.  

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Allows easy management of user accounts without running SQL queries.  





ğŸ“Œ 5. Best Practices for Managing MySQL Users
âœ” Regularly review user accounts to ensure security.  
âœ” Restrict the 'root' user to localhost for better protection.  
âœ” Assign specific privileges instead of full administrative access.  
âœ” Avoid using '%' (wildcard) unless necessary for application access.  

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Improves database security and minimizes risks of unauthorized access.  





ğŸ“Œ 6. Key Takeaways
âœ” Use 'SELECT * FROM mysql.user;' to list all MySQL users.  
âœ” Check host restrictions to control where users can connect from.  
âœ” MySQL Workbench provides an intuitive interface for user management.  
âœ” Ensuring proper user access improves overall database security.  




Queries and other notes in this discussion:

ğŸ“Œ 7. Summary
âœ” Viewing MySQL users helps manage database access and security.  
âœ” Using the 'mysql.user' table or Workbench allows structured user auditing.

âœ” Proper user management ensures database integrity and scalability.  














Dropping Users:
---------------

ğŸš€ Dropping Users in MySQL for Access Management
As individuals leave an organization or their access becomes unnecessary, removing their user accounts ensures database security and prevents unauthorized access.



ğŸ“Œ 1. Why Drop Unused User Accounts?
âœ” Prevents former employees from accessing the database.  
âœ” Reduces security vulnerabilities by limiting active accounts.  
âœ” Keeps MySQL user management clean and efficient.  

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Strengthens security by ensuring only authorized users have access.  




ğŸ“Œ 2. Creating a User (Before Dropping)
âœ” Use the 'CREATE USER' statement to add a new user.  

ğŸ“Œ Example: Creating a User ('bob@codewithmosh.com')
'''sql

'''
ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Adds 'bob' as an authorized user with access from 'codewithmosh.com'.  





ğŸ“Œ 3. Dropping a User
âœ” Use 'DROP USER' to remove accounts when they are no longer needed.  
âœ” Specify the exact host to ensure correct user removal.  

ğŸ“Œ Example: Removing 'bob@codewithmosh.com'
'''sql
DROP USER bob@codewithmosh.com;
'''
ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Ensures 'bob' can no longer access the MySQL server from 'codewithmosh.com'.  



ğŸ“Œ 4. Best Practices for User Management
âœ” Periodically audit user accounts to remove unnecessary access.  
âœ” Restrict user privileges to the minimum required for their role.  
âœ” Review login permissions to ensure compliance with security policies.  
âœ” Ensure that critical accounts (e.g., 'root') are protected against unauthorized modifications.  

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Maintains database integrity while enforcing strong security controls.  



ğŸ“Œ 5. Key Takeaways
âœ” Dropping users is essential for managing access and protecting sensitive data.  
âœ” Always specify the correct host when removing a user to avoid unintended deletion.  
âœ” Regularly review active users to prevent unauthorized access.  
âœ” Proper user management enhances overall MySQL database security.  





Queries and other notes in this discussion:

DROP USER bob@codewithmosh.com;





ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” Removing inactive users prevents unauthorized access.  
âœ” Proper user maintenance ensures database security and organization efficiency.  
âœ” Executing 'DROP USER' removes unnecessary accounts, reducing security risks.  
âœ” Regular audits help keep user access tightly controlled.

ğŸš€ Changing Passwords in MySQL for User Security

ğŸ“Œ 1. Why Change MySQL Passwords?
âœ” Reset passwords for users who forget them.

âœ” Force password changes upon login to ensure fresh credentials.

âœ” Maintains strict security protocols for database access.  





ğŸ“Œ 2. Changing a User's Password (Admin Reset)
âœ” Use the 'SET PASSWORD' statement to update credentials.  
âœ” Specify the user whose password is being modified.  

ğŸ“Œ Example: Updating 'john''s Password
'''sql

'''
ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Immediately updates Johnâ€™s password, allowing secure login.  





ğŸ“Œ 3. Changing Your Own Password
âœ” Administrators or users can change their own passwords without specifying a username.  
âœ” No need for the 'FOR' clauseâ€”modifies the password for the currently logged-in user.  

ğŸ“Œ Example: Changing Your Own Password
'''sql

'''
ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Modifies the password of the active user session securely.

ğŸ“Œ Additional Security Features:

ğŸ“Œ 5. Best Practices for Password Management
âœ” Use strong passwordsâ€”avoid simple credentials like '1234'.  
âœ” Regularly update passwords to prevent unauthorized access.  
âœ” Restrict access to administrative accounts to trusted personnel.  
âœ” Audit user credentials periodically to ensure compliance.  

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Improves overall database security while maintaining operational control.  





ğŸ“Œ 6. Key Takeaways

Granting Privileges:
--------------------
ğŸš€ Granting Privileges in MySQL
Assigning privileges in MySQL ensures controlled database access, allowing users or applications to interact securely without unnecessary permissions.



ğŸ“Œ 1. Why Grant Privileges?
âœ” Limit database access for applications â†’ Prevent structure modifications.  
âœ” Provide administrative privileges for DB admins â†’ Allow full management of databases.  
âœ” Restrict user actions based on role â†’ Ensure security and controlled access.  

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Protects databases from unauthorized modifications and security risks.  





ğŸ“Œ 2. Scenario 1: Granting Privileges to an Application
âœ” Applications should only have permissions to read and write data (not modify tables).  
âœ” Use 'GRANT' to assign necessary privileges to specific databases or tables.  

ğŸ“Œ Step 1: Create an Application User
'''sql
CREATE USER moon_app IDENTIFIED BY 'SecurePassword123!';
'''
ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Creates a user ('moon_app') with a secure password for database access.  

ğŸ“Œ Step 2: Assign Limited Privileges for Data Operations
'''sql
GRANT SELECT, INSERT, UPDATE, DELETE, EXECUTE 
ON sql_store.*
TO moon_app;
'''
ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Allows 'moon_app' to perform read/write operations in 'sql_store', but prevents table modifications.  





ğŸ“Œ 3. Testing Access for 'moon_app'
âœ” Step 1: Create a new connection using 'moon_app' credentials.  
âœ” Step 2: Connect and run queries in the 'sql_store' database.  
âœ” Step 3: Attempt accessing another database ('sql_blog') â†’ Should return an access denied error.  

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Confirms that 'moon_app' has restricted access only to authorized databases.  





ğŸ“Œ 4. Scenario 2: Granting Administrative Privileges
âœ” Admins require broader privileges to manage databases and create objects.  
âœ” Assigning 'ALL PRIVILEGES' provides full access to database management.  

ğŸ“Œ Granting Full Privileges to an Admin ('john')
'''sql
GRANT ALL 
ON *.*
TO john;
'''
ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Provides 'john' full administrative access across all databases and tables.  

ğŸ“Œ Restricting Admin Access to a Specific Database
'''sql
GRANT ALL 
ON sql_store.*
TO john;
'''
ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Limits 'john''s administrative control to only the 'sql_store' database.  





ğŸ“Œ 5. Checking MySQL Privileges
âœ” Use MySQL's reference manual to review supported privileges ('CREATE TABLE', 'ALTER TABLE', 'EXECUTE', etc.).  
âœ” Verify user permissions using SQL queries or MySQL Workbench.  

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Ensures proper role management and database security policies.  





ğŸ“Œ 6. Best Practices for Privilege Management
âœ” Limit privileges to the minimum necessary for each user.  
âœ” Avoid granting 'ALL PRIVILEGES' unless required for an admin.  
âœ” Regularly audit user permissions to maintain security.  
âœ” Use strong passwords to prevent unauthorized access.  

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Prevents accidental or malicious database modifications while maintaining functionality.  





ğŸ“Œ 7. Key Takeaways
âœ” Use 'GRANT' to control user access to databases and tables.  
âœ” Applications should only have read/write permissionsâ€”not table modification rights.  
âœ” Admins can receive full privileges using 'GRANT ALL ON *.*'.  
âœ” Checking and managing privileges regularly improves security and database integrity.  




Queries and other notes in this discussion:

-- 1. web/desktop application

CREATE USER moon_app IDENTIFIED BY '1234';

GRANT SELECT, INSERT, UPDATE, DELETE, EXECUTE 
ON sql_store.*
TO moon_app;

-- 2. admin 

GRANT ALL 
ON *.*
TO john;






ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” Granting privileges ensures users have only the necessary permissions to interact with databases.  
âœ” Application accounts should be restricted to read/write operations for security.  
âœ” Admin users can be granted 'ALL PRIVILEGES' for complete management control.  
âœ” Regular privilege reviews help prevent unauthorized database access.

ğŸ“Œ 1. Why View User Privileges?

âœ” Ensure users have the correct database privileges for their roles.

ğŸ“Œ Summary

Revoking Privileges:
--------------------
ğŸš€ Revoking Privileges in MySQL for Secure User Access
After granting privileges in MySQL, you may need to revoke access due to errors, security risks, or improper permissions. Removing unnecessary privileges ensures controlled database security and prevents unauthorized actions.



ğŸ“Œ 1. Why Revoke Privileges?
âœ” Correct mistakes when granting privileges.  
âœ” Prevent users from abusing excessive access rights.  
âœ” Improve security by restricting unnecessary privileges.  

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Ensures users only have permissions essential for their roles.  




ğŸ“Œ 2. Revoking Privileges in MySQL
âœ” Use 'REVOKE' instead of 'GRANT' to remove privileges.  
âœ” Modify privileges based on changing security or role requirements.  

ğŸ“Œ Example: Removing 'CREATE VIEW' Privilege for 'moon_app'
'''sql
REVOKE CREATE VIEW 
ON sql_store.*
FROM moon_app;
'''
ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Removes 'moon_app''s ability to create views in the 'sql_store' database.  

ğŸ“Œ Granting the Privilege Before Revoking
'''sql
GRANT CREATE VIEW 
ON sql_store.*
TO moon_app;
'''
ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Allows 'moon_app' to create views before the privilege is revoked.  





ğŸ“Œ 3. Best Practices for Privilege Management
âœ” Grant the minimum necessary privileges to each userâ€”avoid excessive access.  
âœ” Regularly review privileges to prevent misuse or security risks.  
âœ” Restrict administrative actions to trusted personnel only.  
âœ” Use 'SHOW GRANTS FOR username;' to verify privileges before revoking.  

ğŸš€ Effect:  
âœ” Maintains database integrity while reinforcing security protocols.  





ğŸ“Œ 4. Key Takeaways
âœ” Revoking privileges is essential for maintaining proper access control.  
âœ” Always verify granted privileges before modifying user permissions.  
âœ” Minimizing unnecessary privileges strengthens overall MySQL security.  
âœ” Regular privilege audits prevent security vulnerabilities.  





Queries and other notes in this discussion: 

GRANT CREATE VIEW 
ON sql_store.*
TO moon_app;


REVOKE CREATE VIEW 
ON sql_store.*
FROM moon_app;



ğŸ“Œ Summary
âœ” Revoking privileges prevents unauthorized modifications and accidental access.  
âœ” Using 'REVOKE' ensures users only have the necessary permissions.  
âœ” Security-focused privilege management prevents data breaches.  
âœ” Proper user role configuration optimizes MySQL security and performance.
